# Parsifal: The Search for the Grail
# 帕西法爾：尋找聖杯

**Charles Kovacs 著｜英中雙語逐段對照**

## Foreword 前言

British authors who have written of the great legends of King Arthur and the Knights of the Round Table have said little of the story of Parsifal. For Thomas Malory it is Gawain who holds a central place, with Parsifal (Sir Percival) relatively unimportant; others have concentrated on the romantic tales of Lancelot and Guinevere.

書寫亞瑟王與圓桌騎士偉大傳說的英國作家們，對帕西法爾的故事著墨甚少。在湯馬斯·馬洛里的作品中，佔據核心位置的是高文，帕西法爾（珀西瓦爾爵士）相對不重要；其他作家則多專注於蘭斯洛特與乘妮薇兒的浪漫故事。

And whilst acknowledging the British origin of these legends, it is to the troubadours of France and Germany, notably Chrétien de Troyes and Wolfram von Eschenbach, that we need to turn for a complete rendering of the wonderful story of Parsifal.

儘管承認這些傳說源自英國，但要獲得帕西法爾這個美妙故事的完整呈現，我們必須轉向法國和德國的行吟詩人，尤其是克雷蒂安·德·特魯瓦和沃爾夫拉姆·馮·艾申巴赫。

Rudolf Steiner recommended this story for pupils of Class 11 in the Waldorf education curriculum (age 16-17 years) as a way of introducing them to world literature and to one of the central problems of our time — the journey of the consciousness soul and the imperative to learn to ask the right questions. Like Parsifal they will all go through their own individual way of finding the Grail as a requirement of living in this century.

魯道夫·施泰納推薦將這個故事用於華德福教育課程的十一年級（16至17歲），作為引領學生進入世界文學的途徑，也是面對我們這個時代核心課題的方式——意識靈魂的旅程，以及學會提出正確問題的迫切性。如同帕西法爾，他們都將以各自獨特的方式去尋找聖杯，這是活在這個世紀的必修課題。

This publication has come about in response to requests from other interested teachers. It is taken from original notes written in 1965 as preparation for a Class 11 main lesson.

本書的出版源於其他感興趣的教師們的請求。內容取自1965年為十一年級主課程所撰寫的原始筆記。

---

## 1. Minnesingers and Troubadors 吟遊詩人與行吟歌手

In these lessons I am going to tell you the story of Parsifal. It is a very old story — going back to the twelfth and thirteenth centuries — and that brings me to the first question we have to ask: why should anyone today bother about an ancient tale — a tale from the Middle Ages, a tale of knights and castles and swordfights? What can a story of medieval knights mean to us who live in the present age of computers and space travel?

在這些課程中，我要為你們講述帕西法爾的故事。這是一個非常古老的故事——可以追溯到十二和十三世紀——這就帶出了我們必須問的第一個問題：為什麼今天的人要關心一個古老的傳說——一個來自中世紀的故事，一個關於騎士、城堡和劍戰的故事？一個中世紀騎士的故事，對於我們這些生活在電腦和太空旅行時代的人，能有什麼意義？

Well, perhaps it is a good story — and a good story is always worth telling. After all, Shakespeare's plays were also not written yesterday — yet they have the power to move the hearts of people nowadays. Great works of poetry don't age — they are above fashions which come and go — they span the centuries, they are bridges between past and present. And the Parsifal story, as it was composed some 800 years ago, is one of these great works of poetry.

嗯，也許它是一個好故事——而好故事永遠值得被講述。畢竟，莎士比亞的戲劇也不是昨天寫的——然而它們至今仍有力量打動人心。偉大的詩歌作品不會老去——它們超越來來去去的時尚潮流——它們跨越世紀，是過去與現在之間的橋梁。而帕西法爾的故事，約在八百年前寫就，正是這樣一部偉大的詩歌作品。

Yet — this isn't why I want to tell you this story. If it were only a matter of making you acquainted with some great work of literature outside the English language, I might as well have chosen the German poet Goethe's tragedy Faust — a play about a man who sells his soul to the devil. Or, perhaps, the Italian poet Dante's Divine Comedy — which is not a comedy at all, but the description of a journey into the depths of hell and to the heights of heaven. As great creations of literature, they are just as important as the Parsifal story. But there is a different reason for choosing Parsifal.

然而——這並不是我要為你們講這個故事的原因。如果只是為了讓你們認識英語以外的某部文學巨著，我大可以選擇德國詩人歌德的悲劇《浮士德》——一個關於把靈魂賣給魔鬼的人的故事。或者，也許可以選擇義大利詩人但丁的《神曲》——它根本不是喜劇，而是對一趟深入地獄深處、直上天堂之巔的旅程的描寫。作為偉大的文學創作，它們與帕西法爾的故事一樣重要。但選擇帕西法爾，有一個不同的理由。

And to give you this reason for taking Parsifal, I want to tell you of two events which actually happened — they are not stories invented by a poet's mind.

為了告訴你們選擇帕西法爾的這個理由，我想講述兩件真實發生過的事件——它們不是詩人腦海中虛構的故事。

The first event took place during the Second World War in America. It was about 1943 or 1944, and by that time science fiction had become very popular. Quite a number of writers specialised in this field, and had their stories published in one or other of the science fiction periodicals. One of these writers sent in a story which was promptly published — a story about a new kind of weapon. But as soon as this magazine appeared in the bookstalls, the American FBI — the Federal Police — confiscated the whole issue and arrested the author. He was accused of a breach of state security because the weapon he had described in his story was the atom bomb. At that time the atom bomb was still a closely guarded military secret — the first experiments at Los Alamos were still under way, and not a whisper of it had yet reached the public — and yet here was this writer who had described the main principle of it, the fusion of uranium, in his science fiction story.

第一件事發生在第二次世界大戰期間的美國。大約是1943年或1944年，那時科幻小說已經非常流行。不少作家專門從事這個領域，在各種科幻期刊上發表作品。其中一位作家投了一篇故事並很快被發表了——一個關於新型武器的故事。但這本雜誌一出現在書攤上，美國聯邦調查局——聯邦警察——就沒收了全部刊物，並逮捕了作者。他被指控洩露國家機密，因為他在故事中描述的武器就是原子彈。當時原子彈仍是嚴格保守的軍事機密——洛斯阿拉莫斯的首批實驗仍在進行中，公眾還一無所知——然而這位作家竟在他的科幻小說中描述了原子彈的基本原理——鈾的裂變。

The poor man was in danger of a stiff prison sentence — but, fortunately for him, he could prove that he knew nothing at all about the Los Alamos experiments and that the story he had written was only the product of his own imagination. He had not betrayed any secrets, because he had never been told any. So he was released in the end with a stern warning, and only years later, when the secret was a secret no longer, could he tell how he barely escaped from a trial for high treason.

這個可憐的人面臨嚴厲的監禁刑罰——但幸運的是，他能證明自己對洛斯阿拉莫斯的實驗一無所知，他所寫的故事完全是自己想像力的產物。他沒有洩露任何機密，因為從來沒有人告訴過他任何機密。因此他最終在一番嚴厲警告後被釋放了，直到多年以後，當這個秘密不再是秘密時，他才能講述自己如何險些因叛國罪而受審。

For the second event we must go back a bit further — 2,500 years ago — to Athens in ancient Greece. It is a public holiday, and the people of Athens are gathered in the great amphitheatre to watch the first performance of a new play by their favourite playwright, Aeschylus. The author himself takes part in the play — he is one of the actors. The play begins — there is deep silence in the audience. And now Aeschylus steps forward and speaks his lines. But he is suddenly interrupted by a wild outcry from somebody in the audience: "He betrays the holy Mysteries! Stop him! Kill him!" And in the same moment, men with drawn swords storm on to the stage. Aeschylus runs from the stage — he escapes from his pursuers and reaches the temple of Dionysos. Here he has at least a temporary sanctuary because no fugitive can be taken from the temple.

第二件事我們必須再回溯一些——兩千五百年前——到古希臘的雅典。那是一個公共假日，雅典人民聚集在大型露天劇場，觀看他們最喜愛的劇作家埃斯庫羅斯的新劇首演。作者本人也參加了演出——他是演員之一。劇目開演了——觀眾席上一片寂靜。現在埃斯庫羅斯走上前來，開始說他的台詞。但他突然被觀眾席上某人的一聲狂呼打斷了：「他洩露了神聖的秘儀！阻止他！殺了他！」就在同一刻，手持出鞘之劍的人們衝上了舞台。埃斯庫羅斯從舞台上逃跑了——他擺脫了追趕者，到達了戴歐尼修斯神殿。在這裡他至少有了暫時的庇護，因為沒有逃亡者可以從神殿中被帶走。

And now Aeschylus asked for a proper trial before the judges of Athens. This was granted to him, and he stood trial for having betrayed the secrets of the Mysteries — a crime punishable by death. But, fortunately, Aeschylus could prove that he had never been initiated into the Mysteries, that he had never been told any of their secrets and so could not betray them. His play, and every word in it, was only the product of his own imagination. And so he was allowed to go free.

於是埃斯庫羅斯請求在雅典的法官面前接受正式審判。這個請求被准許了，他因洩露秘儀的秘密而受審——這是一項可處死刑的罪行。但幸運的是，埃斯庫羅斯能夠證明他從未接受過秘儀的入門啟蒙，從未被告知任何秘密，因此不可能洩露它們。他的劇作，以及其中的每一個字，都只是他自己想像力的產物。於是他被允許自由離去。

It is a strange parallel between these two events, isn't it? Even though they are separated by about 2,400 years. Yet, at either end of this long stretch of time there is the same situation: there is a closely guarded secret, known only to a small circle of men, and there is one person who — out of his own resources, out of his own ingenuity — discovers the secret for himself.

這兩件事之間有一個奇妙的平行，不是嗎？儘管它們相隔約兩千四百年。然而，在這漫長時間跨度的兩端，存在著相同的情境：有一個被嚴密守護的秘密，只有一小群人知道，而有一個人——憑藉自己的能力，憑藉自己的才智——獨自發現了這個秘密。

There is this striking similarity, but there is also a very striking, a very significant difference. The difference lies in the kind of secret which is so jealously guarded.

有這個驚人的相似之處，但也有一個非常驚人、非常重要的差異。差異在於被如此嚴密守護的秘密的種類。

What kind of secret was it that the Mystery temples of ancient Greece kept and which was only divulged to chosen men and women? In the Mystery temples, the seekers for truth were shown a way to higher worlds — to the worlds of the spirit. The secrets of the Mystery temples were secrets of the spirit.

古希臘的秘儀神殿所守護的、只向被選中的男女透露的，是什麼樣的秘密？在秘儀神殿中，真理的追尋者被引導通往更高的世界——精神的世界。秘儀神殿的秘密是精神的秘密。

And what kind of secrets are those which led to the atom bomb? The physicists, the scientists who in the end produced this monster weapon were, at first, not at all searching for a weapon of destruction. Their research had quite a different purpose. For a long time, science believed that all matter, all physical matter, was composed of atoms. Atom is a Greek word which means: indivisible, something that could not be divided. But, after the discovery of radium, it became quite clear that matter could be divided further — that the atom could be divided into electrons, neutrons, protons and photons, smaller particles known as, and that there are even, as we now know, particles of negative matter. The scientists worked on the secrets of matter. The atom bomb was a by-product of the search for the secrets of matter.

而導致原子彈的那些秘密又是什麼樣的呢？那些最終製造出這個怪物武器的物理學家和科學家們，起初根本不是在尋找一種毀滅性武器。他們的研究有著完全不同的目的。長期以來，科學相信所有物質、所有有形物質都由原子組成。原子（Atom）是一個希臘詞，意思是：不可分割的，無法被分開的東西。但是，在鐳被發現之後，情況變得十分清楚：物質可以被進一步分割——原子可以分成電子、中子、質子和光子等更小的粒子，而且正如我們現在所知，甚至還存在反物質粒子。科學家們研究的是物質的秘密。原子彈是探索物質秘密的副產品。

So you have in the Mysteries of ancient Greece, secrets — but they were secrets of the spirit — and you have in modern times, in the experiments of Los Alamos, secrets of matter. In the 2,400 years which lie between the two stories I have told you, humankind has gone from the secrets of the spirit to the secrets of matter. The science fiction writer got into trouble because he had "guessed" some of the secrets of matter; the Greek playwright got into trouble because he had guessed some of the secrets of the spirit.

所以在古希臘的秘儀中，有秘密——但那是精神的秘密——而在現代，在洛斯阿拉莫斯的實驗中，有的是物質的秘密。在我講述的這兩個故事之間的兩千四百年裡，人類從精神的秘密走向了物質的秘密。那位科幻小說作家惹上麻煩，是因為他「猜到了」物質的某些秘密；希臘劇作家惹上麻煩，是因為他猜到了精神的某些秘密。

There is this long stretch of time — 2,400 years — and at one end there are secrets of the spirit, and at the other end there are secrets of matter. And there is a third kind of secret in the middle between them. What kind of secret could it be?

在這漫長的時間跨度——兩千四百年——的一端是精神的秘密，另一端是物質的秘密。而在它們之間，還有第三種秘密。那會是什麼樣的秘密呢？

I would like to leave this question as a question. We shall, perhaps, find an answer to this question as we go on with these lessons. But there is, at least, a name for this third kind of secret. There was a time when people were deeply concerned with this third kind of question or secret — and they called this secret: the secret of the Holy Grail.

我想把這個問題留作一個問題。也許在我們繼續這些課程的過程中，我們會找到這個問題的答案。但至少，這第三種秘密有一個名字。曾經有一個時代，人們深切地關注這第三種問題或秘密——他們稱這個秘密為：聖杯的秘密。

And the story of Parsifal is the story of a man's search for the secret of the Holy Grail.

而帕西法爾的故事，就是一個人尋找聖杯秘密的故事。

And what is the time of the Parsifal story? The time of Parsifal lies roughly — very roughly — in the middle between the time of Aeschylus and the time of the atom bomb. The Parsifal story stands, one could say, between two ages: one — the age that looked for the secrets of the spirit — was coming to an end; the other age — that was going to search for the secrets of matter — was just about to begin.

帕西法爾故事發生在什麼時代？帕西法爾的時代大致上——非常粗略地——位於埃斯庫羅斯的時代和原子彈的時代之間。可以說，帕西法爾的故事站在兩個時代之間：一個時代——追尋精神秘密的時代——正在走向尾聲；另一個時代——將要探索物質秘密的時代——正要拉開序幕。

Now imagine some kind of superhuman intelligence looking down on earth at that time — a superhuman being that could look back into the past, and that could also foresee the future trend of human progress. And this being would ask itself: "Is it possible to save something of the old world for the new world? Is it possible to save the secrets of the spirit for the time that will only look for the secrets of matter?"

現在想像某種超人的智慧在那個時代俯瞰大地——一個能夠回顧過去，也能預見人類進步未來趨勢的超人存在。這個存在會自問：「有沒有可能從舊世界中為新世界保留一些什麼？有沒有可能為一個只會尋找物質秘密的時代保留精神的秘密？」

And in answer to this question, this being would invent the story of Parsifal — a story in which the secrets of the spirit are brought together with a modern mind, the kind of mind we have today. And Parsifal, the hero of this story, is really a modern man, the first modern man. A man who has a modern mind, even though outwardly he follows the customs of his time.

作為對這個問題的回答，這個存在會創造帕西法爾的故事——一個將精神的秘密與現代心智、我們今天所擁有的那種心智結合在一起的故事。而帕西法爾，這個故事的主角，實際上是一個現代人，第一個現代人。一個擁有現代心智的人，儘管外表上他遵循著他那個時代的習俗。

And, you see, this is the reason why we are doing the Parsifal story — because Parsifal is really a modern man, in spite of the medieval trappings. He stands on the threshold between two worlds.

而且，你看，這就是我們要講帕西法爾故事的原因——因為帕西法爾實際上是一個現代人，儘管披著中世紀的外衣。他站在兩個世界的門檻上。

I said that a superhuman intelligence might have thought up this story of Parsifal. I don't know if such a superhuman intelligence existed, but the fact is that around the year 1200 not one but several poets produced Parsifal stories. There are great differences between the stories — no version is exactly like the other, but the main features are the same.

我說過，也許是某種超人的智慧構思了帕西法爾的故事。我不知道這樣的超人智慧是否存在，但事實是，大約在1200年前後，不是一位，而是好幾位詩人創作了帕西法爾的故事。這些故事之間有很大的差異——沒有哪個版本與另一個完全相同，但主要的特徵是一致的。

And, you know, in their time these stories were what we would call "bestsellers" — they were extremely popular. "Bestsellers" is, of course, the wrong word. There were no publishers to print the story, because printing had not yet been invented. Moreover, most of the people who wanted to know the story could not read — they had to listen to professional storytellers who moved from place to place, from castle to castle. And the telling was not in prose — it was in verse. The stories were long poems which the storytellers recited — they even sang them, and used a harp as accompaniment. Some storytellers could only repeat what they had learned from others. But some could and did invent their own stories — they were called troubadours (from trouver = find).

而且，你知道嗎，在它們的時代，這些故事就是我們所說的「暢銷書」——它們極受歡迎。「暢銷書」當然是個不恰當的詞。沒有出版商來印刷這個故事，因為印刷術尚未發明。此外，大多數想知道這個故事的人不識字——他們必須聆聽四處遊走、從一座城堡到另一座城堡的職業說書人。而且講述不是用散文——而是用韻文。這些故事是長篇詩歌，說書人吟誦它們——他們甚至吟唱，並以豎琴伴奏。有些說書人只能重複從他人那裡學來的內容。但有些人能夠也確實創作自己的故事——他們被稱為行吟詩人（troubadour，源自 trouver = 發現、找到）。

The stories made up by the troubadours were all about knights and their deeds — King Arthur, Lancelot, Gawain. One of the first troubadours who introduced a knight called Parsifal was a Frenchman, Chrétien de Troyes. People were not so nationalistic in those times as they are today, and Chrétien de Troyes was quite content to have as his heroes British knights. King Arthur is, of course, a British king — and Parsifal, too, is British. He is called "Parsifal le Gallois" — Parsifal the Welshman.

行吟詩人所創作的故事全是關於騎士和他們的事蹟——亞瑟王、蘭斯洛特、高文。最早將一位名叫帕西法爾的騎士引入故事的行吟詩人之一是法國人克雷蒂安·德·特魯瓦。那個時代的人們不像今天這樣具有民族主義傾向，克雷蒂安·德·特魯瓦對以英國騎士作為他的主人公毫無異議。亞瑟王當然是一位英國國王——帕西法爾也是英國人。他被稱為「Parsifal le Gallois」——威爾斯人帕西法爾。

The next troubadour who composed a Parsifal story was a German knight, Wolfram von Eschenbach. Wolfram is a remarkable person. As a knight, he was trained and brought up to be a fighter, to know how to handle spear and sword. And so, when he describes a fight, he speaks with the authority of an expert. But the education of a knight did not include such useless arts as reading or writing. Wolfram actually mentions in one passage of the poem that he never learned a single letter of the alphabet. Perhaps he was being ironic.

下一位創作帕西法爾故事的行吟詩人是一位德國騎士，沃爾夫拉姆·馮·艾申巴赫。沃爾夫拉姆是一個非凡的人。作為一名騎士，他被訓練和培養成為一名戰士，懂得如何使用長矛和劍。因此，當他描述一場戰鬥時，他是以專家的權威在說話。但騎士的教育並不包括閱讀或書寫這類「無用的」技藝。沃爾夫拉姆在詩篇的一個段落中確實提到，他從未學過字母表中的任何一個字母。也許他是在說反話。

But this is a long poem — in modern print it covers some four hundred pages. It is so long that I would not even attempt to tell you the full story. You will have to be satisfied with a very abbreviated version, otherwise the story plus the necessary explanations and comments would take far more time than we have. I am afraid you would also be bewildered by the many other heroes and stories which are interwoven in the story of Parsifal. It is a cavalcade of his whole time which Wolfram presents, and you would hardly be able to cope with all the names of knights and their ladies and how their lives meet and cross the path of the main hero, Parsifal.

但這是一首長詩——以現代印刷排版來算，大約有四百頁。它如此之長，我甚至不會嘗試為你們講述完整的故事。你們只能滿足於一個大幅縮減的版本，否則故事加上必要的解釋和評論所需的時間將遠超我們所擁有的。恐怕你們也會被交織在帕西法爾故事中的許多其他英雄和故事搞得暈頭轉向。沃爾夫拉姆呈現的是他整個時代的壯闘行列，你們幾乎無法應付所有騎士及其夫人的名字，以及他們的生活如何與主角帕西法爾的道路相遇和交叉。

Medieval German is, of course, very different from modern German. For instance, Wolfram's words "I can't read a single letter" are in the original "ine kan decheinen buochstap", which even the best of you in German would not recognise.

中世紀德語，當然與現代德語非常不同。例如，沃爾夫拉姆說的「我一個字母也不識」，原文是「ine kan decheinen buochstap」，即使你們當中德語最好的人也認不出來。

*Wolfram's insistence that he was illiterate has long been a puzzle for scholars. It is unsafe to assume that he was indeed illiterate. He wrote other works beside Parzival, notably Titurel and several love lyrics. While it may not extend credulity too far to suppose that he dictated Parzival and Titurel to an amanuensis, the writing of love lyrics suggests that Wolfram was not incapable of taking up a pen himself. Otherwise, we have to imagine a monk so magnanimous as to make himself available to Wolfram for a great deal of his time. This seems unlikely to me, though I am not an expert in the practices of medieval monasteries. It is generally supposed that Wolfram was not rich, and that he died comparatively young. The usual interpretation of Parzival scholars is that Wolfram was exercising his gift of irony at the expense of other medieval poets who wore their learning less lightly than did Wolfram himself. — Peter Snow, Editor.*

*沃爾夫拉姆堅稱自己是文盲，這長期以來一直是學者們的一個謎團。假設他確實是文盲並不安全。除了《帕西法爾》之外，他還創作了其他作品，尤其是《提圖雷爾》和幾首情詩。雖然假設他將《帕西法爾》和《提圖雷爾》口述給抄寫員並非不可置信，但情詩的創作表明沃爾夫拉姆並非無力親自執筆。否則，我們就得想像有一位修士慷慨到願意將大量時間奉獻給沃爾夫拉姆。儘管我不是中世紀修道院慣例的專家，但這在我看來不太可能。一般認為沃爾夫拉姆並不富裕，而且相對年輕就去世了。帕西法爾學者們的通常解釋是，沃爾夫拉姆在發揮他諷刺的天賦，取笑那些不像他那樣輕鬆面對學問的其他中世紀詩人。——編者 彼得·斯諾*

There is still one more thing to be said about these long poems made up by troubadours like Wolfram and Chrétien de Troyes. They wrote or dictated these works for the entertainment of lords and ladies, and in this way they are the forerunners of the modern novelists. These stories were the novels, the popular fiction of the twelfth and thirteenth century.

關於沃爾夫拉姆和克雷蒂安·德·特魯瓦這樣的行吟詩人所創作的長詩，還有一件事值得一提。他們創作或口述這些作品是為了娛樂領主和夫人們，從這個意義上說，他們是現代小說家的先驅。這些故事就是十二和十三世紀的小說，當時的通俗文學。

But the troubadours aimed much higher than mere entertainment. They made these stories an instrument to convey their religious convictions, their highest ideals, their philosophy of life. Yet they managed at the same time to introduce humour, to go from tragic moments to comic situations, to touch the deepest problems of human life, and at the same time to relish the description of a good fight.

但行吟詩人的目標遠高於單純的娛樂。他們把這些故事變成了傳達宗教信念、最高理想和人生哲學的工具。然而他們同時也設法引入了幽默，從悲劇時刻轉到喜劇場面，觸及人類生活最深層的問題，同時又津津有味地描寫一場精彩的戰鬥。

It is this skilful blending which makes such a thing as Wolfram's Parsifal a true work of art, a great poem. And, as it is with every good novel, you should, after going through Parsifal, feel that you have learned something, not about a person who exists only in the pages of a book, but about yourself. That is what, in the end, all literature is about.

正是這種巧妙的融合，使得沃爾夫拉姆的《帕西法爾》成為真正的藝術作品，一首偉大的詩篇。而且，就像每一本好小說一樣，你在讀完帕西法爾之後，應該感覺自己學到了什麼——不是關於一個只存在於書頁中的人物，而是關於你自己。這，歸根結底，就是所有文學的意義所在。

---

## 2. The Boyhood of Parsifal 帕西法爾的少年時代

I have given you the general background of the Parsifal story — where it stands in history — and I have also told you of the troubadours, the poets who composed, each in his own way, different versions of the story. And, as I said, I shall follow mainly the tale as told by Wolfram von Eschenbach, but draw also from Chrétien de Troyes and others, for there are beautiful scenes in each of them. So — here begins the story.

我已經為你們介紹了帕西法爾故事的總體背景——它在歷史中的位置——我也告訴了你們關於行吟詩人的事，那些以各自不同的方式創作了這個故事不同版本的詩人。而且，如我所說，我將主要依循沃爾夫拉姆·馮·艾申巴赫所講述的故事，但也會取材於克雷蒂安·德·特魯瓦和其他人的版本，因為每個版本中都有美麗的場景。那麼——故事就此開始。

Spring had come to the countryside. It had come to the fields where the first leaves came from the dark earth, it had come to the vast forest of Saltane where oak and ash trees put out their first green buds. It was a great forest, and even a man on horseback would have taken days to cross it. But only a few paths led through the close ranks of the trees, and only rarely did a wanderer pass on these roads.

春天來到了鄉間。它來到了田野，第一片綠葉從黑暗的泥土中探出頭來；它來到了廣袤的薩爾坦森林，橡樹和梣樹長出了最初的嫩綠芽苞。那是一片浩瀚的森林，即使騎馬也要花上好幾天才能穿越。只有極少數的小徑穿過密密排列的樹木之間，也很少有旅人經過這些道路。

But well away from the roads, deep in the heart of the forest of Saltane, there was a clearing with a few simple huts and a little patch of ground, tilled and worked over by human hands. Strange that people should choose to live so far away, so cut off from their fellow beings.

但在遠離道路的地方，在薩爾坦森林的深處，有一片空地，上面有幾間簡陋的小屋和一小片被人手耕作過的土地。真是奇怪，竟然有人選擇住在如此偏遠的地方，與同胞如此隔絕。

But spring had come to the forest and to the clearing, and as the birds twittered and sang to greet the morning, a youngster, the son of a widow, came from one of the huts in the clearing. He carried short hunting spears, and over his shoulder a bow and a quiver of arrows.

但春天也來到了森林和這片空地，當鳥兒啁啾歌唱迎接清晨時，一個少年——一位寡婦的兒子——從空地上的一間小屋走了出來。他手持短獵矛，肩上背著一張弓和一壺箭。

The youngster was proud of his skill with the hunting spears — no roebuck had ever escaped his aim. But the bow was a new thing: he had fashioned it himself only the day before, and he was eager to try it out.

少年對自己投擲獵矛的技術引以為傲——沒有一隻狍鹿能逃過他的準頭。但弓是新鮮事物：他昨天才親手製作了這把弓，急切地想要試試看。

Deeper he went into the forest, and started to practise with bow and arrow. At first his targets were trees, but he soon got tired of aiming at motionless tree trunks. Then he saw a bird perching on a branch. The youngster quickly took aim and let fly. He saw the bird fall, but the lucky shot gave him no pleasure. It made him feel sad, it made him feel guilty, it made him feel ashamed. Why was this? Puzzled, the youngster turned homewards to the clearing in the forest. To whom should he turn but to his mother for an explanation of this strange sense of guilt?

他走入森林更深處，開始練習射箭。起初他的目標是樹木，但很快就厭倦了瞄準靜止不動的樹幹。然後他看到一隻鳥棲息在樹枝上。少年迅速瞄準，放箭射出。他看到那隻鳥落了下來，但這次幸運的射擊並沒有給他帶來任何喜悅。它讓他感到悲傷，讓他感到愧疚，讓他感到羞恥。為什麼會這樣？困惑的少年轉身往回走，回到森林中的空地。除了他的母親，他還能向誰尋求這種奇怪罪惡感的解釋呢？

Beside him and his mother, there lived only a few men and women in the clearing, simple people who served them and worked the land. They would not be able to answer his question. And beside his mother and the few servants, he had never in his young life seen another human being. The forest and the clearing were all he knew of the world.

在空地上，除了他和母親之外，只住著幾個男女——為他們服務並耕種土地的樸實之人。他們無法回答他的問題。除了母親和那幾個僕人，他在年輕的生命中從未見過其他人。森林和空地就是他所知道的全部世界。

Such a simple life, cut off from the world, it was all he needed. All the knowledge he had — and it was very little — was of this simple kind, and his mother told him nothing that could rouse his curiosity about the great world outside the forest. He knew nothing about the way people lived outside his own world — the forest.

如此簡樸的生活，與世隔絕，對他而言已經足夠。他所擁有的知識——少之又少——都是這種簡樸的知識，而他的母親從不告訴他任何可能激起他對森林外面廣大世界好奇心的事情。他對自己世界以外的人們如何生活一無所知——他的世界就是這片森林。

And so the youngster came to his mother, and asked her: "Why did the killing of a bird make me feel sad?"

於是少年來到母親面前，問她：「為什麼殺死一隻鳥讓我感到悲傷？」

And the mother said: "It is because you have, without need, taken the life of a creature, a life that God gave."

母親說：「那是因為你在不必要的情況下，奪走了一個生命——一個上帝所賜予的生命。」

"God?" said the youngster. "Who is God?" And the mother replied (and here I use the version in verse):

「上帝？」少年說。「上帝是誰？」母親回答道（這裡我採用韻文版本）：

> Son, He is brighter than the day
> And, once, to help man on his way
> He lived as man on earth indeed.
> To Him you turn in every need.
> But dark is he who by his art
> Makes men from God's true ways depart.
> From him keep free your soul and heart.

> 孩子，祂比白晝更明亮，
> 曾經，為了幫助世人走上正路，
> 祂確實以凡人之身生活在塵世。
> 在你每一個需要之中，你可以轉向祂。
> 但黑暗的是那個以詭計
> 使人偏離上帝真道的存在。
> 讓你的靈魂和心遠離他。

It was, perhaps, a simple instruction. But for this youngster who lived such a simple life, cut off from the world, it was all he needed.

這也許是一個簡單的教導。但對這個過著如此簡樸的生活、與世隔絕的少年來說，這就是他所需要的一切。

And so, having satisfied his question, the youngster went out into the forest again, happy to walk on the soft moss and to listen to the song of the birds. But suddenly he heard other sounds in the distance — hoofbeats, and the clatter of steel against steel. Still fresh in his mind was what his mother had just told him, and he said to himself: "Perhaps this is the Evil One, the master of evil — I will have my spears ready and deal with him in a way he shall never forget."

於是，問題得到了解答，少年再次走進森林，快樂地踩在柔軟的苔蘚上，聆聽鳥兒的歌唱。但突然間，他聽到遠處傳來別的聲音——馬蹄聲，以及鋼鐵碰撞的鏗鏘聲。母親剛才告訴他的話還在腦海中鮮活，他對自己說：「也許這就是那個邪惡者，邪惡的主宰——我要準備好我的矛，讓他永生難忘。」

And so, eager for this encounter, he hastened in the direction of the noise. But what he saw as he came nearer made him stop in his tracks. He saw five figures mounted on horses. Each of them was clad from head to foot in steel, and as the sunlight fell on the metal the rays were reflected and made each figure shine, so that it dazzled the eyes. They also carried great shields painted in brilliant colours, and long spears and swords.

於是，他急切地迎向這次遭遇，朝著聲響的方向疾步前進。但當他走近時看到的景象讓他停下了腳步。他看到五個騎在馬上的身影。每一個都從頭到腳披著鋼鐵，當陽光落在金屬上，光線反射出來，使每個身影閃閃發光，耀眼奪目。他們還攜帶著繪有鮮豔色彩的巨大盾牌，以及長矛和劍。

The youngster had never seen anything like it. But had not his mother told him that God was as bright as the day? Surely, these dazzling beings must be God. And so, as the riders passed where he stood, he fell to his knees and lifted his arms and cried out: "O God, help me — O God, give me your blessing!"

少年從未見過這樣的景象。但他的母親不是告訴他，上帝比白晝更明亮嗎？那麼，這些耀眼的存在一定就是上帝了。於是，當騎士們經過他站立的地方時，他跪了下來，舉起雙臂呼喊道：「噢，上帝，幫助我——噢，上帝，賜予我祝福！」

As he had fallen to his knees right in front of the first rider, the whole company had to stop. The first rider said to his companions, "The young fool here must be out of his mind with fear. He might be able to help us in our search, but I have to get some sense into him first." Then the rider said to the kneeling figure in the road, "Don't be afraid of me, my good lad."

由於他直接跪在了第一位騎士的馬前，整支隊伍不得不停下來。第一位騎士對同伴們說：「這個年輕的傻瓜一定是嚇瘋了。他也許能幫助我們搜索，但我得先讓他恢復理智。」然後騎士對跪在路上的人說：「別害怕我，好孩子。」

"Afraid?" said the youngster. "Why should I be afraid of you? I am not afraid of God. Are you not God?"

「害怕？」少年說，「我為什麼要害怕你？我不怕上帝。你不是上帝嗎？」

"No," said the rider. "I can assure you I am not God. But I want to ask you a question. Have you seen two men and a lady passing this way?"

「不，」騎士說。「我可以向你保證我不是上帝。但我想問你一個問題。你有沒有看到兩個男人和一位女士從這裡經過？」

The youngster paid no attention to the question. He stood up and continued to stare at the riders. "You are not God?" he said. "Well, then, I can only say that you are more beautiful than God. Oh, I wish I were like you! And if you are not God, what are you?"

少年完全沒在意這個問題。他站了起來，繼續凝視著騎士們。「你不是上帝？」他說。「那麼，我只能說你比上帝還美。噢，我真希望我能像你一樣！如果你不是上帝，那你是什麼？」

"Upon my word," said the rider, "we have run into the silliest creature in this forest. My good lad, I am a knight."

「我的天，」騎士說，「我們碰上了這座森林裡最傻的傢伙。好孩子，我是一名騎士。」

"A knight," said the youngster. "I have never heard this word before, but I can see it must be the most wonderful thing in the world to be a knight. And what is this?" he asked, pointing to the shield.

「騎士，」少年說。「我從未聽過這個詞，但我看得出來，成為一名騎士一定是世界上最美妙的事情。而這是什麼？」他指著盾牌問道。

"Look," said the knight, "we are in a hurry. Have you seen two men and a lady?"

「聽著，」騎士說，「我們趕時間。你有沒有看到兩個男人和一位女士？」

But the youngster had no ears for the question. "Tell me," he cried, "tell me what this is, and what it is for?"

但少年根本聽不進這個問題。「告訴我，」他喊道，「告訴我這是什麼，有什麼用處？」

"Heaven help me!" said the knight with a sigh. "This thing is called a shield, and I use it to protect myself against an enemy's spear or arrow."

「老天保佑！」騎士嘆了口氣說。「這東西叫做盾牌，我用它來抵擋敵人的矛或箭。」

"And what is all this shiny stuff on your arms and breast?"

「你手臂和胸口上這些閃亮的東西又是什麼？」

"It is called armour," said the knight, "and it also protects me against sword-strokes and the points of spears."

「這叫做鎧甲，」騎士說，「它也能保護我免受劍擊和矛尖的傷害。」

"How wonderful!" cried the youngster, "but I am glad that the roebucks in the forest don't have such armour. If they had, my mother and I would rarely have any meat to eat. But, good knight, tell me — were you born that way, with a shiny armour on you?"

「太奇妙了！」少年叫道，「但我很高興森林裡的狍鹿沒有這樣的鎧甲。如果它們有的話，我和母親就很少能吃到肉了。但是，好騎士，告訴我——你是天生就穿著這身閃亮的鎧甲嗎？」

"No," answered the knight. "No one is born with armour on his body. I was given this armour when I was made a knight."

「不，」騎士回答道。「沒有人生下來就穿著鎧甲。這副鎧甲是我被冊封為騎士時得到的。」

That was good news for the youngster who cried, "Oh, one can be made a knight! Tell me quickly who makes knights, so that I can go to him and also become a knight!"

這對少年來說是個好消息，他喊道：「噢，人可以被封為騎士！快告訴我是誰冊封騎士的，這樣我就可以去找他，也成為一名騎士！」

"Our master, King Arthur," answered the knight, "can raise men to the noble ranks of knighthood. But now, my lad, make way for us. I see that you cannot help us, but we are in haste, and must get on."

「我們的主人，亞瑟王，」騎士回答，「可以將人提升到高貴的騎士行列。但現在，孩子，給我們讓路吧。我看你幫不了我們，但我們很急，必須趕路。」

As the company continued their journey, the first knight said, "I wish I had the good looks of that boy — there is no better-looking man in King Arthur's court. But what a pity that with those fine looks and strong body there is the mind of a nitwit. May heaven protect the poor lad!"

當一行人繼續趕路時，第一位騎士說：「我真希望有那個男孩的好樣貌——在亞瑟王的宮廷裡也找不到長相更好的人了。但可惜的是，有著如此俊美的容貌和強壯的身體，卻有著一個傻瓜的腦袋。願上天保佑這可憐的孩子！」

But the youngster stood for a long time, looking after the knights, until the glitter of armour could be seen no longer, and the hoofbeats could no longer be heard. The little world in which he had lived so long was shattered for him, and his mind was in turmoil. There was now only one thought, one wish in his soul — to find King Arthur and become a knight with shield and sword and armour.

但少年站在那裡很久很久，目送著騎士們遠去，直到鎧甲的閃光再也看不見，馬蹄聲也再也聽不到。他長久以來所生活的那個小世界在他眼前碎裂了，他的內心翻湧不已。此刻在他的靈魂中只有一個念頭、一個願望——找到亞瑟王，成為一名擁有盾牌、劍和鎧甲的騎士。

The forest and the clearing that had been his home, the faithful servants — even his mother — were no longer things that mattered to him: they were like shadows compared with the burning wish, "I want to be a knight." And with this decision in his mind, he hurried homewards.

森林和曾是他家園的空地，忠實的僕人們——甚至他的母親——對他來說都不再重要了：與那灼熱的願望「我要成為騎士」相比，它們就像影子一般。帶著這個決定，他匆匆趕回家去。

His mother was waiting for him with great anxiety. It was long past the time when he should have been back. At long last she saw him coming, and though she felt relieved to see him safe and sound, she received him with reproaches. Did he not know how worried she would be? Why had he kept her waiting so long? What had kept him in the forest?

他的母親焦急地等待著他。早就過了他該回來的時間。終於她看到他回來了，雖然看到他安然無恙讓她鬆了一口氣，但她還是責備了他。他難道不知道她會多麼擔心嗎？為什麼讓她等了這麼久？是什麼在森林裡耽擱了他？

But her son was a changed person. He took no notice of her agitation, he offered no apologies, but burst out, "Mother, I have seen the most beautiful thing in the world!"

但她的兒子已判若兩人。他完全沒有理會她的焦慮，也沒有道歉，而是脫口而出：「母親，我看到了世界上最美麗的東西！」

"Have you?" said the mother, astonished. "What was it?"

「是嗎？」母親驚訝地說。「是什麼？」

"You remember," said the son, "you told me once that the most beautiful beings in the world are the angels. But you are mistaken; I have seen today something more beautiful than angels ever could be."

「你記得嗎，」兒子說，「你曾經告訴我，世界上最美麗的存在是天使。但你錯了；我今天看到了比天使更美麗的東西。」

"Good Lord in Heaven!" cried his mother. "You can't be in your right mind to talk like this. What has come over you?"

「我的天啊！」他的母親叫道。「你說這種話一定是瘋了。你怎麼了？」

But the son answered, "No, mother — I really saw these wonderful beings, and I even know what they are called. They are called 'knights'."

但兒子回答：「不，母親——我真的看到了這些美妙的存在，我甚至知道他們叫什麼。他們叫做『騎士』。」

At the sound of the word *knights*, the mother's face became deathly pale, and then she fainted. The son stood there in dumb amazement. What had he said which could upset his mother so much? With the help of some servants, the lady was laid on her bed, and after a time she recovered consciousness. She sent the servants away, and then said to the youngster:

聽到「騎士」這個詞，母親的臉變得慘白如死，然後就昏了過去。兒子呆呆地站在那裡，驚愕不已。他說了什麼竟能讓母親如此崩潰？在幾個僕人的幫助下，夫人被放到了床上，過了一段時間她恢復了意識。她把僕人們打發走，然後對少年說：

"My dear son, for many years past, my life has only had one purpose: to prevent you from knowing that there is such a thing as knighthood, or knights. You should not see knights, you should not hear of knightly deeds. You should remain in ignorance of anything that has to do with knights. And you come and utter this hateful word to me, and destroy all I have worked for all these years."

「我親愛的孩子，這麼多年來，我的生命只有一個目的：不讓你知道世上有騎士這回事，或者有騎士的存在。你不該看到騎士，你不該聽到騎士的事蹟。你應該對任何與騎士有關的事一無所知。而你來到我面前說出這個可恨的詞，毀掉了我這麼多年的一切努力。」

"But why?" cried the son. "Why did you want to keep from me this knowledge? Why should I not know of knights?"

「但為什麼？」兒子喊道。「你為什麼要對我隱瞞這些？我為什麼不該知道騎士的事？」

"You shall know why," said the mother. "There never was a better knight than your father. No one was better loved by his friends, no one was more feared by his enemies. Of noble blood was he, and so am I, the daughter of a family of high lords and kings. And great were our possessions; great lands were ours, and hundreds of knights were at our service. But by evil and by treachery your father lost his possessions, and he died fighting his enemies. You were the only one left to me, a little child, and in my grief over all I had lost, I promised myself you should not die as your father had died — you should not be tempted to risk your life for the glories and honours of knighthood."

「讓你知道吧，」母親說。「你的父親是世上最好的騎士。沒有人比他更受朋友愛戴，沒有人比他更令敵人畏懼。他出身高貴的血統，我也是，出身於貴族領主和國王的家族。我們擁有巨大的財富；廣袤的土地屬於我們，數以百計的騎士為我們效力。但因為邪惡和背叛，你的父親失去了一切財產，在與敵人的戰鬥中身亡。你是我唯一剩下的，一個小小的孩子，在失去一切的悲痛中，我向自己發誓——你不該像你父親那樣死去——你不該被引誘去為騎士的榮耀和榮譽冒生命危險。」

"That is why I came, with a few faithful servants, to this wilderness, far away from the world of knights and their lust for battle. I gave up all the comforts, all the honours that were still my due, so that I could bring you up in this solitude, safe from the evil ways of the world — safe from the lure of knighthood. And now that I have told you, my dear son, I hope you will see that there is nothing but death and sorrow in the pursuit of knighthood, and you will forget the knights you have seen."

「這就是為什麼我帶著幾個忠實的僕人來到這片荒野，遠離騎士的世界和他們對戰鬥的渴望。我放棄了所有的舒適、所有我仍然應得的榮譽，只為了能在這片孤寂中把你養大，遠離世界的邪惡——遠離騎士的誘惑。現在我已經告訴了你一切，親愛的孩子，我希望你能明白，追求騎士之道只有死亡和悲傷，你會忘記你所見過的那些騎士。」

But her son only shook his head, and said, "All you have told me cannot change my mind, mother. I want to become a knight, I want to go to King Arthur; life would not be worth living if this were denied to me. O mother, you must help me — give me a horse and let me go to King Arthur!"

但她的兒子只是搖了搖頭，說：「你告訴我的一切都無法改變我的決心，母親。我要成為騎士，我要去找亞瑟王；如果不讓我這麼做，生命就沒有意義了。噢，母親，你必須幫助我——給我一匹馬，讓我去找亞瑟王！」

For many days, the lady argued and pleaded with the boy, but he, who had never before disobeyed her, was now changed. He paid no notice to her grief, he paid no notice to her pleading and only repeated stubbornly, "I want to become a knight."

許多天裡，夫人不斷地和男孩爭辯、懇求，但他——一個以前從未違抗過她的人——現在變了。他不理會她的悲傷，不理會她的哀求，只是固執地重複著：「我要成為騎士。」

So the poor woman thought, "Perhaps if I dress him up to look foolish and give him an old mare that can hardly walk any longer, people will laugh at him and mock him, and he will soon turn back and return to me." And so she made him a shirt from sackcloth, and clothes made up of different patches sewn together, and a hood with flaps on either side — it was the usual garb of fools who amused princes with their antics. And she gave him the oldest, most miserable-looking horse she could find as his mount.

於是這個可憐的女人想：「也許如果我把他打扮得像個傻瓜，給他一匹幾乎走不動的老母馬，人們會嘲笑他、奚落他，他很快就會回頭回到我身邊。」於是她用粗麻布給他做了一件襯衣，用不同的布片拼縫成衣服，還有一頂兩側帶有翻蓋的兜帽——那是供弄臣逗樂王公貴族的常見裝束。她還把她能找到的最老、最寒酸的馬給了他當坐騎。

But the boy, who knew nothing of the ways of the world, was as happy as a lark. Here were clothes to ride out in, here was a horse to carry him, here was a light, slender hunting spear to protect him — what more could he want? In his mind, the whole wide world lay open before him, ready to be conquered: King Arthur and his court were only waiting to receive him, and to accept him as one of them — a knight.

但這個對世事一無所知的男孩卻快樂得像隻雲雀。這裡有出門穿的衣服，有馬可以載他，有一把輕便纖細的獵矛來保護自己——他還能要求什麼呢？在他心中，整個廣闊的世界就在他面前展開，等待被征服：亞瑟王和他的宮廷只等著接待他，接納他成為其中一員——一名騎士。

He did not notice how, in the last days before his departure, his mother's face became ever more haggard, he did not notice that she hardly ate anything, he did not hear her sobbing through sleepless nights. His own nights were illumined by beautiful dreams in which he saw himself as a knight of King Arthur, and when he was awake he thought of nothing else.

他沒有注意到，在他出發前的最後幾天裡，母親的面容變得越來越憔悴；他沒有注意到她幾乎什麼都不吃；他也沒有聽到她在無眠之夜的啜泣聲。他自己的夜晚被美麗的夢境照亮——在夢中他看到自己成為了亞瑟王的騎士，而當他醒著的時候，他什麼都不想，只想著這件事。

And so came the day of his departure. He said his goodbyes quickly, for he was impatient to be on his way, and he spurred his old horse into a canter. He rode away, without once looking back.

出發的日子終於到了。他匆匆告別，因為他迫不及待地要上路，催促他的老馬跑起了小跑步。他騎馬離去，一次也沒有回頭看。

But his mother stood and watched as long as she could see him, and when he was out of sight, the grief was more than her weakened body and a heart tried by a lifetime of sorrow could bear. A merciful, sudden death delivered her from the loneliness and worry and pain that would have been her fate.

但他的母親站在那裡，目送他直到再也看不見。當他消失在視線之外時，悲傷已超出她虛弱的身體和飽經一生憂愁的心所能承受的。一次仁慈而突然的死亡將她從本將成為她命運的孤寂、擔憂和痛苦中解脫了出來。

But the son, not knowing that he had seen his mother for the last time, not knowing that his departure had been a death blow to her, rode on, singing gaily, through the dark forest and into the world.

但兒子不知道他已經見了母親最後一面，不知道他的離去對她是致命的一擊，他一邊歡快地歌唱，一邊騎馬穿過黑暗的森林，走進了這個世界。

---

## 3. Outsiders and Caspar Hauser 局外人與卡斯帕·豪瑟

This rather strange upbringing which left a young man, at the age of about seventeen, completely ignorant of any useful knowledge. That he could neither read nor write would not have mattered a great deal in those days — but he also knew nothing of the customs, of the way of life outside the forest.

這種相當奇特的教養方式，使一個大約十七歲的年輕人完全不具備任何實用的知識。在那個年代，他既不會讀也不會寫，這本身並不算太大的問題——但他對森林以外的風俗習慣、生活方式也一無所知。

There was a civilization, the civilization of the early Middle Ages, and this young man was a complete stranger to it — which means he was a stranger to his own time. He was in the same position as a modern child brought up on a desert island who is suddenly, at the age of seventeen, brought into a modern city.

有一個文明——中世紀早期的文明——而這個年輕人對它完全陌生——這意味著他是自己時代的陌生人。他的處境就像一個在荒島上長大的現代孩子，突然在十七歲時被帶進一座現代城市。

Of course, the young man — like the modern child I mentioned — would after a time, after many mistakes and blunders, pick up what he needed to know: young people can adapt themselves to all and any conditions, and this young man would not be any exception. But the first seventeen years of his life, spent in the forest, cut off from the civilization of his time, were also bound to leave their mark on him.

當然，這個年輕人——就像我提到的那個現代孩子——經過一段時間，在許多錯誤和失誤之後，會學會他需要知道的東西：年輕人能夠適應任何條件，這個年輕人也不會例外。但他生命中頭十七年在森林中度過、與他所處時代的文明隔絕的經歷，也注定會在他身上留下印記。

The first seventeen or eighteen years of life are the formative years — they form one's whole attitude to life. Later on one may acquire far more knowledge, gain experience, make a career — but the fundamental attitude to life one has acquired by then will remain.

人生的頭十七、八年是成長定型的歲月——它們形塑一個人對生命的整體態度。日後一個人或許會獲得更多的知識、積累經驗、開展事業——但到那時已經形成的對生命的基本態度將會持續不變。

Let us take a concrete example from history. There was in the eighteenth century a young heir to the throne of France, Prince Louis. As a child he was a clever and charming little fellow. The courtiers who surrounded him, his royal parents, they all doted on him. They petted him, they praised him, they fussed about him. No wish was ever denied to him — in short, he was thoroughly spoilt. At the age of seventeen, Prince Louis became King Louis XV of France. He easily picked up the other forms of kingship — the air of superiority, the gracious smile. But he was quite unable to shoulder the responsibilities of a ruler of a great nation. He could not face the work, he could not face the worry of making decisions. And so he soon left the whole task of government to an ambitious woman — his mistress, Madame de Pompadour. The result was a series of disasters for France which, thirty years later under his son, led to the French Revolution.

讓我們舉一個歷史上的具體例子。十八世紀，有一位年輕的法國王位繼承人——路易王子。小時候他是一個聰明迷人的孩子。圍繞著他的朝臣們、他的皇家父母，都寵愛他。他們呵護他、讚美他、對他百般照顧。沒有任何願望被拒絕——簡而言之，他被徹底寵壞了。十七歲時，路易王子成為法國國王路易十五。他很容易就學會了國王的其他做派——高人一等的氣度、優雅的微笑。但他完全無法承擔一個大國統治者的責任。他無法面對工作，無法面對做決定的煩惱。於是他很快把整個治國任務交給了一個野心勃勃的女人——他的情婦龐巴度夫人。結果是法國遭遇了一連串災難，三十年後在他兒子的統治下，導致了法國大革命。

You see, the first seventeen years had left their mark; it was these years which made Louis XV the kind of person he remained to the end of his life.

你看，頭十七年留下了它們的印記；正是這些年使路易十五成為他終生不變的那種人。

Now, the young man in our story had not been spoilt in this way, but his upbringing in the solitude of the forest had made him a stranger to the whole civilization of his times — he was a stranger to his own time. Outwardly he would soon adapt himself — and even enjoy doing so — but in his heart he would remain a stranger to it or, to use another word, he would remain an "outsider".

現在，我們故事中的年輕人並沒有被這樣嬌慣，但他在森林孤寂中的成長使他成為整個時代文明的陌生人——他是自己時代的陌生人。表面上他很快就會適應——甚至樂在其中——但在他的內心深處，他始終會是一個陌生人，或者用另一個詞來說，他會一直是一個「局外人」。

Outsiders are people who live in a certain time, who take part in all that goes on in that time, often very successfully, yet they do not really fit — they are like beings from another world.

局外人是生活在某個時代的人，他們參與那個時代發生的一切，往往非常成功，但他們並不真正融入——他們就像來自另一個世界的存在。

A typical example of such an outsider is Leonardo da Vinci. Leonardo was greatly respected in his own time — princes competed for the favour of having him work for them. Nor did Leonardo ever lack friends — he was never without companions who sincerely loved him, supported him, and stood by him in times of need. Yet the one thing that mattered most to him — his search for knowledge, his scientific pursuits — were centuries ahead of his time. There was no one with whom he could share this burning interest, and he had to keep it all in the pages of his notebooks. And so Leonardo, who was so popular as a person, so successful as an artist, was all his life a lonely man, an outsider.

這種局外人的典型例子是李奧納多·達文西。李奧納多在他的時代備受尊敬——王公貴族們爭相邀請他為自己效力。李奧納多也從不缺少朋友——他身邊總有真心愛他、支持他、在需要時為他挺身而出的同伴。然而，對他來說最重要的事情——他對知識的追求、他的科學研究——超前了他的時代數個世紀。沒有人能夠與他分享這份熾熱的興趣，他只能把一切都記在筆記本的頁面上。因此李奧納多，這個作為一個人如此受歡迎、作為藝術家如此成功的人，一生都是一個孤獨的人，一個局外人。

It is this inner loneliness which made Leonardo an outsider in his own time; and the young man in our story was, by his strange upbringing, doomed to the same kind of inner loneliness. But, you know, this inner loneliness is also the very thing which brings people like Leonardo or this young man so near to us. We can feel with them, we can sympathise with them, we understand them — and there is a reason why we can understand them so well: because nowadays each one of us is living in just this kind of inner loneliness.

正是這種內在的孤獨使李奧納多成為他自己時代的局外人；而我們故事中的年輕人，因為他奇特的教養方式，也注定了同樣的內在孤獨。但你知道嗎，這種內在的孤獨恰恰是使李奧納多或這個年輕人如此貼近我們的東西。我們能感同身受，我們能對他們產生同理心，我們理解他們——而我們之所以能如此深切地理解他們，有一個原因：因為在今天，我們每一個人都生活在這樣的內在孤獨之中。

No doubt everyone of you has good friends, close friends. Yet there is something in you which remains alone and separate, something that remains alone even when you are in the midst of a crowd of friends.

毫無疑問，你們每個人都有好朋友、親密的朋友。然而在你們的內心深處，有某種東西始終是孤獨的、分離的，即使你置身於一群朋友當中，它仍然是孤獨的。

This inner loneliness is, in a way, the foremost problem of modern humanity, and has become the central theme of a great part of modern literature in every country.

這種內在的孤獨，在某種意義上，是現代人類最首要的問題，也已成為各國大量現代文學的核心主題。

Some writers treat this inner loneliness as something sad and tragic. A French writer used the word "exile" for it: "I feel like a man living in exile — in a foreign land."

一些作家把這種內在的孤獨視為悲傷而悲劇性的東西。一位法國作家用「流亡」來形容它：「我感覺自己像一個生活在流亡中的人——在異鄉。」

Other writers — Samuel Beckett for instance, the man who wrote Waiting for Godot — makes a wild, furious joke of this loneliness.

其他作家——例如撰寫了《等待乘果陀》的乘乘乘·貝克特——則把這種孤獨變成了一個瘋狂而憤怒的笑話。

Another French writer, Sartre, takes this loneliness to its utmost extremes — to the point where this loneliness becomes so precious to you that you hate all other persons — and he put this feeling into the words, "Hell is other people." It is, for Sartre, a kind of hell to have to live together with others.

另一位法國作家沙特，則把這種孤獨推向了最極端——到了這種孤獨對你變得如此珍貴，以至於你憎恨所有其他人——他將這種感受化為這句話：「他人即地獄。」對沙特而言，不得不與他人共同生活就是一種地獄。

And there are writers who seek to escape from this loneliness by the use of drugs. By the use of cocaine and heroin they withdraw into a heaven and a hell of their own making. There are other drugs to get away from one's ordinary self and its loneliness — mescalin, marijuana or Ecstasy.

還有一些作家試圖通過藥物來逃避這種孤獨。通過使用可卡因和海洛因，他們退入一個自己創造的天堂與地獄。還有其他藥物可以讓人逃離日常的自我及其孤獨——麥司卡林、大麻或搖頭丸。

It is not surprising that it is young people who attempt to drown this loneliness in drugs, because it is often young people who feel that loneliness more sharply, more intensely, than some older people.

年輕人試圖用藥物來淹沒這種孤獨，這並不令人驚訝，因為往往是年輕人比一些年長者更尖銳、更強烈地感受到這種孤獨。

But in books written one or two or three centuries ago — or even earlier — you will not find anything about this loneliness, which is the best indication that it was not a general problem. There were the lonely ones, the outsiders, at all times, but they were the exception, not the rule. There were a few people who lived in our kind of loneliness, people like Leonardo, or like the young man in our story.

但在一兩個世紀前、三個世紀前——甚至更早——所寫的書中，你不會找到任何關於這種孤獨的內容，這是最好的證據，說明它不是一個普遍的問題。在任何時代都有孤獨的人、局外人，但他們是例外，而非常態。只有少數人經歷著我們這種孤獨，像李奧納多這樣的人，或像我們故事中的年輕人。

In our time this loneliness is as natural as the air we breathe — we all have to bear it. In past ages it was not so — it needed special circumstances to produce it. And it is nothing less than a feat of genius that the troubadours, these poets who lived 800 years ago, wrote that story of the mother who withdraws with her son to the solitude of the forest, and so creates just the kind of special situation which would make the child an outsider — a lonely modern soul that had to live in the world of 800 years ago.

在我們的時代，這種孤獨如同我們呼吸的空氣一樣自然——我們都必須承受它。在過去的時代並非如此——需要特殊的環境才能產生它。而那些行吟詩人——八百年前生活的詩人們——寫出了一個母親帶著兒子隱居到森林的孤寂中的故事，從而創造了恰好能使這個孩子成為局外人的特殊情境——一個不得不生活在八百年前世界中的孤獨的現代靈魂——這不啻為一種天才之舉。

It would be an unnatural upbringing at any time — to withhold from a child the knowledge, the schooling, the education which existed at that period. And you might think it would be a terrible hardship if you were in a similar position and you had to catch up — now, at your age — with all the things other people of your age had learned already. You might think it would be a hard and terrible thing if you had to start from scratch now.

在任何時代，這都將是一種不自然的教養方式——剝奪一個孩子那個時期已有的知識、學校教育和教養。你可能會認為，如果你處在類似的境況中，不得不在你現在的年紀去追趕同齡人已經學會的一切，那將是可怕的艱辛。你可能會認為，如果你現在必須從零開始，那將是一件艱難可怕的事情。

But, as a matter of fact, something like that did happen to a real person. A real person was once, like the youngster in our story, brought up in complete ignorance and then, without preparation, thrown out into the world. But, in this true and historical case, the motive for keeping the child cut off from the world was not a mother's mistaken love, but sinister intrigues.

但事實上，類似的事情確實發生在一個真實的人身上。一個真實的人曾經，就像我們故事中的少年一樣，在完全無知中被養大，然後毫無準備地被拋入世界。但在這個真實的歷史案例中，讓孩子與世隔絕的動機不是母親誤入歧途的愛，而是險惡的陰謀。

The story of that real and unfortunate person who had a similar upbringing to our young man — this true story — is one of the great unsolved riddles of history. Many books have been written about it, but none of them gives a satisfactory answer.

那個與我們的年輕人有著相似成長經歷的真實而不幸的人的故事——這個真實的故事——是歷史上偉大的未解之謎之一。關於它已經寫了許多書，但沒有一本給出令人滿意的答案。

The facts — the historical facts — of this mystery are these: The place was the city of Nuremberg in Bavaria, in southern Germany. The year was 1812. One fine day in May of that year, a citizen of Nuremberg going for a walk outside the city gates saw a young man in peasant dress who seemed to be drunk, for he could hardly walk. The citizen, being a kind person, offered help to the young man. He discovered that the lad's feet were bleeding, and that he could only speak a few words which made no sense. He certainly was not drunk.

這個謎團的事實——歷史事實——如下：地點是德國南部巴伐利亞的紐倫堡城。時間是1812年。那年五月的一個晴朗日子，一位紐倫堡市民在城門外散步時，看見一個穿著農民服裝的年輕人，看起來像是喝醉了，因為他幾乎走不了路。這位善良的市民向年輕人伸出了援手。他發現這個少年雙腳流血，而且只能說出幾個毫無意義的詞語。他肯定不是喝醉了。

The man took the strange youngster into town and, eventually, the poor creature landed up with the Nuremberg police. The police, too, could not make any sense of the few words which the lad repeated again and again. But when given a piece of paper, he wrote on it in stiff, clumsy capital letters the words Caspar Hauser. Obviously he had been taught to draw these letters without knowing their meaning, but the police assumed that this was his name. As it was impossible to find out where he came from, the police lodged him in an unused prison, in a tower.

那人把這個奇怪的少年帶進了城裡，最終，這個可憐的人被送到了紐倫堡警察局。警察同樣無法從少年反覆重複的幾個詞中理解出任何意義。但當給他一張紙時，他用僵硬笨拙的大寫字母寫下了「卡斯帕·豪瑟」幾個字。顯然他被教會了描繪這些字母，卻不知道它們的含義，但警方認定這是他的名字。由於無法查明他來自何處，警方把他安置在一座塔樓裡一間廢棄的牢房中。

At first it was thought he was a village idiot who had somehow got away from his home. He was about sixteen or seventeen years old, he could not walk properly — he walked like a child taking their first steps — he could not speak, apart from a few words like "horse" and "rider", and as he could not speak he also could not think.

起初人們以為他是一個不知怎麼從家裡跑出來的鄉下傻子。他大約十六七歲，不能正常走路——走起來像剛學步的孩子——他不會說話，除了幾個像「馬」和「騎士」這樣的詞，而因為他不能說話，他也不能思考。

The first three things we learn around the age of one are walking, then speaking, then through speaking, thinking. This boy of seventeen had no real command of any of these.

我們在大約一歲時學會的頭三件事是走路，然後是說話，然後通過說話學會思考。這個十七歲的少年對這三樣中的任何一樣都沒有真正的掌握。

However, there was an intelligence in the way he looked at his interrogators, and so the police were greatly puzzled by this Caspar Hauser. Moreover, he picked up new words with amazing speed, and once he knew the meaning of a word he never forgot it, and used it correctly.

然而，他注視審問者的目光中有一種智慧，因此警方對這個卡斯帕·豪瑟大惑不解。此外，他學習新詞的速度驚人，一旦知道一個詞的含義就再也不會忘記，而且使用正確。

Other strange things about Caspar Hauser were that he would not touch any food except dry bread and water, and he could see in darkness as well as in daylight.

卡斯帕·豪瑟的其他奇怪之處在於，他除了乾麵包和水以外不碰任何食物，而且他在黑暗中看得和白天一樣清楚。

The mayor of Nuremberg ordered an investigation, and a host of lawyers, doctors and government officials descended on Caspar Hauser in his tower. As a result of their investigation, the mayor announced that this young man possessed excellent intelligence, that he was of a goodness and purity rarely to be encountered, but that somehow his whole upbringing had been criminally neglected. And, in conclusion, the mayor decided it was the duty of the city of Nuremberg to look after the poor boy.

紐倫堡市長下令進行調查，大批律師、醫生和政府官員蜂擁到塔樓裡探視卡斯帕·豪瑟。調查結果出來後，市長宣布：這個年輕人具有出色的智力，擁有世間罕見的善良與純真，但他的整個教養被以犯罪的方式忽略了。最後，市長決定照顧這個可憐的少年是紐倫堡城的責任。

The story of Caspar Hauser created a sensation in Nuremberg. People came in droves to visit him, as if he were a strange beast, and they plagued him with questions, which the bewildered boy could not answer. He showed, however, an angelic patience and friendliness to all who invaded his tower.

卡斯帕·豪瑟的故事在紐倫堡引起了轟動。人們成群結隊地來探望他，彷彿他是一隻奇異的動物，用各種問題轟炸他，而困惑的少年無法回答。然而，他對所有闖入他塔樓的人都展現出天使般的耐心和友善。

However, one of these visitors was a school teacher who took a warm personal interest in the youngster. He spent many hours teaching him new words, and was amazed at the speed with which his pupil learned. And, at his request, Caspar Hauser was given into his care.

然而，訪客中有一位學校教師，對這個少年產生了溫暖的個人興趣。他花了許多小時教他新詞，並對學生學習的速度感到驚嘆。應他的請求，卡斯帕·豪瑟被交給了他照顧。

Teaching had, of course, to start from scratch — he even had to learn to walk. Yet, within three weeks, Caspar could converse fairly fluently, he had mastered arithmetic and could play simple tunes on a harpsichord. And he could read and write — all within three weeks!

教學當然必須從零開始——他甚至得學走路。然而，在三週之內，卡斯帕就能相當流利地交談，掌握了算術，還能在大鍵琴上彈奏簡單的曲調。而且他能讀能寫——全都在三週之內！

But there were other things to wonder about. One afternoon, the teacher found his pupil sitting in the garden, absorbed in a book. Swallows circled around his head, pigeons picked crumbs at his feet, a cat purred in his arms, and butterflies had settled on his shoulders. On other occasions, too, he showed a strange power over animals. A vicious dog, kept chained by a neighbour, broke his chains and came snarling and snapping through the hedge — but lay down peacefully at Caspar's feet and licked his hands.

但還有其他令人驚奇的事情。一天下午，教師發現他的學生坐在花園裡，專注地讀著一本書。燕子在他頭頂盤旋，鴿子在他腳邊啄食麵包屑，一隻貓在他懷裡打著呼嚕，蝴蝶停在他的肩膀上。在其他場合，他也展現出一種對動物的奇異力量。鄰居用鏈條拴著的一隻兇惡的狗掙斷了鏈條，齜牙咧嘴地穿過籬笆衝過來——卻安靜地在卡斯帕腳邊躺下，舔著他的手。

Caspar could not think evil of any person, and he called all the people he saw "beautiful". He could not understand what a lie was and he could not tell one himself. For a long time, he referred to himself only as "Caspar", and it was a great discovery when he understood the pronoun "I", and learned to use it.

卡斯帕不會對任何人有惡意的想法，他稱所見到的所有人都是「美麗的」。他無法理解什麼是謊言，自己也不會說謊。很長一段時間裡，他只用「卡斯帕」來稱呼自己，當他理解了代詞「我」並學會使用它時，那對他來說是一個重大的發現。

His power of memory was quite extraordinary. People read to him long lists of names, or figures — hundreds of them — and he could repeat them immediately in the right sequence and without a single mistake. Moreover, he was astonished that not everybody could do it. The young Caspar Hauser was an amazing person.

他的記憶力非同尋常。人們給他讀長長的名字列表或數字——數以百計——他能立即按正確順序重複，而且一個錯誤都沒有。此外，他對不是每個人都能做到這一點感到驚訝。年輕的卡斯帕·豪瑟是一個令人驚嘆的人。

But as he was now able to speak, he could also throw some light on his unknown past, and what he told, and retold, and wrote about it, constitutes the greatest riddle in the mystery of Caspar Hauser.

但既然他現在能說話了，他也能對自己不為人知的過去提供一些線索，而他所講述的、反覆講述的、以及寫下來的內容，構成了卡斯帕·豪瑟之謎中最大的謎團。

The earliest thing he could remember was a small, dark room containing a bed and a chair, and nothing else. He was chained to the bed and could go no further than the chair. He was alone in the darkness and never saw any human being at all. When he woke from his sleep, he found a slice of bread and a jug of water on the chair, although he never saw the person who put the food there whilst he slept. On occasions, the water tasted different — he slept longer than usual, and when he woke up he found himself washed, his hair cut and a new bucket for his bodily needs. He had no idea of time, he did not even know that he was lonely — he knew nothing, thought nothing, heard nothing and saw nothing.

他能記得的最早的事情是一個小小的、黑暗的房間，裡面只有一張床和一把椅子，除此之外什麼都沒有。他被鏈條鎖在床上，走不到比椅子更遠的地方。他獨自一人在黑暗中，從未見過任何人。當他從睡夢中醒來時，會發現椅子上放著一片麵包和一壺水，儘管他從未看到在他睡著時放食物的人。有時候，水的味道不一樣——他會比平時睡得更久，醒來後發現自己被洗過了，頭髮被剪了，還有一個新的便桶。他沒有時間概念，甚至不知道自己是孤獨的——他什麼都不知道、什麼都不想、什麼都聽不到、什麼都看不見。

This state must have gone on for years, for it was only a short time before his release that, whilst he was awake, the door of his prison opened and somebody came in. That somebody spoke a few words, again and again, until Caspar repeated them. These were the first words of human language he had heard, and learned without knowing what they meant. This somebody also made him copy the signs on a piece of paper which meant "Caspar Hauser". It may have taken a couple of days, until this somebody was satisfied. Then he took Caspar Hauser out of that dark cell and, as he was unable to walk more than a few yards, this same somebody put Caspar on his back and carried him, with frequent rests, a few miles. For several days Caspar was moved in this way, until the somebody left him standing just outside Nuremberg and walked off.

這種狀態一定持續了很多年，因為就在他被釋放前不久，在他醒著的時候，牢房的門打開了，有人走了進來。那個人反覆說著幾個詞，直到卡斯帕跟著重複。這些是他聽到的第一批人類語言的詞彙，在不知其含義的情況下學會了它們。那個人還讓他在一張紙上照抄一些符號，那些符號代表「卡斯帕·豪瑟」。這可能花了幾天時間，直到那個人滿意為止。然後他把卡斯帕·豪瑟帶出了那間黑暗的牢房，由於他走不了幾步路，同一個人就把卡斯帕背在背上，頻繁休息，走了好幾英里。就這樣移動了好幾天，直到那個人把他留在紐倫堡城外，然後離開了。

This revelation roused a storm. Newspapers demanded an explanation, they demanded an investigation to find out who had committed this inhuman cruelty. And some people openly accused a noble and ruling family in Germany of having disposed of some awkward relation — a possible heir to the throne — in this ghastly manner.

這一揭露引發了軒然大波。報紙要求解釋，要求進行調查以查明是誰犯下了這種不人道的殘暴行為。一些人公開指控德國的一個貴族統治家族以這種駭人聽聞的方式處理了某個令人尷尬的親戚——一個可能的王位繼承人。

A German High Court judge undertook to solve the mystery, and Caspar Hauser was transferred to his care. Not long after that, the school teacher who had started the whole thing died suddenly. The High Court judge also died unexpectedly and Caspar Hauser was stabbed to death by an unknown assassin. The mystery of Caspar Hauser has never been solved.

一位德國高等法院法官承擔了解開這個謎團的任務，卡斯帕·豪瑟被移交到他的照顧之下。不久之後，那位最初引發整件事的學校教師突然死亡。高等法院法官也意外死亡，而卡斯帕·豪瑟被一名不明身份的刺客刺死。卡斯帕·豪瑟之謎從未被破解。

The story is told by a German writer, Jakob Wassermann, in *The Enigma of Caspar Hauser*, which has also been made into a film.

這個故事由德國作家雅各布·瓦塞爾曼在《卡斯帕·豪瑟之謎》一書中講述，這本書後來也被拍成了電影。

So here we have a real account of a child brought up in ignorance and cut off from the world. Yet this child, let loose on the world at the age of seventeen, was, two years later, so far on that he could study Latin, and translate Julius Caesar.

所以我們有了一個真實的案例：一個在無知中被養大、與世隔絕的孩子。然而這個孩子在十七歲時被放入世界，兩年後就已經進步到能夠學習拉丁文，翻譯凱撒的著作。

If you remember the fantastic speed with which Caspar Hauser learned, if you remember his equally fantastic memory, you will realise that being kept back had not reduced his intelligence — quite the contrary, it had enhanced and strengthened his mental capacities.

如果你記得卡斯帕·豪瑟學習的驚人速度，如果你記得他同樣驚人的記憶力，你就會意識到被阻隔並沒有削弱他的智力——恰恰相反，它增強和提升了他的心智能力。

If the authorities which lay down the laws of education paid attention to this terrible "experiment" that was made two hundred years ago, they would not insist that children of six — or in some schools, five — should learn to read and write. I am quite convinced that you all here would walk through your exams if your schooling had begun, let's say, at ten, or twelve, and not at six. But, things being what they are, we shall have to go on as we do now.

如果制定教育法規的當局注意到兩百年前進行的這個可怕的「實驗」，他們就不會堅持六歲——或在某些學校五歲——的孩子就應該學習讀寫。我完全相信，如果你們的正式教育是從，比如說，十歲或十二歲開始，而不是六歲，你們所有人都能輕鬆通過考試。但事情就是這樣，我們只能繼續按現在的方式做下去。

The fact is that the human mind grows stronger if it is not burdened too early with knowledge, as the case of Caspar Hauser shows. And — to return to our story — the strange upbringing of our young man, which made him an outsider, a lonely soul, in his time, this upbringing which kept him back, also gave him a keener mind. It gave him greater abilities than if he had been brought up in the ordinary way. Our young hero was not at such a disadvantage as it may seem.

事實是，如果人的心智不過早地被知識所負擔，它會變得更加強大，卡斯帕·豪瑟的案例證明了這一點。而——回到我們的故事——我們年輕人的奇特教養方式，使他成為他那個時代的局外人、孤獨的靈魂，這種使他落後的教養方式，同時也賦予了他更敏銳的心智。它給了他比以普通方式被撫養長大更強的能力。我們的年輕英雄並不像看起來那樣處於劣勢。

---

## 4.Parsifal's Early Adventures 帕西法爾的早年歷險

You will have noticed that I spoke of the hero of our story all the time as "the young man" or "the youngster", without using his name. But in doing this I am only following the troubadour, Chretien de Troyes, who also calls his hero for the first chapters of the book only "the young man" - and there is a good reason for this: because this young man does not know his name. And this comes out in the next events of the story, which I shall now relate.


你或許已經注意到，在講述這個故事時，我一直稱呼我們的主人公為「那個年輕人」或「那個少年」，而未使用他的名字。但我這麼做，不過是追隨吟遊詩人克雷蒂安·德·特魯瓦（Chretien de Troyes）的做法——他在書中前幾章也僅僅稱呼主角為「那個年輕人」。這是有充分理由的：因為這個年輕人自己也不知道自己的名字。而這一點，將在接下來的故事中顯現出來。

The young man, decked out in the fool's garb his mother had given him, and mounted on his old mare, rode happily through the forest, and then out into a world of ploughed fields and villages that he had never seen before.

年輕人穿著母親給他的那身丑角衣裳，騎在老母馬上，快活地穿過森林，隨後來到一片他從未見過的世界——翻耕過的田野和村莊。

From time to time, he met other people on the road - a peasant driving a mule, a merchant in a horse-drawn cart. They looked with some surprise at this fellow in fool's dress, and smiled at him. But he took this only as a sign of friendliness, and greeted them with the words, "I bid you a good journey - as my mother has told me," which made them smile even more, but none of them thought of mocking one who was obviously uneducated.

一路上，他不時遇見其他行人——趕著騾子的農夫、坐在馬車上的商人。他們驚訝地打量著這個穿丑角服的傢伙，不禁微笑。但他只把這當作友善的表示，用母親教他的話招呼他們：「祝你們旅途順利——這是我母親囑咐我說的。」這讓他們笑得更開了，但誰也不忍心去嘲弄這個明顯未受教育的人。

And then the road he followed led him through a mountain pass, and riding down a slope he heard a woman's voice - but it was a sound of wailing and crying and sobbing. What could this be? Driven by pity as much as by curiosity, the young man turned his horse in the direction of the voice. Coming round a bend of the path he saw a young woman sitting on a rock. In her lap was the body of a man, the stiffness of his limbs indicating that he was dead.

隨後，他所走的路引他穿過一個山口。下坡時，他聽見一個女人的聲音——但那是哀號、哭泣和啜泣的聲音。這是怎麼回事？出於同情，也出於好奇，年輕人調轉馬頭朝聲音傳來的方向去。繞過路上一個彎道，他看見一個年輕女子坐在岩石上。她膝頭上是一個男人的遺體，四肢僵硬，顯然已經死去。

The young man approached the crying woman, and said, "Lady, my mother told me to offer my services to any woman in distress. So let me help you in your misfortune." The woman lifted her head, but she only looked at him in silence.

年輕人走近那個哭泣的女子，說道：「夫人，我母親告訴我，凡是遇到受苦的女子，都應該獻上我的幫助。所以，請讓我在你的不幸中助你一臂之力。」女子抬起頭，卻只是默默地看著他。

Unabashed, the young man continued, "Tell me, who was it who killed this knight you mourn? Tell me who it is and, I promise you, the murderer shall not escape from my spears. I shall make him pay with his life for the grief he has caused you."

年輕人毫不氣餒，繼續說：「告訴我，是誰殺了你哀悼的這位騎士？告訴我是誰，我向你保證，兇手逃不過我的長矛。我會讓他為帶給你的悲痛付出生命的代價。」

It must have seemed strange to the woman that this lad, without shield or armour, and carrying no better weapon than hunting spears, offered to go and slay an enemy who was bound to be superior in arms and experience. But she recognised that it was not youthful boasting that had moved the young man, but the true spirit of chivalry. And she answered, "My young friend, I honour your good intention, but there is no call for you, a stranger, to revenge the death of this knight, who was betrothed to me, who was my bridegroom. He for whom I grieve was not murdered, but slain in open battle, face to face with the enemy. But you have a noble heart to offer yourself to the service of a stranger - tell me, what is your name?"

對這個女子來說，一個既無盾牌也無盔甲、手中武器不過是幾根獵矛的少年，竟然要去殺死一個在武藝和經驗上必然遠勝於他的敵人，這一定顯得很奇怪。但她認出驅使這年輕人的並非年少輕狂的吹噓，而是真正的騎士精神。於是她回答道：「年輕的朋友，我敬重你的好意，但不需要你這個陌生人去為這位騎士復仇——他是我的未婚夫，是我的新郎。我所哀悼的人並非遭人謀害，而是在正面的戰鬥中，與敵人面對面交鋒時陣亡的。但你有一顆高貴的心，願意為一個陌生人效力——告訴我，你叫什麼名字？」

"'Good son, dear son' - that was the name by which my mother called me," answered the young man.

「『好孩子，親愛的孩子』——我母親就是這樣叫我的。」年輕人回答道。

At these words, the woman looked long and silently at him - and when she spoke again, there was a different tone in her voice. "Is this the only name you know?" she asked.

聽到這話，女子長久而沉默地注視著他——當她再次開口時，聲音裡帶著不一樣的語氣。「這是你知道的唯一名字嗎？」她問。

"It is, indeed - and what better name could I be called by my mother than 'good son, dear son'?" said the young man, a little impatiently.

「確實如此——還有什麼名字比母親叫我『好孩子，親愛的孩子』更好的呢？」年輕人有些不耐煩地說。

Again the woman looked at him in silence before she said, "Well do I know her who called her child by these names - for she is my aunt, Queen Herzeloyde, who went to hide herself and her little son in the forest many a year ago. Queen Herzeloyde was your mother's name, and I am your cousin, Sigune. But you, too, have a name, dear cousin, given to you when you were born - and may God make it a name to be honoured. Your name is 'Parsifal'."

女子又默默地看了他一會兒，然後說道：「那個用這些名字喚自己孩子的人，我很清楚她是誰——因為她是我的姑母，赫澤洛伊德女王（Queen Herzeloyde），多年前帶著年幼的兒子躲進了森林。赫澤洛伊德女王是你母親的名字，而我是你的表姐，西格涅（Sigune）。但你也有一個名字，親愛的表弟，在你出生時就給你取的名字——願上帝讓它成為一個受人尊敬的名字。你的名字是『帕西法爾（Parsifal）』。」

"So, I am Parsifal," said the young man in wonder. "But, cousin Sigune, you called my mother Queen Herzeloyde. Is she a queen, then?"

「所以，我就是帕西法爾，」年輕人驚奇地說。「但是，表姐西格涅，你稱我母親為赫澤洛伊德女王。她是一位女王嗎？」

"Yes, Parsifal," said the woman, "because your father, the noble Gamuret, was king of the land of Norgals. And when he and your elder brothers lost their lives in combat, and when your mother Herzeloyde took refuge in the forest, this knight who now lies dead in my arms, my bridegroom, took upon himself the task of holding and defending it until the true heir - you, Parsifal - could come and wear the crown. It was your land he defended, and it was whilst fighting against a foreign invader that he whom I loved lost his life."

「是的，帕西法爾，」女子說，「因為你的父親，高貴的嘎穆瑞特（Gamuret），是諾加爾斯（Norgals）的國王。當他和你的兄長們在戰鬥中喪生，當你的母親赫澤洛伊德逃入森林避難時，這位如今死在我懷中的騎士——我的新郎——就擔負起守護和捍衛王國的重任，直到真正的繼承人——你，帕西法爾——能夠回來戴上王冠。他守護的正是你的領地，而他正是在抵抗外敵入侵時，我所愛的人失去了生命。」

"Then it is my duty to avenge him and to regain my kingdom!" cried Parsifal. "Sigune, you must tell me which of the roads that meet here shall lead me to the enemy, and I shall not rest until justice is done and my kingdom restored!"

「那麼報仇並奪回我的王國就是我的職責！」帕西法爾喊道。「西格涅，你一定要告訴我，在這裡交會的道路中，哪一條通往敵人，我絕不休息，直到正義得到伸張、我的王國恢復！」

But Sigune knew only too well what the outcome would be if this ignorant lad, unused to the arts of fighting, and armed with nothing better than a short throwing spear, would challenge well-trained fighting men. And so she pointed to a path that would lead Parsifal in the opposite direction - a path that would take him to the lands of King Arthur.

但西格涅太清楚，如果這個無知的少年——不諳戰鬥之術，手中的武器不過是短投矛——去挑戰訓練有素的戰士，結果會是怎樣。於是她指了一條會將帕西法爾引向相反方向的路——一條通往亞瑟王（King Arthur）領地的路。

Sigune refused to leave her place in the mountains, she refused to leave her bridegroom's body. She told Parsifal that she had attendants she could call when she wanted them, but that she wished to remain alone with her grief and with her dead knight.

西格涅拒絕離開她在山中的位置，拒絕離開新郎的遺體。她告訴帕西法爾，她有隨從可以隨時召喚，但她希望獨自守著自己的悲傷和她死去的騎士。

Parsifal rode away on the path Sigune had shown him, richer by the name he had now learned, and poorer by the kingdom that should have been his by inheritance and that had been lost.

帕西法爾沿著西格涅指給他的路騎馬離去，多了他剛知道的名字，卻少了本應屬於他的、已經失去的王國。

A few hours later, hunger and tiredness made him look for shelter, and he was glad to find a peasant who, poor as he was, would not refuse hospitality to a lonely stranger. When Parsifal learned from the man that he was in the realm of King Arthur, everything else - Sigune, the dead knight, his father's kingdom - was forgotten. He had only one desire: to reach King Arthur's court as soon as possible so that he might be made a knight.

幾個小時後，飢餓和疲憊使他尋找歇腳之處。他很高興找到一個農民——雖然貧窮，卻不會拒絕款待一個孤獨的旅人。當帕西法爾從這個人口中得知自己已身在亞瑟王的領地時，其他一切——西格涅、死去的騎士、父親的王國——全被拋到腦後。他只有一個願望：盡快到達亞瑟王的宮廷，好讓自己被封為騎士。

And so, the next morning, at Parsifal's pleading, the peasant took him to the city where King Arthur and the Knights of the Round Table were then in residence.

於是，第二天早上，在帕西法爾的懇求下，農民帶他來到亞瑟王和圓桌騎士（Knights of the Round Table）當時駐蹕的城市。

But when they saw the city before them, the peasant took his leave for, as he said, "The courtiers are all so high and mighty that they would take offence if the likes of me would get near them."

但當他們看到城市出現在眼前時，農民告辭離去，因為他說：「那些朝臣個個自命不凡、高高在上，像我這樣的人靠近他們，只會惹他們生氣。」

And so, Parsifal, in his fool's garb and on his old mare, rode into King Arthur's city. Having never seen a city, he stared at the houses, at the great number of people in the streets, and hardly noticed that they also stared with amusement at him. Nor was young Parsifal aware that he knew nothing of courtly manners - of any kind of manners. A wild man from far-off mountain forests would have known as much of these things as Parsifal.

就這樣，帕西法爾穿著丑角服、騎著老母馬，進入了亞瑟王的城市。他從未見過城市，目不轉睛地盯著房屋和街上熙來攘往的人群，幾乎沒有注意到人們也在饒有興味地盯著他看。年輕的帕西法爾也渾然不知自己對宮廷禮儀一竅不通——甚至對任何禮儀都一無所知。一個來自遙遠山林的野人，在這些事上所知的也不會比帕西法爾少。

Blissfully unaware of his ignorance, Parsifal rode on, full of eager expectation, until he reached a meadow. Beyond the meadow, he saw walls girding a great building: the residence of King Arthur. Parsifal's heart beat faster as he saw the place where all his dreams should be fulfilled. But as he looked with shining eyes at the building - far greater than he had ever imagined it - there came a knight, riding out through one of the gates in the wall, and galloping towards Parsifal.

帕西法爾對自己的無知渾然不覺，滿懷急切的期待繼續騎行，直到來到一片草地。草地那邊，他看見圍牆環繞著一座宏偉的建築——那就是亞瑟王的宮殿。看到自己所有夢想即將實現的地方，帕西法爾的心跳加速了。但就在他閃亮的目光望著那座遠比他想像中更宏大的建築時，一位騎士從圍牆的一個門洞中騎馬衝出，朝帕西法爾疾馳而來。

And what a knight he was! His armour was red - as red as a glowing fire - and his shield, too, was red as a flame, red were his horse's trappings, and even the horse itself was brown-red in colour. The shaft of his spear, the scabbard of his sword, the reins he held - they were all red.

好一個騎士！他的盔甲是紅色的——紅得像燃燒的火焰——盾牌也紅如火舌，馬飾是紅的，連坐騎本身也是棕紅色。他的矛柄、劍鞘、手中的韁繩——全都是紅色的。

As the red knight came nearer, Parsifal saw that he carried in one hand a golden goblet, a golden cup. Parsifal was too dumbfounded to speak when this splendid red apparition approached. But the red knight reined his horse in, and addressed the speechless youth.

當紅騎士走近時，帕西法爾看見他一隻手中拿著一個金高腳杯。當這耀眼的紅色幻影逼近時，帕西法爾驚得說不出話來。但紅騎士勒住了馬，對這個目瞪口呆的少年說話了。

"Blessed be the mother that gave you such good looks. You will find much joy in life, but you will also know grief and pain and sorrow."

「生你的母親真是有福。你此生將經歷許多歡樂，但你也將嘗到悲傷、痛苦和哀愁。」

Parsifal was hardly prepared for such prophecies, but before he could gather his wits, the red knight continued, "You are on your way to King Arthur, and I charge you to give him a message from me."

帕西法爾對這樣的預言毫無心理準備，但還沒等他回過神來，紅騎士又繼續說道：「你正要去見亞瑟王，我要你替我帶一個口信給他。」

"I will, good sir," said Parsifal, with some effort. "What is your message?"

「好的，先生，」帕西法爾費了些力氣說道。「你的口信是什麼？」

"You must know," said the red knight, "that my name is Ither, and that I lay claim to the lands of King Arthur - they should be mine by rights, and not his. In token of my claim, I have just walked into the hall where Arthur and his knights are taking their meal, and I took this golden goblet that stood, filled with wine, before the king. I took it from the table and, I am sorry to say, as I took it I spilled the wine on Queen Guinevere. But, though I regret the mishap to the Queen, it is but of little account. What matters is that, by ancient custom, he who takes a goblet from a king's table announces thereby his claim to the kingdom - and that is what I have done. Now go to King Arthur and tell him that I shall wait here for his best knight to come and fight me for this cup. If I win, the land is mine, and proud Arthur must swear obedience to me as his lord. If I lose, he can keep his cup and his kingdom. Go now, my good lad, and give him this message."

「你需知道，」紅騎士說，「我的名字是伊瑟（Ither），我聲稱對亞瑟王的領地擁有主權——依照權利，那些領地應該是我的，而非他的。為了表明我的權利，我方才走進亞瑟王和他的騎士們正在進餐的大廳，取走了放在國王面前盛滿美酒的這只金杯。我從桌上拿走它時，很遺憾，不小心把酒灑在了桂妮薇兒王后（Queen Guinevere）身上。但是，儘管我對王后的遭遇感到抱歉，這只是小事一樁。重要的是，按照古老的慣例，凡從國王桌上取走酒杯者，即宣告對王國的主權——而我已經這樣做了。現在去告訴亞瑟王，我在此地等候他最強的騎士來為這杯子與我一戰。若我勝了，這片土地就是我的，驕傲的亞瑟必須向我臣服。若我敗了，他可以留著他的杯子和王國。去吧，好小伙子，把這口信帶給他。」

Parsifal, full of wonder about the ways of knights, and full of admiration for the red armour of the knight, cried, "I will, Sir Ither!" and rode his horse at its best speed up to the walls of the palace, through a gate and into a forecourt. Here a number of pages stood together, and one of them, Ivanet by name, came and asked him his business.

帕西法爾對騎士的行事方式充滿好奇，對紅騎士的盔甲滿懷欽羨，喊道：「我去，伊瑟大人！」便策馬全速奔向宮殿圍牆，穿過一道門進入前庭。這裡有幾個侍從聚在一起，其中一個名叫伊瓦涅（Ivanet）的走上前來，問他有何貴幹。

"My business is with King Arthur," said Parsifal. "Show me the hall where he is at his meal."

「我要見亞瑟王，」帕西法爾說。「帶我去他進餐的大廳。」

"The hall is on the ground floor, and the great arch over there is the entrance," said Ivanet. He might have been less forthcoming with his information if he had known what kind of visitor this was.

「大廳在底層，那邊的大拱門就是入口，」伊瓦涅說。如果他知道這是個什麼樣的來客，也許就不會這麼爽快地告知了。

Parsifal rode through the entrance and straight into the great hall, where a throng of knights and ladies were sitting at tables. They were eating and drinking and not a little surprised when a horse and rider appeared in their midst. Parsifal surveyed the great room and the feasting throng calmly from his elevated position, and then said, "God keep you all, brave knights and fair ladies. I give you my greeting, as my mother told me I should."

帕西法爾騎馬穿過入口，直入大廳。大廳裡，一大群騎士和女士們圍坐在桌旁。他們正在吃喝，當一匹馬和一個騎手出現在他們中間時，無不大感驚訝。帕西法爾從高處的馬背上從容地環顧著這間宏大的廳堂和宴飲的眾人，然後說道：「願上帝保佑你們所有人，勇敢的騎士和美麗的女士們。我向你們致意，這是我母親告訴我應該做的。」

By now, all faces in the hall were turned towards him, but he continued, quite unabashed, in his clear voice, "I don't know who of you is King Arthur, for you all look like kings to me, but I have a message for him. Ither, the red knight, who took the golden cup, is waiting on the meadow outside for your best knight to fight him for the cup. He is sorry for spilling wine on the queen. I only wish I had all the red clothes he has on him - they are just what I would like to wear as a knight!"

此時，大廳裡所有的目光都轉向了他，但他毫不畏怯，繼續用清亮的嗓音說道：「我不知道你們誰是亞瑟王，因為在我看來你們都像國王。但我帶了口信給他。那個拿走金杯的紅騎士伊瑟正在外面的草地上等候，要和你們最強的騎士為那只杯子決鬥。他對灑了酒在王后身上感到抱歉。我只希望我能得到他穿的那身紅衣——那正是我夢寐以求的騎士裝束！」

When he had spoken, there was a great babble of voices in the hall. The ladies said how handsome he was, the knights said he looked strong and fit for any fight, but they all wondered if he was in his right mind, riding into King Arthur's banqueting hall in a fool's outfit. But then King Arthur raised his voice: "I am the king, and I thank you for the message. The red knight's insult shall not go unpunished, and every knight of the Round Table will be eager for the task of dealing with the man who dares to claim my kingdom."

他說完之後，大廳裡頓時嗡嗡議論。女士們說他長得真英俊，騎士們說他看起來強壯，適合任何戰鬥，但所有人都懷疑他是不是神志正常——竟然穿著丑角服騎馬闖進亞瑟王的宴會大廳。這時亞瑟王提高了聲音：「我就是國王，感謝你帶來的口信。紅騎士的侮辱絕不會不受懲罰，圓桌的每一位騎士都會爭相承擔對付這個膽敢覬覦我王國之人的任務。」

"No," said Parsifal, to everybody's surprise. "I want the red knight's armour, I want his horse and weapon - therefore let me go and fight him."

「不，」帕西法爾說，令所有人大吃一驚。「我要紅騎士的盔甲，我要他的馬和武器——所以讓我去和他交戰吧。」

And then Sir Kay, one of Arthur's knights, shouted furiously, "You empty-headed young fool, who are you to demand such an honour? You nitwit - it would serve you right to be knocked about by the red knight - perhaps he will knock some sense into your head. Yes - go and fight him, and get his armour ... if you can!"

這時，亞瑟的騎士之一凱爵士（Sir Kay）怒吼道：「你這個腦袋空空的年輕蠢貨，你算哪根蔥，敢要求這樣的榮譽？你這白痴——被紅騎士揍一頓也是你活該——也許他能把一些腦子打進你的腦袋。好啊——去跟他打吧，搶他的盔甲……如果你有本事的話！」

But now King Arthur intervened. "Sir Kay, do not speak in this manner to a youngster whose only fault is his ignorance. And you, my young friend, do not wish for things that only an older man with more experience of combat can attain. Forget about the red knight's splendid armour, and be satisfied with the one I might give you. Come, dismount from your horse, and leave the red knight to someone better able to deal with him."

但這時亞瑟王出面調停了。「凱爵士，不要這樣對一個唯一的過錯就是無知的年輕人說話。而你，我年輕的朋友，不要奢望那些只有更年長、更有戰鬥經驗的人才能取得的東西。忘了紅騎士那華麗的盔甲吧，滿足於我可以給你的裝備就好。來，下馬吧，把紅騎士交給更有能力對付他的人。」

Parsifal, burning with desire for the red armour, and stung by the insults of Sir Kay, answered, "No, I will not dismount, for he who waits outside is also on his horse, and I want to ride out and fight him. And I don't want any other armour or weapon or horse but his. Give me the red knight's armour, King Arthur!"

帕西法爾渴望紅色盔甲，又被凱爵士的侮辱激怒了，回答道：「不，我不下馬，因為在外面等候的人也在馬上，我要騎馬出去和他交戰。除了他的盔甲、武器和馬，我什麼都不要。把紅騎士的盔甲給我，亞瑟王！」

"You are as stubborn as you are young and ignorant," said the king, "but have it your own way. The red armour shall be yours - if you can get it. And may Heaven protect you, that you suffer no great injury in this foolish attempt."

「你的固執和你的年輕無知一樣頑強，」國王說，「但隨你吧。紅色盔甲歸你——如果你能拿到的話。願上天保佑你，在這愚蠢的嘗試中不會受到太大的傷害。」

"Thank you!" cried Parsifal, and he turned his horse to ride out.

「謝謝！」帕西法爾喊道，轉身策馬而出。

Now there was a lady in the hall who had never laughed in all her life. And there was a saying in the court of King Arthur that she would laugh, once - when she saw the knight who would attain the highest honour of all. But though she had seen many a great and famous knight - Sir Lancelot, Sir Gawain, and all the other heroes of the Round Table - she had neither laughed, nor even smiled.

大廳裡有一位女士，一生從未笑過。在亞瑟王的宮廷裡有一個說法：她將會笑一次——當她看到那位將獲得至高榮耀的騎士時。但儘管她見過許多偉大而著名的騎士——蘭斯洛特爵士（Sir Lancelot）、高文爵士（Sir Gawain），以及圓桌的所有其他英雄——她既沒有笑過，甚至連微笑也不曾有。

But as Parsifal, on his old mare, and in his patched-up fool's outfit, rode through the hall to his encounter with the red knight, he passed the table where this lady was sitting. And she looked at him, and burst out laughing.

但當帕西法爾騎著老母馬、穿著補丁累累的丑角服穿過大廳去迎戰紅騎士時，他經過了這位女士所坐的桌旁。她看著他，突然放聲大笑起來。

Sir Kay, the knight who had been so angry with Parsifal, was sitting next to her. And he saw this woman - who had never laughed before - shaking with laughter at the sight of Parsifal, and he remembered what this laughter meant. He was so furious that he forgot his knightly manners and shouted at her, "You had no laughter or smile for the best knights of this court, and now you laugh at this young nitwit?" And in his fit of temper he did something that was against all rules of knighthood: he struck her in the face.

那個對帕西法爾大發脾氣的凱爵士就坐在她旁邊。他看到這個從未笑過的女子因為看到帕西法爾而笑得渾身發抖，想起了這笑聲意味著什麼。他怒不可遏，忘了騎士的禮儀，朝她吼道：「宮中最優秀的騎士你看了都不笑不笑，現在卻對這個小白痴笑？」盛怒之下，他做了一件違反所有騎士準則的事：他打了她一巴掌。

Parsifal saw it, and stared with astonishment. How could a man, a knight, strike a woman? And then another knight at this table said to him, "She suffers for your sake. Her laughter marked you as the knight who will attain the highest honour."

帕西法爾看到了，驚愕地盯著。一個男人，一個騎士，怎能打女人？這時桌旁另一位騎士對他說：「她是為你而受苦的。她的笑聲標誌著你就是那位將獲得至高榮耀的騎士。」

And Parsifal said, "I cannot wait now to make the man who hurt her and who insulted me pay for his behaviour - unworthy of a knight - but one day he will regret it."

帕西法爾說：「我現在無暇讓那個傷害她、侮辱我的人為他那不配騎士身份的行為付出代價——但總有一天他會後悔的。」

And so he rode out of the palace to the meadow where Ither, the red knight, was waiting with the golden cup.

於是他騎馬離開宮殿，來到伊瑟——紅騎士——手持金杯等候的草地上。

As Parsifal turned his head to give a last look at the palace, the place to which he had looked so expectantly when he came, and where he had been met with insults and rude behaviour, he saw somebody coming running after him. It was Ivanet, the page, who had first shown him the entrance to the great hall. Of all the people, high and low, in King Arthur's hall, only the page felt enough concern to come along to see how Parsifal would fare at the hands of the red knight.

帕西法爾回頭最後望了一眼宮殿——那個他來時滿懷期待的地方，他卻在那裡遭遇了侮辱和粗暴的對待——他看見有人追了上來。那是侍從伊瓦涅，最初為他指路去大廳的那個人。在亞瑟王大廳裡的所有人中，無論貴賤，只有這個侍從關心帕西法爾在紅騎士手下會有怎樣的遭遇，而跟了過來。

And so Parsifal, accompanied by the page, rode on to his meeting with the red knight.

就這樣，帕西法爾在侍從的陪伴下，策馬前往他與紅騎士的會面。

---

## 5.The Origins of Knighthood 騎士制度的起源

The men who appear in the Parsifal story are all knights - the whole story begins with young Parsifal's desire to become a knight - and all the motives, all the reasons why people do things, in the story are connected with knighthood.


出現在帕西法爾故事中的人物都是騎士——整個故事從年輕帕西法爾渴望成為騎士開始——故事中所有的動機、所有人們行事的理由，都與騎士制度有關。

Now I want to remind you of some things you have heard already - how this institution of knighthood came into existence - that its origin lies in the German tribes which overran and destroyed the Roman Empire.

現在我想提醒你們一些你們已經聽過的事——這個騎士制度是怎樣產生的——它的源頭在於那些席捲並摧毀羅馬帝國的日耳曼部落。

These Germanic tribes had one outstanding characteristic - a love of fighting, whether it was single combat or in battle. When a boy was born, the father decided if the baby looked like they would grow up into a strong warrior. If he thought the child looked ailing or weakly, the child was killed. That was the beginning of life - and at the end of life, it was shameful for a man to die in his bed ("straw death" they called it).
