If a man could not manage to get himself killed in battle and grew too old, he had himself and all his possessions put on a ship that was set aflame, and he died in the fire, as wind and currents carried the ship away.

這些日耳曼部落有一個最突出的特點——對戰鬥的熱愛，無論是單打獨鬥還是戰場搏殺。當男嬰出生時，父親會判斷這個孩子長大後是否能成為強壯的戰士。如果他認為孩子看起來體弱多病，這個孩子就會被殺死。這就是生命的起始——而在生命的盡頭，一個男人如果死在床上（他們稱之為「稻草之死」），那是莫大的恥辱。如果一個人無法在戰場上戰死而年紀太大了，他就會連同所有財物一起被放在一艘船上，點燃大火，在風和水流將船帶走的過程中，在火焰中死去。

These Germanic tribes believed in an afterlife - a life after death. But they imagined that even that life in another world was made enjoyable by battles between the souls of dead warriors.

這些日耳曼部落相信來世——死後的生命。但他們設想，即便是在另一個世界中的生活，也是通過亡故戰士靈魂之間的戰鬥來獲得樂趣的。

So when they went into battle, they threw themselves into the fighting with a wild, savage joy - with a kind of mad fury, which the Romans came to fear, and to know. They called it *Furor Teutonicus*, the Germanic fury.

因此，當他們上戰場時，就以一種狂野的、蠻荒的快感投入戰鬥——帶著一種瘋狂的狂暴，這讓羅馬人既畏懼又深有體會。他們稱之為 *Furor Teutonicus*——日耳曼狂暴。

This "Germanic fury" was in the very blood of these tribes - it was as strong as hunger or thirst, this lust for fighting. Now, such a terrific force or urge does not simply disappear, vanish, in the course of time - it can only be changed, transformed.

這種「日耳曼狂暴」存在於這些部落的血液之中——這種戰鬥的渴望像飢餓或口渴一樣強烈。然而，如此巨大的力量或衝動不會隨著時間的推移而消失——它只能被改變、被轉化。

And the thing which changed and transformed the Germanic fury, slowly and gradually, was the coming of Christianity. These wild warriors did not, after becoming Christians, simply turn into nice, peaceful citizens - far from it. The first Germanic Christians were as bloodthirsty as their forefathers.

而緩慢地、逐漸地改變和轉化了日耳曼狂暴的，正是基督教的傳入。這些狂野的戰士在成為基督徒之後，並沒有就此變成和善的、溫和的市民——遠遠不是。最初的日耳曼基督徒與他們的祖先一樣嗜血。

But under the influence of Christianity, this fighting spirit was gradually regulated and made to serve a higher purpose - and so arose the institution of knighthood. A knight was still a fighting man - if there were no battles, knights fought each other in tournaments. But the knight was under an obligation to protect women, the poor, and those in distress - he had a duty to keep roads free from robbers, and instead of behaving like a savage, he had to practise courtesy.

但在基督教的影響下，這種戰鬥精神逐漸被規範，並為更高的目的服務——騎士制度就這樣誕生了。騎士仍然是一個戰鬥之人——如果沒有戰爭，騎士就在比武大會上互相較量。但騎士有義務保護婦女、窮人和受苦之人——他有責任使道路不受盜匪侵擾，而且他不能像野蠻人那樣行事，而必須奉行禮節。

In the so-called Dark Ages, when there was very little law and order, the knight, the fighting man, became the guardian of the poor and the weak. In this way, Christianity used the wild fighting spirit for a constructive purpose, and civilization, which had disappeared with the fall of Rome, could begin again.

在所謂的黑暗時代，法律和秩序極為匱乏，騎士——戰鬥之人——成了窮人和弱者的守護者。就這樣，基督教將狂野的戰鬥精神用於建設性的目的，隨著羅馬的衰亡而消失的文明得以重新開始。

But the change, the transformation from Germanic fury to noble Christian knight was not easily and quickly achieved. You could say that in every knight there lurked, under the courtly manners and high ideals - a wild, bloodthirsty creature who could break out at any moment. It was as if in every knight, the dark, old Germanic fury was struggling against the Christian principles - and it was not always the Christian principles that won. You can see it from one of countless historical examples one could give.

但這種從日耳曼狂暴到高貴的基督教騎士的改變、轉化，並非輕易或迅速就能達成的。可以說，在每一個騎士的內心深處，在彬彬有禮和崇高理想之下——潛伏著一頭狂野嗜血的野獸，隨時可能爆發。彷彿在每一個騎士身上，黑暗而古老的日耳曼狂暴都在與基督教的原則抗爭——而並不總是基督教的原則獲勝。在無數可以舉出的歷史例子中，就有這樣一個。

In 1369, during the Hundred Years War between France and England, the English king, Edward III, laid siege to the city of Calais. The defenders were willing to surrender - but first they wanted to know what they had to expect if they let the English come into the city. King Edward, who was in a bad mood because he had lost many of his men during the siege, told the messengers from Calais, "I will not spare a single life in your city!"

1369年，在英法百年戰爭期間，英格蘭國王愛德華三世（Edward III）圍攻加萊城（Calais）。守城者願意投降——但首先他們想知道，如果讓英軍進城，等待他們的將是什麼。愛德華國王因為在圍城中損失了許多部下而心情惡劣，對加萊來的使者說：「我不會饒過你們城裡任何一條性命！」

But this was too much for the English knights who were with him. They entreated the king - they pleaded with him, they reminded him of his knightly vows - and in the end King Edward said reluctantly, "Well, I will spare the population - but six of the leading citizens must be hanged. And that's my last word in this matter."

但這對跟隨他的英格蘭騎士們來說實在太過分了。他們懇求國王——向他苦苦哀求，提醒他的騎士誓言——最終愛德華國王不情願地說：「好吧，我饒過平民——但六個領頭的市民必須被絞死。這是我對此事的最後決定。」

At this stage, the king's wife, Philippa, who had come to visit him, intervened. She fell on her knees before him, and begged him, for her sake, to spare the six. King Edward stood for a moment frowning and in silence. And then he said, "I wish you were not here today. But as you are here, I cannot refuse your request. The city, and all lives in it, are yours."

這時，前來探望國王的王后菲莉帕（Philippa）出面干預。她跪在他面前，央求他看在她的份上饒過那六人。愛德華國王皺著眉頭沉默了片刻。然後他說：「我真希望你今天不在這裡。但既然你來了，我就不能拒絕你的請求。城市和城中所有的生命，都歸你了。」

In such an episode, you can see how the bloodlust of the Germanic tribes was still there, and was barely held under control by the higher principles of Christianity.

在這樣的插曲中，你可以看到日耳曼部落的嗜血本性仍然存在，僅僅被基督教的更高原則勉強壓制著。

Yet there were also people in those times who achieved, one could say, a miraculous transformation of this fighting spirit. One such person was St Francis of Assisi.

然而在那個時代，也有人可以說奇蹟般地實現了這種戰鬥精神的轉化。其中一位就是亞西西的聖方濟各（St Francis of Assisi）。

Francis was, to begin with, determined to become a warrior, a knight. He had already taken part in battles and had fully enjoyed the clash of arms, the feel of danger and the strange intoxication that comes with fighting. But then there came a complete change in his life. As he put it, he felt a call to fight quite a different battle. Against the wishes of his father, he became a monk, and a monk who practised the most extreme forms of self-denial. He became a man devoted only to one task: to help those in need.

方濟各最初一心要成為一名戰士、一名騎士。他已經參加過戰鬥，充分享受了兵戎相見的撞擊、危險的感覺和戰鬥帶來的奇異陶醉。但後來，他的生命發生了徹底的轉變。用他自己的話說，他感到一種召喚，要去打一場完全不同的仗。違背了父親的意願，他成了一個修士——一個奉行最極端的自我克制的修士。他成了一個只獻身於一項使命的人：幫助那些有需要的人。

There is no better illustration than the following story. In the woods near the place where Francis and his followers had settled, there lived a gang of robbers. Three of these robbers, finding themselves short of food, came to the little community, demanding food and brandishing their swords. Francis himself was away at that time, but one of his followers courageously told the robbers what he thought of them. How could they, thieves and murderers, demand food from men of God, who had themselves only collected their scraps by begging? The robbers, somewhat shamefaced, withdrew. But a little later, Francis returned, and when he heard what had happened, he was furious. Not with the robbers, but with the friar who had sent the robbers packing. It was their duty to think of others first, not themselves. And a hungry man was a hungry man - robber or not. In the end, the poor friar had to take all the food there was and carry it himself to the hideout of the robbers in the forest.

沒有比下面這個故事更好的例證了。在方濟各和他的追隨者定居之處附近的森林裡，住著一夥強盜。其中三個強盜因為缺少食物，來到這個小社團，揮舞著劍要求食物。方濟各本人當時不在，但他的一個追隨者勇敢地向強盜們說出了他的看法。他們這些盜賊和殺人犯，怎麼能向上帝的人要食物？他們自己的食物也不過是乞討來的殘羹冷炙。強盜們多少有些慚愧地退走了。但不久之後，方濟各回來了，當他聽說了所發生的事情時，他怒火中燒。不是對強盜生氣，而是對那個把強盜趕走的修士生氣。他們的職責是首先考慮別人，而不是自己。而一個飢餓的人就是一個飢餓的人——不管他是不是強盜。最後，那個可憐的修士不得不把所有的食物都拿上，親自送到森林裡強盜的藏身處。

In Francis, the fighting spirit had, indeed, been transformed into selflessness and compassion.

在方濟各身上，戰鬥精神確實被轉化為了無私和慈悲。

This is the thing we have to keep in mind about this age of knights and chivalry - the continuous struggle between the old love for fighting, and the new ideals which Francis of Assisi represented.

這就是關於騎士與騎士精神的時代，我們必須牢記的要點——古老的戰鬥嗜好與亞西西的方濟各所代表的新理想之間持續不斷的鬥爭。

And this struggle is by no means over; it goes on right into our times. In the Second World War, those of us who were soldiers killed because we had to - but I have seen men who positively enjoyed it. They were "throwbacks", men in whom the ancient forces came to light again. And there is still another way in which that old lust for fighting shows itself. There are millions of people who would not dream of committing any act of violence themselves - but what are the films and television shows they like best?

而這場鬥爭絕未結束，它一直延續到我們的時代。在第二次世界大戰中，我們之中當過軍人的人殺人是因為不得不殺——但我見過有些人確實樂在其中。他們是「返祖者」，是古老力量在他們身上重新顯現的人。而那種古老的戰鬥渴望還有另一種表現方式。數以百萬計的人自己絕不會夢想施行任何暴力行為——但他們最喜歡的電影和電視節目是什麼呢？

Of course, if young children are brought up on a mental diet of such shows and films, these old instincts are made stronger, and they grow up with a hunger for violence. It is not surprising if you then hear of teenage gangs fighting battles amongst themselves, of committing crimes for "kicks", of creating havoc just for the fun of it. It is just the old "fury" which is once again breaking loose and hitting out in all directions.

當然，如果年幼的孩子在這樣的節目和電影的精神飲食中長大，這些古老的本能就會被強化，他們長大後就會渴望暴力。那麼當你聽說少年幫派互相爭鬥、為了「刺激」而犯罪、為了好玩而大肆破壞時，也就不足為奇了。那不過是古老的「狂暴」再次掙脫束縛，向四面八方猛烈出擊。

But something else has also happened. The ancient warlust also had its positive side: it was combined with great courage and with a sense of adventure. As the centuries passed, this courage and sense of adventure led to the Age of Discovery, to the travels of Columbus and Magellan. And still later that courage and a sense of adventure in the human spirit led to modern science.

但另外一些事情也發生了。古老的戰鬥渴望也有其積極的一面：它與巨大的勇氣和冒險精神結合在一起。隨著歲月的流逝，這種勇氣和冒險精神帶來了大發現時代，帶來了哥倫布（Columbus）和麥哲倫（Magellan）的航行。更往後，人類精神中的這種勇氣和冒險精神又催生了現代科學。

The same thing which made us aggressive has also made our science, our inventions, our whole technical progress. On the other hand, there is also the idealism which has led, for instance, to the establishment of the Red Cross.

使我們具有攻擊性的同一種東西，也造就了我們的科學、我們的發明、我們整個技術進步。而另一方面，也有那種理想主義，它催生了例如紅十字會（Red Cross）的建立。

Henri Dunant, the founder of the Red Cross, was - to begin with - a businessman, a Swiss banker. He went to Paris about some business contract, and he could only get the contract with the agreement of the ruler of France, who was then Napoleon III (a great-nephew of the first Napoleon). But this important man, Napoleon III, was not in Paris. The Italians had asked for his help against the Austrians, and he was in Italy leading an army. So this Swiss business man, Dunant, travelled to Italy to get his contract signed - and he came just on the eve of a great battle. Napoleon had no time to see him, but Dunant was allowed to stay, and so the next day he had a grandstand view of the whole battle from a hill.

紅十字會的創始人亨利·杜南（Henri Dunant），最初是一位商人，一位瑞士銀行家。他去巴黎辦一樁商業合同，但只有得到當時法國統治者拿破崙三世（Napoleon III，第一位拿破崙的侄孫）的同意才能拿到合同。但這位重要人物，拿破崙三世，不在巴黎。義大利人請求他幫助對抗奧地利人，他正在義大利指揮軍隊。於是，這位瑞士商人杜南前往義大利尋求簽約——他恰好在一場大戰前夕趕到。拿破崙沒空見他，但杜南獲准留下，於是第二天他在一座小山上擁有了觀看整場戰役的絕佳位置。

This battle, the battle of Solferino, was one of the most murderous battles in history. At the end of the day, when the Austrians were defeated, some 10,000 dead and wounded were left on the battlefield. There were neither doctors nor nurses nor ambulances to attend to them. There wasn't even any water. All night long, their agonised cries rose from the blood-soaked fields. In that night, the business man, Dunant, forgot his business. He gathered helpers from neighbouring villages and organised their work and supplies of water, food, bandages. For three days and nights, the little band worked without rest.

這場戰役——索爾費里諾戰役（battle of Solferino）——是歷史上最血腥的戰役之一。當天結束時，奧軍戰敗，約一萬名死傷者被留在了戰場上。既沒有醫生也沒有護士，也沒有救護車來照看他們。甚至連水都沒有。整夜，他們痛苦的呼號從血浸的田野上升起。在那一夜，商人杜南忘記了他的生意。他從附近村莊召集幫手，組織他們的工作和水、食物、繃帶的供應。整整三天三夜，這支小隊不眠不休地工作著。

In this way did Dunant conceive the idea of the Red Cross. He devoted his life to it. His banking business was ruined by neglect and he lost all he had. But he established the Red Cross, and the Geneva Convention was based on his idea for an organisation to care for wounded soldiers. Then, poor and penniless, he withdrew and lived, unknown, in some valley in Switzerland.

杜南就這樣構想出了紅十字會的理念。他把一生都奉獻給了它。他的銀行事業因為疏於經營而破產，他失去了所有的一切。但他建立了紅十字會，日內瓦公約（Geneva Convention）正是基於他為照顧傷兵而設立組織的構想。之後，他身無分文，隱居在瑞士某個山谷中，無人知曉。

The Red Cross and later organisations like Oxfam are the other side of our inheritance. And both sides go back to the knights of the Middle Ages - the life of a knight was a continuous struggle between these two sides. And we shall see that our hero, Parsifal, had his full share of the aggressive and violent spirit - of the red-hot fury of battle.

紅十字會以及後來的樂施會（Oxfam）等組織，代表了我們遺產的另一面。而這兩面都可以追溯到中世紀的騎士——騎士的生活就是這兩面之間不斷的鬥爭。我們將會看到，我們的主人公帕西法爾也充分擁有那攻擊性和暴力的精神——那戰鬥中白熱化的狂暴。

---

## 6.The Battle with the Red Knight 紅騎士之戰

You have heard how Parsifal was received at the court of King Arthur. He had found little sympathy for his youthful ardour. The king in his courtly manner, Sir Kay in his rude fashion, had both harped on his youth and lack of experience. The king, out of kindness, wanted to protect him from getting hurt by the red knight. Sir Kay, out of unkindness, had wished him to get hurt and be taught a lesson by it. Neither had given him a word of encouragement. It is an attitude young people are bound to encounter often in life - and which, like Parsifal, they are bound to resent. But if their heart is set on something - as Parsifal's was set on the red armour - they will go for it just the same, as Parsifal did.


你已經聽過帕西法爾在亞瑟王宮廷裡受到的接待了。他的青春熱情幾乎沒有得到同情。國王以他高雅的方式，凱爵士以他粗魯的態度，都在強調他的年輕和缺乏經驗。國王出於善意，想保護他不被紅騎士傷害。凱爵士出於惡意，巴不得他受傷、吃一次教訓。兩個人都沒有給他一句鼓勵的話。這是年輕人在生活中必然會經常遇到的態度——而且像帕西法爾一樣，他們必然會對此心生怨忿。但如果他們的心已經認定了什麼——正如帕西法爾認定了紅色盔甲——他們還是會義無反顧地去爭取，就像帕西法爾那樣。

It is in features like this that one can see the great human understanding of the medieval troubadours who composed the story of Parsifal - it is in features like this that the vast gap between our time and their time, our world of mechanical progress and their world of more simple conditions, is bridged. It is features like this which create a link between us and Parsifal, as he rode out to the meadow for his encounter with the red knight.

正是在這樣的細節中，人們可以看到那些創作帕西法爾故事的中世紀吟遊詩人對人性的深刻理解——正是在這樣的細節中，我們的時代和他們的時代之間、我們機械進步的世界和他們較為質樸的世界之間的巨大鴻溝得以跨越。正是這樣的細節，在我們和帕西法爾之間建立了一座橋樑——當他策馬奔向草地，去迎戰紅騎士的時候。

Ither, the red knight, had put the golden goblet down on a stone, to be ready for the fight as soon as one of Arthur's knights came in answer to his challenge. But it was no knight in armour who now came riding towards him, but the young man he had asked to carry his message. So the red knight asked, "Are none of Arthur's knights coming to fight me for the cup?"

紅騎士伊瑟已經把金杯放在一塊石頭上，做好了準備，一旦亞瑟王的騎士前來應戰就可以立即開打。但向他騎來的不是一位身穿盔甲的騎士，而是他託付口信的那個年輕人。於是紅騎士問道：「亞瑟的騎士都不來為這杯子和我一戰嗎？」

"No," answered Parsifal, "but I asked King Arthur for the splendid red armour you wear, and he said I can have it. So take it off - I can hardly wait to put it on!"

「沒有，」帕西法爾回答，「但我向亞瑟王要了你穿的這身華麗紅甲，他說我可以拿走。所以脫下來吧——我都等不及要穿上了！」

"Listen," said the red knight, "are you quite sure that of all the great knights of the Round Table, none has the courage to fight me?"

「聽著，」紅騎士說，「你確定圓桌的所有偉大騎士中，沒有一個有勇氣來和我交戰？」

But Parsifal was in no mood for explanation. He cried, "Take off your armour - put down your arms! I have told you already they are mine - King Arthur gave them to me."

但帕西法爾沒心情解釋。他喊道：「脫下你的盔甲——放下你的武器！我已經告訴過你了，它們是我的——亞瑟王把它們給了我。」

"That's very generous of him," said the red knight, "he might just as well have given you my life - for you have to take my life first, before you can take my armour."

「他可真大方，」紅騎士說，「他乾脆把我的命也給你好了——因為你得先取了我的性命，才能拿走我的盔甲。」

"It's all the same to me," said Parsifal, who was getting impatient. "If you don't hand the things over willingly, you may well lose your life, as well as the armour! Come on - hand over what King Arthur gave to me!"

「對我來說都一樣，」帕西法爾不耐煩了。「你要是不乖乖交出來，你可能連命都丟了，不光是盔甲！快點——把亞瑟王給我的東西交出來！」

The red knight treated the whole thing as a joke, and said, "And by what great deeds have you deserved such a rich reward as my armour?"

紅騎士把整件事當作笑話，說道：「你憑什麼偉大的功績配得上我的盔甲這樣豐厚的獎賞？」

"I shall earn what I deserve!" cried Parsifal angrily. "I shall earn it now!" And with these words, he rode close to the red knight and reached over to grab his bridle.

「我會贏得我應得的！」帕西法爾憤怒地喊道。「我現在就要贏得它！」說著，他策馬靠近紅騎士，伸手去抓他的韁繩。

The red knight reversed his spear shaft, and took it in both hands and struck Parsifal with such force that he fell from his horse. Parsifal scrambled to his feet - but as soon as he stood up, there came another glancing blow that tore some skin from his scalp.

紅騎士反轉矛桿，雙手握住，猛力打向帕西法爾，把他從馬上打了下來。帕西法爾連滾帶爬地站起來——但他剛站穩，又挨了一擊擦過頭皮，撕去了一塊皮。

In that moment, a blazing fury seized Parsifal - there was a red haze before his eyes, and the blood was pounding in his ears. He saw his throwing-spear lying on the ground. He quickly picked it up, and with the marksmanship the years in the forest had given him, he sent it straight through the red knight's eye and into the brain.

就在那一刻，一股燃燒的狂怒攫住了帕西法爾——眼前出現了一片紅色的霧靄，耳中血液狂湧。他看見自己的投矛躺在地上。他迅速撿起，憑著多年森林生活練就的投擲技術，一矛正中紅騎士的眼睛，直穿入腦。

One moment the tall red figure was upright in the saddle, the next he came tumbling to the ground.

前一刻那高大的紅色身影還端坐在馬鞍上，下一刻他就翻滾著摔落在地。

In Parsifal's mind there was only room for one thought: "Now the armour is mine - his weapons are mine - his horse is mine!" With clumsy hands, he tore at the helmet, but having no experience with the straps that held a knight's armour together, he could not get it off. He fumbled with the sword belt, but had no better luck.

帕西法爾的腦海中只有一個念頭：「現在盔甲是我的了——他的武器是我的——他的馬是我的！」他用笨拙的手扯著頭盔，但因為不懂固定騎士盔甲的帶扣，怎麼也解不下來。他擺弄劍帶，也沒有好運。

At this moment, the page, Ivanet, who had watched the whole encounter, recovered from his amazement at the unexpected outcome. He came running along, and said, "I can hardly believe what I saw - you brought down a great and famous knight! But let me help you with the armour - you don't know how to handle these things!"

就在這時，一直在旁觀看整場交鋒的侍從伊瓦涅從意外結局的震驚中回過神來。他跑了過來，說道：「我簡直不敢相信我看到的——你打倒了一位偉大而著名的騎士！但讓我來幫你卸盔甲——你不知道怎麼弄這些東西！」

And so, with the page's help, the dead knight was stripped of his armour. Then the page said, "Now, take off the things you are wearing, and put on the fine clothes that go under the armour."

於是，在侍從的幫助下，死去的騎士被卸去了盔甲。然後侍從說：「現在，脫下你身上穿的東西，換上盔甲裡面穿的精美衣裳。」

"Oh, no," said Parsifal. "I would not dream of changing the clothes my mother gave me - not for the finest garb in the world!"

「噢，不，」帕西法爾說。「我絕不會換掉母親給我的衣服——即使是世界上最好的衣裳也不換！」

No matter how much the page pleaded with him, Parsifal would not give up his patchwork coat. And so, in the end, Parsifal was encased in the red armour from head to foot, but under it he wore the clothes in which he had come from the forest. He did not know it, but this strange combination was a true picture of himself, who had a knight's outer trappings but not yet the inner aims and obligations that made a true knight.

無論侍從怎樣苦苦相勸，帕西法爾都不願放棄那件拼布外衣。最終，帕西法爾從頭到腳披上了紅色盔甲，但盔甲裡面穿的仍是他從森林中帶來的衣服。他自己並不知道，但這奇怪的組合正是他自身的真實寫照——擁有騎士的外在裝束，卻還不具備使人成為真正騎士的內在志向和責任。

When Parsifal was at last in full knightly attire and seated on the red horse, he said to Ivanet, "To you, I give the red knight's fine clothes, and the horse that has carried me until now. Take the golden goblet from yonder stone and bring it back to King Arthur, for I am not going back to his court. I was insulted there by Sir Kay, and a lady was made to suffer for my sake. One day I will come back when I have proved I am as good as any of Arthur's knights, and then the wrong done to the lady will be avenged. Give my greetings to the King!" And with these words, he rode away proudly in his red armour, now he had become the red knight.

當帕西法爾終於穿戴齊整、騎上那匹紅馬時，他對伊瓦涅說：「紅騎士的精美衣物和一直馱著我的那匹馬，都送給你。從那塊石頭上拿起金杯，帶回去交給亞瑟王，因為我不會回他的宮廷去了。凱爵士在那裡侮辱了我，還有一位女士因為我而受苦。總有一天，等我證明了自己不遜於亞瑟的任何一位騎士，我會回來的，那時對那位女士的傷害將得到報復。替我向國王致意！」說完這番話，他披著紅色盔甲傲然策馬而去——現在他成了紅騎士。

The page brought the news and the goblet to King Arthur, and there was great wonder at court that this youngster had slain Ither, a knight of great renown. But Arthur said, "It was not a knightly combat, for knights are forbidden the use of hunting spears when fighting each other. And it was only a stroke of luck that made the throw deadly. In truth, the youngster will not last long - the next knight with experience will make an end of him." To Ither, his dead enemy, King Arthur gave a burial worthy of a great and noble lord.

侍從把消息和金杯帶回給亞瑟王，宮廷中人人驚嘆這個少年竟然殺死了大名鼎鼎的騎士伊瑟。但亞瑟說：「那不是一場騎士式的交戰，因為騎士之間交鋒時禁止使用獵矛。那致命的一投不過是僥倖罷了。說實話，這個小伙子撐不了多久——下一個有經驗的騎士就會要了他的命。」對於他的死敵伊瑟，亞瑟王給予了配得上一位偉大而高貴的領主的葬禮。

But as regards Parsifal, the king was mistaken if he thought he would soon lose the new armour and his life - because good luck was with the youngster, and the whole day he met no other knight who might have challenged him to fight.

但在帕西法爾的問題上，如果國王以為他很快就會失去新盔甲和性命，那就錯了——因為好運伴隨著這個少年，整整一天他都沒有遇到任何可能向他挑戰的騎士。

But towards evening, Parsifal came to a castle, a castle surrounded by great walls and a deep moat with a drawbridge across it. An old man in fine clothes stood on the drawbridge, looking out on the road. Parsifal, as was his habit, said, "Greetings to you, as my mother has told me!"

但傍晚時分，帕西法爾來到一座城堡——城堡四周有高牆和一條深深的護城河，上面架著吊橋。一位穿著講究的老人站在吊橋上，向路上眺望。帕西法爾按照慣例說道：「向您問好，這是我母親教我說的！」

The old gentleman was puzzled by this strange greeting - he was also puzzled to see a knight in armour of great splendour, who carried his shield and spear in the most clumsy fashion.

這位老紳士對這奇怪的問候感到困惑——他同樣困惑的是，看到一位騎士穿著華麗的盔甲，卻用最笨拙的方式拿著盾牌和長矛。

And so, driven as much by curiosity as by the rules of hospitality, the old gentleman - Gurnemant was his name - invited Parsifal to be his guest for the night, an invitation which the young man, tired from the unaccustomed weight on him, gladly accepted.

於是，出於好奇也出於待客之道，這位老紳士——他叫古爾內曼茲（Gurnemant）——邀請帕西法爾留宿一晚。年輕人因為不習慣身上的沉重負擔而疲憊不堪，欣然接受了邀請。

Gurnemant, the host, was not a little surprised when his servants helped the guest out of his armour. Why was the fellow wearing this patchwork garb of a fool? But Gurnemant was too well-mannered to be inquisitive.

主人古爾內曼茲在僕人們幫客人卸下盔甲時著實吃了一驚。這傢伙為什麼穿著這樣一件丑角的拼布衣裳？但古爾內曼茲太有教養了，不會多加追問。

Parsifal, however, had no such scruples. For him, the inside of a castle was full of things he had not seen before, and he hardly stopped asking questions from the moment he set foot inside the courtyard.

然而帕西法爾可沒有這樣的顧忌。對他來說，城堡裡面到處都是他從未見過的東西，從踏入庭院的那一刻起，他就幾乎沒有停止過問問題。

What were those birds sitting with hoods over their heads in cages? Hunting falcons. Why were there basins with water and towels in the banqueting hall? Because eating with one's hands made the fingers greasy, and so one had to clean them. Why was the tower so high? To see approaching enemies from afar.

那些關在籠子裡、頭上戴著兜帽的鳥是什麼？獵鷹。為什麼宴會廳裡有盛水的盆和毛巾？因為用手吃東西手指會油膩，所以得清洗。為什麼塔樓那麼高？為了從遠處看到逼近的敵人。

He hardly stopped asking questions, until they sat down to their meal. Then old Gurnemant said, "I can see, my young friend, that you know very little of a knight's way of life."

他幾乎問個不停，直到他們坐下用餐。這時老古爾內曼茲說：「我看得出來，年輕的朋友，你對騎士的生活方式知之甚少。」

Parsifal admitted candidly that he did not. "But," he added, "I am most anxious to learn."

帕西法爾坦然承認自己確實如此。「但是，」他補充道，「我最渴望的就是學習。」

"Well," said Gurnemant, who had taken a liking to the eager young man, "I am willing to be your teacher - you can stay here and learn from me."

「好，」古爾內曼茲說，他已經喜歡上了這個熱切的年輕人，「我願意做你的老師——你可以留在這裡跟我學。」

Parsifal was overjoyed by this offer, and told his host how grateful he would be. Then Gurnemant said words which became fateful for Parsifal - more fateful than either he or his host could possibly foresee.

帕西法爾對這個提議欣喜若狂，告訴主人他將多麼感激。接著古爾內曼茲說了一番話，這番話對帕西法爾來說意義重大——其影響之深遠，遠超他和主人所能預見的。

It is often so in life that a chance word, spoken without any particular intention, can have quite unexpected and far-reaching consequences. Perhaps it is a mercy that we have no foreknowledge of the effects of our words. The words spoken by Gurnemant were certainly going to play a fateful part in Parsifal's life.

在生活中常常如此：一句無心之語，不帶任何特別意圖，卻可能產生完全意想不到的、深遠的後果。也許我們對自己言語的影響一無所知，倒是一種恩賜。古爾內曼茲所說的話，確實將在帕西法爾的人生中扮演關鍵的角色。

What Gurnemant said was this, "As I am to be your teacher, we can start instructions right now. And one of the first rules of courtly manners - of good behaviour - for a knight, is: don't be inquisitive, don't ask too many questions - as you have done since you came. Such questions may, perhaps, embarrass the person you ask - it may also show you up as ignorant - and so, either way, you will not be liked or respected for it."

古爾內曼茲說的是這番話：「既然我要做你的老師，我們現在就可以開始上課了。而騎士的宮廷禮儀——良好教養——的第一條規則就是：不要好奇心太重，不要問太多問題——就像你來了之後一直在做的那樣。這樣的問題也許會讓被問的人尷尬——也可能暴露你的無知——所以不管怎樣，你都不會因此受人喜歡或尊重。」

Gurnemant was well-intentioned when he gave such sound, practical advice to Parsifal - and Parsifal, eager to live up to the rules of knightly conduct, took that advice to heart. Yet much sorrow was to come from it.

古爾內曼茲給帕西法爾這樣中肯、實際的忠告，確實出於好意——而帕西法爾，急切地想遵循騎士行為準則，把這忠告銘記在心。然而，日後將有許多悲傷由此而來。

But on that evening, Parsifal told Gurnemant his story - how he had left the forest, how he had learned of the kingdom that should be his by rights, how he had fared at Arthur's court, and how he had won the red knight's armour. And he learnt that possessing the armour did not make him a knight - that only when he had been trained and instructed would his host, Gurnemant, perform the ceremony called "accolade" which would make him a knight.

但在那天晚上，帕西法爾向古爾內曼茲講述了自己的故事——他是怎樣離開森林的，怎樣得知了本應屬於他的王國，在亞瑟王宮廷的遭遇，以及他是怎樣贏得紅騎士盔甲的。他還學到，擁有盔甲並不能讓他成為騎士——只有當他受過訓練和教導之後，他的主人古爾內曼茲才會為他舉行被稱為「冊封禮」（accolade）的儀式，使他正式成為騎士。

And so Parsifal stayed with the noble Gurnemant, and learned the proper use of spear and sword, he learned the rules of knightly combat, and he learned the rules of courtesy. Never had a master a more willing pupil, and so it was not long until the day when Gurnemant touched his shoulder with the flat of the sword and said, "I dub thee - knight!" When Parsifal left the castle, he no longer wore the fool's dress under his armour, he no longer greeted people mentioning his mother, he was no longer ignorant of the ways of the world.

於是帕西法爾留在了高貴的古爾內曼茲身邊，學習了矛和劍的正確使用方法，學習了騎士交鋒的規則，也學習了禮節的規範。從未有老師擁有過一個比他更勤奮好學的弟子，因此不久之後就到了古爾內曼茲用劍面輕觸他的肩膀說出「我封你為騎士！」的那一天。當帕西法爾離開城堡時，他的盔甲底下不再穿著丑角服，他不再提著母親的名字跟人打招呼，他也不再對世間的規矩一無所知了。

Yet knighthood was not a matter of knowing things, it was a matter of deeds. In strife and battle, in adventure and danger, a knight had to prove his worth. And so it was in search of action, in search of foes to be challenged and conquered, that Parsifal set out when he left the castle of Gurnemant.

然而騎士精神不在於知識的多寡，而在於行動的實踐。在爭鬥和戰役中，在冒險和危險中，一個騎士必須證明自己的價值。因此，帕西法爾離開古爾內曼茲的城堡時，正是為了尋找行動、尋找值得挑戰和征服的敵手。

Adventure soon came to meet him. It was in the late afternoon that he came to a great city. Outside the city there was a camp of armed men, and Parsifal saw they had the long ladders and wooden towers used for the assault of a city. The city's walls were manned by armed men, ready with bows and arrows and buckets with hot pitch to repel the attackers. The city was besieged. There was, however, no fighting going on, and no-one came to bar his way as he rode past the camp and to the gate of the city. He hammered on the gate, and demanded entry as a stranger who had no part in the quarrel. He was let in, but the men who met him at the gate, all heavily armed, cast dark looks at him and he heard them murmur that, rules of hospitality or not, this was no time to feed a useless visitor.

冒險很快迎面而來。午後時分，他來到一座大城市。城外有一座武裝營地，帕西法爾看到他們有攻城用的長梯和木塔。城牆上站滿了武裝的守軍，備有弓箭和裝滿熱瀝青的桶，準備擊退進攻者。城市正在被圍困。然而當時並沒有戰鬥在進行，也沒有人攔住他，他騎馬經過營地來到城門前。他敲打城門，以一個與爭端無關的陌生人的身份要求進入。他被放了進去，但在城門口迎接他的人都全副武裝，用陰沉的目光打量著他。他聽到他們低聲嘀咕，說不管什麼待客之道，現在可不是養一個沒用的外人的時候。

And coming into the city, Parsifal soon understood the remark. All the people he saw were pale, and mere skin and bones. No smell of cooking came from any house or from the inns - and the stalls in the market place were empty.

進入城內後，帕西法爾很快就理解了那番話的含義。他看到的人個個面色蒼白、骨瘦如柴。沒有任何一戶人家或客棧飄出烹飪的氣味——市場上的攤位也空空如也。

From one of the haggard-looking inhabitants, Parsifal learned that the city was under the rule of a young queen, Condwiramur (from French *conduire amour*, to lead to love), and he made his way to her palace.

從一個面容憔悴的居民口中，帕西法爾得知這座城市由一位年輕的女王統治——康德薇拉穆爾（Condwiramur，源自法語 *conduire amour*，意為「引領至愛」），他便朝她的宮殿走去。

Even in a city under siege and suffering from starvation, the rules of hospitality for a wandering knight could not be broken, and Condwiramur, the queen, received Parsifal with great courtesy - and with apologies for the meagre fare she could only offer. But Parsifal was hardly aware of whatever food was served to him. From the moment he had set eyes on the queen - who was but a girl of his own age - it was as if suddenly everything else in the world had become unimportant, and the only thing that mattered was to be near her. But he could not fail to notice the grave expression on a face which, he thought, was made to smile. In his heart, he was already resolved to be "her" knight, to offer his services to her and to do all he could to dispel her troubles.

即使在一座遭受圍困和饑荒之苦的城市裡，對一位雲遊騎士的待客之道也不能被打破。女王康德薇拉穆爾以極大的禮遇接待了帕西法爾——並為只能提供菲薄的飲食而致歉。但帕西法爾幾乎沒有注意到端上的是什麼食物。從他看到女王的那一刻起——她不過是個和他同齡的女孩——世界上的一切似乎突然變得不重要了，唯一重要的就是待在她身邊。但他不可能不注意到她臉上那嚴肅的表情——他覺得那張臉本該是帶著微笑的。在心中，他已經決定要做「她的」騎士，為她效力，竭盡所能為她排憂解難。

It was Queen Condwiramur herself who told him of her predicament. The armed men outside the city were the troops of a great and powerful lord who wanted to marry her, and whom she had refused. The proud noble, offended by this refusal, had resorted to force. The siege and the long period of hunger had already worn down the people's will to resist the enemy. But so proud was Kingrun, the rejected suitor, of his strength and skill with arms, that he had offered to withdraw his troops if any of Condwiramur's knights would meet and defeat him in single combat. But none of her knights, weakened as they were by hunger, had dared to accept the challenge.

是康德薇拉穆爾女王親自告訴帕西法爾她的困境。城外的武裝人員是一位強大而有權勢的領主的軍隊——這位領主想娶她為妻，而她拒絕了。這位傲慢的貴族因被拒絕而惱怒，訴諸武力。圍城和漫長的飢餓已經消磨了城中百姓抵抗敵人的意志。但被拒絕的求婚者金格倫（Kingrun）對自己的力量和武藝極為自負，他提出如果康德薇拉穆爾的任何一位騎士能在單打獨鬥中擊敗他，他就撤軍。但她的騎士們因飢餓而虛弱不堪，沒有人敢接受挑戰。

When Condwiramur finished her tale, her eyes met with Parsifal's, and what he read in the glance was both a request and a promise. If, in that moment, Condwiramur had asked him to fight the Lord of Hell, he would have undertaken the task happily. But fighting a mere mortal for her sake was a pleasure, a joy, the very thing he longed to do. When he promised Condwiramur to fight Kingrun in single combat, he felt happier than at any time since he had left the forest.

當康德薇拉穆爾說完她的故事時，她的目光與帕西法爾相遇，他從那目光中讀到的既是請求，也是承諾。如果那一刻康德薇拉穆爾要他去與地獄之王作戰，他也會欣然上陣。但為她的緣故與一個凡人交戰，那是一種快樂、一種喜悅，正是他渴望去做的事。當他向康德薇拉穆爾承諾要與金格倫決鬥時，他感到了離開森林以來從未有過的幸福。

When the great lord Kingrun was told that one of Condwiramur's knights had accepted his challenge, he thought of the combat as a pleasant interlude in the tedious business of laying siege to the city. He had never met his equal in battle or tournament, and looked forward to claiming another victim to his strength and skill. It was a combat that was watched from both sides - anxiously from the walls of the city, eagerly from the encircling camp.

當偉大的領主金格倫被告知康德薇拉穆爾的一位騎士接受了他的挑戰時，他把這場決鬥看作圍城這件乏味差事中的一個愉快插曲。他在戰場上和比武場上從未遇到過對手，正期待著以自己的力量和武藝再添一個犧牲品。這場決鬥被雙方觀看——城牆上焦急地注視著，圍城的營地中則急切地觀望著。

From a great distance, the two knights began to ride against each other. First slowly, then faster and faster, the horses covered the distance between them, the knights holding their long spears "at rest" pointing at each other's shield. They crashed into one another with such force that both spears broke and the horses sank to their haunches.

從遠處開始，兩位騎士策馬對衝。先是緩慢，然後越來越快，戰馬飛奔縮短著兩人之間的距離。騎士們將長矛「架穩」，矛尖直指對方的盾牌。他們以巨大的力量撞在一起，兩支長矛都折斷了，戰馬跌坐在後腿上。

Both knights dismounted as quickly as their heavy armour would permit, drew their swords, and struck at each other. Their shields were hacked to pieces, but they still fought on, exchanging blow for blow. But by now Kingrun, who had been so confident of his strength, began to feel the strain - his sword-arm began to feel as heavy as lead - and then Parsifal struck Kingrun's helmet with such force that the blow sent Kingrun staggering, and a second blow sent him crashing to the ground. The next moment, the point of Parsifal's sword was at Kingrun's throat - in the space between helmet and body-armour.

兩位騎士在沉重的盔甲允許的範圍內盡快下了馬，拔出劍，互相劈砍。他們的盾牌被砍成了碎片，但仍然在搏鬥，你來我往地交換著劍擊。但此時，一直對自己的力量信心十足的金格倫開始感到吃力了——他的持劍手臂開始沉重如鉛——接著帕西法爾猛擊金格倫的頭盔，力量之大使金格倫踉蹌後退，第二劍又將他砍翻在地。下一刻，帕西法爾的劍尖已經抵在金格倫的喉嚨上——頭盔和身甲之間的縫隙處。

"Go on - kill me!" croaked the fallen man. "I would have done it to you!"

「來啊——殺了我！」倒地的人嘶啞著說。「換了是我，早就對你下手了！」

"I give you another choice," said Parsifal. "You have to swear to obey two conditions. One is that the siege of the city is lifted - and the other is that you betake yourself to the court of King Arthur and place yourself at the service of the lady who was struck in the face for my sake. And give King Arthur greetings from the red knight!"

「我給你另一個選擇，」帕西法爾說。「你必須發誓遵守兩個條件。第一，解除對這座城市的圍困——第二，你前往亞瑟王的宮廷，去為那位因我而被掌摑的女士效力。並替紅騎士向亞瑟王問好！」

Kingrun was not such a fool as to throw his life away - he swore to abide by the conditions. And so Parsifal returned as victor to Condwiramur. And it will surprise no-one to hear that he married her, and became king of the city called Belrepeire. He was happy - but the real trials of his life were still to come. He had his share of good fortune, but had yet to learn the lessons of grief and sorrow.

金格倫不至於蠢到白白送命——他發誓遵守條件。於是帕西法爾以勝利者的姿態回到康德薇拉穆爾身邊。無人會感到意外的是，他娶了她，成為了被稱作貝爾瑞佩爾（Belrepeire）的城市的國王。他很幸福——但人生真正的考驗尚未到來。他得到了應有的好運，但還要學習悲傷和哀痛的功課。

---

## 7.Arthur - Myth or History? 亞瑟——神話還是歷史？

The stories about King Arthur and his knights contain, of course, a good deal of fighting - war, battle, tournament, joust, single combat - and we know already that there are historical reasons for this interest in strife and violence. It is quite in keeping with the general spirit of those times that the part I told you yesterday began with the killing of the red knight and ended with the combat outside the city of Queen Condwiramur.


關於亞瑟王和他的騎士們的故事，當然包含了大量的戰鬥——戰爭、交鋒、比武大會、騎槍比武、單打獨鬥——而我們已經知道，這種對爭鬥和暴力的興趣有其歷史原因。昨天我講的那部分故事從紅騎士之死開始，以康德薇拉穆爾女王城外的決鬥結束，這完全符合那個時代的普遍精神。

But there is not only all the fighting of the knights of King Arthur - there is also another great fight, another great battle connected with King Arthur, and this other battle is not fought with spears and swords, but with pen and paper. What's more, it is not a battle of the past, but goes on right into the present. It is the question of whether King Arthur and his knights ever really existed or are pure invention.

但除了亞瑟王的騎士們所有的戰鬥之外——還有另一場大仗、另一場與亞瑟王有關的大戰。這另一場戰鬥不是用矛和劍來打的，而是用筆和紙。更重要的是，它不是過去的戰鬥，而是一直延續到今天。這就是亞瑟王和他的騎士是否真實存在過、還是純屬虛構的問題。

You will probably be inclined to think that the King Arthur stories are no more "real" or "true" than the story of Red Riding Hood. But it is not quite so simple.

你大概會傾向於認為亞瑟王的故事和小紅帽的故事一樣，不是「真實的」或「真正的」。但事情並沒有那麼簡單。

You see, the question "King Arthur - man or myth?" as it is usually put, is rather like a detective story, with clues all over the place - and the only difficulty is to read these clues correctly.

你看，「亞瑟王——真人還是神話？」這個通常被提出的問題，頗像一個偵探故事，線索遍布各處——唯一的困難在於如何正確解讀這些線索。

One set of clues is the many places all over Britain and northern France which are, by local tradition, connected with King Arthur - the ruin of Tintagel Castle in Cornwall, or Glastonbury Abbey, or Arthur's Seat in Edinburgh. There is such a wealth of local traditions about Arthur here and there all over Britain that it does not seem likely it all came from nothing, from sheer invention.

第一組線索是遍布不列顛和法國北部的許多地方，根據當地傳統，都與亞瑟王有關——康沃爾的廷塔傑爾城堡（Tintagel Castle）遺址，或格拉斯頓伯里修道院（Glastonbury Abbey），或愛丁堡的亞瑟王座（Arthur's Seat）。不列顛各地關於亞瑟的地方傳說如此豐富，很難相信這一切都是憑空而來、純粹杜撰的。

There was certainly no King Arthur and no Knights of the Round Table at the time when the troubadours wrote all these stories. But the troubadours did not make it all up. They used stories which existed already. Long before the troubadours, there were story-tellers in every village in Britain who spoke of Arthur and his heroes. Neither the bards nor their audience could read or write, but by word of mouth the stories were kept alive from generation to generation. King Arthur was a British hero long, long before the troubadours wrote down the stories - and they took great liberties with them. The troubadours made Arthur and his men behave like knights of their own time - they dressed them up in the costumes of the twelfth and thirteenth century. But the stories of Arthur and Gawain and Lancelot existed already, and the troubadours only "adapted" the stories, added something here and there - they did not "invent" King Arthur.

在吟遊詩人寫下所有這些故事的時代，當然不存在什麼亞瑟王，也沒有什麼圓桌騎士。但吟遊詩人並非憑空捏造。他們使用的是已經存在的故事。早在吟遊詩人之前很久，不列顛每個村莊都有說故事的人在講述亞瑟和他的英雄。無論是吟遊詩人還是他們的聽眾都不識字，但通過口耳相傳，故事代代相傳而不曾斷絕。在吟遊詩人將故事寫成文字之前很久很久，亞瑟王就已經是不列顛的英雄了——而吟遊詩人對這些故事進行了大量的自由改編。他們讓亞瑟和他的部下表現得像他們自己時代的騎士——給他們穿上了十二和十三世紀的服飾。但亞瑟、高文（Gawain）和蘭斯洛特（Lancelot）的故事早已存在，吟遊詩人只是「改編」了這些故事，增添了一些內容——他們並沒有「發明」亞瑟王。

The troubadours chose Arthur and his men because they were already popular heroes - because people wanted to hear more and more of them. For the people of Britain at that time, around 1100-1200, King Arthur was not just one of the British kings, he was the king - the embodiment of the true spirit of Britain. This was and still is today the importance of King Arthur: that in the hearts of the people he remained the hero who stood for the spirit of Britain, for all that is British in the highest and best sense.

吟遊詩人選擇亞瑟和他的部下，是因為他們已經是深受歡迎的英雄——因為人們想聽到越來越多關於他們的故事。對當時大約1100至1200年間的不列顛人民來說，亞瑟王不僅僅是不列顛的一位國王，他就是那位國王——不列顛真正精神的化身。這就是亞瑟王過去和至今仍然重要的原因：在人們的心中，他始終是代表不列顛精神的英雄，代表了一切最崇高、最美好的不列顛品質。

But, you see, this is where the battle over King Arthur is still going on: were these early stories told only by word of mouth just fantasy, a myth? Or did they go back to a real person, a king who once lived and whose fame was never forgotten?

但是，你看，關於亞瑟王的論戰至今仍在進行：那些早期僅靠口頭流傳的故事只是幻想、只是神話嗎？還是它們源自一個真實的人，一位曾經活過的、聲名不朽的國王？

There is still a third possibility. There is a school of thought which maintains that there is an answer which makes Arthur a man and a myth at the same time. They say that "Arthur" was not the name of a person, but the title given to the leader of a special group of men who lived far back in Celtic times. When one leader died, his successor became "Arthur", and so there were many Arthurs, not just one.

還有第三種可能。有一派觀點認為，存在一個答案可以使亞瑟同時既是真人又是神話。他們說「亞瑟」不是一個人的名字，而是授予一個特殊團體首領的頭銜，這個團體早在凱爾特時代就已存在。當一位首領去世時，他的繼任者就成為新的「亞瑟」，因此有許多亞瑟，而不僅僅是一個。

For many generations, this group - the heroes of the Round Table - led by their "Arthur", worked and strove to protect the weak in those barbaric times; they devoted themselves to the task of fighting against oppression, cruelty, of holding robbers and brutal power at bay; they stood against all the evils of a cruel and savage time: they were "knights" before there was knighthood. It was a kind of "brotherhood" - very probably a secret brotherhood, and very likely a brotherhood with branches all over Britain, with each branch having its own leader, its own "Arthur".

一代又一代，這個團體——圓桌英雄——在他們的「亞瑟」率領下，在那些野蠻的年代為保護弱者而努力奮鬥；他們獻身於對抗壓迫、殘暴，阻擋盜匪和蠻橫勢力的任務；他們對抗那個殘酷野蠻時代的一切邪惡：他們在騎士制度產生之前就已經是「騎士」了。那是一種「兄弟會」——很可能是秘密兄弟會，而且很可能在不列顛各地都設有分支，每個分支都有自己的首領、自己的「亞瑟」。

This is the third opinion, the third view about the origin of the Arthur stories: this third view has the advantage that all the clues fit, and that it explains why the memory of Arthur and his heroes was so dear to the people of Britain - and why they saw in Arthur a symbol of what is best in the British: a sense of what is fair and just, and the courage to fight for it.

這就是關於亞瑟故事起源的第三種觀點、第三種看法。這第三種看法的優勢在於所有的線索都吻合，而且它解釋了為什麼亞瑟和他的英雄的記憶對不列顛人民如此珍貴——以及為什麼他們在亞瑟身上看到了不列顛人最優秀品質的象徵：對公平和正義的意識，以及為之戰鬥的勇氣。

And when, many centuries later, the knights of the Middle Ages followed the same ideals, what better model, what better example, could they find than the Arthur whom people still remembered? No wonder the troubadours, who were themselves knights, took up the ancient Celtic stories and made all their knights servants of Arthur.

而在許多世紀之後，當中世紀的騎士們遵循同樣的理想時，他們還能找到什麼比人們仍然記得的亞瑟更好的典範、更好的榜樣呢？難怪那些本身就是騎士的吟遊詩人拾起了古老的凱爾特故事，讓他們筆下所有的騎士都成為亞瑟的臣屬。

The real Arthurs, the Celtic leaders, must have lived well before Christianity reached Britain - they followed the Druid religion, which was a worship of the sun. But by protecting the weak and the poor, by fighting against cruelty and brutality, they were already working for Christian ideals, for Christian principles.

真正的那些亞瑟——凱爾特人的首領——一定生活在基督教傳到不列顛之前很久——他們遵循的是德魯伊教（Druid），那是一種太陽崇拜。但通過保護弱者和窮人、對抗殘暴和野蠻，他們已經在為基督教的理想、基督教的原則而努力了。

And the strange thing is that when the first Christian missionaries did come to the Celtic people of the British Isles, it was as if these people had only been waiting for it - the new religion was readily accepted. It was as if the Arthurs had prepared the way for it. So, when the troubadours in their stories made Arthur a Christian king, they were not so far out, because there was something Christian in the Arthur of olden times.

而奇怪的是，當第一批基督教傳教士來到不列顛群島的凱爾特人中間時，這些人好像一直在等待著它——新的宗教被欣然接受了。彷彿那些亞瑟們已經為它鋪好了路。因此，當吟遊詩人在故事中把亞瑟寫成一位基督教國王時，他們並沒有太離譜，因為古代的亞瑟身上確實有某種基督教的精神。

So, you see, although the stories about King Arthur and his knights do contain a good deal of fantasy, of sheer invention, of fairy tales, it is not all fantasy, and there is, far in the background, something that really happened.

所以你看，儘管關於亞瑟王和他的騎士的故事確實包含了大量的幻想、純粹的虛構和童話元素，但並非全是幻想。在遙遠的背景中，有些事情是真實發生過的。

It is like the story of the Trojan War, which was also for a long time regarded as a myth, until the German archaeologist Heinrich Schliemann dug up the ruins of Troy - still blackened by the fire that had destroyed the city.

這就像特洛伊戰爭的故事，它也曾長期被視為神話，直到德國考古學家海因里希·施里曼（Heinrich Schliemann）挖掘出特洛伊的遺址——依然被毀滅那座城市的大火熏黑著。

And you know - even the fantastic parts of the Arthur stories, the parts which seem no more than fairy tales, are not quite so unreal, so wildly imaginative as it may seem at first sight. Let's take the very beginning of the story of King Arthur. We are told in the story that when Arthur's father, King Uther Pendragon, died, there was confusion and chaos in Britain, for Arthur was still only a babe, and the powerful lords fought each other for the throne.

而你知道——即使是亞瑟故事中奇幻的部分、那些看起來不過是童話的部分，也並不像乍看之下那樣不真實、那樣天馬行空。讓我們看看亞瑟王故事的開頭。故事告訴我們，當亞瑟的父親尤瑟·潘德拉貢王（King Uther Pendragon）去世時，不列顛陷入了混亂和動盪，因為亞瑟還只是個嬰兒，各方強大的領主為王位互相爭鬥。

Merlin, the wizard and guardian of the child, took the little Arthur to foster parents who were willing to look after the boy and to bring him up - but Merlin did not tell them who the boy was, nor did the boy himself know it.

巫師梅林（Merlin），這個孩子的監護人，把小亞瑟交給願意撫養這個男孩的養父母——但梅林沒有告訴他們這個男孩是誰，男孩自己也不知道。

The foster father was himself a knight, Sir Ector, and he took part in the struggle for the crown of Britain. But all the fighting led to nothing - none of the contenders could gain a decisive victory over his competitors. And after many years of this fruitless strife amongst themselves, the lords decided to find by peaceful means who should be king of Britain.

養父本人就是一位騎士，埃克托爵士（Sir Ector），他也參加了爭奪不列顛王冠的鬥爭。但所有的爭鬥都徒勞無功——沒有一個競爭者能夠對其他對手取得決定性的勝利。在經歷了多年這種毫無結果的內鬥之後，領主們決定用和平的方式來決定誰應該成為不列顛的國王。

Merlin the wizard once more took a hand in events. At his advice, the highest priest in Britain, the Archbishop of Canterbury, called all the lords to a meeting in London at Christmas. At this meeting, a sign would be given as to which of them was worthy to wear the crown of Britain. They all came to London, and a great service was held in which they all prayed that the right person should be chosen.

巫師梅林再次插手了事態的發展。在他的建議下，不列顛最高的教士——坎特伯雷大主教（Archbishop of Canterbury）——在聖誕節召集所有領主到倫敦開會。在這次集會上，將有一個徵兆來顯示他們當中誰配戴上不列顛的王冠。他們都來到了倫敦，舉行了一場盛大的禮拜，祈禱正確的人選會被選出。

When they came out of the church, they saw a strange sight in the churchyard - it was a great square stone, and in the stone was an anvil, and through the anvil and stone there was a sword. On the sword was an inscription that read: "He who can pull this sword from the stone is the rightful king of Britain."

當他們走出教堂時，在教堂墓地裡看到了一個奇異的景象——那是一塊巨大的方形石頭，石頭上有一個鐵砧，一把劍貫穿鐵砧和石頭。劍上有一行銘文寫道：「能從石中拔出此劍者，即為不列顛的正統君王。」

Of course, each one of the lords in turn tried to pull the sword out of the anvil and stone, but not one of them was able to move it an inch. And when they turned in bewilderment to the Archbishop, he said: "It seems that the right man is not yet amongst us. We must wait for his coming."

當然，每一位領主都輪流試著從鐵砧和石頭中拔出劍來，但沒有一個人能將它移動一寸。當他們困惑地轉向大主教時，他說：「看來那個合適的人還不在我們中間。我們必須等待他的到來。」

To pass the time of waiting, the lords decided to arrange a tournament amongst themselves. Now, amongst the nobles who had come to this meeting was Sir Ector, the foster father of Arthur, and with him was his son, whose name was Kay, and also young Arthur. Kay, the son of Sir Ector, had already been knighted a short time before, and Arthur, being only a foster brother and of unknown parentage, was the young knight's servant and page.

為了打發等待的時間，領主們決定舉辦一場比武大會。前來參加集會的貴族中有埃克托爵士——亞瑟的養父，同行的還有他的兒子凱（Kay），以及年輕的亞瑟。埃克托爵士的兒子凱不久前已被冊封為騎士，而亞瑟只是養兄弟、身世不明，是這位年輕騎士的僕從和侍從。

When all the nobles were making ready for the tournament, the young knight, Kay, said to Arthur, "When we left our lodging in town this morning, I left my sword behind, as I did not expect a tournament. Go quickly, and bring it to me."

當所有貴族都在為比武做準備時，年輕的騎士凱對亞瑟說：「今天早上我們離開城裡的住處時，我把劍忘在那兒了，因為我沒料到會有比武。快去把它拿來給我。」

Arthur hurried back to the lodging - but everybody in the house had gone out, the doors were locked and he could not get in. What should he do? Kay would be very cross if he came without the sword. But as Arthur made his way back, he passed the now deserted churchyard with the stone and the sword through it. Here was a sword! And without thinking twice, Arthur went over, pulled the sword out without difficulty and brought it to his master.

亞瑟匆忙趕回住處——但屋裡的人都出門了，門鎖著進不去。他該怎麼辦？如果空手回去，凱會非常生氣。但當亞瑟往回走時，他經過了此刻已空無一人的教堂墓地，看到了石頭和插在其中的劍。這裡有一把劍！亞瑟不假思索地走過去，毫不費力地拔出了劍，帶回去交給了主人。

Arthur, who could not read, and had paid little attention to the affairs of the high and mighty lords, had no idea that there was anything special about the sword. But young Sir Kay recognised immediately the sword and the inscription, and realised what had happened. But the temptation of holding this precious sword in his hands was too much for him. He went to his father, Sir Ector, and said, "Look - here is the sword from the stone! I am to be King of Britain." But Sir Ector was not easily deceived. Under his questioning, Kay had to admit, blushing with shame, that it was Arthur who had given him the sword.

亞瑟不識字，對那些高高在上的領主們的事情也毫不關心，完全不知道這把劍有什麼特別。但年輕的凱爵士立刻認出了那把劍和銘文，明白了發生了什麼。但手握這把珍貴之劍的誘惑太大了。他去找父親埃克托爵士，說：「看——這就是石中的劍！我將成為不列顛的國王。」但埃克托爵士不是那麼容易上當的。在他的追問下，凱不得不羞愧地臉紅承認，是亞瑟把劍交給他的。

And then Arthur, much to his surprise, had to go back to the churchyard accompanied by all the lords, and there, before all of them, he put the sword into the stone and drew it out again - but none of the others could do it. And so, in the end, Arthur became King of Britain.

隨後，亞瑟大感意外地被所有領主簇擁著回到教堂墓地，在所有人面前，他把劍插回石頭又拔了出來——但其他人誰也做不到。最終，亞瑟成了不列顛的國王。

This is the story of the famous sword "Excalibur", the "Sword in the Stone". Now, the storytellers of those times - the bards first, and the troubadours later - used such imaginative pictures as symbols: they wanted to convey by this story that Arthur alone possessed something which all the others did not - and they compared this "something" to a sword, the sword Excalibur. And only this "something" made a man worthy to be a leader, a king.

這就是著名的「石中劍」——王者之劍「神劍」（Excalibur）——的故事。那個時代的說故事者——先是吟遊詩人，後是行吟詩人——使用這種富有想像力的圖像作為象徵：他們想通過這個故事傳達的是，亞瑟獨自擁有所有其他人都不具備的某種東西——他們把這種「東西」比作一把劍，就是神劍。而只有這種「東西」才能使一個人配做領袖、配做國王。

What is there in a person's character that would qualify them for leadership? It is the inner strength to make decisions - and right decisions. Just compare the two words "incision" and "decision": incision means a cut, and decision also has "-cision", which means cutting. A firm decision is a cut. And the sword Excalibur is the power to make decisions - the right decisions.

一個人的品格中有什麼能使他配得上領導地位？那就是做決定——而且是正確的決定——的內在力量。只要比較一下「incision（切口）」和「decision（決定）」這兩個詞：incision 意味著切割，而 decision 中也有「-cision」，意思是切斷。一個堅定的決定就是一刀切下。而神劍就是做決定——做正確決定——的力量。

We have in more recent times an example of this power of decision. In 1940, the armies of Hitler overran France - the French armies were in full flight, and the French Government surrendered France to German occupation. The British troops who had been fighting in France had been safely brought back to England by the Dunkirk evacuation - but they had lost all their arms. Britain was practically defenceless against a German invasion, and had obviously no other choice but to do what France had done: surrender to Hitler. Both in Parliament and amongst the people, there were many who thought that all we could do was to try and get tolerably good peace terms from Hitler - the war was lost.

在較近的時代，我們有一個體現這種決斷力的例子。1940年，希特勒的軍隊橫掃了法國——法軍全線潰敗，法國政府向德國投降。在法國作戰的英軍已通過敦刻爾克（Dunkirk）撤退安全返回英格蘭——但他們失去了所有的武器裝備。不列顛在面對德國入侵時幾乎毫無防禦能力，顯然別無選擇，只能效仿法國的做法：向希特勒投降。無論是在議會還是在民間，許多人都認為我們所能做的只是試著從希特勒那裡爭取到尚可接受的和平條件——戰爭已經輸了。

And then came Winston Churchill's famous broadcast, in which he told the world that, under his leadership, Britain would fight on and would never surrender to Hitler. It was one man's decision, one man's determination which, in those bleak days of 1940, saved Britain and the world from Nazi domination.

然後溫斯頓·邱吉爾（Winston Churchill）發表了那篇著名的廣播演說，向全世界宣告，在他的領導下，不列顛將戰鬥到底，絕不向希特勒投降。正是一個人的決斷、一個人的決心，在1940年那些黯淡的日子裡，拯救了不列顛和整個世界免於納粹的統治。

But if one of the old troubadours had heard Churchill's voice in 1940, and if he had witnessed the Battle of Britain, when the RAF defied Hitler's Luftwaffe, that troubadour would have said, "The sword Excalibur has been drawn from the stone - Winston Churchill has the power of Excalibur!"

但如果一位古老的吟遊詩人在1940年聽到了邱吉爾的聲音，如果他目睹了不列顛空戰——英國皇家空軍（RAF）抵抗希特勒的空軍——那位吟遊詩人一定會說：「神劍已經從石頭中被拔出——溫斯頓·邱吉爾擁有神劍的力量！」

So, you see, all these seemingly fantastic stories - the "Sword in the Stone", or that Arthur did not die but only lies asleep under a magic spell - are not idle fancy, they have a hidden meaning.

所以你看，所有這些看似奇幻的故事——「石中劍」，或者亞瑟並未死去而只是在魔咒下沉睡——並非無聊的幻想，它們都有隱含的意義。

But once we understand that seemingly fantastic stories have a meaning, that they illustrate something in pictures, we can also understand such an apparently absurd story as is told about the end of King Arthur - that he has not died, but rests in a kind of sleep in "Avalon", and will return to Britain in her hour of greatest need. When the smaller ships of England sailed out against the Armada - when Nelson in the Battle of Trafalgar foiled Napoleon's invasion plans - when, in the Battle of Britain, a few RAF planes stood up to Hitler's Luftwaffe - these were all hours of need, and in all these hours of need there rose the spirit which once lived in all the Arthurs. In ordinary times, when there is no danger, one might well think that there is no spirit in the British, that it is dead. But it is only under a spell - as Arthur is said to be held in a spell in Avalon. And this spirit has risen every time there was a dire need.

但一旦我們理解了那些看似奇幻的故事有其含義、它們以圖像說明事理，我們也就能理解關於亞瑟王結局的那個看似荒謬的故事——他並未死去，而是在「阿瓦隆」（Avalon）中以一種沉睡的狀態安息，將在不列顛最危急的時刻歸來。當英格蘭的小船駛出迎戰西班牙無敵艦隊（Armada）時——當納爾遜（Nelson）在特拉法加海戰（Battle of Trafalgar）中挫敗了拿破崙的入侵計畫時——當在不列顛空戰中，少數幾架皇家空軍的飛機奮起對抗希特勒的空軍時——這些都是危急時刻，而在所有這些危急時刻，曾經活在所有亞瑟身上的精神都昂然奮起。在太平歲月，當沒有危險的時候，人們很可能以為不列顛人毫無精神可言、那精神已經死了。但它只是被施了魔咒——就像傳說中亞瑟在阿瓦隆被魔咒所困一樣。而每當有危急的需要時，這種精神都會再度復甦。

The bards in very ancient times, and the troubadours of the Middle Ages, spoke a language of symbolic pictures, where we in our time use abstract words, such as "decision", without realising that "decision" was from a Latin word which means "to cut" - and so in the word itself there is already the picture of a knife or sword.

遠古時代的吟遊詩人和中世紀的行吟詩人，使用的是象徵性圖像的語言，而我們在自己的時代使用抽象的詞語，比如「decision（決定）」，卻沒有意識到「decision」源自一個拉丁語單詞，意思是「切割」——因此在這個詞本身就已經包含了一把刀或一把劍的意象。

## 8.The Fisher King 漁人王


Parsifal had been accompanied by good fortune, without quite realising it. It was good fortune which had helped him against the red knight. It was good fortune which had brought him to Gurnemant, who had given him the training and instructions necessary for a knight. And it was good fortune which had brought him to Queen Condwiramur - and in one fight he had earned her hand in marriage and the crown of a king.

帕西法爾一路都有好運相伴，只是他自己並未完全意識到。是好運幫助他戰勝了紅騎士。是好運將他帶到古爾內曼茨（Gurnemant）身邊，讓他接受了騎士所需的訓練與教導。也是好運將他帶到了康德薇拉穆爾（Condwiramur）女王面前——僅一場戰鬥，他便贏得了她的芳心與一國之王的冠冕。

He had been very fortunate indeed, and he lived in great happiness with Queen Condwiramur. But as time passed, he thought more and more often of his mother, Herzeloyde. He did not know that she had died of grief in the very hour he had left the forest, and now that he had achieved knighthood and gained a kingdom he wished that his mother was with him to share his happiness.

他確實非常幸運，與康德薇拉穆爾女王過著幸福的生活。然而隨著時間流逝，他越來越頻繁地想起母親赫策洛伊德（Herzeloyde）。他不知道母親在他離開森林的那一刻便已悲傷而死，如今他已成為騎士、贏得了王國，他多麼希望母親能在身邊，分享他的喜悅。

It would not do to send a messenger - no, he himself would go, tell her how well he had fared, and bring her back with him from the solitude of the forest to his palace. And so he took leave of Queen Condwiramur and set out on his journey to the great forest where he had spent his early youth.

派遣信使是不夠的——不，他要親自去，告訴她他過得有多好，把她從森林的孤寂中帶回宮殿。於是他向康德薇拉穆爾女王告別，踏上了前往那片他度過童年的大森林的旅途。

He rode for a day, but as evening came he found himself in a region quite unknown to him. There were hills, the slopes of which were covered with dense woods, and there were lakes - but nowhere could he see any sign of human habitation. Was there any place where he could find shelter for the night?

他騎行了一整天，但當黃昏降臨時，他發現自己身處一片完全陌生的地域。這裡有山丘，山坡上覆蓋著茂密的樹林，還有湖泊——但放眼望去，看不到任何人煙的跡象。他能在哪裡找到過夜的棲身之所呢？

No knight's castle or peasant's hut could be seen, and so Parsifal rode on until he came to one of the lakes he had seen gleaming between the hills. He was glad to see several people there, whom he took to be fishermen, in a boat on the lake. Parsifal hailed them, and the boat came towards him.

既看不到騎士的城堡，也看不到農夫的小屋，帕西法爾只好繼續前行，直到來到他先前看見在群山之間閃閃發光的一處湖泊旁。他欣喜地看見湖上有幾個人在一條船上，他以為他們是漁民。帕西法爾向他們呼喊，船便朝他駛來。

As the boat approached, he could make out that there was one man in it who was dressed in splendid robes of purple and ermine, such as only a great lord or a king would wear; the other men in the boat seemed to be his servants.

船靠近時，他看出船上有一個人穿著華麗的紫色與貂皮長袍，只有偉大的領主或國王才會穿這樣的衣服；船上其他人似乎都是他的僕從。

Nearer came the boat, and now Parsifal could see that the face of the noble lord or king was pale, and bore the mark of great suffering. When Parsifal asked him where he could find shelter, the sad-looking man said, "For thirty miles around, there is no house, except one that is nearby. At the end of yonder cliff, you will find an uphill path. Follow that path, and you will reach a castle. I myself will be your host there tonight, when I have done with fishing in the lake."

船更近了，帕西法爾這時看清那位貴族領主或國王的面容蒼白，帶著深重苦難的印記。當帕西法爾問他哪裡可以找到棲身之處時，那位面帶哀容的男子說：「方圓三十里之內沒有別的房舍，只有附近一處。在那邊懸崖的盡頭，你會找到一條上坡的小路。沿著那條路走，你就會到達一座城堡。今晚等我在湖上捕完魚，我便是你的主人。」

Parsifal thanked the man whom he called in his mind the "Fisher King", and followed the directions given to him. The path up the hill was steep and winding, and more than once he thought of turning back, for he could see no sign of a castle, and began to wonder if he had not been sent on a fool's errand. But, just as the sun set, he saw in the golden glow of the dusk over the trees the towers of a castle. There was a great moat around the castle, but as Parsifal approached, a drawbridge was let down, and the gate was opened to receive him.

帕西法爾感謝了這位他在心中稱為「漁人王」的人，便依照指引前行。上山的小路陡峭而蜿蜒，他不止一次想要回頭，因為看不到城堡的任何蹤跡，開始懷疑自己是不是被人捉弄了。然而，就在太陽落下的時候，他在暮色的金光中看見了樹林之上聳立的城堡塔樓。城堡四周環繞著寬闊的護城河，但隨著帕西法爾走近，吊橋放了下來，城門打開迎接他。

Pages and squires came to meet him in the forecourt, and when Parsifal told them the Fisher King had sent him, they bade him welcome. They helped him from the saddle, they led him to a chamber in the castle where they helped him out of his armour, they brought hot water and towels for him to clean himself, and a silken coat to put on in place of his travel-stained clothes.

侍從和年輕扈從在前庭迎接他。當帕西法爾告訴他們是漁人王派他來的，他們便歡迎他入內。他們扶他下馬，引他進入城堡的一間房間，幫他卸下盔甲，送上熱水和毛巾讓他洗漱，並遞上一件絲綢外袍，替換他那沾滿旅途塵土的衣裳。

All this was the kind of hospitality one would expect from the house of a rich and noble lord. But, in one way, there was something strange and unusual about this castle. There was none of the laughter and shouting that one would hear in any castle - no one hummed a song, or whistled, and even the pages acted with the gravity of old men and not like young boys. Knights or squires or pages - they all showed Parsifal every courtesy, but there was no smile on any face, and they all seemed weighed down with some great sorrow or unhappiness.

這一切都是人們期望從一位富有而高貴的領主府邸所能享受到的殷勤款待。然而，這座城堡在某方面卻透著一股奇異與不尋常。這裡聽不到任何城堡通常會有的歡笑與喧嘩聲——沒有人哼歌，沒有人吹口哨，就連小侍從們也帶著老者般的莊重，絲毫不像年輕男孩。無論是騎士、扈從還是小侍從——他們都對帕西法爾彬彬有禮，但每張臉上都沒有笑容，他們似乎都被某種巨大的悲傷或不幸壓得喘不過氣來。

Parsifal wondered what was the matter with them - and what was the matter with the lord of the castle, whose pale, suffering face he could not forget. But he well remembered the instructions of his teacher, Gurnemant: a knight must not be inquisitive, he must not ask too many questions. In any case, if these people had troubles, it was of no concern to him. And so he asked no questions, and when he was ready, he was led to the banqueting hall.

帕西法爾很想知道他們到底怎麼了——城堡主人那張蒼白而受苦的面容更是讓他難以忘懷。但他清楚地記得老師古爾內曼茨的教誨：騎士不該多管閒事，不該問太多問題。何況，即便這些人有煩惱，那也不關他的事。於是他什麼也沒問，準備妥當後便被引領至宴會大廳。

It was a more lordly hall than Parsifal had ever seen - even King Arthur had nothing to equal it. Chandeliers with hundreds of wax candles spread soft light, and in three great fireplaces logs gave warmth as well as a pleasant scent. Along the walls were many couches on which knights rested, and by each couch stood a table.

這是帕西法爾從未見過的、比任何廳堂都更為莊嚴華貴的大廳——即便亞瑟王也沒有能與之相比的。枝形吊燈上數百支蠟燭灑下柔和的光芒，三座巨大的壁爐裡的木柴既散發著溫暖，又帶來宜人的馨香。沿著牆壁擺放著許多長椅，騎士們在上面休憩，每張長椅旁都放著一張桌子。

Raised above the others was the place of honour, occupied by the Fisher King. Although the hall was well heated, he wore a robe of fur, and seemed to shiver in it. He said to Parsifal, "Forgive me that I don't stand up to greet you - but, believe me, this is not lack of courtesy that prevents me. Please take your seat beside me."

高居眾人之上的是主位，坐著漁人王。儘管大廳暖意融融，他仍裹著毛皮長袍，似乎在其中瑟瑟發抖。他對帕西法爾說：「請原諒我不能起身迎接你——但請相信，這並非出於無禮。請在我身邊就座。」

Parsifal had hardly sat down, when a page appeared before the Fisher King, carrying a sword with a golden hilt in a jewel-encrusted sheath. The host took the sword and gave it to Parsifal. "It is for you, my honoured guest," he said. "It will serve you well. It will never break, except in one danger - and what that danger is, is only known to the master who made the sword."

帕西法爾剛剛坐下，便有一名侍從出現在漁人王面前，捧著一把金柄寶劍，劍鞘上鑲滿了珠寶。主人接過劍，遞給了帕西法爾。「這是給你的，我尊貴的客人，」他說。「它會好好為你效力。它永遠不會折斷，除非遭遇一種危險——而那種危險是什麼，只有鑄劍的大師才知道。」

Parsifal was still stammering his thanks for the precious gift when he became aware that all talking in the great hall had ceased. He, too, fell silent, and in the hush a side door opened and in came a man carrying a long spear in his hand. The man carried the spear through the hall, and Parsifal saw to his amazement that the point of the spear was red with blood and that drops spattered on the floor as the man walked through before finally leaving by another door.

帕西法爾還在結結巴巴地為這珍貴的禮物致謝時，便意識到大廳裡所有的交談都停止了。他也隨之沉默。在一片寂靜中，一扇側門打開了，一個人手持一支長矛走了進來。那人擎著長矛穿過大廳，帕西法爾驚異地看到矛尖上殷紅如血，血滴隨著那人走過而濺落在地板上，最後那人從另一扇門離去。

Deep sighs could be heard - but not a word was spoken, and Parsifal was greatly puzzled to know what it all meant. But he wondered even more about what followed.

深沉的嘆息聲此起彼伏——但沒有人說一句話，帕西法爾完全不明白這一切意味著什麼。然而接下來發生的事更讓他驚奇不已。

For now entered a lady, followed by two pages: she carried in her hands a vessel - it seemed a crystal - that shone so brightly that all the other lights in the hall seemed pale and wan. And Parsifal heard a whisper: "The Grail..."

因為此時走進一位女子，身後跟著兩名侍從：她雙手捧著一個器皿——看起來像是水晶——散發出如此耀眼的光芒，以至於廳中所有其他的光都顯得黯淡無華。帕西法爾聽到一聲低語：「聖杯……」

And as this thing, called "Grail", was carried by the lady round the hall, a great number of pages placed golden bowls before every guest - and then the strangest of all things came to pass. For, as the Grail was carried past the tables, each bowl was filled with the food the guest liked best.

當這件被稱為「聖杯」（Grail）的聖物由那位女子捧著繞行大廳時，許多侍從在每位賓客面前擺上了金碗——然後最奇異的事情發生了。當聖杯經過餐桌旁時，每個金碗都盛滿了那位賓客最喜愛的食物。

Parsifal, too, found the golden plate before him filled with food that no one had served - and as he saw that all the others were eating, he too helped himself. And when they had all eaten, the lady with the thing called "Grail" left.

帕西法爾也發現面前的金盤裡裝滿了無人端上的食物——看到其他人都在用餐，他也開始享用。當所有人都吃過之後，那位捧著聖杯的女子便離開了。

By now, Parsifal felt very curious indeed - but he also remembered the rule not to show curiosity. What would his host think of him if he showed such bad manners as to ask questions? And so he kept silent.

此時帕西法爾心中確實充滿了好奇——但他也記著不可流露好奇心的規矩。如果他問東問西，那樣失禮的舉動會讓主人怎麼看他呢？於是他保持沉默。

When the meal was over, the host said, with a deep sigh, "I think your bed is ready - you must be tired after your long journey, so I advise you to go and rest." Parsifal rose, thanked the host once again for the gift of the sword and, wishing him a good night, left. All the other knights, too, rose, and some came to show him to his room.

宴席結束後，主人深深嘆了口氣，說道：「你的床應該準備好了——長途旅行之後你一定累了，我建議你去休息吧。」帕西法爾起身，再次感謝主人贈劍之恩，祝他晚安後便告退了。其他騎士也紛紛起身，有幾位前來為他引路到房間。

Parsifal was, indeed, tired, but he was also bewildered by what he had seen. And now that he was alone, he could also feel a strange premonition of distress. It followed him even in his sleep, for his night was disturbed by nightmare dreams - he felt himself pierced by swords, and trampled under horses. With a groan, he woke up, covered in sweat - but he was glad to see that it was already daylight. He expected that at any moment pages would appear to help him with his armour. But no one came.

帕西法爾確實很疲倦，但他也被所見之事弄得困惑不已。如今獨處一室，他還能感受到一種奇異的不安預感。這種感覺甚至跟隨他進入了夢鄉，整夜都被噩夢擾攘——他感到自己被劍刺穿，被馬蹄踐踏。他呻吟著醒來，渾身冷汗——但他慶幸天已經亮了。他期待著侍從隨時會出現幫他穿上盔甲。但沒有人來。

"Have they all been called away to fight an enemy?" thought Parsifal. "They could at least have told me - I would gladly have helped them!" But it was no use speculating, and no use waiting any longer. He got up and put on his clothes and, with some trouble, got into his armour. There was the sword he had once taken from the red knight, as well as the precious blade he had received from the Fisher King. He put them both on, and left his room.

「他們是不是都被召去抵禦敵人了？」帕西法爾心想。「他們至少可以告訴我一聲——我很樂意出手相助！」但臆測無濟於事，再等下去也沒有意義。他起身穿好衣服，費了些功夫套上盔甲。那裡有他曾從紅騎士那裡奪來的劍，以及漁人王贈予他的珍貴寶劍。他佩上兩把劍，走出了房間。

There was still not a soul to be seen in the castle, so Parsifal went out into the courtyard. There he saw his horse tethered to a post, and his spear and shield leaning against the post. He knocked at some doors and windows, but there was no answer. "Well," he thought, "they don't expect me to say goodbye, it seems, but they have made things ready for my departure, so I will be on my way."

城堡中仍然看不到一個人影，帕西法爾便走到庭院中。他看見自己的馬被拴在柱子上，長矛和盾牌靠在柱子旁。他敲了幾扇門和窗戶，但都沒有回應。「好吧，」他心想，「看來他們不指望我道別，但已經為我的出發做好了準備，那我就上路吧。」

He mounted and rode towards the gate. The drawbridge was down and Parsifal rode over it - but before he had come to the end of the bridge, somebody behind him began to pull it up, so that Parsifal's horse had to jump to clear the gap. Parsifal turned round furiously, and shouted, "What manners are these - not letting me ride to the end of the bridge?"

他翻身上馬，向城門騎去。吊橋已經放下，帕西法爾策馬通過——但還沒到橋的盡頭，身後便有人開始收起吊橋，帕西法爾的馬不得不跳躍才能跨過缺口。帕西法爾怒氣沖沖地回過頭，大喊道：「這是什麼待客之道——不讓我走完這座橋！」

From the ramparts of the castle there came a voice in answer, "Ride on, fool! You were too stupid to use your mouth for anything but gulping food last night - and a curse on you for not asking what you should have asked!"

城堡的城垛上傳來一個聲音作為回答：「走吧，蠢貨！你昨晚只知道用嘴巴大吃大喝——你本該問的卻不問，詛咒降臨在你身上！」

It was a shock for Parsifal that from people who had been so courteous the day before there should now come rudeness and curses. He shouted, "What do you mean by all this? What question should I have asked?" But there was again silence, and no one answered his shouting. And Parsifal felt again that dark foreboding which had been with him all night.

這對帕西法爾來說是一記沉重的打擊——昨天還如此彬彬有禮的人，如今竟出口粗魯、口吐詛咒。他大聲喊道：「你們這是什麼意思？我應該問什麼問題？」但又是一片沉默，無人回應他的呼喊。帕西法爾再次感受到那整夜纏繞著他的陰暗預感。

Upset and unhappy without knowing why, Parsifal spurred his horse and went down the winding path which had brought him up to the castle the night before. Soon he reached the cliff where the path began - and then he was again in that region where no human habitation could be seen for miles. But as he rode on, the countryside became more familiar to him - surely he had passed this little stream, and that mountain, when he rode from the forest to King Arthur's court? He must be getting nearer to the great forest, and to his mother.

帕西法爾心煩意亂卻不知原因何在，他策馬沿著前一夜帶他上山的蜿蜒小路飛奔而下。很快他到達了小路起始處的懸崖——然後他又回到了那片方圓數里看不到人煙的地方。但隨著他繼續前行，眼前的鄉野越來越眼熟——他從森林前往亞瑟王宮廷時，肯定經過了這條小溪和那座山？他一定是越來越靠近那片大森林，靠近他的母親了。

Turning a bend, he came upon a sight he had seen before - a woman with a dead knight. Surely it could not be his cousin, Sigune, who had once told him his name? But as he came nearer he saw that it was Sigune. She looked aged by many years, she was dressed in black, and the dead man lying in a coffin at her feet was embalmed.

轉過一個彎，他看到了一幕似曾相識的景象——一個女人守著一位死去的騎士。這不會是他的表姊西古內（Sigune）吧，就是那位曾經告訴他名字的人？但走近一看，確實是西古內。她看上去蒼老了許多年，身著黑衣，躺在她腳邊棺材裡的死者已被防腐保存。

And the woman, too, recognised Parsifal. She said, "Don't be surprised, cousin, to find me here! I have sworn to stay with my dead lover until my own life comes to an end. But if there is one thing that can bring joy to me in my grief, it is the sight of the new sword on your side. I know this sword, and it tells me that you have been to the Fisher King."

那女子也認出了帕西法爾。她說：「表弟，看到我在這裡不必驚訝！我已發誓守在我死去的愛人身旁，直到自己生命的盡頭。但若在我的哀傷中還有一件事能帶來歡喜，那就是看到你腰間的這把新劍。我認得這把劍，它告訴我你去過漁人王那裡。」

"Yes," said Parsifal. "He gave it to me!"

「是的，」帕西法爾說。「是他送給我的！」

"Dear cousin," cried Sigune with joy, "you have been to the castle Montsalvage, the castle of salvation. You are indeed blessed, for you have seen the holy Grail!"

「親愛的表弟，」西古內喜悅地喊道，「你去過蒙薩爾瓦什城堡（Montsalvage），那座救贖的城堡。你真是蒙福之人，因為你見到了聖杯！」

"Well," said Parsifal, "I did not know the castle was called Montsalvage, but I saw some strange things there."

「嗯，」帕西法爾說，「我不知道那座城堡叫蒙薩爾瓦什，但我確實在那裡看到了一些奇異的事物。」

"Surely," said Sigune, "you have asked the question - the question that brings the highest honour there is in the world?"

「那你一定問了那個問題吧，」西古內說，「那個能帶來世上最高榮譽的問題？」

"Question?" said Parsifal. "No, I did not ask any question!"

「問題？」帕西法爾說。「不，我什麼問題都沒問！」

"You wretch!" cried the woman. "You saw that ailing, suffering man, the Fisher King, and you felt no pity, no compassion that would make you ask what was his hurt, or why he was ailing?"

「你這可憐蟲！」那女子喊道。「你看到了那個病弱、受苦的人，漁人王，你心中竟然沒有絲毫憐憫、絲毫同情，讓你開口問問他的傷痛是什麼，他為何受苦？」

"It would not have been fitting for a knight..." stammered Parsifal.

「這對一個騎士來說是不合禮的……」帕西法爾結結巴巴地說。

"Not fitting?" cried Sigune. "You fool, you wretched fool! If you had thought less about what is fitting, and if you had a spark of pity in your heart, you would have gained honour beyond your dreams. Know, then, that it was ordained by that which is called the Grail that the Fisher King, who did a great wrong, shall suffer from a painful wound until a knight comes who will - out of himself, and unbidden - ask: 'Why are you ailing?' And as soon as that question is asked, the Fisher King's wound would be healed, and the knight who asked the question would be rewarded with the highest honour in the world. But you, in your dumbness and in your pride and in your cold-heartedness - you, for whom the Fisher King and his whole company of knights of the Grail have been waiting - you have kept silent, and so you have lost the greatness that was to be yours! And, having failed to ask, you are cursed. A curse is on you - go away, accursed one, and leave me alone!"

「不合禮？」西古內喊道。「你這個傻瓜，你這個可悲的傻瓜！如果你少想一點什麼合不合禮，如果你心中有一絲憐憫，你就會獲得超乎夢想的榮耀。那麼你聽好了：聖杯曾有神諭，漁人王因為犯了大錯，必須承受痛苦的傷痛，直到有一位騎士——出於自己的心意、無人提示——開口問道：『你為何受苦？』只要那個問題一出口，漁人王的傷口就會癒合，而問出問題的騎士將獲得世上最高的榮譽。但你，你這愚鈍的、驕傲的、冷酷的人——漁人王和他麾下整個聖杯騎士團一直在等待的那個人——你卻保持沉默，因此你失去了本該屬於你的偉大！而且，因為你沒有開口問，詛咒降臨在你身上。你被詛咒了——走吧，被詛咒的人，別再煩我！」

To Parsifal, every word she spoke was like a shattering blow. His mother dead - and he had caused her death! A curse on him - and he had caused the curse! His mind could not grasp it.

對帕西法爾而言，她的每一句話都如同粉碎性的打擊。母親死了——而他造成了她的死亡！詛咒降臨在他身上——而他自己招來了詛咒！他的心智無法承受這一切。

"But cousin Sigune," said Parsifal, "for me the place was only a night's shelter... I am on my way back to my mother in the forest, and I gave little thought to anything else!"

「但是表姊西古內，」帕西法爾說，「對我而言那不過是一夜的棲身之所……我正在回森林裡找母親的路上，沒有想太多別的事！」

"To your mother?" said Sigune. "She died of a broken heart the day you rode away, thinking of nothing else but your own glory as a knight. You thought only of yourself when you left your mother - you thought only of yourself when you kept silent before the Fisher King, and so you have lost your mother, and you have lost your place amongst the knights of the holy Grail!"

「回去找你母親？」西古內說。「你策馬離去的那天，她就傷心而死了，而你滿腦子只想著自己作為騎士的榮耀。你離開母親時只想到自己——你在漁人王面前沉默時也只想到自己，所以你失去了母親，也失去了在聖杯騎士團中的位置！」

At long last, he said, "I cannot bring my mother back to life again, but I can at least go back to the Grail Castle, to Montsalvage, and ask the question I did not ask the first time."

沉默了很久，他終於說道：「我無法讓母親重新活過來，但我至少可以回到聖杯城堡蒙薩爾瓦什，問出我第一次沒有問的問題。」

Sigune looked up and said, "Poor Parsifal - no one can find the castle of the Grail by seeking for it. Only the Grail itself guides those who are meant to find Montsalvage - all others can never find it. It was given to you from birth, by the noble blood you have inherited. It was your precious inheritance to find the Grail Castle, but you have forfeited this inheritance."

西古內抬頭望著他說：「可憐的帕西法爾——沒有人能靠尋找而找到聖杯城堡。只有聖杯本身會引導那些注定要找到蒙薩爾瓦什的人——其他人永遠無法找到它。這是你與生俱來的、由你繼承的高貴血脈所賦予的。找到聖杯城堡是你珍貴的遺產，但你已經喪失了這份遺產。」

Without a word, Parsifal turned his horse and rode back the way he had come. He remembered well every mile he had travelled that day, and he was determined to find that cliff and that path winding uphill through the woods to the castle.

帕西法爾一言不發，撥轉馬頭，沿原路返回。他清楚地記得那天走過的每一里路，他決心要找到那處懸崖和那條穿過林間蜿蜒上山通往城堡的小路。

For hours he explored every hill, for hours he searched for that lake on which he had seen the fishermen - but he found neither lake nor cliff nor path. It was as if it all had been a dream, and there was no longer any trace of it.

他花了好幾個小時搜尋每一座山丘，花了好幾個小時尋找他曾看到漁民的那個湖泊——但他既找不到湖泊，也找不到懸崖，更找不到那條小路。一切彷彿只是一場夢，再也沒有留下任何痕跡。

## 9.Manichaeism and the Albigenses 摩尼教與阿爾比派


Parsifal's meeting with the Fisher King is, like the sword of King Arthur, a symbolic picture and is not to be taken as a physical event. But these pictures - the ailing Fisher King, the castle Montsalvage, the thing called the "Grail" - are not as easily explained or interpreted as King Arthur's sword.

帕西法爾與漁人王的相遇，就像亞瑟王的那把劍一樣，是一幅象徵性的圖像，不應被視為一個實際的物質事件。但這些圖像——病弱的漁人王、蒙薩爾瓦什城堡、被稱為「聖杯」的事物——卻不像亞瑟王的劍那樣容易解釋或詮釋。

These pictures of the castle Montsalvage and the holy Grail are, one could say, a message, a last message sent by men who saw their world destroyed by evil forces - a message they sent out so that in the future their most sacred beliefs should not be forgotten. But the message had to be put into such a form that the enemies would not recognise it, and would let it pass. The Grail story is a picture, a symbolic picture, in which a certain group of people, threatened with destruction, sent a message to the humankind of the future.

蒙薩爾瓦什城堡和聖杯的這些圖像，可以說是一則訊息，一則由那些眼見自己的世界被邪惡勢力摧毀的人們所發出的最後訊息——他們將這則訊息傳送出去，好讓他們最神聖的信仰在未來不至於被遺忘。但這則訊息必須以一種敵人無法辨識、從而不會加以阻攔的形式呈現。聖杯故事就是這樣一幅圖畫、一幅象徵性的圖畫，在其中，一群面臨滅絕威脅的人們向未來的人類發出了訊息。

You know that in the early centuries of the Middle Ages the Roman Catholic Church was all-powerful, and that it suppressed ruthlessly any "heresies" - which means any ideas which did not conform with the "dogma" of the Church. "Dogma" means the official teaching of the Church. To be a "heretic", to express any view forbidden by "dogma", was to invite torture and death at the stake. Nevertheless, there were "heresies", there were people who dared to defy the Church. In the twelfth century, around 1100, one particular heresy had found large support in the south of France, in Provence. The people who followed this heresy were called "Albigenses".

你們知道，在中世紀早期的幾個世紀裡，羅馬天主教會擁有至高無上的權力，並且無情地鎮壓一切「異端」——也就是任何不符合教會「教條」的思想。「教條」（dogma）就是教會的官方教義。成為「異端」，表達任何被教條所禁止的觀點，就意味著招來酷刑和火刑。然而仍然有「異端」存在，仍然有人膽敢反抗教會。十二世紀，約在一一〇〇年前後，有一種特殊的異端在法國南部的普羅旺斯（Provence）獲得了大量的支持。追隨這種異端的人被稱為「阿爾比派」（Albigenses）。

The Albigenses in the south of France were so numerous that there were practically no Roman Catholics left amongst them. They had their own churches, their own priests, their own Bible. Nobles and merchants and peasants - every class had taken up this heresy. Poetry and the arts flourished amongst them, and they preached tolerance towards any form of religion - something quite unheard of in those days. Jews and Muslims could live unmolested and undisturbed amongst them - in fact, they had very cordial relations with the Arabs in Spain.

法國南部的阿爾比派人數眾多，以至於他們當中幾乎沒有羅馬天主教徒了。他們有自己的教堂、自己的牧師、自己的聖經。貴族、商人和農民——每個階層都接受了這種異端信仰。詩歌和藝術在他們中間蓬勃發展，他們宣揚對任何宗教形式的寬容——這在當時是聞所未聞的事。猶太人和穆斯林可以在他們之間安居，不受騷擾——事實上，他們與西班牙的阿拉伯人有著非常友好的關係。

All this was, of course, a thorn in the flesh of the Church of Rome. If this sort of thing was allowed to go on, the heresy might spread to other lands - in fact, it was already spreading. And so Pope Innocent III in Rome called for a "Crusade" - not a crusade against Turks or Muslims, but against Christians, the Albigensians.

這一切當然讓羅馬教會如芒在背。如果任由這種情形繼續下去，異端可能會蔓延到其他地區——事實上已經在蔓延了。於是教皇英諾森三世（Pope Innocent III）在羅馬號召了一次「十字軍」——不是對付突厥人或穆斯林的十字軍，而是對付基督徒——阿爾比派的十字軍。

And he found volunteers for this crusade: some, no doubt, came from religious fanaticism, but most of them came in the hope of plunder from the rich cities of Provence. And thus the "Albigensian Crusade" came about.

他找到了參加這次十字軍的志願者：其中一些人無疑是出於宗教狂熱，但大多數人是希望從普羅旺斯富庶的城市中掠奪財富而來。於是「阿爾比派十字軍」就此展開。

The Middle Ages is known for being a cruel and bloodthirsty period, but the brutality of that Albigensian Crusade was, even for those days, hair-raising. City after city went up in flames, whole populations were massacred and those who died quickly were the lucky ones.

中世紀以殘暴和嗜血著稱，但那場阿爾比派十字軍的殘忍，即便以那個時代的標準來看，也令人毛骨悚然。一座又一座城市被付之一炬，整座城鎮的居民遭到屠殺，而那些迅速死去的人反而算是幸運的。

The last Albigensians took refuge in a castle in the Pyrenees, Montségur. When this last refuge was surrounded by the "Crusaders", it was only a matter of time until the end came. After surrendering, the remaining Albigenses were dragged down the mountain path from the castle. Rather than renouncing their beliefs, men, women and children chose to be burned alive in one great fire. And so the Albigensian heresy was wiped out.

最後的阿爾比派信徒躲進了庇里牛斯山的一座城堡——蒙塞居爾（Montségur）。當這最後的避難所被「十字軍」包圍時，末日的來臨只是時間問題。投降之後，殘存的阿爾比派信徒被拖下城堡的山路。男人、女人和兒童寧可被活活燒死，也不願放棄自己的信仰，他們選擇了在一場熊熊大火中殉道。阿爾比派異端就這樣被消滅了。

But there was still an Albigensian "underground" movement (as we would call it), a movement which worked in two ways, to keep their cherished beliefs alive. One way was the troubadours: many of the troubadours were secretly Albigenses, and they used their art, their poetry, to express in a veiled form - in a form the Roman Church would not recognise - the faith and the knowledge which the so-called "Crusade" had removed from the earth.

但阿爾比派的「地下」運動（用今天的話來說）仍然存在，這一運動通過兩種方式來保存他們珍視的信仰。一種方式是行吟詩人（troubadours）：許多行吟詩人暗地裡就是阿爾比派信徒，他們運用自己的藝術和詩歌，以隱晦的形式——以羅馬教會無法辨認的形式——表達那場所謂「十字軍」已從世間抹除的信仰與知識。

The stories of King Arthur and his knights had already been popular, and now the troubadours - or, rather, some of them - added a new feature to these stories: they introduced the story of the Grail and the Knights of the Grail.

亞瑟王和他的騎士們的故事早已廣為流傳，如今行吟詩人——或者更確切地說，其中一部分人——在這些故事中加入了一個新的元素：他們引入了聖杯和聖杯騎士的故事。

So the Grail story is a veiled expression of a kind of Christianity which was not allowed to live openly and which was at that time brutally suppressed and persecuted as a heresy. And this brings us to the question: what was the Albigensian heresy? In what way was it different from the Roman Church, or any Church which exists today?

所以聖杯故事是一種不被允許公開存在的基督教形式的隱晦表達，這種基督教在當時作為異端被殘酷地鎮壓和迫害。這就把我們帶到了一個問題：阿爾比派的異端究竟是什麼？它與羅馬教會，或今天任何教會有何不同？

To find an answer to this question, we must go back in history, very far back, to about 4,000 years before our present time.

要找到這個問題的答案，我們必須追溯歷史，追溯到非常久遠的過去，大約在我們這個時代之前四千年。

About 4,000 years ago, there spread in the Middle East, in the region which is now called Iran or Persia, a remarkable religion. This religion saw the whole world, the universe, earth and humanity, involved in a great war between the powers of light and the powers of darkness. Light did not mean just physical light, but also the hosts of all that is good and wholesome in the world, and darkness was not mere physical darkness but the visible expression of all that is evil and corrupting in the world.

大約四千年前，在中東地區——也就是今天稱為伊朗或波斯的地方——傳播開來一種了不起的宗教。這種宗教將整個世界、宇宙、大地和人類視為光明力量與黑暗力量之間一場偉大戰爭的參與者。光明不僅僅意味著物質的光，更代表世間一切美善、健康事物的集合；而黑暗也不僅僅是物質的黑暗，而是世間一切邪惡與腐敗的可見表現。

The Lord of the hosts of light was called Ahura Mazda, and the Lord of the powers of darkness was Ahriman.

光明大軍的主宰被稱為阿胡拉·馬茲達（Ahura Mazda），而黑暗勢力的主宰則是阿里曼（Ahriman）。

The people of ancient Persia regarded every human being as involved in this great cosmic strife. Every human deed helped either one side or the other - the light or the darkness. And the ancient Persians felt themselves as servants of the Kingdom of Light, and all their work here on earth was in the service of the Lord of Light.

古波斯人認為每一個人都捲入了這場偉大的宇宙之爭。人的每一個行為都在幫助其中一方——光明或黑暗。古波斯人視自己為光明王國的僕人，他們在大地上的一切勞作都是為光明之主效力。

This religion had very far-reaching, practical results. The Persians, for instance, were able to change a kind of wolf into a useful domestic animal, the dog. By this transformation of a savage beast into man's faithful friend, they had gained a victory over the powers of darkness. Far more important was another "transformation" - they changed a kind of wild grass into the first kind of cereal, and all our wheat and oats and barley are descendants of this original plant, cultivated 4,000 years ago. The whole of agriculture began 4,000 years ago in this region, and then spread to Asia and to Europe. The Persians were also the first to change wild roses into garden roses, and wild trees into the fruit trees we have now.

這種宗教產生了非常深遠而實際的成果。例如，波斯人能夠將一種狼轉化為有用的家畜——狗。通過將兇猛的野獸轉變為人類忠實的朋友，他們贏得了一場對黑暗力量的勝利。更重要的是另一種「轉化」——他們將一種野草轉變為最初的穀物，我們今天的小麥、燕麥和大麥都是四千年前培育的這種原始植物的後裔。整個農業在四千年前從這個地區開始，然後傳播到亞洲和歐洲。波斯人也是最早將野玫瑰培育為花園玫瑰、將野生樹木培育為我們如今所有的果樹的民族。

Now, when I say they "changed" wild trees, it sounds much simpler than it really is. If you take the seeds of a sweet, juicy apple and plant them, the tree you grow will not bear any apples you could eat - they will only be wild apples, "crab apples". If you want eating apples, you must "graft" a twig of a good apple tree on to a young, new wild tree. Once there is a tree bearing sweet apples, any amount of wild trees can be changed by grafting. And it is the same with every fruit tree, and with every rose. But how did the Persians get their first tree bearing sweet apples - the first bush with garden roses? That is what we do not know. Even our most advanced methods cannot change a crab apple without grafting.

現在，當我說他們「改變」了野生樹木時，這聽起來比實際情況要簡單得多。如果你把一顆甜美多汁的蘋果種子種下，長出的樹不會結出你能吃的蘋果——那只會是野蘋果、「海棠果」。如果你想要可食用的蘋果，你必須把好蘋果樹的枝條「嫁接」到一棵年幼的新野樹上。一旦有了一棵結出甜蘋果的樹，再多的野樹都可以通過嫁接來改良。每一種果樹、每一種玫瑰都是如此。但波斯人最初是怎樣得到第一棵結甜蘋果的樹——第一叢花園玫瑰呢？這是我們不得而知的。即使是我們最先進的方法，也無法不經嫁接就改良海棠果。
