So this religion of ancient Persia had immense practical consequences - it changed man from a hunter and a nomad, to a farmer who lived a settled life - it made cities and civilization possible.

所以古波斯這種宗教產生了巨大的實際影響——它將人從獵人和遊牧者轉變為定居生活的農民——它使城市和文明成為可能。

And then centuries passed - the Persians became decadent, and their religion degenerated to a formality. Christianity came - but it made very few converts in Persia. Then, about AD 300, a Persian, Mani, founded a new religion, a kind of synthesis between the religion of Persia and Christianity. He spoke of Christ as the Lord of Light, who had come to earth to help man in the struggle against the powers of darkness.

然後幾個世紀過去了——波斯人變得頹廢，他們的宗教退化為一種形式。基督教傳來——但在波斯幾乎沒有贏得什麼信徒。大約在公元三百年，一位波斯人摩尼（Mani）創立了一種新宗教，是波斯宗教與基督教的某種綜合。他稱基督為光明之主，來到世間幫助人類與黑暗力量作鬥爭。

But there was a significant change in the teaching of Mani. First of all, this war between light and darkness was not like human wars which aim at the destruction of the enemy. When Christ, the King of Light, gains a victory over evil, that evil is transformed, changed into a higher good. In every evil, there is hidden a higher force for good which lies, as it were, enchanted in it - waiting to be awakened and released. The second change was that Mani put the emphasis on changes in ourselves, not on changing animals or plants. What is savage and wild in the soul has to be transformed to become a power for good.

但摩尼的教義中有一項重要的改變。首先，光明與黑暗之間的戰爭不像人類戰爭那樣以消滅敵人為目標。當光明之王基督戰勝邪惡時，那邪惡會被轉化，變成一種更高的善。每一種邪惡中都隱藏著一種更高的善的力量，彷彿被魔法封印其中——等待著被喚醒和釋放。第二項改變是摩尼將重點放在了改變我們自身，而不是改變動物或植物上。靈魂中野蠻和狂暴的東西必須被轉化，成為向善的力量。

This teaching of Mani made him enemies amongst the Persians and the Christians. The Persians were against him for bringing new ideas into their religion, and the Christians were horrified at the idea of regarding evil as something that could be redeemed and transformed. In the end, Mani was executed by order of the king of Persia.

摩尼的這些教義使他同時樹敵於波斯人和基督徒。波斯人反對他將新思想引入他們的宗教，基督徒則對將邪惡視為可以被救贖和轉化之物這一觀念感到震驚。最終，摩尼被波斯國王下令處死。

But Mani already had followers - they were called "Manicheans". These Manicheans were expelled from Persia - but the Christian countries of Europe gave them no better welcome. They were accused of worshipping evil and persecuted. Yet, in spite of this, the Manichean teaching spread, particularly in southern France, in Provence.

但摩尼已經有了追隨者——他們被稱為「摩尼教徒」（Manicheans）。這些摩尼教徒被驅逐出波斯——但歐洲的基督教國家也沒有給他們更好的對待。他們被指控崇拜邪惡並遭受迫害。然而儘管如此，摩尼教的教義仍然傳播開來，尤其是在法國南部的普羅旺斯。

And the Albigenses belonged to this stream. Their heresy was the heresy of Mani. The destruction of the Albigenses was only the last chapter in a long story of persecution.

而阿爾比派正屬於這一脈絡。他們的異端就是摩尼的異端。阿爾比派的毀滅不過是漫長迫害歷史中的最後一章。

This Albigensian heresy still carried the stamp of its Persian origin - they still spoke of the "Kingdom of Light" and the "Kingdom of Darkness". And Wolfram, the troubadour, speaks as a Manichean, as a follower of Mani, when he lets the mother say to Parsifal, about God:

阿爾比派異端仍然帶著波斯起源的印記——他們仍然談論「光明王國」和「黑暗王國」。而行吟詩人沃夫朗（Wolfram）在讓母親對帕西法爾談論上帝時，正是以摩尼教徒、摩尼追隨者的口吻說的：

> Son, He is brighter than the day
> And, once, to help man on his way
> He lived as man on earth indeed.
> To Him you turn in every need.
> But dark is he who by his art
> Makes men from God's true ways depart.

> 孩子，祂比白晝更為光明，
> 為了引導人類前行，
> 祂曾確實以人的形體生活於世間。
> 你在一切需要中向祂轉求。
> 但那以其詭計
> 使人偏離上帝正道的，是黑暗的化身。

It is a Manichean picture of the Kingdom of Light and the Kingdom of Darkness.

這是一幅摩尼教式的光明王國與黑暗王國的圖像。

The Manicheans, the Albigenses spoke about "darkness" and "light" in the human soul. We, today, do not use these words - we use a different terminology. We now have a science called "psychology", the science of the soul. And the psychologists have discovered that, besides the conscious mind, there is also a "subconscious" part of the soul. A great part of our soul is immersed in darkness and we are, normally, not aware of what goes on in this darkness of the subconscious. But it does happen, in certain cases, that something "erupts" from the depths of the subconscious into the conscious mind - we call such an eruption a "neurosis".

摩尼教徒、阿爾比派談論人類靈魂中的「黑暗」和「光明」。我們今天不使用這些詞彙——我們用不同的術語。我們現在有一門叫做「心理學」的科學，即靈魂的科學。心理學家發現，除了意識心智之外，靈魂中還有一個「潛意識」的部分。我們靈魂的很大一部分浸沒在黑暗中，我們通常不知道這潛意識的黑暗中發生了什麼。但確實會在某些情況下，有某種東西從潛意識深處「爆發」進入意識心智——我們稱這樣的爆發為「神經症」（neurosis）。

Some psychologists made a special study of such cases, for these "eruptions" can give an indication of what goes on in the darkness of the subconscious. The two great names in the science of the subconscious are Sigmund Freud and Carl Jung. There is still a great controversy going on about the merits of either school of thought - but one thing is quite certain: that in this darkness of the subconscious we carry - each one of us, no matter how good we are in our conscious behaviour - a host of evil, destructive inclinations.

一些心理學家專門研究了這類案例，因為這些「爆發」可以指示潛意識黑暗中究竟發生了什麼。潛意識科學中的兩個偉大名字是西格蒙德·佛洛伊德（Sigmund Freud）和卡爾·榮格（Carl Jung）。關於兩個學派各自的功過至今仍有巨大的爭論——但有一點是確定的：在潛意識的黑暗中，我們每個人——無論在意識行為中多麼善良——都攜帶著大量邪惡的、破壞性的傾向。

Normally we are spared a knowledge of this hell in our subconscious, but if there is a crack in the dam which keeps the evil forces down, when they rise they can destroy the whole human personality. I am afraid these cases are on the increase, they are becoming more frequent. The psychiatrists who have to deal with these patients are faced with a great problem. There are useful drugs, but the drugs are not a cure - they only suppress the symptoms. A real cure could only consist in changing, in transforming these evil, destructive forces which come to the surface into something positive, something good and wholesome.

通常我們被免於知曉潛意識中這個地獄的存在，但如果壓制邪惡力量的堤壩出現了裂縫，當它們湧上來時，可以摧毀整個人格。恐怕這類案例正在增加，變得越來越頻繁。必須面對這些病患的精神科醫生面臨著巨大的難題。有一些有用的藥物，但藥物不是治療——它們只是壓制症狀。真正的治癒只能在於改變，在於將浮出表面的那些邪惡的、破壞性的力量轉化為積極的、善良而健康的東西。

So, you see, modern psychology, modern psychiatry, is faced with the same problem that occupied the Manicheans, the Albigenses. We do not use the same words, we do not use the same terminology, but the modern psychologist (who may not believe in God - or Christ - or the Kingdom of Light) is faced with the problem of the Manicheans and the Albigenses. He is faced with the problem of transforming the evil that is boiling up from the subconscious of his patient.

所以你看，現代心理學、現代精神醫學面對的正是摩尼教徒、阿爾比派所關注的同一個問題。我們不使用同樣的詞彙，不使用同樣的術語，但現代心理學家（他可能不相信上帝——或基督——或光明王國）面對的正是摩尼教徒和阿爾比派的問題。他面對的是如何轉化從病人潛意識中沸騰而出的邪惡。

Now Professor Freud, who was the first to investigate these dark forces, coined a word for this change, for this transformation that is necessary to save the mental balance of the patient - he called it "sublimation". The task of the doctor would be to help the patient to "sublimate" the dark urges of the subconscious.

佛洛伊德教授是第一個研究這些黑暗力量的人，他為拯救病人心理平衡所必需的這種改變、這種轉化創造了一個詞——他稱之為「昇華」（sublimation）。醫生的任務是幫助病人「昇華」潛意識中黑暗的衝動。

Freud coined the word "sublimation", but, in practice, it happens very rarely that it is possible to bring about such a "sublimation". In the rare cases where it did happen, it is not even known why this particular patient succeeded. Professor Jung, the other great name in analytical psychology, maintained that patients with strong religious convictions stood a much better chance than others. He regarded religion as a decisive creative force.

佛洛伊德創造了「昇華」這個詞，但在實踐中，能夠實現這種「昇華」的情況非常罕見。在那些確實發生的罕見案例中，人們甚至不知道為什麼這個特定的病人能夠成功。分析心理學中另一位偉大人物榮格教授則認為，擁有強烈宗教信念的病人比其他人有更大的成功機會。他視宗教為一種決定性的創造力量。

And now we can go back to the Albigenses. They were well aware of that dark part of the human soul - they did not need neuroses to make them aware of it. They said: "This dark and evil element in us is not here by accident - it is in our organisation, in the body, in the blood." The blood that circulates in the body was for them not merely a physical substance, but also the physical carrier of emotions, passions, instincts, desires, including the dark urges of the subconscious. And they spoke of something that can work right into the blood, right into the depths of the subconscious, and can transform the evil and destructive urges into powers for good, into wholesome forces, and that is what they called "the holy Grail".

現在我們可以回到阿爾比派了。他們深知人類靈魂中那黑暗的部分——他們不需要神經症來提醒自己這一點。他們說：「我們內在這個黑暗而邪惡的元素不是偶然存在的——它在我們的組織中、在身體中、在血液中。」在他們看來，體內循環的血液不僅僅是一種物質，更是情感、激情、本能、慾望——包括潛意識中那些黑暗衝動——的物質載體。他們談到一種能夠深入血液、深入潛意識深處的東西，能夠將邪惡的、破壞性的衝動轉化為向善的力量、健康的力量，而那就是他們所稱的「聖杯」。

In the first chapter we saw that modern science is concerned with the secrets of matter while the ancient mysteries were concerned with the secrets of the spirit. Mani and his followers, the Manicheans, the Albigenses, were concerned with a third kind of secret - the secrets of the soul. How can the conscious mind change and transform the subconscious? How can the subconscious evil be transformed into good?

在第一章中我們看到，現代科學關注的是物質的奧祕，而古代的秘儀關注的是精神的奧祕。摩尼和他的追隨者、摩尼教徒、阿爾比派，則關注第三種奧祕——靈魂的奧祕。意識心智如何能改變和轉化潛意識？潛意識中的邪惡如何能被轉化為善？

You see, if we human beings were only physical matter, a kind of complicated machine, there would be no question of good and evil - machines have no moral problems. If we were only pure spirits, there would also be no question of good or evil - we would be good without any possibility of evil. But, as souls, we stand in between spirit and matter, and are faced with moral decisions. In our conscious mind, we would always want to do the right thing, but our subconscious sends out urges and promptings which interfere with our good moral intentions.

你看，如果我們人類只是物質，一種複雜的機器，就不存在善與惡的問題——機器沒有道德問題。如果我們只是純粹的精神，也不存在善與惡的問題——我們會純然為善，沒有為惡的可能。但作為靈魂，我們處於精神與物質之間，面臨著道德的抉擇。在我們的意識心智中，我們總是希望做正確的事，但我們的潛意識卻不斷送出衝動和驅力，干擾我們善良的道德意圖。

Normally we can keep these subconscious forces in check, we can control them. The criminal, the murderer is a person who cannot control them. But the Manicheans set out to do more than just control the subconscious - their aim was to transform it. And that spiritual power that can work into the subconscious, into the organic processes which send up the blind urges and promptings, this power they called the "Grail".

通常我們可以抑制這些潛意識的力量，我們可以控制它們。罪犯、兇手就是無法控制這些力量的人。但摩尼教徒的目標不僅僅是控制潛意識——他們的目標是轉化它。而那種能夠深入潛意識、深入那些送出盲目衝動和驅力的有機過程的靈性力量，他們稱之為「聖杯」。

In the story, the word "Grail" is used for a vessel - a cup, perhaps - but this is only a picture, a symbolic picture. It was never said in any other form what this power called "Grail" is - it was understood that every individual had to find the reality of the Grail for themselves. And those who had found it were called "Knights of the Grail". There were such people, such "Knights of the Grail", and they were of importance in the early Middle Ages. It was said - the Knights of King Arthur fight the brutish robbers and keep them in check by the power of the sword. But the Knights of the Grail go amongst the evil people and prevail on them to change, to become good. They do this by the power of their words. The Knights of Arthur are the knights of the sword - the Grail Knights are the knights of the word.

在故事中，「聖杯」一詞被用來指代一個器皿——或許是一個杯子——但這只是一幅圖畫、一幅象徵性的圖畫。從未有人以其他形式言明這種被稱為「聖杯」的力量究竟是什麼——人們理解的是每個人都必須為自己找到聖杯的真相。而那些找到它的人被稱為「聖杯騎士」。這樣的人確實存在過，這樣的「聖杯騎士」在中世紀早期非常重要。據說——亞瑟王的騎士們用劍的力量對抗粗暴的強盜並壓制他們。但聖杯騎士則走到邪惡的人當中，感化他們改變、變得善良。他們依靠的是語言的力量。亞瑟王的騎士是劍的騎士——聖杯騎士則是語言的騎士。

But when the Albigensian Crusade destroyed all these traditions, there were two ways in which they were saved and preserved. One way was through the troubadours - Wolfram, Chretien de Troyes and others. And there was another way.

但當阿爾比派十字軍摧毀了所有這些傳統時，它們通過兩種途徑得以保存和延續。一種是透過行吟詩人——沃夫朗、克雷蒂安·德·特魯瓦（Chretien de Troyes）等人。還有另一種途徑。

The stories of the troubadours reached only the ruling classes, the nobles and knights. For the peasants, the villains (meaning villagers, not criminals) and serfs, the work of the troubadours was as remote as the moon - but they, too, were fond of stories, and what they enjoyed were the old tales, the fairy stories which are now only for young children. All the fairy stories - Snow White, Cinderella - which are now only for children were, originally, tales going round amongst the peasant population. The famous Grimm's fairy tales were collected by two brothers called Grimm from amongst peasants who had passed them on for many generations.

行吟詩人的故事只流傳到統治階層——貴族和騎士。對於農民、平民（villain 在此意為村民，而非罪犯）和農奴來說，行吟詩人的作品遙不可及——但他們也喜愛故事，他們喜歡的是古老的傳說、那些如今只給幼童聽的童話故事。所有的童話故事——白雪公主、灰姑娘——如今只是給孩子的故事，但最初是在農民之間流傳的故事。著名的格林童話就是格林兄弟（Grimm）從世代口耳相傳的農民中間蒐集而來的。

And some of these fairy tales originated amongst the Albigenses. Let us take one which is perhaps the best known.

而這些童話故事中，有一些起源於阿爾比派信徒之間。讓我們來看一個也許是最為人知的故事。

A man, passing a garden, saw a beautiful rose, and went in and picked the flower to bring to his daughter, called Beauty. But the owner of the garden was a hideous Beast. The Beast caught the man plucking the flower, and he could only save his life by promising the Beast to send him his daughter, Beauty. So Beauty was sent to the Beast, and though she was surrounded by servants and given beautiful rooms to live in, she was unhappy, and she refused the Beast's wish that she should marry him. One day, the wish to be free from this horrible creature from whom she could not escape became so strong that she wished he were dead.

一個人經過一座花園，看見一朵美麗的玫瑰，便走進去摘下花要帶給他的女兒，名叫美女（Beauty）。但花園的主人是一隻可怕的野獸（Beast）。野獸抓住了摘花的人，他只有答應把女兒美女送來才能保住性命。於是美女被送到了野獸那裡。儘管她被僕人環繞，住在美麗的房間裡，她仍然不快樂，拒絕了野獸要她嫁給他的願望。有一天，她想要擺脫這個她無法逃離的可怕生物的念頭變得如此強烈，以至於她希望他死去。

With this wish in her mind, she went into the garden, and saw, to her shock, the Beast lying motionless on the ground. At the sight, she felt shame for her wish, and pity for the poor, ugly creature. She ran to a fountain, and brought water and tried to revive him. The Beast opened his eyes, and said: "You wish me dead - and, as you will not marry me, I want to die." And then Beauty, moved by compassion, said: "No, you shall not die - I agree to marry you." In that moment, the hideous Beast changed - he became a handsome young prince. Her words had broken the evil spell of a magician who had turned him into an ugly beast until a beautiful girl would, by her free will, agree to marry him.

帶著這個願望，她走進花園，震驚地看到野獸一動不動地躺在地上。看到這一幕，她為自己的願望感到羞恥，為這個可憐、醜陋的生物感到同情。她跑到噴泉邊，捧來水試圖救活他。野獸睜開眼睛說：「你希望我死——既然你不願嫁給我，我寧願去死。」於是美女被憐憫所動，說：「不，你不會死的——我願意嫁給你。」就在那一刻，醜陋的野獸發生了變化——他變成了一位英俊的年輕王子。她的話語打破了一個魔法師的邪惡咒語，那魔法師曾將他變成醜陋的野獸，直到有一位美麗的女孩出於自己的自由意志同意嫁給他。

This story and all the others which tell of frogs, or bears, which are really princes, who are freed from their enchantment by a girl willing to accept their companionship, are all tales of Manichean origin - they are tales to make us aware that the dark forces of the subconscious can be transformed into powers of good. Fairy tales, too, have a meaning, and are, in fact, often quoted in modern books on psychology.

這個故事以及所有那些講述青蛙或熊——實際上是王子——被一位願意接受他們陪伴的女孩從魔法中解救出來的故事，都是摩尼教起源的故事——它們是讓我們意識到潛意識中的黑暗力量可以被轉化為善良力量的故事。童話故事也有其深意，事實上在現代心理學著作中經常被引用。

## 10.Parsifal Joins King Arthur's Knights 帕西法爾加入亞瑟王的騎士團


Parsifal searched in vain for the lake where he had first seen the Fisher King, for the Castle Montsalvage on the forest-clad hill. He searched in vain, but he would not give up. His cousin Sigune had called him "accursed" because he had failed to ask a question that was expected of him. From what she said, it seemed that this question would have restored the health of the ailing Fisher King, which would have brought great honour to Parsifal himself. And although he longed to return to his wife, Queen Condwiramur, he could not go back until he had discharged this debt, until he found the Grail and its guardian knights and had asked the question. And so, whilst every wish in him drew him back to the city of Belrepeire and its beautiful queen, he did not return but continued his search.

帕西法爾徒勞地尋找著他第一次見到漁人王的那個湖泊，尋找著那座矗立在森林覆蓋的山丘上的蒙薩爾瓦什城堡。他徒勞地搜尋，但不肯放棄。表姊西古內稱他「被詛咒的」，因為他未能問出人們期望他問的問題。從她的話語中，似乎那個問題本可以恢復病弱漁人王的健康，也會為帕西法爾本人帶來極大的榮耀。儘管他渴望回到妻子康德薇拉穆爾女王身邊，但他不能回去——除非他還清了這筆債，找到聖杯和守護它的騎士們，並問出那個問題。因此，雖然他內心的每一個願望都將他拉回美麗之城貝爾蕊珮爾（Belrepeire）和他美麗的女王身邊，他仍未回頭，繼續著他的搜尋。

Weeks passed, and months - the seasons changed, winter came, and Parsifal rode through a landscape blanketed in snow, still on his quest for Montsalvage, the castle of the Grail.

數週過去了，數月過去了——季節更迭，冬天來了，帕西法爾騎馬穿行在白雪覆蓋的大地上，仍在尋找蒙薩爾瓦什，聖杯的城堡。

He did not know it, but he had come near a camp of King Arthur and his knights. They had put up tents near a forest where they intended to enjoy a sport greatly favoured by all knights - the sport of falconry. For this sport, the hunters rode out with falcons perched on their gauntlets. The falcon's heads were covered with a hood. When the hunters saw a flight of wild geese, they took the hoods off the falcons and threw them into the air. The birds took wing, pounced on some of the wild geese and brought them down. Then they had to be lured back by scraps of food, to be caught again.

他自己並不知道，但他已來到亞瑟王和他的騎士們紮營的附近。他們在一片森林旁搭起了帳篷，打算享受所有騎士都熱衷的一項運動——鷹獵。進行這項運動時，獵人們騎馬出行，獵鷹站在他們的護手上。獵鷹的頭上罩著頭套。當獵人看到一群野雁飛過時，他們取下獵鷹的頭套，將牠們拋向空中。鷹隻振翅高飛，撲向一些野雁將牠們擊落。然後必須用食物碎片將獵鷹引誘回來，重新捕獲。

Such a hunt had been on just a day before Parsifal passed this way. But one of Arthur's falcons had not come back - it had stayed all night in the woods. And just as Parsifal came riding by, a flight of wild geese rose in the cold morning air, and the falcon flew after them and struck one goose with its claws. But the goose escaped with a wound on its neck, and from this wound three drops of blood fell on the snow, right in front of Parsifal.

就在帕西法爾經過此地的前一天，這樣一場獵鷹活動剛剛舉行過。但亞瑟王的一隻獵鷹沒有回來——牠整夜都待在林中。就在帕西法爾騎馬經過時，一群野雁在清冷的晨空中飛起，那隻獵鷹追了上去，用爪子擊中了一隻野雁。但那隻雁帶傷逃脫了，從頸部的傷口上滴落了三滴血，正好落在帕西法爾面前的雪地上。

As his eyes fell on the pink patches in the white snow, Parsifal reined his horse in. By some strange accident, the pink and white before him seemed to form a face, the face of Condwiramur. Perhaps no one else would have seen any resemblance between the patches and a human face, but for Parsifal - who could not help thinking of her on his lonely quest - it was as if the hand of a master painter had created a living likeness of her in the snow.

當他的目光落在白雪上那粉紅色的斑點時，帕西法爾勒住了馬。由於某種奇妙的巧合，眼前的粉色與白色似乎構成了一張面容——康德薇拉穆爾的面容。或許其他人不會在這些斑點中看出任何與人面的相似之處，但對於帕西法爾——在孤獨的旅途中他無法不想念她——這彷彿是一位大師畫家之手在雪中創造了她的栩栩如生的肖像。

He could not take his eyes from the image in the snow - he lost all sense of place and time, and was completely lost in thought about Condwiramur.

他無法將目光從雪中的影像上移開——他失去了對地點和時間的一切感覺，完全沉浸在對康德薇拉穆爾的思念之中。

A squire had been sent out to look for the lost falcon. He saw a knight in red armour, staring down from his horse at the snow, and he asked the knight politely if he had seen a trained falcon. But the red knight took not the slightest notice of him - the squire was annoyed at this rude rudeness, and returned to the camp with bitter complaints about a strange knight who showed no respect for a servant of King Arthur. This was an insult that could not be allowed to pass. Immediately, one of King Arthur's knights rode out into the forest to teach the stranger better manners. He approached the stationary horseman, and shouted a challenge at him. But Parsifal was completely lost in thought, and did not even hear that someone had shouted at him. He remained as still as a statue. The other knight was not used to being treated as if he were nothing. He lowered his spear and galloped towards Parsifal. But, at the last moment, some kind of sixth sense warned Parsifal and roused him. He turned his horse, lowered his spear and with one mighty thrust he threw the attacker from the saddle. He then turned again to the contemplation of the pink patch in the snow, forgetting everything else around him.

一名扈從被派出去尋找走失的獵鷹。他看見一個身穿紅色盔甲的騎士騎在馬上盯著雪地，便禮貌地問他是否看到了一隻馴養的獵鷹。但那位紅騎士完全沒有理睬他——扈從對這種無禮十分惱火，回到營地憤憤不平地抱怨一個陌生騎士對亞瑟王的侍從毫無尊重。這種侮辱不能就此罷休。立刻，一位亞瑟王的騎士騎馬前往森林，要教訓那個陌生人懂得禮貌。他策馬來到那個一動不動的騎者面前，大聲向他挑戰。但帕西法爾完全沉浸在沉思中，甚至沒有聽到有人在向他喊叫。他靜止如雕像。那位騎士不習慣被如此無視。他放下長矛，策馬向帕西法爾衝去。但就在最後一刻，某種第六感警覺了帕西法爾，將他喚醒。他撥轉馬頭，放下長矛，一記猛刺將攻擊者掀下馬鞍。然後他又轉回去凝望雪地上的粉紅色斑點，將周圍的一切拋之腦後。

The knight of King Arthur had fallen on his arm and broken it, so he could not continue the argument in a knightly fashion. He limped back to the camp and made it known how ill he had fared at the hands of this stranger.

那位亞瑟王的騎士摔斷了手臂，無法以騎士的方式繼續較量。他一瘸一拐地回到營地，讓眾人知道他在那個陌生人手下吃了多大的虧。

There was keen competition amongst the knights for the privilege of teaching this ruffian courtly manners. In the end, it was Sir Kay whom King Arthur appointed to deal with the stranger. Sir Kay was the knight who had insulted Parsifal when he came to the court, and who had struck the lady whose laughter had proclaimed Parsifal the best of all knights.

騎士們爭相要去教訓這個粗人懂得宮廷禮儀。最終，亞瑟王指派凱伊爵士（Sir Kay）去對付那個陌生人。凱伊爵士就是帕西法爾初到宮廷時侮辱過他的那位騎士，也是動手打了那位因大笑宣告帕西法爾是最優秀騎士的女士的人。

Sir Kay rode into the forest until he saw the stranger, gazing at the snow before him. He rode close to him and shouted at him: "Get ready to fight, you lout!" And when the stranger continued to stare at the snow without moving, Sir Kay took his spear and gave that silent figure such a clout on the helmet that the whole forest rang.

凱伊爵士策馬進入森林，直到看見那個陌生人凝視著面前的雪地。他靠近了他，對著他大喊：「準備戰鬥吧，你這粗人！」當那個陌生人繼續一動不動地盯著雪地時，凱伊爵士舉起長矛在那沉默身影的頭盔上狠狠敲了一下，整個森林都迴盪著響聲。

This did the trick - it woke Parsifal promptly, but too promptly for Sir Kay, for the next moment he received a spear-thrust against his shield which sent him flying through the air and crashing down so heavily that he broke his right arm and left leg.

這招奏效了——它立刻喚醒了帕西法爾，但對凱伊爵士來說太立刻了，因為下一瞬間他的盾牌就挨了一記長矛的猛刺，把他掀到空中，重重摔在地上，右臂和左腿都斷了。

Then Parsifal returned again to his daydreams over the patch of snow whilst Sir Kay managed to climb on to his horse and returned to the camp with a tale of woe.

然後帕西法爾又回到了對雪地上斑點的白日夢中，而凱伊爵士勉強爬上馬，帶著一肚子苦水回到了營地。

But now one of King Arthur's best knights, Sir Gawain, undertook to save the honour of the Round Table. Gawain had listened to the tales about the odd behaviour of the stranger, and it seemed to him that this was not the action of someone who was deliberately insulting King Arthur and his knights. This was not a man spoiling for a fight - perhaps the riddle of this stranger could be solved in a different manner.

但此時亞瑟王最優秀的騎士之一高文爵士（Sir Gawain）決定挺身而出，維護圓桌騎士的榮譽。高文一直在聽關於那個陌生人古怪行為的描述，他覺得這不像是有人在故意侮辱亞瑟王和他的騎士們。這不是一個尋釁滋事的人——也許可以用另一種方式解開這個陌生人的謎團。

And so, to everybody's surprise, Gawain rode into the forest unarmed, without spear or shield. Like the others before him, Gawain saw the strange knight looking down on the snow before him, lost to the world and motionless. And, like the others before him, Gawain spoke to the stranger - he pleaded with him, he reproached him for lack of courtesy, for insulting King Arthur, and he promised him that if he would now come peacefully to the king he would be forgiven and made welcome.

於是，讓所有人驚訝的是，高文沒有攜帶武器，既無長矛也無盾牌，騎馬進入了森林。和之前的人一樣，高文看見那位陌生騎士低頭凝視面前的雪地，對世界渾然不覺，一動不動。和之前的人一樣，高文也開口了——他懇求他，責備他缺乏禮節、侮辱了亞瑟王，並承諾如果他願意和平地去見國王，他將得到寬恕和歡迎。

But this well-mannered approach had as little effect as had the challenge of the others before Gawain. It brought no reply, no response from the stranger - he kept on staring at the snow. Now Gawain himself looked at the snow patch which held the stranger's attention, and thought he could discern the outline of a woman's face in the spots of blood on the snow. "Well!" he said to himself. "A man in love! There's no accounting what such men will do. But there is only one way to bring this fellow to his senses." And Gawain dismounted, took the scarf he was wearing, and spread it over the pink patch in the snow.

但這種彬彬有禮的方式同樣毫無效果，就如同之前其他人的挑戰一樣。陌生人沒有回答，沒有反應——他繼續盯著雪地。這時高文自己也看向那片吸引陌生人注意的雪地斑點，他似乎能在雪上的血跡中辨認出一張女人面容的輪廓。「好吧！」他自言自語道。「一個陷入愛情的人！這樣的人做什麼都不奇怪。但只有一個辦法能讓這傢伙清醒過來。」高文下了馬，取下自己佩戴的圍巾，蓋在雪地上的粉紅色斑點上。

And it worked. Parsifal looked up and around like a man waking from a dream. He was surprised to see the snow around him trampled by many horses' hooves. He was surprised to see that the point of his spear was broken - and who was the unarmed knight before him?

這辦法奏效了。帕西法爾抬起頭，環顧四周，像一個從夢中醒來的人。他驚訝地看到周圍的雪地被許多馬蹄踐踏過。他驚訝地看到自己的矛尖斷了——而眼前這位沒有武裝的騎士是誰？

It took some time until he could take in what Gawain told him - that he had fought two knights and made them invalids. But he was very pleased to hear that one of the two invalids was Sir Kay. He had once promised that he would punish Sir Kay for striking a lady - and now this old score was settled. As Sir Kay had been punished, there was no longer any reason which could keep him from King Arthur's court, and he gladly accepted Gawain's invitation.

他花了好一會兒才理解高文告訴他的事——他打了兩個騎士，把他們打成了傷殘。但聽說其中一個傷殘者是凱伊爵士時，他非常高興。他曾發誓要懲罰凱伊爵士打女士之過——如今這筆舊帳終於清了。既然凱伊爵士已經受到了懲罰，就再沒有什麼理由阻擋他去亞瑟王的宮廷了，他欣然接受了高文的邀請。

And so Parsifal came to King Arthur, and was given a warm welcome. The lad who had once ridden his horse into the banqueting hall, who had come in fool's dress and knew nothing of chivalry, now returned as a knight of fame, who had rescued Queen Condwiramur and gained himself a crown.

帕西法爾就這樣來到了亞瑟王面前，受到了熱烈的歡迎。那個曾經騎馬闖進宴會大廳、穿著愚人衣裳、對騎士精神一無所知的少年，如今以一位聞名遐邇的騎士身分歸來——他曾拯救了康德薇拉穆爾女王，並為自己贏得了王冠。

This knightly deed had earned him an honour coveted by every Christian knight - he was invited by King Arthur to join the companionship of the Round Table, to become a Knight of the Round Table. A banquet was given to celebrate the occasion, and it was a great moment in Parsifal's life when he took his seat at the Round Table, as the equal and companion of the most valiant knights in Christendom. This was the moment when the great dream which had lured him from the forest came true.

這些騎士功業為他贏得了每個基督教騎士都夢寐以求的榮譽——亞瑟王邀請他加入圓桌騎士團，成為一名圓桌騎士。為此舉行了一場慶祝宴會，當帕西法爾在圓桌旁坐下，與基督教世界最英勇的騎士們平起平坐時，那是他一生中偉大的時刻。那個曾將他從森林中引出的偉大夢想，此刻終於成真了。

But this day, which might have been a day of supreme happiness for Parsifal, was also the day on which a cruel blow of fate destroyed his happiness.

但這一天，本可以成為帕西法爾至高幸福的一天，卻也是命運殘酷的一擊摧毀他幸福的一天。

They were all still sitting at the banquet and the whole company was in gay and happy mood, when a visitor arrived, at whose sight they all fell silent. The gay laughter ceased, and awe and fear descended like a cloud upon the company. The strange visitor was a woman, richly dressed in silk and brocade. Jewels gleamed on her headband, and on her dress - but all the rich ornaments could do little to improve her appearance. Her long sharp nose was like the beak of a hawk, her skin was sallow, and beneath bushy eyebrows a pair of piercing black eyes looked grimly at the company.

眾人仍然圍坐在宴席旁，整個聚會都沉浸在歡樂愉快的氣氛中，這時一位來客到了，所有人一看到她就沉默了。歡快的笑聲戛然而止，敬畏和恐懼像一片烏雲般籠罩了整個聚會。這位奇異的來客是一位女子，穿著華麗的絲綢和錦緞。珠寶在她的頭帶和衣裙上閃耀——但所有華美的裝飾都無法改善她的容貌。她尖長的鼻子如同鷹鉤，膚色蠟黃，濃密的眉毛下一雙銳利的黑眼睛冷酷地審視著在座眾人。

As she stared at them in silence, Parsifal heard the knights beside him whisper, "That's Cundry - Cundry, the messenger of the Grail, the servant of the Fisher King - her coming bodes evil for someone!" And when Parsifal heard these words, a great dread gripped his heart.

當她沉默地注視著他們時，帕西法爾聽到身旁的騎士低聲說：「那是昆德莉（Cundry）——昆德莉，聖杯的信使，漁人王的僕從——她的到來預示著有人要遭殃！」聽到這些話，帕西法爾心中湧起了巨大的恐懼。

Cundry, the grim-faced woman, approached King Arthur, and said: "King of the Britons, you have brought shame upon yourself and upon all who serve you. You have allowed a man without honour, a man accursed, to take his place amongst you."

面色嚴厲的昆德莉走向亞瑟王，說道：「不列顛之王，你為自己和所有為你效力的人帶來了恥辱。你竟讓一個沒有榮譽的人、一個被詛咒的人在你們中間佔有一席之地。」

"It can't be true!" answered the King. "The Knights of the Round Table are all men of high honour. Who is the one you accuse of being unworthy?"

「這不可能是真的！」國王回答道。「圓桌騎士們都是品行高潔之士。你指控的那個不配之人是誰？」

And the woman pointed with a long bony finger at Parsifal, and cried: "There is no honour amongst you, as long as he is in your midst!"

那女子伸出瘦長的手指指向帕西法爾，喊道：「只要他在你們當中，你們之間就沒有榮譽可言！」

A gasp went through the whole great company of lords and ladies, and all eyes turned on Parsifal. And now the grim messenger spoke to him, "You may think that I am ugly, but although God has favoured you with good looks, you are a hundred times more repulsive than I am, for your heart and soul are rotten. You saw the suffering of the king whom I serve, but you did not find it in your heart to ask him what ailed him. You saw the wonders of the Grail, and your dull mind had no question. If you, unworthy son of a noble knight, had but asked once, my master would have blessed you, and your reward would have been higher than any you can dream of. But, as you have stayed dumb, the servants of the Grail must remain in grief - and you, blind fool, you are cursed!"

一聲驚呼傳遍在座的領主和貴婦之間，所有的目光都轉向帕西法爾。然後那位嚴厲的信使對他說道：「你也許覺得我醜陋，但儘管上帝賜予了你俊美的外表，你比我醜惡百倍，因為你的心和靈魂是腐爛的。你看到了我所侍奉的國王的苦難，卻不曾發自內心地問他一聲他為何受苦。你看到了聖杯的奇蹟，你遲鈍的頭腦卻沒有任何問題。如果你——一位高貴騎士不配的兒子——哪怕問了一次，我的主人就會祝福你，你的回報將超越你所能夢想的一切。但既然你啞口無言，聖杯的僕從們就只能繼續悲傷——而你，盲目的傻瓜，你被詛咒了！」

Before Parsifal could utter a word, Cundry strode out, and no one knew where she went.

帕西法爾還沒來得及說一句話，昆德莉便大步走出，無人知道她去了哪裡。

Shattered as he was, Parsifal could find comfort in the many knights and ladies who surrounded him and who assured him of their friendship, their love, their sympathy. But all those new-found and loyal friends could not shake him from the decision he had made when Cundry's accusation was shouted at him. He said: "As long as the curse is on me, I cannot belong to this noble company. As long as the curse is on me, I cannot return to the woman I love. I cannot find joy in life again, and I cannot rest until I have found Montsalvage again, and the suffering king, and have asked the question. I cannot rest, and I shall not rest, until I have found the Grail!"

儘管他備受打擊，帕西法爾仍能從圍繞他的眾多騎士和貴婦那裡得到安慰，他們向他保證他們的友誼、愛和同情。但所有這些新近結交的忠誠朋友都無法動搖他在昆德莉的指控聲中做出的決定。他說：「只要詛咒還在我身上，我就不能屬於這個高貴的團體。只要詛咒還在我身上，我就不能回到我所愛的女人身邊。我無法重新找到生命的歡樂，我也無法安寧，直到我再次找到蒙薩爾瓦什、那位受苦的國王，並問出那個問題。我不能安寧，也不會安寧，直到我找到聖杯！」

The lords, the ladies, King Arthur himself, tried to persuade him to stay, in spite of Cundry's curse, but he would not listen to them. He knew that all the honours of the court would leave a bitter taste if he could not redeem the curse laid on him, if he could not make up for his omission.

領主們、貴婦們，亞瑟王本人，都試圖說服他留下，不顧昆德莉的詛咒，但他不肯聽從。他知道，如果不能解除加諸在他身上的詛咒，如果不能彌補他的過失，宮廷的一切榮耀都會留下苦澀的滋味。

And so the next day Parsifal set out into the bleak winter weather on a search which might be endless, for he knew that only those whom the Grail called could find it. It was not the Grail which called him now, only his own desire. The future that lay before him as he rode away was as bleak and as grey as the wintry sky above him.

於是第二天，帕西法爾踏入了蕭瑟的冬日寒風中，開始了一場也許永無盡頭的搜尋，因為他知道只有聖杯召喚的人才能找到它。此刻召喚他的不是聖杯，只是他自己的渴望。當他策馬遠去時，擺在他面前的未來如同頭頂的冬日天空一樣蕭瑟灰暗。

So it was that when Parsifal reached his fondest dream, his highest ambition - to become a knight of King Arthur - this fulfilment became at the same time hollow and meaningless. He had to seek for something else, in loneliness, not knowing if he would ever find it.

就這樣，當帕西法爾實現了他最珍貴的夢想、最高的雄心——成為亞瑟王的騎士——這個成就卻同時變得空洞而毫無意義。他必須在孤獨中尋找別的東西，不知道自己是否能夠找到。

It is often so in life that when we achieve our ambition we often find it is but an empty shell and that what we really want, our true aim, is something quite different.

人生中常常如此：當我們實現了自己的抱負時，往往發現那不過是一個空殼，而我們真正想要的、我們真正的目標，是完全不同的東西。

## 11.The Quest of the Question 追問的追尋


If there is one thing in the Parsifal story which goes "against the grain", and which offends our sense of justice, it is the curse, the punishment laid on him for no good reason at all. It offends our sense of justice that he is punished for such a small matter: not asking a question. And what makes it worse is that he did not even know that this question was expected of him. He only refrained from asking that question because he followed the rules he had been taught by that old knight, Gurnemant.

如果帕西法爾的故事中有一件「令人難以接受」、冒犯我們正義感的事情，那就是加諸在他身上的那個詛咒、那個毫無正當理由的懲罰。他僅僅因為這麼一件小事——沒有問一個問題——就受到了懲罰，這冒犯了我們的正義感。更糟糕的是，他甚至不知道人們期望他問那個問題。他之所以沒有問，只是因為他遵守了老騎士古爾內曼茨教給他的規矩。

It is an absurdity - it does not make sense. But do you think that Wolfram, the poet who wrote the story, was not aware of it? For the people of his own time, the rank injustice of it all was even more upsetting than it is for us - and if Wolfram put such a story before them, then he did it intentionally.

這是一件荒謬的事——毫無道理可言。但你認為寫這個故事的詩人沃夫朗沒有意識到這一點嗎？對他那個時代的人來說，這種赤裸裸的不公比對我們來說更加令人不安——而如果沃夫朗將這樣的故事呈現在他們面前，那他是有意為之的。

It was as if he wanted to say: take the whole thing superficially, and it will seem an absurdity - you must go deeper to understand what I am driving at.

彷彿他想說：如果你從表面去理解這一切，它看起來就是一件荒謬的事——你必須深入探究，才能理解我真正的用意。

Now take that terrible thing Parsifal is said to have done: he had not asked a question. Wolfram goes out of his way to emphasise that the whole guilt of Parsifal consists just in this: he had not asked a question.

現在來看帕西法爾被指控犯下的那件可怕的事：他沒有問一個問題。沃夫朗刻意強調，帕西法爾的全部罪過就在於此：他沒有問一個問題。

Are questions all that important? Well, in our time, in the present age, they are.

問題真的這麼重要嗎？在我們這個時代，在當今的時代，確實如此。

All science begins with questions - why? how? where does this come from? Science is nothing else but a continuous process of asking questions. And as soon as an answer is found, it leads to new questions. Every experiment in the laboratory is a question, every rocket sent into space is a question. All technical progress of our time has come from questions.

一切科學都始於問題——為什麼？怎麼回事？這從哪裡來的？科學不過就是一個不斷提問的過程。一旦找到了一個答案，它就會引出新的問題。實驗室裡的每一次實驗都是一個問題，每一枚射向太空的火箭都是一個問題。我們這個時代所有的技術進步都來自於問題。

But it is not only the scientist who occupies themselves with questions. Not everybody can be a scientist. But think of the millions who read and enjoy a detective story. And the pleasure of a detective story is that you live for a time with the question: "Who dunnit?" Or think of the popular panel games on television, the question and answer games. Think of all the people who spend hours on crossword puzzles. Or think of modern art - every modern painting is an experiment, and a puzzle, and people buy these paintings because they like to be puzzled - which means to have a question.

但關注問題的不僅僅是科學家。不是每個人都能成為科學家。但請想想那些閱讀和享受偵探故事的千百萬人。偵探故事的樂趣就在於你暫時與一個問題共處：「兇手是誰？」或者想想電視上流行的問答節目、問答遊戲。想想所有花好幾個小時做填字遊戲的人。或者想想現代藝術——每一幅現代畫作都是一次實驗、一個謎題，人們購買這些畫作是因為他們喜歡被困惑——也就是說，喜歡有一個問題。

We question everything - the relationship between parents and children, the relations between the sexes, religion, government, standards of behaviour - these all give rise to questions.

我們質疑一切——父母與子女之間的關係、兩性之間的關係、宗教、政府、行為準則——這一切都引發問題。

It has to be like that, for it is in the nature of our time to ask questions. We won't take anything for granted - we ask and ask again. Our time is the age of questions.

這是必然的，因為提問正是我們這個時代的本質。我們不願將任何事物視為理所當然——我們不斷地追問再追問。我們的時代是問題的時代。

But it was not so in the past. In past ages, people were not so curious, they did not ask so many questions. Nor did the Church encourage questions. The few who began to ask questions were called "heretics" and burnt at the stake.

但過去並非如此。在過去的時代，人們沒有這麼好奇，他們不會問這麼多問題。教會也不鼓勵提問。少數開始提問的人被稱為「異端」，被燒死在火刑柱上。

But there was already in those dark ages something else... it was a kind of preparation for the coming age, for the future. The ceaseless seeking and searching and questioning of our time had a kind of preparation - a first dim beginning. You see, a knight of that time did not always stay in his castle or in the court of his king. From time to time, the knight set out on a journey in search of a difficult and dangerous task.

但在那些黑暗的時代，已經存在著別的東西……那是一種為即將到來的時代、為未來所做的準備。我們這個時代不息的尋找、搜索和追問有一種前兆——一個最初朦朧的開端。要知道，那個時代的騎士並不總是待在城堡或國王的宮廷裡。他們會時不時地啟程，去尋找一項困難而危險的任務。

Such a seeking for a great, worthy task was called a "quest", and all the story books of that time, all the stories told by the troubadours, glorified the quests of knights, the quests of Lancelot or Guinevere.

這種對偉大而崇高任務的追尋被稱為「探索」（quest），那個時代所有的故事書、行吟詩人講述的所有故事，都在頌揚騎士的探索——蘭斯洛特（Lancelot）或桂妮薇兒（Guinevere）的探索。

But the word "quest" means the same as the word "question" - it means a seeking, a searching.

但「quest」（探索）這個詞與「question」（問題）意義相同——它意味著尋找、搜索。

The knights were not yet ready to seek and search with their minds - they did not ask questions: they had to do their seeking and searching physically, by going on a quest. Their quests were the beginning, the preparation for the age of questions in which we live nowadays.

那時的騎士們還沒有準備好用頭腦去尋找和搜索——他們不會提問：他們必須用身體去尋找和搜索，通過踏上探索之旅。他們的探索是一個開端，是為我們如今所生活的問題時代所做的準備。

The other poets in Wolfram's time wrote stories about such "quests" - but Wolfram was different. He was himself a "heretic" who dared to ask questions forbidden by the Church. And his hero was to be a hero of the future, a hero of the time when people would ask questions as Wolfram did.

沃夫朗同時代的其他詩人寫的都是這種「探索」的故事——但沃夫朗不同。他本人就是一個「異端」，敢於提出教會所禁止的問題。而他的英雄要成為未來的英雄，一個屬於人們像沃夫朗一樣提問的時代的英雄。

That is why Parsifal is from the beginning shown to be full of questions. He was to be a hero of questions - full of curiosity.

這就是為什麼帕西法爾從一開始就被描繪成充滿問題的人。他注定要成為問題的英雄——充滿好奇心。

But Wolfram, the poet, wanted also to show that there were enemies who did not want this new force of questioning coming into the world.

但詩人沃夫朗也想展示，有些敵人不希望這股提問的新力量來到世間。

There was the Roman Church, but, you know, the Church would not have held such power if there had not been a majority of people who were against anything new, against any curiosity: they all wanted things to go on for ever in the same way. They were the people who supported the Church and held it in power.

有羅馬教會，但你們知道，如果不是大多數人反對一切新事物、反對一切好奇心——他們都希望一切永遠保持不變——教會就不可能擁有如此大的權力。正是這些人支撐著教會，維持著它的權力。

And Wolfram described such a person in the old man, Gurnemant, who became the teacher of Parsifal. Gurnemant is not an evil person - he has the best intentions - but he is too old, too wrapped up in old conventions and rules to understand the new thing that is in Parsifal. That's why this old man, Gurnemant, stops Parsifal's flow of questions, by telling him: "It's bad manners, it's wrong to ask questions."

沃夫朗在老人古爾內曼茨身上描繪了這樣一個人——他成了帕西法爾的老師。古爾內曼茨不是一個邪惡的人——他出發點最好——但他太老了，太沉浸在舊有的規矩和禮法中，無法理解帕西法爾身上那種新的東西。所以這位老人古爾內曼茨阻止了帕西法爾的追問，告訴他：「問問題是失禮的，問問題是不對的。」

In this meeting between the old man and the young man, the poet gave a picture of the meeting between the past and the future. The past, with the authority of age and experience, imposes on the young man the dead conventions and rules which have been observed for centuries. The ignorant young man, Parsifal, can do nothing else but accept these rules, and all his curiosity, all the questions which are natural to him, are stifled - they are buried under the conventions he learns from the old man.

在這場老人與年輕人的相遇中，詩人描繪了一幅過去與未來相遇的畫面。過去憑藉年齡和經驗的權威，將那些被遵守了幾個世紀的死板規矩和禮法強加在年輕人身上。無知的年輕人帕西法爾別無選擇只能接受這些規矩，他所有的好奇心、他那些與生俱來的問題全都被壓制了——被他從老人那裡學到的禮法規矩深深埋葬。

Later, when Parsifal comes to the Fisher King, he does not act out of his own true nature, he is not true to himself, but follows a rule which is, really, foreign to him - he does not ask any questions.

後來，當帕西法爾來到漁人王面前時，他沒有按照自己真正的本性行事，他對自己不忠實，而是遵循了一條其實與他格格不入的規矩——他沒有問任何問題。

That is a great pity, but it is not something you could blame Parsifal for. It is not a crime - but if nothing else happened Parsifal would live to the end of his life not according to his own true nature, but according to conventions which are foreign and unnatural to him. He would not be a free human being - for to be free means to act from one's own inner being - he would be a slave of conventions. And to live like that is a curse. That is what the story means by the "curse" on Parsifal. To be unfree, to be a slave of conventions - that is a curse.

這非常可惜，但你不能因此責怪帕西法爾。這不是犯罪——但如果沒有別的事情發生，帕西法爾將一輩子都不按照自己真正的本性生活，而是按照與他格格不入、不合本性的禮法規矩生活。他不會是一個自由的人——因為自由意味著按照自己內在的本性行事——他將成為禮法規矩的奴隸。而那樣活著就是一種詛咒。這就是故事中帕西法爾身上「詛咒」的含義。不自由、做禮法規矩的奴隸——那就是一種詛咒。

Parsifal has to be rescued from this fate: to become a conventional good knight, he must be rescued, and his own true nature, his thirst for questions, must be reawakened in him. He needs a hard shaking to bring to light in him what is buried under the conventions - and that is what happens to him.

帕西法爾必須從這個命運中被拯救出來：從變成一個循規蹈矩的好騎士的命運中被拯救，他自己真正的本性、他對問題的渴求必須在他心中重新被喚醒。他需要一次猛烈的震撼，才能將那些被禮法規矩掩埋的東西重新帶到光明中——而這正是發生在他身上的事。

So, you see, what happens to Parsifal seems to be a punishment, a grossly unfair punishment - but it is really a kind of painful education, a re-education, to bring out his true nature. Parsifal has lost his true self - he has to find himself again, and all the unhappiness now inflicted on him is for the purpose of making him what he really is: a man of the future, a man who asks questions.

所以你看，發生在帕西法爾身上的事看起來像是一種懲罰，一種極不公平的懲罰——但它實際上是一種痛苦的教育、一種再教育，為的是喚出他真正的本性。帕西法爾失去了真正的自我——他必須重新找到自己，而現在加諸在他身上的所有不幸，目的都是讓他成為他真正的樣子：一個未來的人、一個會提問的人。

In a school, it can happen that pupils who make poor progress are put in a lower class - but this is not done to punish them, it is done to give them a better chance. Well, Parsifal, by his strange upbringing and nature, belonged to an older class - he belonged to the future, to the age of questions. But this true nature of Parsifal had been stifled, and so he had to go into a lower class, he had to go back to something which was a preparation for the future - he had to go on a quest. In history, humankind had to go on quests before it could reach the age of questions. And so Parsifal has to go on a quest until the power of questions has grown in him again.

在學校裡，進步不好的學生有時會被降到低一級的班——但這不是為了懲罰他們，而是為了給他們更好的機會。帕西法爾由於他奇特的成長經歷和本性，原本屬於更高的年級——他屬於未來，屬於問題的時代。但帕西法爾的這種真正本性被壓制了，所以他不得不到低一級的班，不得不回到為未來做準備的階段——他必須踏上探索之旅。在歷史上，人類必須先經歷探索的時代，才能抵達問題的時代。帕西法爾也必須踏上探索之旅，直到提問的力量在他心中重新成長起來。

That is why he is sent on a quest - the quest of the holy Grail. He searches for the holy Grail. This is his quest - but it is not a punishment, it is a rescue, a help and an education. He did not ask the question - so he must go on a quest, the quest of the holy Grail.

這就是為什麼他被派上了探索之旅——聖杯的探索。他在尋找聖杯。這是他的探索——但這不是懲罰，而是拯救、幫助和教育。他沒有問出那個問題——所以他必須踏上探索之旅，聖杯的探索之旅。

Wolfram, the poet, could not say openly what he meant - not in the time in which he lived. He could only put it cautiously into pictures - but by making the story of Parsifal's punishment so absurd, he really put up a signpost: "Please don't take this superficially - there is a deeper meaning behind it."

詩人沃夫朗不能公開說出他的意思——在他生活的那個時代不能。他只能小心翼翼地用圖像來表達——但通過讓帕西法爾受懲罰的故事如此荒謬，他實際上立了一塊路標：「請不要表面化地看待這件事——背後有更深的含義。」

But this part of the story also has a very realistic meaning for our own time. Science has become what it is - the greatest force in modern life - only by asking questions. But as science grew and developed, there also came people who were regarded as authorities. Unfortunately, these "authorities of science" became, and still are, just as narrow-minded and dogmatic as the medieval Church - they suppress new questions and persecute "heretics".

但故事的這一部分對我們當今的時代也有非常現實的意義。科學之所以成為它現在的樣子——現代生活中最偉大的力量——僅僅是因為提問。但隨著科學的成長和發展，也出現了一些被視為權威的人。不幸的是，這些「科學權威」變得——而且至今仍然——和中世紀的教會一樣狹隘和教條——他們壓制新的問題，迫害「異端」。

Not so long ago, a biologist submitted to the British Medical Association a report on a remarkable discovery he had made, a discovery which could save countless lives and shorten many illnesses. The wise men of the British Medical Association shook their heads. It was quite impossible - and no doctor worthy of that name would make use of this discovery.

不久以前，一位生物學家向英國醫學會提交了一份關於他所做的一項了不起的發現的報告，這項發現可以拯救無數生命、縮短許多疾病的病程。英國醫學會的權威們搖了搖頭。這完全不可能——沒有一個名副其實的醫生會採用這項發現。

And when the biologist, in spite of this judgement of top scientists, carried on with his research, he was shunned, scorned and condemned by the whole profession. It took him fifteen years to break through this wall of prejudice and convention - and then his discovery was hailed as one of the great advances in medicine. The name of the discovery was penicillin, and the scientist who discovered it was Alexander Fleming. Fleming refused to take a patent on his discovery - he gave it to the world without making any profit for himself. This was the man whom the "authorities" had scorned and treated as a fool.

而當那位生物學家不顧頂尖科學家的這一判斷繼續他的研究時，他被整個專業界排斥、蔑視和譴責。他花了十五年才突破這道偏見和成規的高牆——然後他的發現才被譽為醫學的偉大進步之一。這項發現的名字叫盤尼西林（penicillin），發現它的科學家是亞歷山大·弗萊明（Alexander Fleming）。弗萊明拒絕為他的發現申請專利——他將它無償地獻給了世界。而這正是那些「權威」曾經蔑視並當作傻瓜對待的人。

These authorities were just like old Gurnemant. No one had asked before Fleming: "Can a fungus kill bacteria?" And when asked that question, he was told it was wrong to ask such a question.

這些權威就像老古爾內曼茨一樣。在弗萊明之前，沒有人問過：「黴菌能殺死細菌嗎？」而當他提出這個問題時，他被告知問這樣的問題是不對的。

That is the danger - that the scientists themselves forget that questions are the lifeblood of science, and that no one has the right to say that this question is allowed, and that question is not allowed. Once I spoke to a very intelligent scientist about such questions as: "Is there life after death?" And he said, with enormous authority: "This is not a question that concerns science - this is not a scientific question. Believe it, or don't believe it, but leave science out of it!" I could only answer: "What is your authority to say so? Perhaps your own corner of science has nothing to say to this question. But what right have you to set limits to science as a whole? How can you know that a science for such questions is not possible? You talk like the medieval Church, or like the old man, Gurnemant, in the story of Parsifal."

危險就在於——科學家們自己忘記了問題是科學的生命線，沒有人有權說這個問題可以問、那個問題不可以問。有一次我和一位非常聰明的科學家談論這樣的問題：「死後有來生嗎？」他以極大的權威口氣說：「這不是科學關心的問題——這不是科學問題。信不信由你，但別把科學扯進來！」我只能回答：「你有什麼權威這麼說？也許你所在的科學角落對這個問題無話可說。但你有什麼權利為整個科學設限？你怎麼知道針對這類問題的科學是不可能的？你說話的口氣就像中世紀的教會，或者像帕西法爾故事裡的老人古爾內曼茨。」

So, when you go out into the world, don't let the Gurnemants in the world, clever as they are, tell you what questions are scientific or permissible. No one has the right to stifle the questions you want to ask. Remember Parsifal, who nearly lost himself by accepting some outworn conventions.

所以，當你們走向世界時，不要讓世上的那些古爾內曼茨們——無論他們多麼聰明——來告訴你什麼問題是科學的或被允許的。沒有人有權壓制你想要提出的問題。記住帕西法爾，他差點因為接受了一些陳腐的禮法規矩而失去了自己。

## 12.The Grail 聖杯


Parsifal had sworn to himself that he would neither return to his wife nor resume his place amongst the knights of the Round Table until he had found the castle of the Grail -- until the quest of the holy Grail was accomplished. But when he made this promise, he had little idea of what kind of task he had set himself.

帕西法爾（Parsifal）曾對自己發誓，在找到聖杯城堡之前、在聖杯的追尋完成之前，他不會回到妻子身邊，也不會回到圓桌騎士之中。但當他立下這個誓言時，他幾乎不知道自己所承擔的是怎樣的任務。

He travelled through many lands, and his life was in constant danger. Armed knights challenged him, and barred his way. Robbers and bandits waylaid him for the sake of riches they expected to find on him. On other occasions he had to come to the rescue of the weak and oppressed against their persecutors. But it was as if he had a charmed life, because in all those encounters he overcame his opponents, or drove them to flight. A charmed life -- but to Parsifal it was a life empty and hollow unless he could find the castle of the Grail. He found no thrill in the dangers, and no satisfaction in his victories.

他穿越了許多國度，生命時刻處於危險之中。全副武裝的騎士向他挑戰，擋住他的去路。強盜和匪徒為了他身上可能攜帶的財寶而伏擊他。還有些時候，他不得不挺身而出，解救受迫害的弱者。但他彷彿擁有一種受護佑的生命——在所有這些遭遇中，他不是擊敗了對手，就是將他們趕得四散逃竄。受護佑的生命——但對帕西法爾而言，除非他能找到聖杯的城堡，否則這生命是空虛而蒼白的。危險不能帶給他興奮，勝利也不能帶給他滿足。

There were times when he found himself far from any village or castle, and the gleaming arc above him reminded him of the shining vessel he had seen in the Fisher King's hall: it reminded him of the Grail, and filled his heart with indescribable longing.

有時候，他發現自己遠離任何村莊或城堡，頭頂上閃爍的弧光令他想起在漁王大廳中見過的那個閃耀的器皿：它令他想起聖杯，心中充滿了難以言喻的渴望。

But the silvery crescent in the sky enclosed only darkness -- there was only darkness between its horns, and it seemed to Parsifal like a picture of himself. He felt he was himself such an empty vessel, such an empty shell -- and this void in him could only be filled if he could find the goal of his quest.

但天空中那銀色的新月只圍著黑暗——月牙的兩角之間只有黑暗，對帕西法爾來說，這彷彿是他自身的寫照。他覺得自己就是這樣一個空虛的器皿、這樣一個空殼——而他內心的空虛，唯有找到追尋的目標才能填滿。

At times, he had experiences between sleeping and waking, dream-like visions which filled him with dread. He saw in such a vision a little chapel on a roadside. And, in this dream, he entered the chapel and saw, lying on the altar, a dead knight. A candle burnt with a steady flame in front of the dead man. As Parsifal in this dream vision stared at the strange sight, a vast arm appeared and its hand reached for the candle and extinguished it ... and all was darkness.

有時，他在半睡半醒之間會經歷一些如夢般的異象，令他充滿恐懼。在這樣一個異象中，他看見路旁有一座小禮拜堂。在這個夢境中，他走進禮拜堂，看見祭壇上躺著一位死去的騎士。死者面前燃著一支安靜燃燒的蠟燭。當帕西法爾在夢中凝視這奇異的景象時，一隻巨大的手臂出現了，它的手伸向蠟燭，將它熄滅了……一切陷入黑暗。

Beset by dangers outside, harassed by this feeling of emptiness in himself, disturbed by such visions, Parsifal yet continued with his search.

外有重重危險包圍，內有空虛之感折磨，又被這些異象所困擾，帕西法爾仍然繼續著他的追尋。

But, as time passed, his heart became filled with bitterness and resentment. What had he done to deserve such a fate? It was easy for people who enjoyed happy and contented living to believe in a kind, loving God. But it had not been a kind, loving God for Parsifal. He felt no reason to feel grateful to such a God -- and he began to look with scorn at the people he saw on Sundays or feast-days going to church. Let them believe that their prayers meant anything -- but he himself would have nothing to do with this kind of thing!

然而，隨著時間流逝，他的心漸漸充滿了苦澀與怨恨。他做了什麼要承受這樣的命運？那些享受幸福而滿足的生活的人，當然容易相信一個仁慈而慈愛的上帝。但對帕西法爾而言，上帝並不仁慈，也不慈愛。他覺得沒有理由對這樣一位上帝感恩——他開始帶著鄙夷的目光看那些星期天或節日去教堂的人。讓他們相信祈禱有什麼意義吧——但他自己要與這一切毫無瓜葛！

Yet, in spite of the bitterness and hardness and despair he felt, he continued with his search -- he continued on his quest -- out of stubbornness, out of a grim determination not to abandon a task he had set himself.

然而，儘管他感到苦澀、冷酷與絕望，他仍然繼續著他的追尋——他繼續著他的探索——出於一種頑強，出於一種不願放棄自己立下的使命的堅定決心。

It was already the fifth year of the quest -- for five years he had travelled far and wide, and searched in vain. Now it was early spring again, but Parsifal had no eyes for the buds on the trees, for the fresh green in the fields. Nor did he notice that several knights he met on the road wore neither armour nor weapons, and were dressed in white. He did not notice the strange looks they gave him, nor did he hear when one of them shouted after him: "Don't you know what day it is?"

這已經是追尋的第五年了——五年來他四處奔走，徒勞無功。如今又是早春，但帕西法爾無心注意樹上的嫩芽、田野裡的新綠。他也沒有注意到路上遇見的好幾位騎士既不穿甲冑也不帶武器，而是一身白衣。他沒有注意到他們投向他的奇異目光，也沒有聽見其中一個人在他身後喊道：「你不知道今天是什麼日子嗎？」

Immersed in his own unhappy thoughts, and oblivious of the world around him, he rode on, and followed the road which took him into a forest. And, deep in the forest, Parsifal came upon a hut, a hermit's hut.

沉浸在自己不快的思緒中，對周遭的世界渾然不覺，他繼續騎行，沿著一條路進入了一片森林。在森林深處，帕西法爾發現了一間小屋，一間隱士的小屋。

A hermit was a person who withdrew from life in the world, who withdrew to the solitude of a forest or a mountain, to devote themselves entirely to a life of prayer and meditation.

隱士是一種從塵世生活中退隱的人，他們退到森林或山間的孤寂中，全心全意地獻身於祈禱和冥想的生活。

Parsifal, in the mood he was in, would have passed the hermit's hut without giving it a thought. But the hermit himself came out and hailed him. "Sir Knight," said the hermit. "What has come upon you that you ride out in armour, with spear and sword, on this day of all days?"

以帕西法爾當時的心境，他本來會毫不在意地經過隱士的小屋。但隱士自己走了出來，招呼他。「騎士先生，」隱士說，「是什麼事讓你在今天這個日子裡全副武裝、持矛帶劍地騎馬出行？」

Parsifal looked at the hermit -- a man whose hair and beard were white with age. To old people, courtesy was due, and so he answered the question, and said: "I have learned to be ready for a fight at any time. Why should I not be ready for danger on this day?"

帕西法爾看著隱士——一個頭髮和鬍鬚因年邁而花白的人。對年長者應當以禮相待，於是他回答了這個問題：「我已學會隨時準備戰鬥。為什麼我不應該在這一天防備危險呢？」

The hermit shook his head and said: "Perhaps you are not a Christian. Perhaps you are a Muslim and then, of course, you don't know what this day is. But amongst Christian folk this is a day on which no battle is fought, on which no tournament takes place, a day on which no sword must be drawn -- no knight will put on his armour, or carry weapons. Today, Sir Knight, is Good Friday, the day on which Christ died on the cross. On this day, in remembrance of Him who gave his life but would not allow Peter to defend him with a sword -- on this day, no Christian will engage in fight or harm another man. Are you not a Christian?"

隱士搖了搖頭說：「也許你不是基督徒。也許你是穆斯林，那自然不知道今天是什麼日子。但在基督教徒當中，今天是不可以打仗的日子，不舉行比武的日子，不可以拔劍的日子——沒有騎士會穿上盔甲或攜帶武器。騎士先生，今天是耶穌受難日（Good Friday），基督在十字架上受死的日子。在這一天，為了紀念那位獻出生命卻不允許彼得以劍為他辯護的主——在這一天，沒有基督徒會與人爭鬥或傷害他人。你難道不是基督徒嗎？」

Parsifal answered: "I remember the rule now -- one of the many I was taught once, and they brought me little happiness. But whether I am still a Christian, I don't know. I have not entered a church nor offered a prayer to God for many a year -- and I would be a liar if I did. I do not even want to think of God -- for all he has given me is a fruitless search and grief and sorrow. He has forgotten me -- and I have forgotten Him."

帕西法爾回答：「我現在想起這條規矩了——那是我曾經被教過的許多規矩之一，但它們沒給我帶來什麼幸福。至於我是否還是基督徒，我不知道。我已經許多年沒有進過教堂，也沒有向上帝獻過任何祈禱——如果我那樣做了，那是在說謊。我甚至不想去想上帝——因為他給我的全是徒勞的追尋、悲傷與痛苦。他忘記了我——而我也忘記了他。」

The hermit sighed, and then said: "This is but foolish speech, Sir Knight -- there may well come the day when you will give thanks to God for the very thing that is now your complaint. Come, dismount -- take off the armour and let me be your host on this holy day. Perhaps you can tell me what has made you so sore at God and the world."

隱士嘆了口氣，然後說：「這不過是愚蠢的話，騎士先生——總有一天你會因現在讓你抱怨的事而感謝上帝。來吧，下馬——卸下盔甲，讓我在這個聖日招待你。也許你能告訴我，是什麼讓你對上帝和這個世界如此憤恨。」

Parsifal hesitated -- what use was it to tell this old man of his troubles? But then he felt that it would be a relief to unburden himself to a sympathetic listener. And soon, divested of armour and weapons, he told the hermit that he had now been for many years on a fruitless quest, a quest for the Grail.

帕西法爾猶豫了——把自己的煩惱告訴這個老人有什麼用呢？但隨後他覺得，向一個有同情心的傾聽者傾吐心事也許會讓自己輕鬆一些。很快，他卸下了盔甲和武器，告訴隱士他已經在一場徒勞的追尋中走過了許多年——追尋聖杯。

"A quest for the Grail," said the hermit. "Don't you know that no one can find the Grail unless the Grail itself calls him? I know it -- for I have been myself a knight of the Grail before I became a hermit."

「追尋聖杯，」隱士說，「你難道不知道，除非聖杯本身召喚他，否則沒有人能找到聖杯嗎？我知道這件事——因為在成為隱士之前，我自己就是一位聖杯騎士。」

"You have been a knight of the Grail?" cried Parsifal, astonished.

「你曾經是聖杯騎士？」帕西法爾驚訝地叫道。

"Yes," answered the hermit, "and, as you are a seeker for the Grail, I will tell you the story. At the time of Christ, there lived amongst the Jews a pious man, and a follower of Christ. His name was Joseph of Arimathea. When Jesus Christ celebrated the Last Supper with his disciples, it was in the house of this man Joseph. And the cup which Jesus and the disciples used to drink the wine of the Last Supper remained with Joseph, and he guarded it as a holy relic -- for it has power such as no other vessel has.

「是的，」隱士回答，「既然你是聖杯的追尋者，我就把這個故事告訴你。在基督的時代，猶太人當中住著一個虔誠的人，一個基督的追隨者。他的名字叫亞利馬太的約瑟（Joseph of Arimathea）。當耶穌基督與門徒們舉行最後的晚餐時，就是在這個約瑟的家中。耶穌和門徒們在最後的晚餐中用來飲酒的杯子留在了約瑟手中，他把它當作聖物守護著——因為它擁有任何其他器皿都沒有的力量。

"And there was also another holy thing that remained in the keeping of Joseph -- it was the spear that pierced the side of Christ when he was on the cross.

「還有另一件聖物也留在約瑟的保管之下——那就是基督在十字架上時刺穿他肋旁的長矛。

"In secret, the cup and the spear were passed from generation to generation, and only their guardians knew where they were. In time the cup and the spear came into the keeping of a noble knight, a man without fault and without vice. He was told in a dream to build a castle to house the cup and the spear, and that the best knights would be called to serve and guard the holy things.

「秘密中，聖杯和聖矛一代又一代地傳承下去，只有它們的守護者知道它們在哪裡。最終，聖杯和聖矛來到了一位高貴騎士的守護之下，一個無瑕無疵的人。他在夢中被告知要建造一座城堡來安置聖杯和聖矛，最優秀的騎士將被召喚來侍奉和守護這些聖物。

"The cup is called 'Grail', and every year on Good Friday a white dove appears and places a wafer in the Grail. This gives the Grail its powers, for those who see the Grail need no other nourishment -- they are nourished by seeing it. And those who are ill need no other cure -- they are healed by the sight of the Grail.

「這只杯被稱為『聖杯』（Grail），每年在耶穌受難日，一隻白鴿會出現，將一片聖餅放入聖杯中。這賦予了聖杯它的力量，因為看到聖杯的人不需要其他食物——他們因看見聖杯而獲得滋養。那些生病的人也不需要其他治療——他們因看見聖杯而被治癒。

"Yet there is one man who is ill and suffering who sees the Grail, but the sight does not cure him, it does not help him, and he must bear the pain until a strange and dark prophecy is fulfilled."

「然而有一個人，他生病而痛苦，他看到了聖杯，但那景象並不能治癒他，也不能幫助他，他必須承受痛苦，直到一個奇異而黑暗的預言被實現。」

"Who is this man? Why must he suffer? What is the prophecy?" cried Parsifal, who realised that all this concerned him deeply.

「這個人是誰？他為什麼必須受苦？那預言是什麼？」帕西法爾叫道，他意識到這一切與他有著深切的關聯。

And the hermit answered: "He is the King of the Grail -- they also call him the Fisher King -- and his name is Anfortas. It was his father who built the Castle Montsalvage that houses the Grail, and who was the king and leader of the first knights of the Grail. When the father became old and infirm, there appeared on the Grail in shining letters the name 'Anfortas', and that meant that Anfortas should become Lord of the Grail. Anfortas was a valiant knight, and did great deeds in the service of the Grail. But no servant of the Grail must seek adventure and battle to gain glory and honour for himself. Only selfless deeds can be done by the knights of the Grail. Anfortas sinned against this rule, and for a woman's sake, to gain glory and favour in her eyes, he went and made war. It was in these fights that he received the wound, and no herb or ointment in the world can heal it -- nor does the Grail heal it. That is his punishment for having failed, as King of the Grail, to remain free of vanity and selfishness.

隱士回答道：「他是聖杯之王——人們也稱他為漁王（Fisher King）——他的名字是安佛塔斯（Anfortas）。建造蒙薩爾瓦奇城堡（Castle Montsalvage）來安置聖杯的是他的父親，他是第一批聖杯騎士的國王和領袖。當父親年老體衰時，聖杯上以閃亮的文字出現了『安佛塔斯』的名字，這意味著安佛塔斯應當成為聖杯之主。安佛塔斯是一位英勇的騎士，為聖杯立下了偉大的功績。但聖杯的侍奉者不可為自己追求冒險和戰鬥以獲取榮耀和名望。聖杯騎士只能行無私之事。安佛塔斯違反了這條規則，為了一個女人，為了在她眼中贏得榮耀與寵愛，他發動了戰爭。就在這些戰鬥中他受了傷，世上沒有任何草藥或膏藥能治癒它——聖杯也不能治癒它。這是他作為聖杯之王未能擺脫虛榮和自私的懲罰。

"But when he was brought back to the castle, there appeared writing on the Grail. It said:

「但當他被帶回城堡時，聖杯上出現了文字。上面寫著：

> Wait for the day when he comes who
> Will ask, "O King, what ails you?"
> And when he asks, the wound will heal.
> Yet no one must to him reveal
> That he should ask. Out of his own
> Compassion he must ask, alone.

> 等待那一天，他到來
> 問道：「國王啊，你受了什麼苦？」
> 當他問出，傷口便將痊癒。
> 然而無人可以向他揭示
> 他應當開口。唯有出於自身的
> 悲憫之心，他必須獨自發問。

"And since then Anfortas has been in never-ending pain, hoping and living for the day when the deliverer should come. But I myself left the service of the Grail to pray for him -- to pray that God may hasten the day of his deliverance. For, you see, I am his brother, Trevrizent. I gave up the privilege of seeing the Grail, the honour of being its keeper, in the hope that my sacrifice would be accepted by God and shorten my brother's suffering.

「從那時起，安佛塔斯就處於無盡的痛苦中，期盼著、活著只為等待拯救者到來的那一天。而我自己離開了聖杯的侍奉，為他祈禱——祈禱上帝加速他得到解救的日子。因為，你看，我是他的兄弟，特瑞弗里森特（Trevrizent）。我放棄了見到聖杯的殊榮、作為守護者的榮耀，希望我的犧牲能被上帝接受，縮短我兄弟的痛苦。

"Since then, I heard that a knight did come -- but, alas, the fool asked no question at all, and so he earned shame and not honour."

「後來，我聽說有一位騎士確實來了——但，唉，那個傻瓜根本沒有問任何問題，因此他所得到的是恥辱而非榮耀。」

The hermit fell silent -- and Parsifal too sat in silence. Should he tell Trevrizent, the hermit, who he was? But he had never lacked courage, and so he said to the old man: "I was that knight -- I kept silent at the sight of the Grail, and at the sight of the suffering king. I am Parsifal, the son of Herzeloyde -- I was brought up in the forest, and when I left the forest and learned the rules of courtesy I was foolish enough to let them prevent me from asking the question."

隱士沉默了——帕西法爾也默默坐著。他應該告訴隱士特瑞弗里森特他是誰嗎？但他從不缺乏勇氣，於是他對老人說：「我就是那位騎士——我在聖杯面前保持了沉默，在看到受苦的國王時也沉默了。我是帕西法爾，赫策洛伊德（Herzeloyde）的兒子——我在森林中長大，當我離開森林、學到了禮儀的規矩時，我愚蠢地讓那些規矩阻止了我開口發問。」

At these words, Trevrizent took the young man's hand in his own, and said: "Whatever you have done, I am glad to see you, Parsifal, for you are my sister's son. Queen Herzeloyde was my sister -- and the sister of the poor King Anfortas. The blood of the Kings of the Grail is in you, Parsifal, and that is why you found the Grail's castle!"

聽到這些話，特瑞弗里森特握住了年輕人的手，說：「無論你做了什麼，我都很高興見到你，帕西法爾，因為你是我姐姐的兒子。赫策洛伊德王后是我的姐姐——也是可憐的安佛塔斯國王的姐姐。聖杯諸王的血液流淌在你的身體裡，帕西法爾，這就是你能找到聖杯城堡的原因！」

"Yes," said Parsifal, with bitterness, "and that privilege given to me by my blood -- I have lost it, and now I cannot find the Grail, and have spent years in a fruitless quest."

「是的，」帕西法爾苦澀地說，「而那個因血統而賜予我的殊榮——我已經失去了它，如今我找不到聖杯，在一場徒勞的追尋中度過了許多年。」

"No," said the hermit. "I feel it is no accident that has brought you here on Good Friday. The Providence that has brought you to me will also guide you at the right time to the Grail, and to the deliverance of your uncle, the Lord of the Grail. And when that time comes, you will, perhaps, also understand that your long search and what you suffered was not in vain."

「不，」隱士說，「我覺得在耶穌受難日把你帶到這裡來並非偶然。那將你帶到我面前的天意，也將在正確的時刻引導你走向聖杯，走向你叔叔——聖杯之主——的解救。而當那一天到來時，你或許也會明白，你漫長的追尋和所受的苦難並非徒然。」

For the first time in many years there was again hope in Parsifal's heart, and a feeling that there was a higher wisdom in human destiny.

多年以來，帕西法爾的心中第一次再次燃起了希望，以及一種感覺——人的命運中蘊含著更高的智慧。

He was a different man when, some days later, after Easter, he left Trevrizent to continue his quest for the Grail.

幾天之後，復活節過後，當他離開特瑞弗里森特繼續追尋聖杯時，他已是一個不同的人了。

## 13.Darkness and the God of Freedom 黑暗與自由之神


Wolfram describes in his story how Parsifal, embittered by his fruitless search, turns against God. In the whole literature of the Middle Ages, this is the first time that somebody says: "I can do without God -- I can do without religion."

沃夫蘭（Wolfram）在他的故事中描述了帕西法爾如何因徒勞的追尋而心生怨恨，轉而對抗上帝。在整個中世紀文學中，這是第一次有人說：「我可以不需要上帝——我可以不需要宗教。」

The Middle Ages were the age of faith -- and the time in which men left their homes and families to go on a crusade, to fight and die for their religion. People of other faiths also died and suffered willingly for their beliefs -- there were Jews and Muslims equally ardent in their religion. But there was no one who would say: "I do not need a belief in God -- I do not need any religion at all."

中世紀是信仰的時代——那是人們離開家園和家人去參加十字軍東征、為他們的宗教而戰鬥和犧牲的時代。其他信仰的人同樣甘願為信仰而受苦犧牲——猶太教徒和穆斯林在宗教上同樣熱忱。但沒有人會說：「我不需要信仰上帝——我根本不需要任何宗教。」

And when Parsifal in his bitterness quite openly defies all the religions, when he refuses to take part in any prayer or worship, when he shows that he can live without religion, then he does not speak as someone of the Middle Ages -- he speaks with the voice of a modern person.

而當帕西法爾在他的苦澀中公然蔑視所有宗教，當他拒絕參與任何祈禱或崇拜，當他展示他可以不需要宗教而活時，他說話的方式不像中世紀的人——他以現代人的聲音在說話。

By some kind of intuition, by some kind of prophetic foresight, the poet, Wolfram, realised that his hero, Parsifal, the hero of the future, must also come to the point where he rejects God, denies God, and lives without religion. In his prophetic vision, Wolfram realised that in the future people would come to the point where they turned away from religion -- and so his hero Parsifal must come to the same point, and so speak as no one of the age of chivalry, the age of faith, would have spoken.

憑藉某種直覺，某種先知般的預見力，詩人沃夫蘭意識到他的英雄——帕西法爾——這位未來的英雄，也必須走到拒絕上帝、否認上帝、不要宗教而活的地步。在他的預言性洞見中，沃夫蘭意識到，在未來，人們將會走到背離宗教的地步——因此他的英雄帕西法爾也必須走到同樣的地步，說出騎士時代、信仰時代的人不會說出的話。

Parsifal wears the dress and armour of a medieval knight -- but he is a modern man even in his rejection of God and religion.

帕西法爾穿著中世紀騎士的服飾和盔甲——但即使在他拒絕上帝和宗教這件事上，他也是一個現代人。

The time which, for the poet, Wolfram, was a distant future, that time has now arrived -- it is our present time. There is not just one single Parsifal saying: "I can live without religion, without God" -- there are millions.

對詩人沃夫蘭而言是遙遠未來的那個時代，如今已經到來——它就是我們的現在。不只有一個帕西法爾在說：「我可以不要宗教、不要上帝而活」——而是數以百萬計。

If the Middle Ages were the age of faith, the age of belief, then our time is the age of unbelief, the age of atheism.

如果中世紀是信仰的時代，那麼我們的時代就是不信的時代、無神論的時代。

There are millions today who say: "I can only believe in something I can see or touch -- and I won't believe in something like 'God' or 'spirit' which can't be seen or touched." You might think that this is the result of the progress of science -- you might think that science has taught us to trust only our senses. But it is not so at all.

今天有數百萬人說：「我只能相信我看得見、摸得著的東西——我不會相信像『上帝』或『靈性』這樣看不見、摸不著的東西。」你可能會認為這是科學進步的結果——你可能認為科學教會了我們只信任自己的感官。但事實根本不是如此。

What science has shown us is that we cannot trust our senses at all. There are the infrared and ultraviolet rays of the sunlight, which our eyes cannot see -- but they exist, and are real just the same. There is magnetism: a piece of magnetic iron looks and feels just the same as a piece of ordinary iron. We have no sense organ for magnetic forces -- but magnetism exists, and is real, just the same. There are electromagnetic waves and radiations pouring in from the cosmos, from space, which we are just beginning to investigate -- there are forces which, a few decades ago, one could not even guess at, but our senses can tell us nothing about them.

科學向我們展示的是，我們根本不能信任自己的感官。有紅外線和紫外線——我們的眼睛看不到它們——但它們存在，同樣真實。還有磁力：一塊磁鐵看起來和摸起來與一塊普通鐵完全一樣。我們沒有感知磁力的感官——但磁力存在，同樣真實。還有從宇宙、從太空傾瀉而來的電磁波和輻射，我們才剛剛開始研究——有些力量在幾十年前人們甚至無法猜想，而我們的感官對它們一無所知。

The lesson of science is that our senses, our eyes and ears, are not fit to judge what exists and what does not exist, what is real and what is not real.

科學的教訓是：我們的感官、我們的眼睛和耳朵，不足以判斷什麼存在、什麼不存在，什麼是真實的、什麼不是真實的。

What are the forces used in hypnosis? We have no instrument to measure the forces which produce hypnosis, we have no sense organ to show us what they look like, but they work, they are real, just the same.

催眠中使用的是什麼力量？我們沒有任何儀器能測量產生催眠效果的力量，我們沒有感官能告訴我們它們是什麼模樣，但它們起作用，它們同樣是真實的。

And so, when people say: "I don't believe in God, I don't believe in a spiritual world, because I can't see them," it is not because of science, but for a very different reason. The reasons why people become atheists, materialists, are not in the world around us, not in the science which explores this world, but in the human soul, in the human mind.

所以，當人們說「我不相信上帝，我不相信靈性世界，因為我看不見它們」時，這不是因為科學，而是出於一個完全不同的原因。人們成為無神論者、唯物主義者的原因，不在我們周圍的世界中，不在探索這個世界的科學中，而是在人的靈魂中、在人的心智中。

One of the most common assumptions is that the human mind remains always the same. We assume that, although a caveman or a knight of the Middle Ages was not quite so clever as we are, they did not know as much as we know today, nevertheless the human mind was always the same, and worked in the same way. But this very common assumption is not at all borne out by facts. One has to read the works of anthropologists, and the works of historians who study the mentality of the past, to come to the true picture. And the true picture shows enormous changes, even over a few centuries.

最常見的假設之一是，人的心智始終保持不變。我們假設，雖然穴居人或中世紀騎士不如我們聰明，他們的知識不如我們今天多，但人的心智始終是相同的，以相同的方式運作。但這個非常普遍的假設完全不被事實所支持。人們必須閱讀人類學家的著作，以及研究過去心態的歷史學家的著作，才能得到真實的圖景。而真實的圖景顯示了巨大的變化，即使只隔幾個世紀。

Take the Middle Ages -- the age of faith. If you read and study any books written in the eleventh, twelfth and thirteenth centuries, then you realise that certain words had a different meaning, and even a different effect, in those days from what they have today.

以中世紀——信仰的時代——為例。如果你閱讀和研究任何十一、十二和十三世紀寫的書，你就會意識到，某些詞語在那個時代有著不同的含義，甚至不同的效果，與今天截然不同。

When someone of the Middle Ages used or heard a word like "God" or "Jesus," these words filled the soul with a warm, living glow, with an inner radiance. Just as, for us, the word "death" rouses automatically a certain feeling of dread -- or, to take a very concrete example, the words "lemon juice" can bring the taste of lemon juice to your tongue -- so for someone of the Middle Ages words like "God" or "Christ" had a certain effect, a warm inner glow -- a feeling of comfort and security.

當一個中世紀的人使用或聽到像「上帝」或「耶穌」這樣的詞語時，這些詞語會讓靈魂充滿一種溫暖的、活生生的光輝，一種內在的光芒。就像對我們來說，「死亡」這個詞會自動喚起某種恐懼感——或者，舉一個非常具體的例子，「檸檬汁」這幾個字能讓你舌頭上泛起檸檬汁的味道——同樣地，對中世紀的人來說，「上帝」或「基督」這樣的詞語有一種特定的效果，一種溫暖的內在光輝——一種安慰和安全的感覺。

Young children, who are a bit like human beings of the past, still often have such a feeling -- this feeling of warmth and comfort when they hear or speak the name of God.

幼小的孩子——在某種程度上像是過去的人類——仍然常常有這樣的感覺——當他們聽到或說出上帝的名字時，這種溫暖與安慰的感覺。

In the Middle Ages this feeling was common, it was natural, and this is why the Middle Ages were the age of faith -- because the words "God" and "Christ" still had this effect, a kind of warmth and comfort.

在中世紀，這種感覺是普遍的、自然的，這就是為什麼中世紀是信仰的時代——因為「上帝」和「基督」這些詞語仍然有這種效果，一種溫暖與安慰。

But, if you go then to later centuries and read books, even religious books, written by devout, pious people, you can see -- it is quite obvious -- that the words no longer have the same effect. The warmth, the glow, has become less and less.

但是，如果你接著翻到後來幾個世紀，閱讀那些書籍——即使是虔誠、敬虔的人寫的宗教書籍——你可以看到，非常明顯的是，那些詞語不再有同樣的效果。那份溫暖、那份光輝，變得越來越微弱。

And in our time, the glow has gone. The words "God" or "spirit" have become like empty shells. For a small number of people, there is still a little glow in these words -- and they are the few sincerely religious people who still exist today.

而在我們的時代，那份光輝已經消失了。「上帝」或「靈性」這些詞語變得像空殼一樣。對少數人來說，這些詞語中還有一點微弱的光——他們是今天仍然存在的少數真誠的宗教信仰者。

But for the great majority of present-day humanity, these words have no longer any effect, or any meaning. Some are afraid of admitting it -- they pretend that there is something in the words -- and they are the people who cling to some religious practice, as a custom, as a habit, but it is a pretence.

但對於當今人類的絕大多數來說，這些詞語不再有任何效果，也沒有任何意義。有些人害怕承認這一點——他們假裝這些詞語中有某些東西——他們就是那些把某種宗教實踐當作習俗、當作習慣而緊抓不放的人，但那不過是假裝。

A great number of people, however, cannot see any point in pretending -- they cannot see any point in putting up a show, they are content to live without religion, and without God.

然而，大量的人看不出假裝有什麼意義——他們看不出做戲有什麼意義，他們滿足於不需要宗教、不需要上帝地活著。

These are the three possibilities -- the few who can still feel something in the word "God" and so find satisfaction in their religion; those who cannot feel anything, but pretend they can; and those for whom the words "God", "Christ", "Spirit" have no meaning, and who say so and live without religion.

這就是三種可能——少數人仍能在「上帝」這個詞中感受到某些東西，因而在宗教中找到滿足；那些什麼也感受不到卻假裝能感受到的人；以及那些覺得「上帝」、「基督」、「靈性」這些詞毫無意義，並且坦然說出來、不要宗教而活的人。

But there is still another alternative. There are people for whom the word "God" has no longer any meaning -- it is as if there is an emptiness, a vacant place in the soul where there used to be a radiance, a glow. But these people feel the necessity to seek for a new meaning of the words "God", "Christ", "Spirit".

但還有另一種選擇。有些人覺得「上帝」這個詞不再有任何意義——彷彿靈魂中曾經有光芒、有光輝的地方，現在只剩下一片空虛，一個空位。但這些人感到有必要去尋找「上帝」、「基督」、「靈性」這些詞的新意義。

The old meaning, the old light and glow, have gone from the words -- there is only emptiness and darkness left -- but there is perhaps a new meaning to be found, somehow, somewhere.

舊的意義、舊的光明和光輝，已經從這些詞語中消失——只剩下空虛和黑暗——但也許有一個新的意義等待被發現，在某處，以某種方式。

It might be a long search to find the new meaning -- it may be a fruitless search, it may be a search in the wrong places, it may be a search for something which cannot be found. But there are people who search for this new meaning -- and this is the modern search for the holy Grail.

尋找新的意義也許是一場漫長的追尋——也許是一場徒勞的追尋，也許是在錯誤的地方尋找，也許是在尋找一些永遠找不到的東西。但確實有人在尋找這個新的意義——而這就是現代的聖杯追尋。

So, as a person of this present age, you have really four alternatives. You either still feel some reality in the name of God, and so can pray and worship in sincerity. Or you do not feel anything, but find it to your advantage to make a show of religion. Or you do not pretend, and do without religion. Or you become a seeker, a person in search of a new meaning of the words, a modern Parsifal in search of the Grail.

所以，作為這個時代的人，你真的有四種選擇。你要麼仍然在上帝的名字中感受到某種真實，因而能真誠地祈禱和崇拜。要麼你什麼也感受不到，但覺得做一場宗教的表演對你有利。要麼你不假裝，不要宗教而活。要麼你成為一個追尋者，一個尋找這些詞語的新意義的人，一個現代的帕西法爾，在追尋聖杯。

These are the four roads before you, and every person in our time has to take one of these four roads. But whatever road you take, it is your own choice.

這就是擺在你面前的四條路，我們這個時代的每個人都必須走其中一條。但無論你走哪一條路，那都是你自己的選擇。

No one else can take the decision for you -- no one else has the power to make you go on one road or the other. It is your own personal decision.

沒有其他人能替你做這個決定——沒有其他人有權力讓你走這條路或那條路。這是你個人的決定。

But it was not like that in the past. In the past, when the names of "God" or "Christ" still had, one could say, an almost magical power, this was not anybody's personal choice. It was a kind of instinct. You know that there are kinds of birds which migrate; when autumn comes, they fly to a warmer climate, to the south, to Africa. They do not fly from choice, they do not fly south because they have, each one, decided that this is the best thing to do when winter comes. A swallow does not have to think and to decide about it -- the force of instinct compels the swallow to fly south, and the force of instinct guides it to Africa.

但過去並非如此。在過去，當「上帝」或「基督」的名字還擁有——可以這麼說——幾乎是魔力般的力量時，這不是任何人的個人選擇。那是一種本能。你知道有些鳥類會遷徙；當秋天來臨時，它們飛向更溫暖的氣候，飛向南方，飛向非洲。它們不是出於選擇而飛，它們飛向南方不是因為每一隻都做出了「冬天來了最好這樣做」的決定。一隻燕子不需要去思考和決定這件事——本能的力量迫使燕子飛向南方，本能的力量引導它去非洲。

As far as religion is concerned, someone of the Middle Ages was no more their own master than the swallow is. As the swallow inherits the instinct which drives it south, so someone of the Middle Ages inherited the instinct which made them feel warmth and comfort in the name of God. It was in their blood -- it was something inherited from their fathers.

就宗教而言，中世紀的人並不比燕子更能主宰自己。正如燕子繼承了驅使它飛往南方的本能，中世紀的人也繼承了讓他們在上帝的名字中感受到溫暖和安慰的本能。那是在他們的血液中——是從他們的祖先那裡繼承的。

It was only natural for these people to say "God of my fathers" -- it was a correct description, because the feeling for God was an inherited instinct, something in the blood, inherited from their fathers and forefathers.

這些人說「我祖先的上帝」是很自然的——那是一個準確的描述，因為對上帝的感受是一種遺傳的本能，是血液中的某種東西，從他們的父親和祖先那裡繼承而來的。

But this instinct died, just as many other instincts which people used to have died in the course of history. The instinct that was inherited, that was in the blood -- that instinct died, and that is why the glow disappeared and the age of faith disappeared.

但這種本能死去了，正如人們曾經擁有的許多其他本能在歷史進程中死去一樣。那種遺傳的、存在於血液中的本能——那種本能死去了，這就是為什麼光輝消失了，信仰的時代消失了。

You think, perhaps, that people took up science from the fourteenth/fifteenth century onwards because they became suddenly clever -- they became suddenly smarter than the people who lived a few centuries earlier. No -- they took to science because the instinct which had guided and directed people as it guides and directs the swallows, this instinct had died out.

你也許會想，人們從十四、十五世紀開始投入科學，是因為他們突然變聰明了——突然比幾個世紀前的人更精明了。不是的——他們投入科學是因為那個像引導和指揮燕子一樣引導和指揮人們的本能，已經死去了。

If the swallows lost their instinct, they would have to make maps and use a compass to find their way to Africa -- they would have to explore and discover. It does not happen to the swallows, but it did happen to human beings. Our instincts died and, bewildered in a strange world, we began to explore.

如果燕子失去了本能，它們就必須製作地圖、使用指南針來找到去非洲的路——它們就必須去探索和發現。這不會發生在燕子身上，但它確實發生在了人類身上。我們的本能死去了，在一個陌生的世界中茫然無措，我們開始了探索。

The individual of the Middle Ages found certainty and comfort in God by an instinct, not out of any personal choice.

中世紀的個人是憑著本能——而非出於任何個人選擇——在上帝之中找到確定感和安慰的。

But in us, today, that instinct is dead. And whatever road we take, whichever of the four roads I have mentioned we choose, it is a personal choice -- it is a free choice.

但在我們今天的人身上，那種本能已經死去了。無論我們走哪條路，無論我們選擇我提到的四條路中的哪一條，那都是一個個人的選擇——一個自由的選擇。

We are deprived of the guidance of instinct, we are left in the dark -- but we have gained something: we have gained freedom, the freedom to choose the road on which we want to travel.

我們被剝奪了本能的指引，我們被留在黑暗中——但我們獲得了某些東西：我們獲得了自由，選擇我們想走的路的自由。

We could not say: "God of our fathers," as people used to say. We would have to say "God of freedom," God who leaves us free to seek Him or to forget Him.

我們不能像人們過去那樣說「我們祖先的上帝」。我們得說「自由的上帝」——那位讓我們自由地去尋找他或遺忘他的上帝。

But the price of this freedom is that the instinct died, and that we are left in darkness.

但這份自由的代價是：本能死去了，而我們被留在了黑暗之中。

And now think of that dream picture which Parsifal saw when he was on his fruitless quest and his soul turned away from religion. In this dream picture he saw a chapel, which is a religious building. In the chapel there was the corpse of a knight. This dead knight is a symbolic picture of that dead instinct that used to guide people.

現在想想帕西法爾在徒勞的追尋中、當他的靈魂背離宗教時所看到的那個夢中景象。在這個夢境中，他看見一座禮拜堂，那是一座宗教建築。禮拜堂中有一位騎士的屍體。這位死去的騎士是那個曾經引導人們的已死本能的象徵圖景。

There was a solitary burning candle, which is the last glow of that inner light, that inner glow people used to experience. A hand extinguishes the candle, and all is in darkness. This darkness is a picture of the soul once the guidance of instinct and the warm comforting glow of faith have gone.

有一支孤獨燃燒的蠟燭，那是人們曾經體驗的內在之光、內在光輝的最後一絲微光。一隻手熄滅了蠟燭，一切陷入黑暗。這黑暗是一幅靈魂的圖景——當本能的指引和信仰溫暖安慰的光輝消失之後的靈魂。

The dream is really a picture of Parsifal's own soul -- the dream describes in a picture what actually happens -- that he turns away from religion. Darkness has come to Parsifal -- as it has come for us.

這個夢實際上是帕西法爾自己靈魂的圖景——這個夢用一幅畫面描述了實際發生的事情——他背離了宗教。黑暗降臨在帕西法爾身上——正如它降臨在我們身上一樣。

The only thing that remains to Parsifal is also what remains for us: that in the darkness, out of our own choice, out of freedom, we seek for a new meaning of God, that we seek for the Grail.

帕西法爾僅存的東西也是我們僅存的：在黑暗中，出於我們自己的選擇，出於自由，我們尋找上帝的新意義，我們追尋聖杯。

But in that darkness, which leaves us free to choose, which leaves us free to seek the Grail or not to seek it, in that darkness something does take place, something happens to Parsifal on his lonely and seemingly hopeless quest.

但在那黑暗中——那讓我們自由地選擇、自由地去追尋聖杯或不去追尋的黑暗中——確實有某些事情發生了，在帕西法爾孤獨而看似無望的追尋中，有某些事情發生在他身上。

Do you get the impression that Parsifal is enjoying this fruitless quest for the Grail? No -- he suffers. He is sad, unhappy -- a man weighed down by grief. He does not like suffering -- no human being does. But do you think that a person who never suffered -- if there is such a person -- could feel pity or compassion for another human being? We can only sympathise, we can only feel for someone else's suffering because we have suffered ourselves.

你是否覺得帕西法爾在享受這場徒勞的聖杯追尋？不——他在受苦。他悲傷、不快樂——一個被悲痛壓垮的人。他不喜歡受苦——沒有人喜歡。但你覺得一個從未受過苦的人——如果有這樣的人的話——能對另一個人感到憐憫或同情嗎？我們之所以能同情、能為他人的苦難而感同身受，只因為我們自己也曾受過苦。

Of course, we can always feel a bit sorry for somebody who is ill or in trouble, just because we have known at least a little suffering ourselves. But true, deep sympathy, a true compassion (which means literally to share the suffering of others) -- true compassion is only born out of our own deep and real suffering.

當然，我們總是能為生病或遇到麻煩的人感到一點惋惜，只因我們自己至少也體驗過一些苦難。但真正的、深切的同情，真正的悲憫（compassion，字面意思是分擔他人的苦難）——真正的悲憫只能從我們自己深刻而真實的苦難中誕生。

The thick-skinned fellow whom nothing can hurt is not likely to take much notice of other people's distress.

那個什麼都傷不到的厚臉皮的人，不太可能會去注意別人的困境。

Parsifal was thick-skinned enough to leave his mother without even noticing that she suffered. He was thick-skinned enough to kill the red knight and have no other thought but to get hold of his armour. There was very little compassion in Parsifal.

帕西法爾曾經粗心到離開母親時甚至沒有注意到她在受苦。他粗心到殺了紅騎士卻只想著拿到他的盔甲。帕西法爾身上幾乎沒有什麼悲憫之心。

Imagine for a moment he had come to the Fisher King and had asked: "What is wrong with you?" He would have asked out of curiosity, but not out of compassion. What he needed to learn was compassion, and the only way to learn it was through suffering himself. He had to suffer.

想像一下，如果他當時來到漁王面前問了：「你怎麼了？」他會是出於好奇而問，而非出於悲憫。他需要學會的是悲憫，而學會悲憫的唯一途徑是自己經歷苦難。他必須受苦。

And that inner darkness, being deprived of religion, living without any feeling for God -- that was part of the suffering.

而那內在的黑暗——被剝奪了宗教、對上帝毫無感覺地活著——那是苦難的一部分。

Think of the Middle Ages, when people still had the instinct and felt warmth and comfort at the name of God. It was not a time of much pity and compassion. A saint like Francis of Assisi could feel compassion -- he was a man who had imposed suffering on himself, by denying himself all the things that others lived for: wealth, honours, good food. But Francis was an exception. The other people of his time, with all their great faith and ardent religion, were pitiless to the point of cruelty.

想想中世紀，當人們仍然擁有那種本能，在上帝的名字中感受到溫暖和安慰。那不是一個充滿憐憫和悲憫的時代。像亞西西的聖方濟（Francis of Assisi）這樣的聖人能感受到悲憫——他是一個將苦難加諸於自身的人，他拒絕了別人所追求的一切：財富、榮譽、美食。但方濟是個例外。他那個時代的其他人，儘管有著偉大的信仰和熱切的宗教，卻殘忍到毫無憐憫的地步。
