In our time -- godless, without religion as it is -- there is far more compassion, a far greater feeling for the needs and suffering of other people. We have not only gained freedom, but compassion, since the old instincts died and disappeared.

在我們的時代——如此不信神、沒有宗教——卻有著遠為更多的悲憫，對他人的需要和苦難有著遠為更深的感受。自從那些古老的本能死去和消失以來，我們不僅獲得了自由，還獲得了悲憫。

And such a feeling as pity or compassion is something spiritual in the world. If the materialists, the atheists, are right -- if there is no God, if there is neither soul nor spirit in a person -- then we would be no more than machines, complicated machines. Why should one machine feel for another machine? Machines do not feel -- but we do. And any action performed out of pity, mercy, compassion, is proof of our spiritual nature -- and of God, who is pure compassion and love.

而像憐憫或悲憫這樣的感受，是這個世界中屬靈的東西。如果唯物主義者、無神論者是對的——如果沒有上帝，如果人身上既沒有靈魂也沒有靈性——那我們不過是機器，複雜的機器。一台機器為什麼要為另一台機器而感動？機器沒有感受——但我們有。而任何出於憐憫、慈悲、悲憫的行動，都是我們靈性本質的證明——也是上帝的證明，因為上帝就是純粹的悲憫和愛。

I have known people who were convinced atheists, materialists, but they were shining examples of true kindness and active compassion. But no matter how they denied God -- in their feelings, in their actions they proved and asserted the spirit in the human being and the spirit of God.

我認識一些堅定的無神論者、唯物主義者，但他們是真正善良與積極悲憫的光輝典範。但無論他們如何否認上帝——在他們的感受中、在他們的行動中，他們證明並肯定了人之中的靈性和上帝的靈性。

In the darkness, where the old instinct lies dead, there grows the freedom of choice -- and there grows compassion, the new instinct that leads to a new meaning of God: not the God of the fathers, but the God of freedom, the God of mercy and compassion, the God of love.

在黑暗中，在舊本能已死之處，生長出選擇的自由——也生長出悲憫，那個引向上帝的新意義的新本能：不是祖先的上帝，而是自由的上帝，慈悲與悲憫的上帝，愛的上帝。

Parsifal had to live through the darkness, to seek God in freedom. He had to suffer -- to learn compassion. And then he could, on a Good Friday, the day on which Christ suffered on the cross, meet the hermit who could give him new hope and an understanding that his suffering was necessary and would, in the end, lead to the Grail.

帕西法爾必須活過那黑暗，在自由中尋找上帝。他必須受苦——去學會悲憫。然後他才能在一個耶穌受難日——基督在十字架上受苦的日子——遇到那位隱士，那位能給予他新希望的人，讓他理解他的苦難是必要的，最終會引向聖杯。

## 14.The Conclusion of the Quest 追尋的終章


At this stage of the story, the poet Wolfram introduces a new figure called Feirefiz. And in Wolfram's thirteenth-century story this child, Feirefiz, the son of a white father and a black mother, had skin that was both black and white. Keep in mind when you hear this part of the story that, as insensitive as this might sound in modern times, the poet wants to convey something -- there is a psychological meaning to this.

在故事的這個階段，詩人沃夫蘭引入了一個新角色，名叫費雷菲茲（Feirefiz）。在沃夫蘭這個十三世紀的故事中，這個孩子費雷菲茲——一個白人父親和黑人母親的兒子——擁有黑白相間的皮膚。請記住，當你聽到故事的這個部分時，儘管在現代聽起來可能不夠敏感，但詩人想要傳達某些東西——其中有一個心理層面的含義。

Moreover, having decided that he needs in his story such a person, Wolfram also wanted to make this man a brother -- or at least a half-brother -- of Parsifal, and had to invent a story to make this possible. And in Wolfram's story this child that may seem odd or nonsensical; but I think we know already that Wolfram is not a poet who lets his fantasy roam without reason, and that there is a meaning, a symbolic meaning in his fantastic inventions. The new figure now brought into the story is a man who is black and white, like a magpie.

此外，既然他決定故事中需要這樣一個人物，沃夫蘭還想讓此人成為帕西法爾的兄弟——或至少是同父異母的兄弟——因此不得不編造一個故事來實現這一點。這看起來也許奇怪甚至荒謬；但我想我們已經知道，沃夫蘭不是一個讓幻想毫無理由地馳騁的詩人，他那些奇幻的創造背後有著深意——象徵性的深意。現在被帶入故事的新角色，是一個黑白相間的人，像一隻喜鵲。

You remember that Parsifal was the son of Herzeloyde, who brought him up in the forest, and of a king, Gamuret, who was killed in battle when Parsifal was still a little child.

你記得帕西法爾是赫策洛伊德的兒子，母親在森林中將他撫養長大；他的父親是國王嘎穆雷特（Gamuret），在帕西法爾還是小孩子時就戰死了。

Now this father of Parsifal, Gamuret, was in his younger days himself a knight who went on quests in search of adventure. And Gamuret, in his youth, roamed very far indeed. He travelled to lands far from Europe, and came to a land where the people had dark skin and were not Christians. These 'Moors', as Europeans called them, were ruled by a queen who was very beautiful, and the young Gamuret fell in love with her. He fought for her against her enemies and, in the end, he married her and a son was born who was called Feirefiz.

這位帕西法爾的父親嘎穆雷特，在年輕時也是一位四處冒險的騎士。年輕的嘎穆雷特確實遊歷得很遠。他旅行到遠離歐洲的國度，來到一個人們膚色深暗且非基督徒的地方。這些被歐洲人稱為「摩爾人」（Moors）的人由一位非常美麗的女王統治，年輕的嘎穆雷特愛上了她。他為她與敵人作戰，最終他娶了她，生了一個兒子，取名為費雷菲茲。

Within a year of his birth, his mother, the queen, died. Gamuret, the father, left the child and the whole kingdom in the trust of the queen's brother, and set out again on further adventures. He returned to Europe, to Christian lands, where he met Herzeloyde and married again. As we know, he was later killed in battle.

費雷菲茲出生不到一年，他的母親——女王——就去世了。父親嘎穆雷特將孩子和整個王國託付給女王的兄弟，再次啟程踏上新的冒險。他回到歐洲、回到基督教的土地，在那裡遇到了赫策洛伊德，並再次結婚。如我們所知，他後來戰死沙場。

And so, while Parsifal grew up in the forest, there grew up in a far-away land his half-brother, Feirefiz.

於是，當帕西法爾在森林中成長時，在一個遙遠的國度，他的同父異母的兄弟費雷菲茲也在成長。

Feirefiz was brought up as a pagan by his uncle, and in time he became king of this land of Moors. But the blood of his father was strong in Feirefiz -- he, too, loved adventure and knightly combat, and so he left his kingdom and went on a quest in search of danger and fights.

費雷菲茲由叔叔撫養長大，信奉異教，最終他成為了這個摩爾人國度的國王。但他父親的血液在費雷菲茲體內十分強烈——他也熱愛冒險和騎士的戰鬥，因此他離開了自己的王國，踏上了尋找危險和戰鬥的旅程。

He was travelling one day through a forest when he saw, coming from the opposite direction, a knight in splendid red armour. This red knight was, of course, Parsifal. For Feirefiz such a meeting could only have one purpose -- a fight -- and he called out immediately a challenge. For Parsifal it was also a matter of course to accept the challenge.

一天，他穿越一片森林時，看見從對面方向走來一位穿著華麗紅色盔甲的騎士。這位紅騎士自然就是帕西法爾。對費雷菲茲而言，這樣的相遇只有一個目的——戰鬥——他立刻喊出了挑戰。對帕西法爾來說，接受挑戰也是理所當然的事。

And so they both lowered their spears and galloped against each other. But when the spears crashed on the shields, neither of them could unseat the other. Both recognised that, for a combat with spears, they were too evenly matched. They threw the spears down and drew their swords. Parsifal used the sword he had once received from the Fisher King, the sword that would never break except in one danger -- a danger only known to the sword's maker.

於是他們雙雙放低長矛，策馬向對方衝去。但當長矛撞擊在盾牌上時，誰也無法將對方擊下馬來。兩人都意識到，在長矛格鬥中，他們實力太過旗鼓相當。他們扔下長矛，拔出了劍。帕西法爾用的是他從漁王那裡得到的那把劍——那把除了一種危險外永遠不會斷裂的劍——而那個危險只有劍的鍛造者才知道。

The two knights struck at each other with their swords, they hacked at each other's shields, they hit with such force that fiery sparks flew from helmet and armour -- but even the most forceful blows brought no decision.

兩位騎士用劍互相砍擊，他們劈砍彼此的盾牌，每一擊都力道巨大，火花從頭盔和盔甲上飛濺——但即使是最猛烈的攻擊也無法分出勝負。

They had already been fighting for a whole hour -- the forest ringing with the clash of their weapons -- and they were both near the end of their great strength, when Parsifal summoned all the strength left to him and struck a mighty blow at his opponent's helmet -- and with this blow, his sword broke!

他們已經戰鬥了整整一個小時——森林中迴盪著武器碰撞的聲響——兩人都快要耗盡全力，這時帕西法爾集中了僅存的所有力量，向對手的頭盔猛烈揮出一劍——隨著這一擊，他的劍斷了！

With the sword of the Fisher King broken, Parsifal was weaponless, and at the other man's mercy. But the other knight did not strike him down, he called out: "There is no honour in winning when the opponent cannot fight back! Let us call a truce and rest."

漁王之劍斷裂後，帕西法爾手無寸鐵，任由對方處置。但那位騎士並沒有將他擊倒，他喊道：「在對手無法還擊時獲勝是沒有榮譽的！讓我們休戰休息吧。」

And so they dismounted, and stretched their tired limbs on the soft moss of the forest. They took off their helmets, and Parsifal was surprised to see the face of the other knight, which was unlike any he had seen before. But the sight also brought back memories that his mother had once told him of his father's first marriage, and the child born from that marriage.

於是他們下了馬，在森林柔軟的苔蘚上伸展疲憊的四肢。他們摘下頭盔，帕西法爾驚訝地看到對方騎士的面孔，那是他從未見過的。但這景象也喚起了記憶——母親曾告訴他父親的第一次婚姻，以及那次婚姻中出生的孩子。

He asked: "What is your name?" And the other answered: "I am Feirefiz, the son of Gamuret."

他問道：「你叫什麼名字？」對方回答：「我是費雷菲茲，嘎穆雷特的兒子。」

"I, too, am a son of Gamuret," said Parsifal, "and now I know why my sword would not serve me, but broke -- it would not serve me against my brother."

「我也是嘎穆雷特的兒子，」帕西法爾說，「現在我知道為什麼我的劍不願為我效力而斷裂了——它不願讓我用來對付自己的兄弟。」

The two knights who had just fought each other so grimly now embraced each other, deeply moved that they had each found a brother.

剛才還在殊死搏鬥的兩位騎士現在擁抱在一起，為各自找到了一個兄弟而深深感動。

And when Feirefiz heard of Parsifal's quest for the Holy Grail, he swore that he would not leave his new-found brother, but join him in the quest, no matter how long it would take.

當費雷菲茲聽聞帕西法爾追尋聖杯的故事後，他發誓不會離開他新找到的兄弟，而要與他一同追尋，無論需要多長時間。

So Parsifal was no longer alone in his quest -- he now had a companion, his brother, Feirefiz.

帕西法爾在追尋中不再孤單——他現在有了一個同伴，他的兄弟費雷菲茲。

And as the two now continued the journey, they had another meeting, which made Parsifal feel that his quest was no longer so hopeless as he had once thought. They rode on through the forest and came upon a hut. Parsifal dismounted, and entered the hut -- and in it he found his cousin, Sigune, with the coffin of her dead bridegroom.

當兩人繼續旅程時，他們又有了一次相遇，這讓帕西法爾覺得他的追尋不再像他曾經認為的那樣絕望。他們繼續騎行穿過森林，發現了一間小屋。帕西法爾下馬，走進小屋——在裡面他發現了他的表妹席古涅（Sigune），以及她已故未婚夫的棺木。

But this time Sigune did not drive him away and call him "accursed", as she had on their last meeting. She looked aged and haggard -- but she spoke in a mild voice, when she said: "Long has been your quest, Parsifal -- and you have suffered, as I have suffered since death took the man who was to become my husband. Here on earth I could not become his wife -- but I feel bound to him by my love, which death cannot end. And now I know that my grief will not last much longer. Soon my life will come to its end, and I shall be united with him who has gone from earth before me. But, as my own suffering will soon be ending, so will yours, Parsifal. Go in peace -- we both shall find what we seek!"

但這次席古涅沒有像上次見面那樣趕他走、叫他「受詛咒的人」。她看起來蒼老而憔悴——但她以溫和的聲音說：「你的追尋已經很漫長了，帕西法爾——你受了苦，就像自從死亡帶走了那個本該成為我丈夫的人之後我也在受苦。在這世上我無法成為他的妻子——但我感到我的愛把我與他聯繫在一起，死亡無法將這份愛終結。如今我知道我的悲傷不會再持續太久了。很快我的生命將走到盡頭，我將與先我而去的他團聚。但正如我自己的苦難即將結束一樣，你的也是，帕西法爾。安心上路吧——我們都將找到我們所尋找的！」

There was not despair, but hope and joy in Sigune's voice, and when Parsifal left her, he felt a deep confidence filling his heart as he continued the journey with his brother.

席古涅的聲音中沒有絕望，而是希望和喜悅。當帕西法爾離開她時，他感到一種深深的信心充滿了他的心，繼續與兄弟一起踏上旅程。

The next stage of this journey brought them to King Arthur and his knights. They came upon a large camp with men-at-arms and knights, for King Arthur had just ended a great war against a powerful enemy. Gawain, the loyal friend of Parsifal, had been the hero of this war, and it was Gawain who welcomed Parsifal and Feirefiz and presented them to King Arthur.

旅程的下一段把他們帶到了亞瑟王（King Arthur）和他的騎士們面前。他們來到一個大營地，裡面有武士和騎士，因為亞瑟王剛剛結束了一場對抗強敵的大戰。帕西法爾的忠實朋友高文（Gawain）是這場戰爭中的英雄，正是高文歡迎了帕西法爾和費雷菲茲，並將他們引見給亞瑟王。

Although Parsifal had still not found the Grail, he could accept a day's hospitality from the King of Britain, and so he found himself sitting at table with the knights of King Arthur.

儘管帕西法爾仍未找到聖杯，他可以接受不列顛國王一天的款待，於是他發現自己與亞瑟王的騎士們同席而坐。

And as they were sitting together, there came once again a visitor -- a visitor whom they all recognised from her rich garments, and from the jewels on her headband and dress -- it was Cundry, the messenger of the Grail.

正當他們坐在一起時，又來了一位訪客——一位他們都從她華麗的衣裳和頭飾、裙子上的珠寶認出的訪客——那是聖杯的使者坤德里（Cundry）。

Once again, silence fell at her coming -- and in every mind there was the question of what ill, what curse, her coming might portend. But they all noticed that she wore now the Grail's coat of arms -- the sign of a white dove.

又一次，她的到來使全場沉默——每個人心中都在想，她的到來會帶來什麼不幸、什麼詛咒。但他們都注意到，她現在身上佩戴著聖杯的紋章——白鴿的標誌。

Cundry gave a courteous greeting to King Arthur -- then she approached Parsifal ... and fell to her knees before him. And when she looked up, her sharp and ugly features were transformed by an expression of great joy.

坤德里對亞瑟王致以恭敬的問候——然後她走向帕西法爾……跪倒在他面前。當她抬起頭時，她尖銳而醜陋的面容被一種巨大喜悅的表情所轉化。

She said: "Parsifal, your quest is ended. The secrets of the Grail are written in the stars. As the planets move and change in their prescribed paths, they proclaim in heavenly writing the destiny of men and women here on earth. And the heavenly writing now speaks that your hour of destiny has come.

她說：「帕西法爾，你的追尋結束了。聖杯的祕密書寫在星辰之中。行星沿著既定的軌道運行和變化，它們以天上的文字宣告著世間男女的命運。而天上的文字現在宣告，你命運的時刻已經到來。

"You are called to the Grail, and you are called to the highest honour the Grail can give, for your name has appeared on the holy chalice. Your name, Parsifal -- we saw it written as the name of the new King of the Grail.

「你被聖杯召喚了，你被召喚去接受聖杯所能給予的最高榮耀，因為你的名字已經出現在聖杯上。你的名字，帕西法爾——我們看見它被寫下，作為新的聖杯之王的名字。

"He who was king before, your uncle, Anfortas -- the Fisher King -- now waits for you to come and ask the question that will end his pain, and he will then serve you gladly and faithfully as his lord.

「在你之前的國王，你的叔叔安佛塔斯——漁王——如今等待著你來問那個將終結他痛苦的問題，然後他將歡喜而忠誠地以你為主來侍奉你。

"And by the command written on the Grail, your wife, Condwiramur, has been brought to Montsalvage, to the castle of the Grail, to be at your side as queen. She, too, is now waiting for you there.

「根據聖杯上寫下的命令，你的妻子康薇拉慕（Condwiramur）已被帶到蒙薩爾瓦奇，聖杯的城堡，在你身邊作為王后。她此刻也在那裡等著你。

"We also saw it written that you are not to come alone. Your brother Feirefiz is to come with you to Montsalvage.

「我們還看見上面寫著，你不可以獨自前來。你的兄弟費雷菲茲要和你一起到蒙薩爾瓦奇。

"And I, who once brought you ill tidings and sorrow, I am to lead you and your companion to that place which no one can find unless he is called.

「而我，曾經給你帶來壞消息和悲傷的人，我將帶領你和你的同伴前往那個除非被召喚否則無人能找到的地方。

"So speaks the writing -- so speak the stars -- so speaks the Grail!"

「文字如此說——星辰如此說——聖杯如此說！」

What Parsifal felt at this message was not pride, it was not even joy, but a deep humility. As a young lad he had been ambitious and eager, he had felt pride and arrogance -- but that was not the man he was now. He had suffered and had come to the end of his quest.

帕西法爾聽到這個消息時的感受不是驕傲，甚至不是喜悅，而是深深的謙卑。作為一個年輕小夥子，他曾經雄心勃勃、急切渴望，他曾感到驕傲和傲慢——但那不是他現在的樣子了。他經歷了苦難，走到了追尋的盡頭。

Once again Parsifal had to leave King Arthur and his knights -- he was not meant to be one of them. But this leave-taking was not like the others before -- this time he was leaving to receive the highest honour: the kingship of the Grail. And so the lady who had, by her laughter, prophesied the highest honour for him, had been right.

帕西法爾又一次不得不離開亞瑟王和他的騎士們——他註定不是他們中的一員。但這次的離別與之前的不同——這一次他是去接受最高的榮耀：聖杯的王位。因此那位曾以她的歡笑預言他將獲得最高榮耀的女士，說得沒錯。

And so Parsifal saw again that lake where he had encountered the Fisher King without knowing he was his uncle. He saw again the winding, uphill path -- but this time a band of knights came to meet him and his companions, and to form a guard of honour for the chosen one, the coming guardian of the Grail.

帕西法爾再次看到了那片湖泊——他曾在那裡遇見漁王卻不知道對方是他的叔叔。他再次看到了那條蜿蜒上行的小路——但這一次有一隊騎士前來迎接他和他的同伴，為被選中的人、即將到來的聖杯守護者組成了儀仗隊。

And when they entered the castle Montsalvage, there was no longer sadness on any face which welcomed them -- there was joy and happiness.

當他們進入蒙薩爾瓦奇城堡時，每一張迎接他們的臉上不再有悲傷——只有歡喜和幸福。

Parsifal and Feirefiz were relieved of their armour and, dressed in white cloaks, they entered the great hall that had been in Parsifal's mind so often in the years of his quest.

帕西法爾和費雷菲茲卸下了盔甲，穿上白色披風，走進了那座在追尋的歲月中無數次浮現在帕西法爾腦海中的大廳。

And there, half sitting, half leaning on a couch, was the sufferer, Anfortas, the Fisher King. Parsifal embraced him, and said: "Uncle, I failed you sadly the first time I came here, and so have made your pain last longer than it should have been. It means more than the kingship to me that I can now bring you relief and health. Anfortas -- what is ailing you?"

在那裡，半坐半靠在一張長椅上的，是那位受苦者——安佛塔斯，漁王。帕西法爾擁抱了他，說：「叔叔，我第一次來到這裡時辜負了你，因此讓你的痛苦持續得比應有的時間更長。能為你帶來解脫和健康，這對我來說比王位更重要。安佛塔斯——你受了什麼苦？」

And, at these words, the wound closed and healed, and strength and vigour returned -- and the sick paleness of Anfortas changed to the glow of health. He rose from his couch -- then he knelt down before Parsifal and said: "Son of my sister Herzeloyde -- let me be the first to vow allegiance and faithful service to you as the chosen and worthy King of the Grail!"

聽到這些話，傷口閉合並痊癒了，力量和精力恢復了——安佛塔斯病態的蒼白變成了健康的紅潤。他從長椅上站起來——然後跪在帕西法爾面前說：「我姐姐赫策洛伊德的兒子——讓我成為第一個向你這位被揀選的、堪當的聖杯之王宣誓效忠和忠誠侍奉的人！」

Then a lady came forward, and it seemed to Parsifal that she was even more beautiful than the picture of her he had carried in his mind for so many years -- more beautiful than the picture he had once seen formed by blood and snow. They spoke few words, Condwiramur and Parsifal -- for what they felt could not be put into words.

然後一位女士走上前來，帕西法爾覺得她比他心中多年來攜帶的她的形象更加美麗——比他曾在血與雪中看到的圖景更加美麗。康薇拉慕和帕西法爾只說了很少的話——因為他們的感受無法用言語表達。

And then they all sat down in that great hall and made ready to see the wonder of the Grail. Once again, the spear was carried through the hall -- and then came the maiden who carried the shining chalice whose light was nourishment.

然後他們都在那座大廳中坐下，準備見證聖杯的奇蹟。又一次，聖矛被抬過大廳——然後走來了那位捧著閃耀聖杯的少女，聖杯的光芒就是滋養。

When it was all over, Feirefiz said: "I saw a beautiful maiden who held her arms as if she carried something -- but there was nothing in her hands. Why did she act in this fashion?"

一切結束之後，費雷菲茲說：「我看到一位美麗的少女，她伸出雙臂彷彿捧著什麼東西——但她手中空無一物。她為什麼要這樣做？」

And then he was told that, being a heathen, he could not see the Grail. But Feirefiz wanted to see the Grail -- and he also had lost his heart to the maiden, Repanse de Joie, who was the only one allowed to carry the Grail.

然後有人告訴他，因為他是異教徒，所以看不見聖杯。但費雷菲茲想要看到聖杯——而且他也愛上了那位少女，喜悅的勒龐絲（Repanse de Joie），她是唯一被允許捧持聖杯的人。

So Feirefiz was baptised and became a Christian, and he became the husband of her who carried the Grail. It was, after the king, the next highest honour amongst the servants of the Grail.

於是費雷菲茲受洗成為了基督徒，他成為了那位捧持聖杯的少女的丈夫。在聖杯的侍奉者中，這是僅次於國王的最高榮耀。

The next day, after Parsifal had become Grail King, Cundry, the messenger, brought the news that Sigune had passed away in the hut with her bridegroom's coffin -- and, at Parsifal's order, Sigune and her bridegroom were buried in one grave.

第二天，在帕西法爾成為聖杯之王之後，使者坤德里帶來了消息：席古涅已經在那間存放著未婚夫棺木的小屋中離世了——按帕西法爾的命令，席古涅和她的未婚夫被合葬在同一座墳墓中。

Parsifal himself remained the King and Guardian of the Grail. Two sons were born to him and Condwiramur. They both became great knights, and the adventures of his elder son, Lohengrin -- the knight of the white swan -- form another one of the stories written by the troubadours.

帕西法爾自己一直是聖杯的國王和守護者。他和康薇拉慕生了兩個兒子。他們都成為了偉大的騎士，而他長子羅恩格林（Lohengrin）——白天鵝騎士——的冒險故事，構成了遊吟詩人筆下的另一個傳說。

And they say that this son of Parsifal became the last of the kings of the Grail. After Lohengrin, the son of Parsifal, the spear and the chalice were given to someone called "Prester John" -- the Priest John -- who guards it in secret, and can only be found by means that cannot be said openly.

據說帕西法爾的這個兒子成為了最後一位聖杯之王。在帕西法爾之子羅恩格林之後，聖矛和聖杯被交給了一位名叫「祭司約翰」（Prester John）的人——他在秘密中守護著它，只有透過無法公開言說的方式才能找到。

The story of Parsifal -- who set out as a fool and learned compassion through suffering -- that story ends with his finding the Grail, and becoming its Guardian. Yet there is much that happened in the Parsifal story, to the whole story, in later centuries -- in the centuries after the troubadours. There even came a book, which is a kind of parody -- a deliberate caricature of the Parsifal story and, as it happens, this parody is also one of the great works of literature. We shall first discuss some questions which arise from Wolfram's tale, and then go on to the parody that came later.

帕西法爾的故事——他以一個傻瓜的身份啟程，透過苦難學會了悲憫——那個故事以他找到聖杯、成為其守護者而結束。然而在後來的世紀裡——在遊吟詩人之後的世紀裡——帕西法爾的故事又發生了許多事情。甚至出現了一本書，那是一種戲仿——對帕西法爾故事的刻意諷刺，而且碰巧，這部戲仿作品本身也是偉大的文學作品之一。我們先討論沃夫蘭的故事所引發的一些問題，然後再談那部後來的戲仿之作。

## 15.Feirefiz: Faith and Intellect 費雷菲茲——信仰與理智


The last part of Wolfram's *Parzival* raises a number of questions -- such as, for instance, why he introduces Feirefiz at this stage, Parsifal's brother with black and white skin.

沃夫蘭的《帕西法爾》（*Parzival*）最後一部分引出了許多問題——例如，為什麼他在這個階段引入費雷菲茲，帕西法爾那位黑白皮膚的兄弟。

But instead of simply telling you that he means -- symbolically -- this or that, I want you to discover the meaning for yourselves.

但我不想簡單地告訴你他象徵性地意味著這個或那個，我想讓你們自己去發現其中的含義。

Now the first piece of material is a comparison -- how the same thing appeared to the medieval mind, as compared to the modern mind.

現在第一個素材是一個比較——同一件事物在中世紀的心靈中如何呈現，與在現代心靈中如何呈現。

A teacher of the Middle Ages would, for instance, say to his pupils: "A piece of charcoal is a dark lump. But once you get it burning, it gives light and warmth. The light and warmth are hidden in the coal -- but once it is burning, the hidden light and warmth come out.

舉例來說，一位中世紀的教師會對他的學生說：「一塊木炭是一團黑色的東西。但一旦你把它點燃，它就會發出光和熱。光和熱隱藏在煤中——但一旦它燃燒起來，隱藏的光和熱就會顯現。

"And," the teacher would continue, "it is like this with you, my pupils. You have all kinds of faculties in you -- you may not even know about them: they are still hidden. My task as a teacher is to bring out that which is still a hidden faculty -- to bring out the hidden flame that is waiting to be awakened." This kind of poetic picture was natural for the Middle Ages.

「而且，」這位教師會繼續說，「你們也是如此，我的學生們。你們身上有各種各樣的能力——你們甚至可能不知道它們：它們仍然是隱藏的。作為老師，我的任務就是把那些仍然隱藏的能力引導出來——把等待著被喚醒的隱藏火焰引導出來。」這種詩意的圖景對中世紀來說是自然的。

The modern mind does not speak like this about coal. The modern mind says: all plants contain a chemical called "chlorophyll". This chlorophyll has the peculiarity that it can absorb energy from the sunlight, and this energy is used by the plant. The plant takes carbon dioxide from the air, and breaks it up, by means of this sun-energy, into carbon and oxygen. The carbon remains with the plant -- the oxygen is returned to the air. The plant builds its body from the carbon. And when I burn wood or coal, I bring carbon and oxygen together again: the carbon is oxidized.

現代人的心靈不會這樣談論煤。現代人的心靈會說：所有植物都含有一種叫做「葉綠素」的化學物質。葉綠素的特性是能夠吸收陽光的能量，而這種能量被植物利用。植物從空氣中攝取二氧化碳，並借助這種太陽能將其分解為碳和氧。碳留在植物體內——氧被釋放回空氣中。植物用碳來構建它的身體。而當我燃燒木材或煤時，我使碳和氧重新結合：碳被氧化了。

Now compare these two ways of looking at a piece of coal. The medieval man knew nothing of chlorophyll -- or solar energy. He used the coal as a kind of poetic picture. But you can see that this poetic picture spoke to the feelings of his pupils. They did not learn any science from it -- but it had a strong appeal to their feelings.

現在比較一下這兩種看待一塊煤的方式。中世紀的人對葉綠素一無所知——也不知道太陽能。他把煤當作一種詩意的圖景。但你可以看到，這種詩意的圖景打動了學生們的感受。他們沒有從中學到任何科學——但它對他們的情感有著強烈的感染力。

The modern scientific description does not touch any feelings, but it satisfies something else, something that is called our intellect. We would not be satisfied with a poetic picture -- we want an intellectually satisfying explanation of what is going on in the world around us.

現代科學的描述不觸動任何感受，但它滿足了另一種東西，一種叫做我們的「理智」（intellect）的東西。我們不會滿足於一幅詩意的圖景——我們想要一個在理智上令人滿意的解釋，來說明周圍世界中正在發生的事情。

At the time of Wolfram, in the thirteenth century, the intellect did not dominate as it does today. It was, as I have told you, the age of faith, the age when the religious instinct provided people with all the explanations they needed. They did not need intellectual explanations -- and if you had told them about chlorophyll or solar energy, they would not have understood it. The time for the intellect had not yet come.

在沃夫蘭的時代，十三世紀，理智並不像今天這樣佔主導地位。正如我告訴過你們的，那是信仰的時代，宗教本能為人們提供了他們所需的一切解釋的時代。他們不需要理智的解釋——如果你告訴他們葉綠素或太陽能，他們根本聽不懂。理智的時代還沒有到來。

Yet there was intellect already at that time -- there was intellectual work on a great scale, but not in Europe, not amongst Christians. It was amongst the Muslims, amongst the Arabs.

然而在那個時代已經有了理智——有著大規模的智識工作，但不在歐洲，不在基督徒之中。那是在穆斯林之中、在阿拉伯人之中。

In the seventh century, Muhammad had led the Bedouin tribes of Arabia out of the desert lands. Under their banner, the green flag with the crescent moon, the Arab expansion eventually reached from Spain, over North Africa, to the Black Sea, and so formed, geographically, a half-moon or crescent around the south of Europe.

七世紀，穆罕默德帶領阿拉伯半島的貝都因部落走出了沙漠。在他們的旗幟——繪有新月的綠旗——下，阿拉伯的擴張最終從西班牙延伸到北非，直到黑海，在地理上形成了一個圍繞歐洲南部的半月形或新月形。

The Arabs recognised a good thing when they saw it, and so they learned not only from the countries under their dominion, they learned from the great empires of the East, India and China. The Arabs were the first to bring all this knowledge from hundreds of sources together and to sort it out.

阿拉伯人見到好東西就能辨認出來，因此他們不僅從被他們統治的國家學習，還從東方的偉大帝國——印度和中國——學習。阿拉伯人是第一批將來自數百個來源的知識匯集在一起並加以整理的人。

And so came the first universities, with divisions into various "faculties" or departments.

於是最早的大學出現了，設有各種「院系」或科系。

At a time when the whole of Europe possessed perhaps 10,000 books, one Arab university in Cordoba alone had 100,000 books. At a time when in Europe only clergy of the higher ranks of the Church could read or write, the Arab universities had thousands of students learning physics, chemistry, astronomy, medicine.

在整個歐洲可能只有一萬本書的時候，單單哥多華（Cordoba）一所阿拉伯大學就有十萬本書。在歐洲只有教會高級神職人員才能讀寫的時候，阿拉伯的大學裡有成千上萬的學生在學習物理、化學、天文和醫學。

Also at that time when the people of Europe had to work with an abacus, with beads on strings, if they wanted to work out a difficult sum like 15 x 12, the Arabs brought from India the so-called Arabic numerals, and worked out all the rules of arithmetic we have now. They took these rules even further, and started algebra -- which is an Arabic word, meaning the "reunion of broken parts".

同樣在那個時候，當歐洲人如果想算一道像 15 x 12 這樣的難題，還得用算盤——串珠上的珠子——來計算時，阿拉伯人已經從印度引進了所謂的阿拉伯數字，並制定了我們現在使用的所有算術規則。他們甚至更進一步，創立了代數（algebra）——這是一個阿拉伯語詞彙，意思是「殘缺部分的重新組合」。

And if there is one thing which is purely intellectual, it is algebra. The Arab civilisation, with its science, with its mathematics, was indeed a civilisation of the intellect, and several centuries ahead of Europe, which still lived in feelings and instincts -- except for a few advanced minds here and there.

如果有什麼東西是純粹理智的，那就是代數。阿拉伯文明，以其科學和數學，確實是一個理智的文明，比歐洲領先了好幾個世紀。歐洲那時仍然活在感受和本能之中——只有少數零星的先進心靈例外。

This was the position at the time when Wolfram wrote his *Parzival*.

這就是沃夫蘭寫作《帕西法爾》時的狀況。

But, as I have mentioned, the Albigenses, these Manichean heretics in Provence, were on the best of terms with their Arab neighbours across the Pyrenees in Spain. They knew and they admired the intellectual achievements of the Arabs.

但正如我提過的，阿爾比派（Albigenses），這些普羅旺斯（Provence）的摩尼教異端，與庇里牛斯山（Pyrenees）另一邊西班牙的阿拉伯鄰居關係極好。他們了解並欽佩阿拉伯人在智識上的成就。

And so did the secret Manichean, Wolfram. He speaks in one part of the story of the movements of the planets, and he calls them "Zval", "Almustri", "Almuret", which are the Arab names for Saturn (Zuhal), Jupiter (Al Mushtori), Mars (Al Ahmar). Wolfram knew something of the scientific and intellectual civilisation of the Arabs.

祕密的摩尼教徒沃夫蘭也是如此。他在故事的一個段落中談到行星的運行，並稱它們為「Zval」、「Almustri」、「Almuret」——這些是土星（Zuhal）、木星（Al Mushtori）、火星（Al Ahmar）的阿拉伯名稱。沃夫蘭對阿拉伯人的科學和智識文明有所了解。

But Wolfram could see further than that. We know already that he could foresee a time when the instinctive religious feeling of his own time would die out, would disappear. But as this instinct grew less and less, something else would grow more and more -- the intellect. Those two things go together -- it is just like the phases of the moon. As the dark part grows smaller, the silvery crescent grows bigger. And as the instinctive religious feeling began to withdraw, there began in Europe the growth of intellect.

但沃夫蘭能看得更遠。我們已經知道，他能預見到一個時代，那時他所處時代本能的宗教感受將會消亡、消失。但當這種本能越來越弱時，另一種東西會越來越強——理智。這兩件事是相伴而行的——就像月亮的盈虧。當黑暗的部分變小時，銀色的新月就變大。隨著本能的宗教感受開始退縮，歐洲開始了理智的成長。

And Wolfram, the heretic -- who knew both sides, who loved the Christian faith but also admired the intellectual knowledge of the Arabs -- Wolfram could foresee a time when the growing intellect in Europe would reach out and welcome the intellectual knowledge of the Arabs.

而沃夫蘭——這位異端——他了解雙方，他熱愛基督教信仰，但也欽佩阿拉伯人的智識知識——沃夫蘭能預見到一個時代，屆時歐洲日益成長的理智將會伸出手來，歡迎阿拉伯人的智識知識。

At the time of Wolfram, there were not many such people yet. There were some, however, and one of them was a Scotsman. He came from the border country around Abbotsford and Dryburgh Abbey. His name was Michael Scot. This Michael Scot went all the way to Cordoba in Spain and studied at the great Arab university there. But when, after many years of travel in Italy and Germany, he came back to his native Scotland, the people felt there was something uncanny about a man who had learned intellectual knowledge -- it was something devilish. They feared him too much to burn him at the stake -- but they did call him a sorcerer and a wizard, who had sold his soul to the devil.

在沃夫蘭的時代，這樣的人還不多。然而確實有一些，其中一位是蘇格蘭人。他來自亞博斯福（Abbotsford）和德萊堡修道院（Dryburgh Abbey）周圍的邊境地區。他的名字叫邁可·斯科特（Michael Scot）。這位邁可·斯科特一路跋涉到西班牙的哥多華，在那裡偉大的阿拉伯大學學習。但當他在義大利和德國旅行了多年之後回到蘇格蘭故鄉時，人們覺得一個學會了智識知識的人有某種令人不安的東西——那是某種魔鬼般的東西。他們太害怕他以至於不敢把他綁上火刑柱——但他們確實稱他為巫師和術士，一個把靈魂賣給了魔鬼的人。

And so, Michael Scot, the wizard, became a Scottish legend. But he was a real, historical person whose studies in Cordoba and Toledo made people in Scotland highly suspicious of him. Again here was someone willing to approach secret, "heretical" ideas with an open mind, and that made him seem dangerous.

就這樣，巫師邁可·斯科特成為了蘇格蘭的傳說。但他是一個真實的歷史人物，他在哥多華和托萊多（Toledo）的學習使蘇格蘭人對他高度懷疑。這又是一個願意以開放心態接觸祕密的、「異端的」思想的人，而這使他顯得很危險。

In Wolfram's time, most people still felt that there was something uncanny and devilish in the cold light of the intellect of the Arabs. They felt their own, instinctive religious feeling as a warmth, like the light of the sun, and the Arabs' intellectual knowledge as a light that gave no warmth, like the light of the moon. And they wanted no part of it.

在沃夫蘭的時代，大多數人仍然覺得阿拉伯人那冰冷的理智之光中有某種詭異和魔鬼般的東西。他們把自己本能的宗教感受看作一種溫暖，像太陽的光芒；而阿拉伯人的智識知識則像月亮的光——不帶溫暖的光。他們不想與之有任何關係。

And in Wolfram's time there were still Crusaders -- Christians and Muslims fought each other grimly. The Crusaders still thought they could conquer the Muslims -- and the Muslims still thought of conquering Europe one day.

在沃夫蘭的時代，十字軍仍在征戰——基督徒和穆斯林殊死搏鬥。十字軍仍然以為他們能征服穆斯林——穆斯林也仍然想著有一天征服歐洲。

Yet Wolfram, in his intuitive knowledge, foresaw a time when the warm, instinctive forces would come to an end, when Europe itself would be ready for the intellect, and would receive the intellectual knowledge of the Arabs with open arms.

然而沃夫蘭，憑藉他直覺的知識，預見到一個時代——那時溫暖的、本能的力量將會終結，歐洲自身將為理智做好準備，並張開雙臂接受阿拉伯人的智識知識。

And now, think of this situation: Crusaders and Arabs fighting each other in the East, but Wolfram realising that there would be no winners in this fight, and that, in future, something had to come from the Arabs, something they had achieved before Europe: intellectual knowledge.

現在，想想這個局面：十字軍和阿拉伯人在東方互相廝殺，但沃夫蘭意識到這場戰鬥中不會有贏家，而且在未來，某些東西必須從阿拉伯人那裡傳來，某些他們比歐洲更早取得的東西：智識知識。

And in this situation, you have the answer to the riddle of Feirefiz, the elder half-brother of Parsifal -- Feirefiz, the king of the Arabs.

在這個情境中，你就有了費雷菲茲之謎的答案——帕西法爾的同父異母的兄長費雷菲茲，阿拉伯人的國王。

Feirefiz represents the intellectual knowledge that the Arabs had, that was born amongst the Arabs, before it came to Europe. And Parsifal can only meet Feirefiz, the intellect, when he has lost the religious instinct, when religion, as his time knew it, no longer meant anything to him.

費雷菲茲代表的是阿拉伯人所擁有的智識知識——那種在傳入歐洲之前誕生於阿拉伯人之中的知識。而帕西法爾只有在失去了宗教本能之後，在他那個時代所知的宗教對他不再有任何意義之時，才能與費雷菲茲——理智——相遇。

He has lost the religious instinct, and from now on, that which came from the Arabs was to be his companion and brother -- we could say: a part of himself.

他失去了宗教本能，從此以後，那來自阿拉伯人的東西將成為他的同伴和兄弟——我們可以說：他自身的一部分。

Parsifal, the hero of the future, cannot depend on religious feelings -- he must find his way with intellect, as we all must do now: that is the meaning of the meeting with Feirefiz.

帕西法爾，這位未來的英雄，不能依賴宗教情感——他必須用理智來找到自己的路，正如我們所有人現在必須做的：這就是與費雷菲茲相遇的意義。

But why is Feirefiz black and white? Wolfram wanted to point out something very important about intellectual knowledge. Take a man who is intellectually very gifted: he has a brilliant intellect -- does this make him also a good man? No -- he can use his intellect for good or for evil, and a good many people use it sometimes for good and sometimes for evil. The intellect, for itself, is neither, or it is both good and evil -- it has both possibilities.

但為什麼費雷菲茲是黑白相間的？沃夫蘭想要指出關於智識知識的一件非常重要的事情。拿一個在智識上非常有天賦的人來說：他有著出色的理智——但這是否也使他成為一個好人？不——他可以把他的理智用於善，也可以用於惡，而許多人有時用它做好事，有時用它做壞事。理智本身既不善也不惡，或者說它既善又惡——它同時擁有兩種可能性。

Wolfram could see just that far. But we, today, know much more about the moral ambiguity of the intellect than Wolfram ever did.

沃夫蘭能看到的就這麼遠了。但我們今天對理智的道德模糊性的了解，遠比沃夫蘭所知的要多。

Take just some of the achievements of intellectual science. It has prolonged human life and reduced infant mortality. But it has, at the same time, created the problem of over-population. There is not enough food in the world to feed everyone -- and the situation will be worse in fifty years time.

就拿智識科學的一些成就來說吧。它延長了人類的壽命，降低了嬰兒死亡率。但同時，它也製造了人口過剩的問題。世界上沒有足夠的食物來養活每一個人——而五十年後情況還會更糟。

The intellect has created a wonderful vehicle for locomotion, the motor car. But think of the congestion and the pollution to our planet.

理智創造了一種出色的交通工具——汽車。但想想那擁堵和對我們星球的污染吧。

It would not be difficult to make a long list of things created by the intellect which are both a blessing and a curse.

列一長串由理智所創造的既是祝福又是詛咒的事物，並不困難。

So, Wolfram chose to represent this ambiguity in Feirefiz by making him both black and white.

因此，沃夫蘭選擇透過讓費雷菲茲黑白相間來表現這種模糊性。

But, whether we like it or not, we cannot do without intellect -- it has become our constant companion, as Feirefiz became the companion of Parsifal. And, we are told in the story, that when Parsifal is called to the Grail, he is told he must bring Feirefiz with him.

但不管我們喜不喜歡，我們離不開理智——它已經成為我們永恆的同伴，正如費雷菲茲成為帕西法爾的同伴一樣。而且故事告訴我們，當帕西法爾被聖杯召喚時，他被告知必須帶上費雷菲茲一同前來。

The search for the Grail is the search for God, for the divine spirit in the world. In the past, it was feeling, it was instinct which gave people the conviction that there is a God, that there is a spiritual world besides the world of the senses.

對聖杯的追尋就是對上帝的追尋，對世間神聖靈性的追尋。在過去，是感受、是本能給予人們確信——確信有一位上帝，除了感官的世界之外還有一個靈性的世界。

This instinct works no longer, or only in a few people. Today, if anyone seeks at all to find a new meaning in the old words, they must seek with their intellect -- they cannot leave their intellect behind.

這種本能已不再起作用，或只在少數人身上起作用。今天，如果有人想在那些古老的詞語中尋找新的意義，他們必須用自己的理智去尋找——他們無法把理智拋在身後。

If there is any answer to the quest of the Grail, then this answer must also satisfy the intellect, and not only feelings and emotions. One could say -- for our time, a religious path to the Grail is not enough, it must be an intellectual, a scientific path. And that is why Parsifal can only come to the Grail with Feirefiz.

如果聖杯的追尋有任何答案，那麼這個答案也必須能滿足理智，而不僅僅是感受和情感。可以說——在我們的時代，通往聖杯的宗教之路是不夠的，它必須是一條智識的、科學的道路。這就是為什麼帕西法爾只能與費雷菲茲一起來到聖杯面前。

But we are told that Feirefiz, when he comes to Montsalvage, cannot see the Grail, until he is baptised and becomes a Christian.

但故事告訴我們，費雷菲茲來到蒙薩爾瓦奇時看不見聖杯，直到他受洗成為基督徒。

It is something that happens all the time -- it happens amongst the most intellectual people of today, the scientists, that they stand before wonderful revelations of God's wisdom in the world, and do not recognise them for what they are.

這是一直在發生的事——就在今天最具智識的人們——科學家們——身上發生著：他們站在上帝在世間的智慧的奇妙啟示面前，卻不認得那是什麼。

But sometimes one of the great scientists does realise what they behold, and tries to convey what this experience is. Such a great modern scientist was Einstein.

但有時候，某位偉大的科學家確實意識到了他們所看到的，並試圖傳達這種體驗是什麼。這樣一位偉大的現代科學家就是愛因斯坦（Einstein）。

Einstein's "Theory of Relativity" is about the most abstract intellectual construction ever conceived by a human mind, and is as important for the modern astronomer as it is for the atomic scientist. Yet this great intellect, this great scientist, said the following words: "The most beautiful thing we can experience is the mysterious. It is the source of all true art and science. He to whom this experience is a stranger, who cannot pause to wonder and stand rapt in awe, is as good as dead. His eyes are closed."

愛因斯坦的「相對論」大概是人類心智所構想過的最抽象的智識建構，它對現代天文學家和原子科學家同等重要。然而，這位偉大的理智、這位偉大的科學家，說過這樣的話：「我們所能體驗到的最美麗的事物就是神祕。它是一切真正藝術和科學的源泉。對這種體驗感到陌生的人，不能停下來去驚嘆、不能肅然起敬地佇立的人，與死人無異。他的眼睛是閉著的。」

Einstein says, in other words: There is something in the world for which I have no words -- I call it the mysterious. I can experience it, and those who can't are as good as dead or blind.

換句話說，愛因斯坦在說：世界上有某種我找不到言語來形容的東西——我稱之為神祕。我能體驗到它，而那些無法體驗到的人，與死人或盲人無異。

Einstein is a kind of Feirefiz, an intellectual who has seen what the other scientists do not see.

愛因斯坦就是一種費雷菲茲——一個看見了其他科學家所看不見的東西的智識者。

The figure of Feirefiz is not the only puzzle in the Parsifal story -- there is the question of what does Sigune mean, the woman with the dead bridegroom? Or what is the meaning of Cundry? Or Anfortas?

費雷菲茲的角色不是帕西法爾故事中唯一的謎題——還有席古涅是什麼意思，那個與死去未婚夫在一起的女人？坤德里的意義是什麼？安佛塔斯又是什麼意思？

But it is not necessary to get all the questions answered in a work of art like Parsifal. It is a good thing to have questions, and to live with them.

但在帕西法爾這樣的藝術作品中，沒有必要得到所有問題的答案。擁有問題，並與它們共處，這是一件好事。

In the next chapter we shall go from Wolfram's *Parzival* to a great work of literature which is a kind of parody of it.

在下一章中，我們將從沃夫蘭的《帕西法爾》轉向一部偉大的文學作品——一部可以說是對它的戲仿之作。

## 16.Don Quixote 堂吉訶德


The story of Parsifal was written about 1200 — it was just one of many romances which glorified the quests of knights and praised the virtues of chivalry. The troubadours had started this kind of story and it became very popular. One hundred, two hundred, three hundred years later, stories of knightly adventures were still highly popular — but the authors who produced these stories were no longer troubadours, they were no longer people who wished to convey some great meaning in the form of pictures.

帕西法爾（Parsifal）的故事大約寫於一二○○年——那只是眾多歌頌騎士追尋、讚美騎士精神的浪漫傳奇之一。吟遊詩人開創了這類故事體裁，後來極受歡迎。一百年、兩百年、三百年之後，騎士冒險故事仍然大為盛行——但撰寫這些故事的作者不再是吟遊詩人了，他們不再是那些希望透過意象傳達某種偉大意義的人。

Some of those later writers, like Edmund Spenser, did not bother to make their heroes into living beings, human beings of flesh and blood. The knights in Spenser's *Faerie Queen* are personifications of this or that virtue, but you can never feel with them, or for them, as you can with Parsifal. They are not flesh and blood, but allegories.

那些後來的作者中，有人如史賓塞（Edmund Spenser），根本不費心將他們的英雄塑造成活生生的人、有血有肉的人。史賓塞《仙后》（*Faerie Queen*）裡的騎士是這種或那種美德的人格化身，但你永遠無法像對帕西法爾那樣與他們同感共鳴。他們不是血肉之軀，而是寓言。

Other writers took quite a different line. They realised that what made people read such a story was the thrill of danger, of adventures — and the flights of fantasy. And so they invented as their heroes knights who were incredibly handsome, incredibly virtuous, incredibly brave, heroes who never put a foot wrong (as Parsifal had done) — and these authors also invented maidens of incredible beauty, attacked or endangered or enslaved by monsters, demons, giants, dragons, wizards, and of course the brave knight unfailingly killed and destroyed the monster and rescued the beauty in distress.

另一些作者則走了截然不同的路線。他們意識到，讓人們閱讀這類故事的吸引力在於危險的刺激、冒險——以及奇幻的飛翔。於是他們筆下的英雄都是無比英俊、無比高尚、無比勇敢的騎士，從不犯錯（不像帕西法爾那樣）——這些作者還虛構了美得不可方物的少女，遭受妖怪、惡魔、巨人、惡龍、巫師的攻擊、危害或奴役，而勇敢的騎士總是毫無例外地殺死並摧毀怪物，解救危難中的美人。

The love scenes in these stories were overly sentimental, the battle scenes became ever more wildly exaggerated, and the villains of the piece — sorcerers, giants — ever more impossible creatures, without rhyme or reason. But there was no longer any deeper meaning at all in these stories.

這些故事裡的愛情場景過分煽情，戰鬥場面也越來越荒誕誇張，而反派角色——巫師、巨人——則越來越是不可能存在的怪物，毫無道理可言。然而，這些故事中已經沒有任何更深層的意義了。

Yet these later romances still sold like hot cakes. Printing had already been invented, more people could read and write, and they bought this stuff — they wallowed in it, they lapped it up. This is all the more surprising, as by then the time of knights and of chivalry was already past.

然而，這些後來的浪漫傳奇依然暢銷如熱蛋糕。印刷術已經發明，更多的人能讀能寫，他們買下這些東西——沉溺其中，津津有味地閱讀。這更令人驚訝的是，那時候騎士和騎士精神的時代早已過去。

Gunpowder had been invented, which made armour quite useless. America had been discovered, science was in its beginning, the Reformation was shaking all traditional beliefs — so what use was a knight in armour in this new world?

火藥已被發明，使得盔甲完全無用。美洲已被發現，科學方興未艾，宗教改革正在動搖一切傳統信仰——在這個新世界裡，穿著盔甲的騎士還有什麼用處？

But it seems as if the people were, in a way, scared of this new world that was dawning, as if they wanted to escape — at least in their reading, in books — to the past, to a time of brave knights, of wizards and magic.

但看起來人們似乎在某種程度上害怕這個正在黎明的新世界，好像他們想要逃避——至少在閱讀中、在書本裡——回到過去，回到勇敢騎士、巫師與魔法的時代。

And so, at the very moment when the real knight became an antiquity, when chivalry and knighthood were finished for good, at that moment of history, people all over Europe avidly read stories about utterly unreal knights, and about utterly meaningless and wildly fantastic quests of one kind or another. People read these stories to escape from the real world in which they lived.

就這樣，在真正的騎士成為古董的那一刻，在騎士精神和騎士制度徹底終結的那一刻，在歷史的那一刻，全歐洲的人們如飢似渴地閱讀著那些關於完全不真實的騎士、以及各種完全無意義且荒誕奇幻的追尋冒險的故事。人們閱讀這些故事是為了逃離他們所生活的現實世界。

And so we come to the time around the year 1600. By that time, Spain already had large colonies in America, England had become Protestant, Spain had sent the great Armada against England, and the Armada had been destroyed.

如此我們來到大約一六○○年前後。那時，西班牙在美洲已經擁有龐大的殖民地，英格蘭已經成為新教國家，西班牙曾派遣偉大的無敵艦隊攻打英格蘭，而無敵艦隊已被摧毀。

Yet, at that time, 400 years after Wolfram and his Parsifal, people were still reading these stories of handsome knights, noble maidens and fearsome monsters.

然而，就在那個時代，在沃夫朗（Wolfram）和他的帕西法爾問世四百年之後，人們仍然在閱讀這些關於英俊騎士、高貴少女和可怖怪物的故事。

And then, about this year 1600, a Spaniard wrote a book which made an end of all this — it is a book that belongs to the great works of art, and it is also one of the few funny books in world literature.

後來，大約在一六○○年，一個西班牙人寫了一本書，終結了這一切——這是一本名列偉大藝術作品之林的書，也是世界文學中少數幾本詼諧有趣的書之一。

The man who wrote the book, Miguel de Cervantes, was a soldier and adventurer (he lost his left hand in a sea battle with the Turks, and was for five years a slave in Turkish captivity). Cervantes knew from his own experience the real adventures of his own time, and he saw how ridiculous the fictitious, invented adventures of these romantic stories were.

寫這本書的人，米格爾·德·塞萬提斯（Miguel de Cervantes），是一名軍人和冒險家（他在與土耳其人的海戰中失去了左手，又當了五年的土耳其人俘虜）。塞萬提斯從自己的親身經歷中了解他那個時代的真實冒險，也看出了這些浪漫故事中虛構捏造的冒險是多麼荒謬。

And so he wrote his book, *Don Quixote*,\* in which he exposed the foolishness of all the other stories so popular in his time.

於是他寫了這本書，《乾訶德》（*Don Quixote*）\*，揭露了在他那個時代盛行的所有其他故事的愚蠢。

This book, *Don Quixote*, is also the first real novel — that means a novel about real people, and the real world they live in. It is a long story — and I am very sorry that I can only give a little time to it, for it is a masterpiece.

這本書《乾訶德》也是第一部真正的小說——意思是一部關於真實的人、以及他們所生活的真實世界的小說。這是一個很長的故事——我非常抱歉只能花一點時間來講它，因為它是一部傑作。

The hero of the story is an elderly gentleman, Quesada by name, a gaunt, haggard man with hollow cheeks and a little pointed beard. This man, well over fifty, owns some land in a village in Spain and has a few farmhands who work for him, and so he has plenty of time to read books. He loves to read books — but only one kind of book, the books about knights and quests and heroic adventures.

故事的主人公是一位年長的紳士，名叫凱薩達（Quesada），身材瘦削、面容憔悴，兩頰凹陷，留著一小撮尖鬍子。這個人年過五十，在西班牙某個村莊擁有一些田地，還有幾個替他幹活的長工，因此他有大把時間讀書。他酷愛讀書——但只讀一種書，那就是關於騎士、追尋和英雄冒險的書。

He reads and reads — and in time the fictitious heroes become more real to him than the world around him. His brain becomes completely befuddled by these wild and woolly stories — he comes to believe that all these knights he reads about are real persons, he believes that the wizards and dragons and giants exist really somewhere in the world.

他讀啊讀——漸漸地，那些虛構的英雄在他心中比周圍的世界更加真實。他的頭腦完全被這些荒誕離奇的故事搞糊塗了——他開始相信他所讀到的那些騎士都是真實存在的人，他相信巫師、龍和巨人真的存在於世界的某個地方。

And, in the end, he is so caught by this madness, that he decides to become himself such a glorious knight. His name, Quesada, an ordinary Spanish name, is not good enough for a knight going on a quest, so he calls himself "Don Quixote".

最終，他深陷於這種瘋狂之中，決定自己也要成為這樣一位光榮的騎士。他的名字凱薩達，一個普通的西班牙名字，對於一位將要踏上冒險旅途的騎士來說還不夠體面，於是他給自己取名為「乾訶德」。

There is a woman housekeeper and a young niece who look after him, but Don Quixote is well aware that these good, practical women have no understanding for chivalry and for the great destiny that is in store for him. And so he does not tell them of his intentions, but one day, when they are both out, he gets into his rattling pieces of armour, mounts the noble horse, Rosinante, and armed with his priceless weapons, rides out on his quest.

一個女管家和一個年輕的外甥女照顧著他，但乾訶德深知這些善良而務實的女人不會理解騎士精神，也不會理解等待著他的偉大命運。所以他沒有告訴她們自己的計畫，而是在某天趁她們都不在時，穿上他那副叮噹作響的盔甲，騎上高貴的戰馬「駑駟難得」（Rosinante），帶著他那些無價的武器，踏上了冒險之旅。

But what about armour? Don Quixote finds in the attic rusty bits of armour from his great-grandfather's day and fits them together with bits of string and wire. He has some difficulty with the helmet, for the visor, the piece that covers the eyes, is missing, but he makes himself a visor from cardboard and ties it with strings on to the helmet. There is a spear, and an ancient rusty sword, and a badly dented shield — but to Don Quixote they are wonderful shining weapons, to defy all the monsters of the world.

可是盔甲呢？乾訶德在閣樓裡找到了他曾祖父時代留下的生鏽盔甲碎片，用繩子和鐵絲把它們拼裝在一起。頭盔讓他費了些功夫，因為面甲——遮護眼睛的那部分——不見了，但他用硬紙板自製了一個面甲，用繩子繫在頭盔上。還有一根長矛，一把古老生鏽的劍，以及一面嚴重凹陷的盾牌——但在乾訶德眼中，這些都是燦爛奪目的武器，足以對抗世上所有的怪物。

And, finally, there is a miserable old carthorse in his stable, a creature with every bone sticking out — but to Don Quixote the horse is a beautiful mare, and is given the name "Rosinante".

最後，他的馬廄裡有一匹可憐的老駑馬，瘦骨嶙峋——但在乾訶德眼中，這匹馬是一匹美麗的寶馬良駒，被賜名「駑駟難得」。

A knight must have a lady in whose honour he performs great deeds and to whom he sends all the enemies he has overcome in single combat. Our poor Don Quixote, a dried-up elderly bachelor, has no lady, but he sees a buxom village girl (she milks the cows next door) and he decides that she is to be the lady to whom he will send his captives and, as her name is also ordinary, she is re-christened in his mind "Donna Dulcinea".

騎士必須有一位貴婦人，為她的榮耀而建立功勳，並將所有在決鬥中戰勝的敵人遣送到她面前。我們可憐的乾訶德，一個乾瘦的老單身漢，並沒有這樣一位淑女，但他看到了一個豐腴的村姑（她在隔壁擠牛奶），便決定她就是那位他要把俘虜們送去的貴婦人；因為她的名字也很普通，在他心裡便被重新命名為「達辛妮亞夫人」（Donna Dulcinea）。

The first adventures go — as can be expected — badly for him. People think he is mad — they laugh at him in his silly outfit, or play up to him for fun and regard it as a great joke.

最初的冒險——可以預料——對他來說很不順利。人們以為他瘋了——嘲笑他那身傻氣的裝扮，或者逢迎配合他尋開心，把它當成一個天大的笑話。

Then Don Quixote meets some people driving mules on the road. He stops the mule drivers and demands of them that they should acknowledge to him that there is no more beautiful woman in the world than his lady "Donna Dulcinea". The mule drivers tell him not to be a nuisance and to make way, whereupon Don Quixote lowers his spear and spurs his horse. Poor Rosinante makes an effort to gallop, but stumbles over her own feet and falls down, throwing Don Quixote off a few yards away.

接著乾訶德在路上遇到了一些趕騾子的人。他攔住騾夫，要求他們向他承認世上再沒有比他的貴婦人「達辛妮亞夫人」更美的女子。騾夫們叫他別添麻煩、讓開路，乾訶德於是放低長矛，策馬衝鋒。可憐的駑駟難得勉力奔馳，卻被自己的腳絆倒，把乾訶德摔出了好幾碼遠。

The armour is too heavy for him to get up, and the mule drivers, enraged by his aggressive manner, take their sticks and give him a thorough thrashing.

盔甲太重了，他爬不起來，而騾夫們被他的挑釁態度激怒，抄起棍子把他痛打了一頓。

After this misadventure, Don Quixote limps home, to the ministrations of housekeeper and niece, but he is not cured. Soon he sets out again, but this time with a "squire", a simple, down-to-earth peasant, Sancho Panza, who is persuaded by the hope of great treasures, which Don Quixote promises, when he has killed a few wizards.

這場不幸之後，乾訶德跛腳回到家中，接受管家和外甥女的照料，但他並未痊癒。不久他又出發了，這次帶了一個「侍從」——一個樸實、腳踏實地的農民，桑乔·潘薩（Sancho Panza），被乾訶德許諾的巨大寶藏所吸引——只要殺掉幾個巫師就行了。

And so we come to the most famous of Don Quixote's adventures.

於是我們來到了乾訶德最著名的一次冒險。

Soon after their departure, the knight and his squire came in sight of thirty or forty windmills which rise from that plain, and as soon as Don Quixote saw them, he said, "Fortune is guiding our affairs better than we ourselves could have wished. Do you see over yonder, my friend Sancho Panza, thirty or more huge giants? I intend to do battle with them and slay them!"

出發後不久，騎士和他的侍從看見平原上矗立著三四十座風車。乾訶德一看到它們，便說：「命運引導我們的事務比我們自己所能期望的還要好。你看那邊，我的朋友桑乔·潘薩，三十多個巨大的巨人！我打算與他們交戰，把他們統統殺掉！」

"What giants?" said Sancho, amazed.

「什麼巨人？」桑乔驚訝地說。

"Those giants you see over there," replied his master, "with long arms — some of them are almost two leagues in length."

「你看那邊那些巨人呀，」他的主人回答道，「長著長長的手臂——有些手臂差不多有兩里格那麼長。」

"Take care, sir," said Sancho, "those are not giants, but windmills, and the things which seem to you their arms are their sails which, when they are whirled round by the wind, turn the millstones!"

「小心啊，先生，」桑乔說，「那些不是巨人，是風車，那些看起來像手臂的東西是它們的風帆，風一吹就轉動磨石！」

"It is clear," answered Don Quixote, "that you are not experienced in adventures. Those are giants — and if you are afraid, turn aside and pray whilst I enter into fierce battle with them!"

「很明顯，」乾訶德回答道，「你對冒險沒有經驗。那些就是巨人——你要是害怕的話就退到一邊去禱告吧，我這就去跟他們展開激戰！」

Uttering these words, he clapped spurs to Rosinante, without heeding the cries of his squire, Sancho, who warned him that he was not going to attack giants, but windmills. But so convinced was he that they were giants that he neither heard his squire's shouts, nor did he notice what the things were, even when he came near them. Instead, he rushed on, shouting in a loud voice: "Flee not, you cowards — one knight alone attacks you!"

說完這番話，他策馬加鞭，不顧侍從桑乔的叫喊——桑乔警告他說那不是巨人而是風車。但他如此堅信那些就是巨人，既聽不見侍從的呼喊，即使靠近了也看不出那些東西到底是什麼。他反而衝了過去，大聲叫道：「別逃，你們這些懦夫——一位騎士獨自來挑戰你們了！」

At that moment, a slight breeze arose, and the great sails began to move. When Don Quixote saw this, he shouted again: "Even though you flourish a thousand arms, you shall pay for your insolence!"

就在那時，一陣微風吹起，巨大的風帆開始轉動。乾訶德見狀，又喊道：「就算你們揮舞一千條手臂，也要為你們的傲慢付出代價！」

With these words, he covered himself with his shield, couched his spear, charged at Rosinante's full gallop and rammed the first mill that stood in his way.

說完這番話，他用盾牌護住身體，端起長矛，策馬全速衝鋒，撞上了擋在路上的第一座風車。

He ran his spear into the sail, but the wind twisted it with such violence that it shivered the spear to pieces, dragging him and his horse after it, and rolling him over and over on the ground, sorely damaged.

他把長矛刺進了風帆，但風以猛烈的力量扭轉了風帆，長矛被震得粉碎，連人帶馬被拖著在地上翻滾了好幾圈，傷勢慘重。

Sancho Panza rushed up to his assistance as fast as his donkey could gallop, and when he reached the knight he found that the poor man was unable to move, such was the shock that Rosinante had given him in the fall.

桑乔·潘薩騎著毛驢盡快趕去救援，等他趕到騎士身邊，發現這個可憐的人根本動不了，摔得太重了。

"God help us!" said Sancho. "Did I not tell you, sir, to mind what you were doing — for these were only windmills! Nobody could have mistaken them unless he had windmills in his brains."

「老天保佑！」桑乔說，「先生，我不是告訴過您要小心您在做什麼嗎——那些不過是風車！除非腦袋裡也裝著風車，否則誰會認錯。」

"Hold your peace, dear Sancho!" answered Don Quixote, "for in knights' quests things can change very strangely. I am convinced that a magician has changed those giants into windmills to deprive me of the glory of victory... But in the end his evil arts will be of little avail against my good sword."

「安靜吧，親愛的桑乔！」乾訶德回答道，「因為在騎士的冒險追尋中，事情可以奇妙地改變。我確信是某個魔法師把那些巨人變成了風車，好剝奪我的勝利榮耀……但最終他的邪惡法術在我的好劍面前不會得逞。」

"God settle the issue in his own way!" cried Sancho, as he helped his master to rise and to remount Rosinante, who was almost disjointed by the fall.

「願上帝以他自己的方式來裁決！」桑乔喊道，一邊扶著他的主人站起來，重新騎上幾乎散了架的駑駟難得。

The knight and his squire were still discussing the last adventure, when they saw a large, dense cloud of dust rolling towards them. Don Quixote turned to Sancho and said: "This is the day on which I shall show the might of my arm, and on which I shall do deeds that shall be written in the book of fame for ages to come. Do you see that dust cloud? It is churned up by a mighty army marching this way."

騎士和他的侍從還在討論上一次的冒險，這時他們看到一大團濃密的塵土朝他們滾滾而來。乾訶德轉向桑乔說：「今天就是我展示臂力的日子，今天我將做出千秋萬代都會寫進功勳簿的壯舉。你看那團塵雲嗎？那是一支強大的軍隊正朝這邊行軍揚起的。」

"If so, there must be two armies," said Sancho, "for here on this side there is as great a cloud of dust!"

「如果是那樣的話，一定有兩支軍隊，」桑乔說，「因為這邊也有同樣大的一團塵雲！」

Don Quixote turned round to look at it, and seeing it was so, he rejoiced, for he fancied there were indeed two armies coming to fight each other. In fact, the clouds of dust were raised by two large flocks of sheep, rams and ewes, which were being driven along the same road from opposite directions but, owing to the dust, they could not be seen until they came near.

乾訶德轉頭望去，發現果然如此，不禁大喜，因為他以為確實有兩支軍隊即將交戰。事實上，那團塵雲是兩大群綿羊——公羊和母羊——從相反方向沿同一條路趕過來揚起的，只是因為塵土飛揚，走近了才看得清。

Sounds could be heard, and Don Quixote said, "Can you hear the neighing of horses, the blaring of trumpets and the rattle of the drums?"

能聽到一些聲音了，乾訶德說：「你聽到馬匹的嘶鳴、號角的轟鳴和戰鼓的隆隆聲了嗎？」

"I hear nothing," answered Sancho, "but the bleating of sheep and lambs."

「我什麼都沒聽到，」桑乔回答，「只有綿羊和小羊的咩咩叫。」

"You are a coward!" said Don Quixote angrily, "and the fear you are in allows you neither to see nor to hear correctly, for it is the effect of fear to disturb the senses. But if you are so afraid, stand aside, and leave the fighting to me, for I alone am sufficient to give victory to the army whose side I am going to take!"

「你是個膽小鬼！」乾訶德怒道，「你心中的恐懼讓你既看不清也聽不清，因為恐懼就是會擾亂感官。你要是這麼害怕，就站到一邊去，把戰鬥交給我，因為我一個人就足以為我要支持的那支軍隊贏得勝利！」

And with these words, he gave Rosinante the spurs and, with his spear couched, rode down the hillside like a thunderbolt.

說完這番話，他策馬加鞭，端起長矛，如雷霆般從山坡上衝了下去。

Sancho shouted to him, "Come back, master — I swear to God that those you are going to charge are only sheep! There are no knights or shields! What are you doing?"

桑乔對他喊道：「回來，主人——我對天發誓，你要衝鋒攻擊的只是一群羊！沒有什麼騎士也沒有什麼盾牌！你在幹什麼呀？」

Don Quixote, however, did not turn back, but charged on, shouting at one of the dust clouds to follow him against the other one. And then he dashed into the midst of the flock of sheep and speared some of them with such fury, as if he were fighting his mortal enemies.

然而乾訶德並不回頭，繼續衝鋒，對著其中一團塵雲大喊，要它跟著他去攻打另一團。然後他衝進羊群中間，怒不可遏地用長矛刺死了幾隻羊，彷彿在與不共戴天的仇敵搏鬥。

The shepherds who came with the flock shouted at him to leave off, but seeing that words were of no avail, they began to throw stones at him as big as a fist. Don Quixote took no notice of the stones, but galloped to and fro, shouting that the king of the enemies should come forward and fight him, man to man. But then one stone hit him in the ribs, a second hit his mouth and broke a few teeth, and a third one crushed two fingers of his hand. And when another stone fell with great force on his helmet, Don Quixote fell off his horse and on to the ground, and lay unconscious. The shepherds thought they had killed him, and they quickly collected the half dozen dead sheep and drove their flocks away in great haste, without further enquiry.

跟著羊群的牧羊人大聲喊他住手，但見喊叫沒有用，就開始向他扔拳頭大的石頭。乾訶德毫不理會石頭，來回馳騁，叫喊著要敵人的國王出來與他單打獨鬥。但接著一塊石頭砸中了他的肋骨，第二塊砸中了他的嘴巴，打掉了幾顆牙齒，第三塊壓碎了他一隻手的兩根手指。又一塊石頭重重地砸在他的頭盔上，乾訶德從馬上摔下來，倒在地上不省人事。牧羊人以為把他打死了，趕緊收拾了被他殺死的六隻羊，急急忙忙趕著羊群離開了，沒有再多過問。

All this time, Sancho had stood on a hillock, watching his master's mad escapade, tearing his beard, moaning and cursing. But seeing him lying on the ground, and the shepherds out of sight, he came down the hill to help his master, who was just regaining consciousness.

這段時間，桑乔一直站在一個小山丘上，看著他主人的瘋狂鬧劇，扯著自己的鬍子，又呻吟又咒罵。但看到主人倒在地上，牧羊人也走遠了，他便下山去幫助剛恢復意識的主人。

He said: "Did I not tell you that those were not armies, but sheep?"

他說：「我不是告訴過您那不是軍隊，是羊嗎？」

To which Don Quixote answered: "That accursed wizard has again used his black art and made the men change into sheep! But if you would follow them, you would see that, as soon as they are out of my reach, they become again an army on the march!"

乾訶德回答道：「那個該死的巫師又使用了他的黑魔法，把那些士兵變成了羊！你要是跟著他們走，就會發現他們一離開我的範圍，就又會變回一支行軍中的大軍！」

It is not possible to tell you all the hundreds of adventures of Don Quixote — how he took a country inn for an enchanted castle, or how he held a waterfall at bay all night, and so on. I will end up by relating how he acquired a magic helmet:

我不可能一一告訴你們乾訶德數以百計的冒險——他怎麼把鄉間客棧當成了魔法城堡，怎麼整夜與一道瀑布對峙，等等。最後我就講講他是怎麼得到一頂魔法頭盔的：

There were two villages, but only one of them had a barber, and this barber used to come two or three times a week to the other village to shave the men who did not want to grow beards. On these occasions, he took with him a basin in which he made the soap lather for his customers. He was on one of these journeys, riding on a mule, when a light drizzle began to fall; as the barber did not want his new hat to get stained by rain, he took it off and put the basin on his head, for he did not like to get his hair wet. To his misfortune, he encountered on the way Don Quixote and Sancho Panza.

有兩個村莊，其中只有一個有理髮師，這位理髮師每週要去另一個村莊兩三次，替那些不想留鬍子的人刮鬍子。每次去的時候，他帶著一個為顧客打肥皂泡的銅盆。有一次他騎著騾子上路，天空飄起了毛毛細雨；理髮師不想讓他的新帽子被雨水弄髒，就脫下帽子，把銅盆扣在頭上，因為他不想淋濕頭髮。不幸的是，他在路上遇到了乾訶德和桑乔·潘薩。

Don Quixote took the barber to be a knight with a helmet of gold. And when he saw the poor fellow draw near, he shouted a challenge, and with lance couched, galloped at him.

乾訶德把這個理髮師當成了一個戴著黃金頭盔的騎士。當他看到那可憐的人走近時，大聲喝斥挑戰，端起長矛朝他衝去。

The barber, seeing so unexpectedly this wild apparition dashing against him, had no other way of avoiding the thrust of the spear than to fall off his mule. But no sooner did he touch the earth, than he sprang up, more nimbly than a deer, and ran away, leaving the basin on the ground behind him.

理髮師冷不防看到這個瘋狂的幽靈般的人物朝他撲來，除了從騾子上摔下來之外，沒有別的辦法躲避長矛的刺擊。但他腳一沾地，就像一頭鹿一樣敏捷地跳起來，撒腿就跑，把銅盆丟在了地上。

Having driven his enemy to flight, Don Quixote was satisfied, and told Sancho to pick up the "helmet". Sancho lifted it and said: "That's a good basin — worth a couple of shillings anywhere!"

趕跑了敵人之後，乾訶德很滿意，叫桑乔撿起那頂「頭盔」。桑乔拿起來說：「這是個好銅盆——到哪兒都值幾個先令！」

But Don Quixote took it from him and put it on his head — and was only surprised that the lower half, the visor, was missing. He said: "This enchanted helmet must have fallen into the hands of someone who did not know what a wonderful thing it was — and so he melted one half of the pure gold down, and changed the other half so that it may appear like a barber's basin. But I shall wear it just the same, for I know its true worth and magic!"

但乾訶德從他手裡拿過來，扣在自己頭上——只是驚訝下半部分，也就是面甲，怎麼不見了。他說：「這頂魔法頭盔一定是落入了某個不知道它有多珍貴的人手中——所以他把一半的純金熔掉了，又把另一半改造得看起來像個理髮師的銅盆。但我照樣要戴它，因為我知道它的真正價值和魔力！」

With this story, we leave the brave knight, Don Quixote, a man who really had some of the qualities of the true knights — courage, love for battle, the wish to combat evil — but, alas, lacked common sense and lived in a world of fantasy and illusion. And we also leave Sancho Panza, who had commonsense, but could not understand noble ideals and principles at all.

講到這裡，我們就要告別勇敢的騎士乾訶德了，他確實具備真正騎士的某些品質——勇氣、對戰鬥的熱愛、與邪惡搏鬥的願望——但可惜的是，他缺乏常識，活在一個幻想和虛妄的世界裡。我們也告別桑乔·潘薩，他雖然有常識，卻完全無法理解崇高的理想和原則。

It is a long story — it tells not only the escapades of Don Quixote, but gives at the same time a most realistic picture of life in Spain at that time. That is why it is the first novel in the modern sense — a novel about real people, and the real world they live in.

這是一個很長的故事——它不僅講述了乾訶德的種種荒唐事蹟，同時也極為寫實地描繪了當時西班牙的生活面貌。這就是為什麼它是現代意義上的第一部小說——一部關於真實的人以及他們所生活的真實世界的小說。

In the end, Don Quixote returns to La Mancha, his home village, and falls ill. But as death approaches he awakes from his dream of knights, and he dies with a clear mind, whilst Sancho Panza cries and weeps at the loss of a master who — even in his folly — was a good man, a man who strove for goodness.

最終，乾訶德回到拉曼查（La Mancha），他的家鄉村莊，病倒了。但在死亡臨近之際，他從騎士的幻夢中醒來，在神志清明中離世，而桑乔·潘薩則為失去這位主人而痛哭流涕——即使在他的愚行中，他也是一個好人，一個追求善良的人。

*\* Pronounced Don Kee-hoti, or, particularly in Britain, Don Quick-sot.*

*\* 發音為 Don Kee-hoti，在英國尤其常讀作 Don Quick-sot。*

---

## 17.Sancho Panza's Common Sense 桑喬·潘薩的常識


We are all familiar with people who do not want to grow old. There is something sad and unfortunate about an older person trying desperately to act the part of youth.

我們都認識那些不想變老的人。一個年長的人拼命想扮演年輕人的角色，這裡面有些悲哀和不幸。

Such an unfortunate person is Don Quixote. It is all right for the young Parsifal to be eager for glory, fame, the honours of knighthood, but the same bright eagerness in a man well into his fifties is plain silly.

乾訶德就是這樣一個不幸的人。年輕的帕西法爾渴望榮耀、名聲、騎士的榮譽，這無可厚非，但同樣的熱切渴望出現在一個年過五旬的人身上，就是純粹的愚蠢了。

Cervantes, the author of the Don Quixote story, makes his hero from the start a parody of Parsifal by giving him the same ambitions as Parsifal — but as a man of fifty, when he should know better.

塞萬提斯，《乾訶德》的作者，從一開始就把他的主人公塑造成帕西法爾的滑稽模仿——賦予他與帕西法爾相同的雄心壯志，但他是個五十歲的人了，本該更明事理。

But Don Quixote does even worse than that. Humankind has grown older since the time of the troubadours — the age of chivalry is past — but Don Quixote hankers after that earlier, younger stage, and so tries to live in his own time as if knights and chivalry still existed.

但乾訶德所做的比這更糟。自吟遊詩人的時代以來，人類已經成長得更老了——騎士時代已經過去——但乾訶德留戀那個更早的、更年輕的階段，因此試圖在自己的時代中生活，彷彿騎士和騎士精神仍然存在。

Don Quixote is not only an elderly man acting like a young one, he is a man of the seventeenth century acting as if he lived in the twelfth century — he is an anachronism.

乾訶德不僅是一個老年人裝作年輕人，他還是一個十七世紀的人裝作自己生活在十二世紀——他是一個時代錯置者。

"Anachronism" means someone or something which does not fit into the time in which it appears. Don Quixote, galloping about in his rusty armour in a time which already had cannons and muskets, is an obvious anachronism. There are other, less obvious anachronisms in the present time — they are just as ridiculous as Don Quixote on his noble steed, Rosinante.

「時代錯置」（Anachronism）是指某人或某事物與其所處的時代格格不入。乾訶德穿著他那身生鏽的盔甲在一個已經有了大砲和火槍的時代四處馳騁，是一個明顯的時代錯置。在當今也有其他較不明顯的時代錯置——它們和騎著高貴戰馬駑駟難得的乾訶德一樣可笑。

So Don Quixote is an anachronism, a man who does not fit into his own time. But neither did Parsifal: you remember I pointed out again and again that Parsifal, in spite of the medieval trappings, is a stranger in his own time, and so is Don Quixote.

所以乾訶德是一個時代錯置者，一個與自己時代格格不入的人。但帕西法爾也是如此：你們記得我一再指出，帕西法爾儘管穿著中世紀的外衣，在他自己的時代裡卻是個陌生人，乾訶德也是這樣。

What is the difference? Parsifal looks forward, into the future — he represents the future in his own time. Don Quixote looks backwards, he hankers for the past — he is a hero of the past.

差別在哪裡？帕西法爾向前看，望向未來——他代表著他那個時代的未來。乾訶德向後看，留戀過去——他是過去的英雄。

You see, Don Quixote is in everything the opposite, the counterpart of Parsifal.

你們看，乾訶德在每一方面都是帕西法爾的反面、對照。

Parsifal is isolated from his time by his upbringing in the forest. For Parsifal, the vast forest of Saltane is the wall which keeps him separated from the civilization of his time, and which makes him an "outsider".

帕西法爾因為在森林中長大而與他的時代隔絕。對帕西法爾來說，索爾坦（Saltane）那片廣闊的森林就是將他與他那個時代的文明隔開的一道牆，使他成為一個「局外人」。

What isolates Don Quixote, what makes him an outsider? Books, his library of romantic stories of knights. It is his reading, his books, which create a mental wall around him and make him an outsider, a stranger in his own time.

是什麼使乾訶德孤立，什麼使他成為局外人？是書，是他那些騎士浪漫故事的藏書。是他的閱讀、他的書籍在他周圍築起了一道精神的牆，使他成為一個局外人，一個在自己時代中的陌生人。

If you consider that the paper of the books is made from wood pulp, and comes from forest trees, so that Don Quixote's library is really a little forest, you see the subtle irony of Cervantes in making Don Quixote a victim of his books.

如果你想到書頁的紙張是由木漿製成，來自森林中的樹木，那麼乾訶德的藏書室其實就是一片小小的森林，你就能看出塞萬提斯讓乾訶德成為他自己藏書的受害者時所用的那種微妙的反諷。

Parsifal is laughed at for his fool's dress, and his great ambition is to obtain a knight's armour. Once he has put on the red knight's armour, he is no longer an object of ridicule.

帕西法爾因為他的傻瓜裝束而被嘲笑，他最大的志向就是得到一套騎士的盔甲。一旦他穿上了紅騎士的盔甲，就不再是被嘲笑的對象。

But Don Quixote is laughed at just because he wears a knight's armour — this armour is now something foolish, it has become a fool's dress.

但乾訶德之所以被嘲笑，恰恰就是因為他穿了騎士的盔甲——這副盔甲現在成了愚蠢的東西，變成了傻瓜的衣裝。

Parsifal finds later on a companion in his quest, Feirefiz, who as we know represents the intellect. Don Quixote also finds a partner — a squire, the stout little peasant, Sancho Panza.

帕西法爾後來在追尋中找到了一個同伴費雷費茲（Feirefiz），我們知道他代表著智性。乾訶德也找到了一個搭檔——侍從，矮胖的小農民桑乔·潘薩。

Just as Don Quixote is a parody, a caricature of Parsifal, so Sancho Panza, the uneducated peasant, is a caricature of Feirefiz.

正如乾訶德是帕西法爾的滑稽模仿、諷刺漫畫，桑乔·潘薩這個沒受過教育的農民就是費雷費茲的諷刺漫畫。

But this companionship of Don Quixote and Sancho Panza became in the writing of Cervantes far more than a mere parody, it became in fact a story of modern humankind in its own right — it became a funny and exaggerated picture, but yet a true picture, of modern life.

但是，在塞萬提斯的筆下，乾訶德和桑乔·潘薩的這對搭檔遠不止是一種滑稽模仿，它實際上本身就成了一個關於現代人類的故事——一幅滑稽而誇張的圖畫，但卻是現代生活的一幅真實圖畫。

There is Don Quixote, full of noble ideals, full of fine great ideas, but without any practical commonsense. He cannot recognise windmills for what they are, he cannot recognise sheep for what they are — and so all his lofty aspirations end in catastrophe, they end in complete failure.

乾訶德滿懷崇高的理想、滿腦子美好偉大的想法，卻毫無實際的常識。他認不出風車的本來面目，認不出羊的本來面目——因此他所有崇高的抱負都以災難收場，以徹底的失敗告終。

And there is Sancho Panza, the sturdy peasant to whom ideas and ideals are as incomprehensible as Chinese. But he has one side of the thing called intellect — he is practical, he sees windmills and sheep for what they are. And every time his master embarks on one of his escapades, he tries to stop him, though in vain.

而桑乔·潘薩，這個壯實的農民，對他來說思想和理想就像天書一樣費解。但他擁有所謂「智性」的一面——他很務實，他看得出風車就是風車、羊就是羊。每次他的主人準備進行一場荒唐的冒險時，他都試圖阻止，儘管總是徒勞。

And so Don Quixote and Sancho Panza are a perfect picture of the idealist, the dreamer, and of the down-to-earth, practical person.

就這樣，乾訶德和桑乔·潘薩構成了理想主義者、夢想家與腳踏實地的務實之人的完美寫照。

These two characters are haunting the world right up to the present moment.

這兩個角色一直到今天都纏繞在這個世界上。

Take such a thing as the great organisation of all countries of the earth, the United Nations, or UN. It was founded as a great idea, a great ideal after the last war, with the intention to prevent any future wars. The nations of the world should settle any differences they had by discussion. And if two quarrelling nations could not come to an agreement, the whole assembly should decide by vote who was right or wrong.

就拿世界上所有國家的偉大組織聯合國來說。它在上一次大戰之後作為一個偉大的理念、偉大的理想而創立，旨在防止未來的任何戰爭。世界各國應該通過討論來解決他們之間的分歧。如果兩個爭吵的國家無法達成協議，全體大會就應該透過投票來裁定誰對誰錯。

A very beautiful idea, but already at the beginning there were Sancho Panzas who said it would not work. And, in some respects, they were right. Now, decades after the foundation, the UN is faced with the crisis of becoming an ineffectual bureaucratic talking-shop without teeth.

一個非常美麗的理念，但從一開始就有桑乔·潘薩式的人說它行不通。在某些方面，他們是對的。如今，聯合國成立數十年之後，面臨著淪為一個無效的官僚清談場所、缺乏實際約束力的危機。

Yet, the Sancho Panzas were also wrong. In those fifty years, the UN has provided peace-keeping forces in several of the world's trouble spots, as well as its work with health, education and care of refugees.

然而，桑乔·潘薩們也錯了。在那五十年裡，聯合國在世界上幾個動盪地區提供了維和部隊，還在衛生、教育和難民救助方面做了大量工作。

You see, if we listened only to the Sancho Panzas, no unselfish action, no moral deed would ever be performed — there would only be brutal self-interest, and let the devil take the weakest.

你看，如果我們只聽桑乔·潘薩的，任何無私的行為、任何道德的舉動都不會被實現——只剩下殘酷的利己主義，讓最弱的人自生自滅。

But if we only listened to the Don Quixotes, we would be forever dreaming up beautiful ideas for the benefit of all humankind — only these beautiful ideas would come tumbling down like a house built of cards as soon as they came in touch with reality.

但如果我們只聽乾訶德的，我們就會永遠為全人類的利益編織美好的夢想——只是這些美好的理念一碰到現實就會像紙牌搭的房子一樣轟然倒塌。

In fact, there is a Don Quixote and a Sancho Panza in every one of us. Nearly everyone has some ideas on how the world could be put right — never mind whether the idea is practicable or not. And nearly everyone has that practical streak which says: look out for yourself, get as much as you can for yourself, and don't worry about the rest of the world. In some people the Sancho Panza is stronger, and in some people it is the Don Quixote.

事實上，我們每個人心中都有一個乾訶德和一個桑乔·潘薩。幾乎每個人都有一些關於如何讓世界變好的想法——不管這些想法是否可行。幾乎每個人也都有那種務實的一面，它說：照顧好你自己，為自己儘量多爭取，別操心世界其他的事。有些人心中的桑乔·潘薩更強一些，有些人心中的乾訶德更強一些。

You find the Don Quixotes and the Sancho Panzas in every walk of life. There is the painter who believes in their own genius, and would rather starve than paint something that could be sold easily. And there is the artist who works only for their bread and butter, and does not care in the least whether their products are great works of art or not.

各行各業都能找到乾訶德和桑乔·潘薩。有些畫家堅信自己的天才，寧願餓死也不願畫那些容易賣掉的東西。也有些藝術家只為謀生而創作，絲毫不在乎自己的作品是否算得上偉大的藝術品。

There is the author who writes only books which contain, in their opinion, an important message, whether they have a big sale or not. And there is the "hack", as they are called, who will write anything that sells.

有些作家只寫在他們看來包含重要訊息的書，不管是否暢銷。也有所謂的「文字匠」，什麼能賣就寫什麼。

You can find Don Quixotes and Sancho Panzas easily and quickly. You can find Don Quixotes amongst doctors and lawyers, amongst Members of Parliament, and in government.

你能輕易而快速地找到乾訶德和桑乔·潘薩。在醫生和律師中、在國會議員中、在政府裡，你都能找到乾訶德。

But there are also the people who are able to strike the right balance between them — people who are inspired by high ideas, but are also able to make them practical and workable.

但也有一些人能夠在兩者之間取得恰當的平衡——他們被崇高的理想所鼓舞，同時也能使這些理想付諸實際、可行運作。

There is hardly anything great and good in the world that was not at first declared cranky, fantastic, the outcome of a deluded mind, and so these things might have been, if the people concerned had only been dreamers, Don Quixotes. But they could harness the plain commonsense of Sancho Panza to their dreams, and so make them real and practical.

世界上幾乎所有偉大而美好的事物，起初都曾被宣稱為古怪的、異想天開的、妄想的產物——如果相關的人只是空想家、乾訶德，那這些事物確實可能就只是空想。但他們能夠將桑乔·潘薩那種樸實的常識與他們的夢想結合起來，使之成為現實而可行的。

And now you can see that Wolfram in his Parsifal story, and Cervantes in his story of Don Quixote, are both making the same point. Parsifal cannot come to the Grail without Feirefiz, and Don Quixote must have his Sancho Panza beside him — or, in other words: high hopes and ambitions are fine, but you must have common sense trotting beside you.

現在你們可以看到，沃夫朗在他的帕西法爾故事裡，和塞萬提斯在他的乾訶德故事裡，表達的其實是同一個要點。帕西法爾沒有費雷費茲就無法到達聖杯，乾訶德身邊必須有桑乔·潘薩——換句話說：崇高的希望和抱負固然好，但你必須有常識在身旁相伴。

But now, look at the whole thing from the side of Feirefiz, or from the side of Sancho Panza. Would Feirefiz have come to the Grail without Parsifal? It is through Parsifal that Feirefiz is brought to the Grail. On his own, Feirefiz would never get to the Grail.

但現在，讓我們從費雷費茲或桑乔·潘薩的角度來看整件事。沒有帕西法爾，費雷費茲能到達聖杯嗎？正是通過帕西法爾，費雷費茲才被帶到聖杯面前。靠他自己，費雷費茲永遠到不了聖杯。

And what about Sancho Panza? Without Don Quixote, Sancho Panza would have remained all his life a brutish oaf, a coarse lout who could see no further than his next plate of sausage and onions.

那桑乔·潘薩呢？沒有乾訶德，桑乔·潘薩一輩子都只會是一個粗野的笨蛋，一個粗俗的鄉巴佬，目光不超過下一盤香腸和洋蔥。

But in all the silly escapades of his master, Sancho Panza comes to realise the noble motives, the high ideals, which move Don Quixote. And, although all those lofty ideals usually end with Don Quixote getting hurt, Sancho Panza learns to respect these ideals — he discovers that great ideals are something to be respected, even if they lead to dismal failure.

但在他主人所有愚蠢的冒險中，桑乔·潘薩逐漸認識到驅動乾訶德的那些崇高動機和崇高理想。儘管那些崇高的理想通常都以乾訶德受傷收場，桑乔·潘薩卻學會了尊重這些理想——他發現偉大的理想是值得尊重的，即使它們導致了慘淡的失敗。

And so, when Don Quixote dies, Sancho Panza speaks with deep affection of his master, and with great respect. Don Quixote, a crank and a fool though he was, has lifted Sancho Panza from his oafish existence to an awareness of ideals, he has become a better man.

所以，當乾訶德去世時，桑乔·潘薩以深深的愛和崇高的敬意談論他的主人。乾訶德雖然是個怪人和傻瓜，卻把桑乔·潘薩從粗鄙的生存狀態提升到了對理想的覺知，使他成為了一個更好的人。

I said before that we each have something of Don Quixote in us, and something of Sancho Panza. And it is very tempting to think: I will let Sancho Panza have his way, I will follow Sancho Panza and forget that silly dreamer, Don Quixote. But then Sancho Panza is the poorer for it — he will only become more and more brutish and oafish, and so will you.

我之前說過，我們每個人心中都有一些乾訶德的成分，也有一些桑乔·潘薩的成分。讓桑乔·潘薩說了算、跟著桑乔·潘薩走、忘掉那個愚蠢的夢想家乾訶德，是很誘人的想法。但那樣的話桑乔·潘薩反而更貧乏——他只會變得越來越粗野和遲鈍，你也是如此。

And, you know, Cervantes, the writer who invented Don Quixote, who made such savage fun of this poor demented fellow who fights against windmills, Cervantes himself could not help liking Don Quixote — he could not help feeling a kind of kinship with Don Quixote. You can easily see why.

而且，你們知道嗎，塞萬提斯，這位創造了乾訶德的作家，他如此辛辣地嘲弄這個與風車搏鬥的可憐瘋子，塞萬提斯自己卻忍不住喜歡乾訶德——忍不住對乾訶德有一種惺惺相惜的感覺。原因不難理解。

Take this famous battle with the windmills — Don Quixote sees something in the windmills which no one else sees: giants, demons, monsters. But now, ask yourselves — what is a poet? A poet is someone who sees the same things you and I see, but they see more, they see something we do not perceive.

就拿這場著名的風車之戰來說——乾訶德在風車中看到了別人看不到的東西：巨人、惡魔、怪物。但現在，問問你們自己——什麼是詩人？詩人就是看到你我同樣看到的事物的人，但他們看到了更多，看到了我們察覺不到的東西。

To be an artist, a poet or a painter, you must have a good dose of Don Quixote, a good dose of that imagination which could see giants where we see only windmills.

要成為一個藝術家、詩人或畫家，你必須有一大劑量的乾訶德——一大劑量的那種能在我們只看到風車的地方看到巨人的想像力。

I could well imagine a poet writing verses in which they compare a windmill — or, for that matter, any large piece of machinery — with a giant or a monster. And if you think of some factory towns, darkened by smoke from belching chimneys, the idea of some smoke-belching dragon has come to more than one writer.

我完全能夠想像一位詩人寫詩，將一座風車——或者任何大型機器——比作一個巨人或怪物。如果你想到某些工廠城鎮被噴煙的煙囪所籠罩的景象，噴煙巨龍的意象不止出現在一位作家的腦海中。

The poet needs some of that imagination which Don Quixote has. Of course, there is a difference between being carried away by his imagination, as Don Quixote was, and the poet who controls the imagination and creates a work of art.

詩人需要乾訶德所擁有的那種想像力。當然，像乾訶德那樣被想像力牽著走是一回事，而詩人駕馭想像力並創造出一件藝術品則是另一回事。

But the vivid imagination must be there in the poet — and that is why the artist, the poet, the painter, will always have a weak spot for Don Quixote: they feel a distant kinship.

但詩人心中必須有那份鮮活的想像力——這就是為什麼藝術家、詩人、畫家永遠會對乾訶德有一份偏愛：他們感受到一種遙遠的親緣。

And the Sancho Panzas of the world, who only laugh at Don Quixote, are also the people to whom all art — poetry, painting, music — means not so very much.

而世上的桑乔·潘薩們，那些只會嘲笑乾訶德的人，也正是對所有藝術——詩歌、繪畫、音樂——都不太在意的人。

I said, once before, "... a good book not only tells a story — it tells you also something about yourself." Wolfram's *Parzival* is such a "good book", and so is the story of Don Quixote.

我以前曾說過：「……一本好書不僅講一個故事——它也告訴你一些關於你自己的事。」沃夫朗的《帕西法爾》就是這樣一本「好書」，乾訶德的故事也是如此。

I have already mentioned here Henry Dunant, the founder of the Red Cross. The idea which came to him after the battle of Solferino — the idea that all nations should agree to treat wounded enemies as they would treat their own — that idea was pure Don Quixote at the time, an impracticable fantasy. But he was also a practical man, a business man, and he made his idea a practical reality.

我之前已經提到過亨利·杜南（Henry Dunant），紅十字會的創始人。索爾費里諾戰役（Solferino）之後他萌生的那個想法——所有國家都應同意像對待自己的傷兵一樣對待受傷的敵人——在當時那個想法純粹就是乾訶德式的，一個不切實際的幻想。但他同時也是一個務實的人、一個商人，他把他的理想變成了切實的現實。

Or if Alexander Fleming had been only a Sancho Panza, he would have stopped going on with penicillin as soon as the "experts" condemned it. But he had enough of Don Quixote in him to carry on, even if he was called "fool". And in the end he succeeded.

又假如亞歷山大·弗萊明（Alexander Fleming）只是一個桑乔·潘薩，他一定會在「專家」否定盤尼西林的時候就放棄了。但他心中有足夠的乾訶德成分讓他堅持下去，即使被稱為「傻瓜」。最終他成功了。

Dr Thomas Barnardo was another one who had the right mixture of Don Quixote and Sancho Panza.

巴納多博士（Dr Thomas Barnardo）是另一個正確調和了乾訶德和桑乔·潘薩的人。

---

## 18.Simplicissimus 痴兒西木傳


The story of Don Quixote, a kind of parody of Parsifal, a Parsifal in reverse, was written around 1600 — a short time after the Great Armada of Spain had been destroyed in the English Channel.

乾訶德的故事，一種帕西法爾的滑稽模仿、一個顛倒的帕西法爾，大約寫於一六○○年——西班牙的偉大無敵艦隊在英吉利海峽被摧毀後不久。

This story of Don Quixote marks the end of the "romances", the wonder tales about knights and fabulous quests. In 1600, the knight with his high ideals had been made ridiculous — a figure of fun — a creature who no longer fitted into the new age.

乾訶德的故事標誌著「浪漫傳奇」——關於騎士和奇妙冒險的故事——的終結。在一六○○年，懷著崇高理想的騎士已被弄成了滑稽可笑的人物——一個不再適合新時代的角色。

And what now came over Europe were wars which were, indeed, no longer fought with any regard for the rules of chivalry. What now came over Europe were the Wars of Religion — the wars between Roman Catholics and Protestants. The worst of these religious wars was in Germany. It is called, after its long duration, the Thirty Years' War (1618-48).

接下來降臨到歐洲的是戰爭，而且的確不再遵守任何騎士精神的規則。接下來降臨到歐洲的是宗教戰爭——天主教徒和新教徒之間的戰爭。這些宗教戰爭中最慘烈的發生在德國。因為持續時間之長，它被稱為三十年戰爭（1618-48）。

The political details, the battles, the beginning and end of that Thirty Years' War, do not concern us here. It was a war full of inhuman brutalities, a war in which the soldiers of either party robbed, plundered and murdered the civilian population without much regard as to which side they were on.

那場三十年戰爭的政治細節、戰役、起因和結局，在這裡我們不必關心。那是一場充滿非人殘暴行為的戰爭，雙方的士兵都搶劫、掠奪和屠殺平民百姓，根本不太在乎他們屬於哪一方。

Any troop of soldiers coming upon a village or homestead would regard the peasants as their legitimate prey, and do with them as they liked — burn houses and crops, and take the sheep and cattle.

任何一隊士兵遇到村莊或農舍，都把農民當作合法的獵物，想怎麼處置就怎麼處置——燒毀房屋和莊稼，奪走綿羊和牛。

At that time of terror, extending over thirty years, a German writer, Hans Jacob Christoffel von Grimmelshausen, wrote a book that has also become one of the classics of world literature. It is the story of a little boy, suddenly orphaned by the horrors of this war. There are many parts of that story which even in our time, used to atrocities as we are, make hair-raising reading. But this boy, uneducated and ignorant, is also a kind of Parsifal, who enters the world without preparation. And he is a Parsifal who has very much come down in the world — he is not the son of a king and a fine lady, he is not even a landed gentleman, like Don Quixote. He is the son of simple peasants.

在那段長達三十年的恐怖時期，一位德國作家，漢斯·雅各布·克里斯多夫·馮·格里美豪森（Hans Jacob Christoffel von Grimmelshausen），寫了一本同樣成為世界文學經典的書。它講的是一個小男孩的故事，因這場戰爭的恐怖而突然淪為孤兒。故事中的許多段落，即使在我們這個對暴行已習以為常的時代，讀起來仍然令人毛骨悚然。但這個沒有受過教育、一無所知的男孩，也是一種帕西法爾，毫無準備地進入了這個世界。而他是一個已經大大落魄了的帕西法爾——他不是國王和貴婦人的兒子，他甚至不是一個鄉紳，像乾訶德那樣。他是普通農民的兒子。

This boy is not going out into the world urged on by the desire for knightly honours, he is driven from his home when soldiers burn it down and kill his parents.

這個男孩不是出於對騎士榮譽的嚮往而走向世界的，而是在士兵燒毀他的家、殺害他的父母之後，被迫離開了家園。

So, you see, with this book we are no longer in the world of lofty principles, of noble aims, of knights sworn to protect the weak and poor. We are in a world of harsh and brutal realities.

所以你看，在這本書裡，我們已不再身處崇高原則、高貴目標、立誓保護弱小的騎士的世界。我們身處嚴酷而殘暴的現實世界。

Yet there is in this boy still something of the innocence, something of that purity, which was in Parsifal, which was in Caspar Hauser.

然而，這個男孩身上仍然有某種天真、某種純潔——那種存在於帕西法爾身上、存在於卡斯帕·豪澤（Caspar Hauser）身上的品質。

This boy — like Parsifal — does not know his name; the first person he meets eventually gives him a name: "Simplicissimus", which is Latin, meaning "the most simple-minded". And that is also the name of the book — *Simplicissimus*.

這個男孩——像帕西法爾一樣——不知道自己的名字；他遇到的第一個人最終給了他一個名字：「痴兒西木」（Simplicissimus），這是拉丁文，意思是「最單純的人」。這也是這本書的書名——《痴兒西木傳》。

Now when you hear parts of this story, remember that there are children of this kind living today — orphans of wars they cannot understand, waifs and strays torn from their homes by some twist of politics; there are such children today in many parts of the world, in Cambodia, in Bosnia and in Rwanda in Africa. The tales of some of these children of our time might not be so different from the tale of this boy of the Thirty Years' War, 300 years ago.

當你們聽到這個故事的片段時，請記住今天仍有這樣的孩子——因他們無法理解的戰爭而成為孤兒，因政治的某些變故而被從家中連根拔起的流浪兒；在今天的世界許多地方——在柬埔寨、在波士尼亞、在非洲的盧安達——都有這樣的孩子。我們這個時代某些孩子的故事，與三百年前三十年戰爭中這個男孩的故事或許並無太大不同。

The story is told by the hero, Simplicissimus, himself, who remembers as a grown-up man the stormy events of his childhood.

這個故事由主人公痴兒西木自己講述，他以成年人的身份回憶童年那些風雨飄搖的往事。

When soldiers have burned the homestead, the boy runs away, not knowing where to go and what to do. He comes into a forest, and there he finds a hermit, and now the following dialogue takes place. Try and notice the similarities with Parsifal, but also the differences.

當士兵們燒毀了農舍，男孩逃了出來，不知道該去哪裡、該做什麼。他來到一片森林，在那裡找到了一位隱士，接下來的對話就此展開。試著注意與帕西法爾的相似之處，也要注意其中的不同。

> Hermit: What is your name?
> Simplicissimus: My name is "boy".
> H: I can see well enough that you are not a girl. But how did your father and mother call you?
