> S: What is a father or mother?
> H: Well, who gave you that shirt you are wearing?
> S: Oh, that was my mam.
> H: And what did your mam call you?
> S: She said: "boy" — or sometimes "rascal", or "silly ass", or "good-for-nothing".
> H: Who was your mam's husband?
> S: She had no such thing.
> H: Surely there was a man living with her?
> S: Yes, that was my dad.
> H: What was his name?
> S: His name was "dad".
> H: Did your mam call him "dad", or did she sometimes use another name?
> S: Yes — she called him sometimes "drunken pig", or "lazy brute".
> H: You are an ignorant creature — you don't know your own name, nor the name of your mam and dad!
> S: Neither do you, Mister Clever.
> H: Have your mam and dad taught you to do God's bidding?
> S: No, there was a girl who looked after our bedding.
> H: Have you not been taught the Lord's Prayer?
> S: That they did. Our Father which art heaven, hallowed name, to thy kingdom come, thy will come down on earth as it says in heaven, give us debts as we give our debtors, lead us not into temptation but deliver us from the kingdom, the power and the glory for ever. Amen.
> H: God help us — do you know nothing about our Lord?
> S: Oh yes — my mam brought him home from church and put him on the wall of the room we ate in.
> H: You poor boy. If I only knew where your parents are, I would bring you home.
> S: Wouldn't be much use — the house is burnt down.
> H: Who were the people who burnt the house?
> S: There were some iron men — they sat on things as big as oxen, but had no horns. And those men slaughtered our pigs and sheep and cows, and so I ran away, and then was the house burnt.
> H: Where was your dad then?
> S: Oh, the iron men took him and tied him up and put salt on the soles of his bare feet and then our old goat was set to lick his soles — and dad laughed and laughed, and then in the end he told the iron man where he had hidden a pot, and they dug it up and it was full of round things that glittered.
> H: And what happened then?
> S: I don't know. My mam had run away, and the girl screamed, and I ran away too, and the house was burnt. And now I don't know where to go.
> H: You can stay with me, lad, and I will try to look after you, and to educate you as a good Christian.

> 隱士：你叫什麼名字？
> 痴兒西木：我叫「小子」。
> 隱：我看得出你不是女孩。可你爸媽怎麼叫你？
> 西：什麼是爸媽？
> 隱：那，你身上穿的這件衣服是誰給的？
> 西：噢，是我媽。
> 隱：那你媽怎麼叫你？
> 西：她叫我「小子」——有時候也叫「小壞蛋」，或者「蠢驢」，或者「沒出息的」。
> 隱：你媽的丈夫是誰？
> 西：她沒有那種東西。
> 隱：肯定有一個男人跟她住在一起吧？
> 西：有的，那是我爹。
> 隱：他叫什麼名字？
> 西：他叫「爹」。
> 隱：你媽叫他「爹」，還是有時候叫別的名字？
> 西：有——她有時候叫他「醉豬」，或者「懶鬼」。
> 隱：你真是個無知的傢伙——連自己的名字都不知道，也不知道你爸媽的名字！
> 西：你也不知道呀，聰明先生。
> 隱：你爸媽有沒有教你遵行上帝的旨意？
> 西：沒有，有個姑娘幫我們整理被褥。
> 隱：那你沒有被教過主禱文嗎？
> 西：教過的。我們在天上的父，願名聖，願國來，願旨意行在地上如在天上說的一樣，赦免我們的債如同我們赦免人的債，不叫我們遇見試探，救我們脫離國度、權柄、榮耀直到永遠。阿門。
> 隱：天啊——你對我們的主一無所知嗎？
> 西：噢知道——我媽從教堂把他帶回家，掛在我們吃飯的房間牆上了。
> 隱：可憐的孩子。要是我知道你父母在哪兒就好了，我就送你回家。
> 西：送回去也沒用——房子已經燒掉了。
> 隱：是什麼人燒了房子？
> 西：有些鐵人——他們騎在像牛那麼大的東西上面，但沒有角。那些人把我們的豬、羊、牛都宰了，所以我就逃跑了，然後房子就燒了。
> 隱：那你爹呢？
> 西：噢，鐵人把他抓走綁起來，在他光腳底上撒鹽，然後讓我們的老山羊來舔他的腳底——爹就笑啊笑啊，最後他告訴鐵人他把一個罐子藏在哪裡，他們就把它挖出來了，裡面裝滿了圓圓的閃亮東西。
> 隱：後來呢？
> 西：我不知道。我媽跑掉了，那個姑娘尖叫，我也跑了，房子就燒了。現在我不知道該去哪裡。
> 隱：你可以跟我住，孩子，我會照顧你，教你做一個好基督徒。

The next part of the story is told by Simplicissimus himself — he looks back, as a grown-up man — to his first weeks with the Hermit:

故事的下一部分由痴兒西木自己講述——他以成年人的身份回顧他與隱士共度的最初幾週：

> Now when I first saw the Hermit read the Bible, I could not conceive with whom he should speak so secretly, for I saw the moving of his lips, yet no man spoke to him. I marked, however, by his eyes that it all had something to do with the book.
>
> So I marked where he kept it, and when he had laid it aside, I crept there and opened it, and at my first attempt came upon the story of Job and the picture that stood at the beginning of the chapter. I looked at the figures in the picture, and I asked them all kinds of questions. They gave me no answer, and I got very cross with them. "You little clowns!" I said. "I know you talk well enough with the old man — so why do you pretend to be silent with me? And I see you are up to no good, for that poor chap's house is in flames, and no one is doing anything about it. But you wait — I'll get some water and quench the fire." I got up to fetch water, when the hermit came in and asked: "Where are you going?"
>
> "Look," I said, "I know what's going on in that book. The house is a-burning, and soon there will be more iron men coming to kill the sheep as they have done with ours!"
>
> "Stay," said the Hermit, "can't you see these figures are not alive?" To which I answered, "It's you who can't see what they are up to — but I know, I have seen it all happen before. Keep an eye on the sheep whilst I go to fetch water."
>
> "Child," said the Hermit, "they are not alive — they are only made to call up before your eyes things long past."
>
> "How could that be?" said I, "seeing that you have long talks with them?"
>
> And then the Hermit could not help laughing, and said, "They can't talk — but what they do, and what they are, I can see from these black lines." But I answered, "My eyes are better than yours, and I can't see anything on the lines but black squiggles. So how can you see more than I do?" And he said, "I will teach you this art called 'reading', and then you will see what I see in the lines."

> 當我頭一次看見隱士讀聖經時，我想不出他到底在跟誰說悄悄話，因為我看到他嘴唇在動，卻沒有人跟他說話。不過我從他的眼神看出，這一切都跟那本書有關。
>
> 於是我記住了他把書放在哪裡，等他把書放下以後，我就悄悄溜過去打開它，第一次翻開就看到了約伯的故事，以及章節開頭的那幅插畫。我看著畫裡的人物，問了他們各種各樣的問題。他們不回答，我就非常生氣。「你們這些小丑！」我說，「你們跟老人家明明聊得挺好——幹嘛跟我裝啞巴？而且我看你們也不是什麼好人，那個可憐人的房子都著火了，也沒人管。等著——我去打水來滅火。」我正要起身去打水，隱士走進來問：「你要去哪兒？」
>
> 「你看，」我說，「我知道那本書裡在幹嘛。房子著火了，很快又有鐵人來殺羊了，就像他們殺我們的羊一樣！」
>
> 「站住，」隱士說，「你看不出這些人不是活的嗎？」我回答說：「看不出他們在做什麼的是你——我知道，我以前都看過這些事發生。你看好羊，我去打水。」
>
> 「孩子，」隱士說，「他們不是活的——他們只是用來在你眼前喚起久遠以前的事。」
>
> 「怎麼可能呢？」我說，「你明明跟他們聊了那麼久。」
>
> 隱士忍不住笑了，說：「他們不會說話——但他們做什麼、是什麼，我都能從這些黑色的線條裡看出來。」但我回答說：「我的眼睛比你好，可我在那些線條上只看得見黑色的彎彎曲曲。你怎麼能比我看到更多呢？」他說：「我教你一門叫做『閱讀』的技藝，然後你就能看到我在那些線條裡看到的東西了。」

The boy was — for his simple-mindedness — called "Simplicissimus" by the Hermit. He spent two years in the solitude of the forest with the old man, learned to read the Bible, and about the Christian faith, but nothing else about the world.

男孩因為他的頭腦簡單，被隱士稱為「痴兒西木」。他與老人在森林的孤寂中度過了兩年，學會了讀聖經和基督教信仰，但對世界的其他一切一無所知。

And then, after two years, the Hermit died, and Simplicissimus left the forest. He was a very strange-looking creature then, dressed in rags, with his hair growing down to his shoulders. He had some horrifying adventures, running into soldiers torturing peasants, and into peasants taking their revenge on the soldiers. But, in the end, he found the governor of a city who took pity on the waif who could not say where he came from, and did not know where he was going to. This governor had the boy cleaned up and employed him as a page.

兩年之後，隱士去世了，痴兒西木離開了森林。那時候他的模樣很古怪，衣衫襤褸，頭髮長到肩膀。他經歷了一些恐怖的冒險，遇到了士兵拷打農民，也遇到了農民報復士兵。但最後，他遇到了一個城市的總督，總督可憐這個說不出自己從哪裡來、也不知道自己要去哪裡的流浪兒。總督讓人給男孩收拾乾淨，僱用他做侍童。

But Simplicissimus was utterly ignorant of the ways of the world — he made terrible blunders, and the other servants in the household made things worse by playing on his foolishness. Such an occasion was when he saw a dance for the first time in his life. He tells again the story in his own words:

但痴兒西木對世間的規矩一竅不通——他犯了許多可怕的錯誤，府中的其他僕人還利用他的無知使情況更糟。其中一次就是他第一次看到舞會的時候。他再次用自己的話講述了這個故事：

> I saw in that room gentlemen and ladies twisting about so quickly that everything spun about, and with such stamping and noise that I deemed they had all gone mad. I could not imagine what they could intend with this rage and fury. The very sight was so terrible that my hair stood on end.
>
> I saw that they were my governor's guests who, up till noon, had been in their right senses, and I wondered what had suddenly driven them to such madness.
>
> I wanted to know what this insane behaviour could mean, and so I asked another servant in the room what all this lunacy was about. And he, with a very serious face, told me that all the people present had agreed to stamp down the floor of the room — they wanted to break the floor.
>
> "Good heavens!" I said. "We are one floor up, and if the floor breaks, we shall all fall a long way, and break our legs and necks!"
>
> "Yes, indeed," said the fellow, "but they have been drinking, and in their intoxication just don't care what's going to happen! What's more," he said, "when they feel that they are in danger of falling down, each man grabs a lady, for it is said that when couples fall, holding one another, neither partner will suffer any great harm."
>
> I believed all this tale, and there fell upon me such a fear of cracking my skull or breaking my bones when the floor gave way that I knew not where to stand. And, just then, the musicians who had paused for a while struck up again, and I saw every man run to a lady, like soldiers when the trumpets sound the alarm. I expected that any moment now the floor would fall to pieces under me — I felt myself already plunging down. And now these madmen began to jump, so that I could really feel the floor shaking. I thought, "Now your life is at stake!" and in my deadly fear I looked for a partner to fall down with.
>
> I saw a very noble lady in a corner, in conversation with my governor. I caught her all unawares by the arm, like a bear, and clung to her — but then she struggled to shake me off. I was in real despair, and started to scream as if they would murder me.
>
> The music stopped all of a sudden — the dancers and their partners stopped their hopping, and the noble lady complained loudly about that lout (meaning me) who still clung desperately to her arm.
>
> My governor was greatly annoyed, and commanded I should be soundly whipped and then locked up somewhere. Yet the grooms who had sympathy for me spared me the whipping and locked me up in a goose-pen under the staircase.

> 我看見房間裡的紳士和淑女們飛快地扭轉身體，所有東西都在旋轉，跺腳的聲音那麼響，我以為他們全都瘋了。我無法想像他們這種暴怒和狂躁到底要幹什麼。光是這副景象就已經可怕得讓我毛骨悚然了。
>
> 我看到他們是我家總督的客人，直到中午還好好的，我不明白是什麼突然把他們逼瘋的。
>
> 我想知道這種瘋狂行為是什麼意思，就問了房間裡的另一個僕人，這一切瘋瘋癲癲是怎麼回事。他一本正經地告訴我，在場所有人都約好了要把地板跺塌——他們想踩破地板。
>
> 「天哪！」我說，「我們在二樓，要是地板塌了，我們都會摔很遠，腿和脖子都得摔斷！」
>
> 「可不是嘛，」那傢伙說，「但他們都喝了酒，醉了就不管後果了！而且，」他說，「當他們覺得要摔下去的時候，每個男人就抓住一個女人，因為據說摟在一起摔下去的話，兩個人都不會受太大的傷。」
>
> 我全信了這套鬼話，一想到地板塌下去我會腦袋開花或者骨頭摔斷，就嚇得不知道往哪兒站。就在這時，休息了一會兒的樂師們又奏起音樂，我看見每個男人都朝一位女士跑去，就像聽到警報號角的士兵。我覺得地板隨時會在我腳下碎裂——我已經感覺自己在往下墜了。然後這些瘋子開始跳起來，我真的能感覺到地板在搖。我心想：「現在你的命危在旦夕了！」在致命的恐懼中，我趕緊找一個一起摔下去的搭檔。
>
> 我看見角落裡一位非常高貴的女士，正在和我的總督說話。我像一隻熊一樣出其不意地抓住她的手臂，死死抱住——她掙扎著想擺脫我。我真的絕望了，開始尖叫，好像他們要殺我似的。
>
> 音樂突然停了——舞者和他們的舞伴們停止了蹦跳，那位高貴的女士大聲抱怨那個粗人（就是我）還在死死抓著她的手臂不放。
>
> 我的總督非常惱怒，下令痛打我一頓然後鎖起來。但那些同情我的馬夫免了我一頓打，把我鎖進了樓梯下面的鵝圈裡。

Yet this same Simplicissimus who acts so foolishly looks with his childish mind upon the world in which he finds himself, and observes things which the normal "clever" people never notice. He says:

然而，就是這個行為如此愚蠢的痴兒西木，用他那顆童稚的心審視他所身處的世界，觀察到了那些正常的「聰明人」從不留意的事情。他說：

> When I was with my old Hermit, I had always wondered why God had made it a law that one should not worship idols. Why should anybody be so silly as to worship anything else but God? But no sooner had I come into the great world, than I marked how wellnigh everybody had his own special idol to worship. Some had their god in their moneybags, and they put all their trust in this god. Others made popularity their god, and they fancied that this god would lift them up to become half gods themselves. And there were women, who made their own beauty their idol. Every day, instead of other offerings, they adorned that god with paint, ointment, powders, and gave many hours to the service of that idol.
>
> Even as much of these, and a great number of other idols, were worshipped, so the true God was held in contempt. I had read of Christ's command that we should not only love our brothers, but even our enemies. Yet, amongst those who called themselves Christians, there was not only hatred of enemies, but so much envy and malice and quarrels between brothers, and brothers and sisters, and parents and children, as if they had never heard of that command.
>
> As to the competition between businesses of the same trade — I would not have thought it possible that a man should wish another man's ruin in order to increase his own profits!
>
> And I wondered still more when I saw people sit down in a game of cards or dice. Of them that took part, only one could win, but they all expected to win, which was utterly foolish — yet they who did so called me a simpleton.
>
> It came to me that to be honest and God-fearing and considerate of others was to be stupid in this world — but I would rather be that kind of fool than be called clever and be of their kind!

> 跟老隱士在一起的時候，我一直在想，上帝為什麼要定下不可崇拜偶像的律法。誰會蠢到去崇拜上帝以外的東西呢？可是我一進入這個大千世界，就發現幾乎每個人都有自己專門崇拜的偶像。有些人把錢袋當作他們的神，把一切信賴都寄託在這位神身上。有些人把名望當作他們的神，幻想這位神能把他們提升為半神。還有些女人把自己的美貌當作偶像。她們每天不是獻上別的祭品，而是用脂粉、油膏、粉末來裝飾這位神，每天花好幾個小時來侍奉這尊偶像。
>
> 這些偶像，還有許許多多其他偶像，受到如此多的崇拜，真正的上帝卻遭到蔑視。我讀過基督的誡命，說我們不僅要愛弟兄，甚至要愛仇敵。然而，在那些自稱基督徒的人當中，不僅有對仇敵的仇恨，兄弟之間、兄弟姊妹之間、父母和孩子之間還有那麼多的嫉妒、惡意和爭吵，好像他們從來沒聽過那條誡命似的。
>
> 至於同行之間的競爭——我原本以為不可能有人會希望別人破產來增加自己的利潤！
>
> 當我看到人們圍坐在一起打牌或擲骰子時，我更加驚訝。參加賭博的人裡面只有一個人能贏，但他們全都指望自己能贏，這簡直愚蠢透頂——可就是這些人管我叫傻瓜。
>
> 我明白了，在這個世界上，做一個誠實、敬畏上帝、顧及他人的人就是愚蠢——但我寧願做那種傻瓜，也不願被稱為聰明人、跟他們一樣！

This Simplicissimus, this late Parsifal, of 300 years ago, is — as you see — much nearer to our time, and a good deal that this simple-minded fellow said could just as well be said in the present day.

這位痴兒西木，這位三百年前的晚期帕西法爾——如你們所見——離我們的時代近得多了，這個心思單純之人所說的許多話，放在今天來說也完全適用。

---

## 19.The Pure Fool 純真的愚者


Parsifal, Don Quixote and Simplicissimus — the poor waif — have all three something in common. And this "common denominator" is already present in the name of Parsifal.

帕西法爾、乾訶德和痴兒西木——可憐的流浪兒——這三者有一個共同點。而這個「公約數」已經存在於帕西法爾的名字之中了。

I do not have to tell you that many books have been written about Parsifal, and all these books try in one way or another to interpret Parsifal's name. It is quite clear to every student of the story that, as in Wolfram's story everything has a meaning, there must be a special meaning in the name of his hero. But what this meaning is — that is a point where opinions differ widely.

我不必告訴你們，已經有許多書寫過帕西法爾，所有這些書都以某種方式試圖解讀帕西法爾的名字。對於每一個研究這個故事的人來說，很顯然，既然沃夫朗的故事中萬事皆有深意，他的主人公的名字一定有特殊的含義。但這個含義到底是什麼——在這一點上意見分歧很大。

As the name is first used by Chretien de Troyes, the French troubadour, it is quite reasonable to assume that the name is made up of French — medieval French — words. And then, according to the old tongue, *langue d'oc*, as it is called, the name would mean "through the valley" — that means a hero who passes through the valley to gain the mountain peak, or in other words, "through darkness into light". That is one interpretation.

由於這個名字最早是法國吟遊詩人克雷蒂安·德·特魯瓦（Chretien de Troyes）使用的，合理地假設這個名字由法語——中世紀法語——的詞彙構成。那麼，按照古代的「奧克語」（*langue d'oc*），這個名字的意思是「穿越山谷」——也就是說一位英雄穿越山谷到達山峰，換句話說就是「穿越黑暗進入光明」。這是一種詮釋。

However, in that ancient French language, the *langue d'oc*, there were no uniform spelling rules, and with a slightly different spelling the name could mean "pierce the veil" — it means a hero who sees his life and his destiny first veiled — he does not know what he is meant to do, but in the end he breaks through the veils, the veils are pierced. That is a second interpretation.

然而，在那種古法語，即奧克語中，並沒有統一的拼寫規則，稍微不同的拼寫就會讓這個名字的意思變成「刺穿面紗」——意思是一位英雄起初看到自己的人生和命運被面紗遮蔽——他不知道自己的使命是什麼，但最終他衝破了面紗，面紗被刺穿了。這是第二種詮釋。

There is still a third one. The German musician, Wagner, was not a professional etymologist (the science which deals with the origin of words is called "etymology"). His profession was to write operas — and he is in the world of opera what Beethoven is in the world of symphonies.

還有第三種。德國音樂家華格納（Wagner）不是專業的詞源學家（研究詞彙起源的學問叫做「詞源學」）。他的專業是寫歌劇——他在歌劇界的地位就如同貝多芬（Beethoven）在交響樂界的地位。

Now Wagner wanted to write an opera, *Parsifal* — in fact, he did write it, and I only wish you could go to hear it: it is often performed at Easter, for some of the most moving music ever written is contained in the scene where Parsifal meets the Hermit on Good Friday.

華格納想寫一部歌劇《帕西法爾》——實際上他確實寫了，我真希望你們能去聽一聽：它常在復活節期間演出，因為帕西法爾在受難日遇見隱士的那一場，包含了有史以來最動人的音樂。

Now when Wagner was working on this opera, *Parsifal*, he did not go about it as any other composer would — Verdi, or Mozart, for instance. If they had a story they liked, a plot they liked, they were only concerned to get the music for the story — they were not worried about the origin of the story.

華格納創作這部歌劇《帕西法爾》時，他的做法與其他作曲家不同——比如威爾第（Verdi）或莫札特（Mozart）。如果他們有了一個喜歡的故事、一個喜歡的情節，他們只關心為故事配上音樂——他們不在乎故事的來源。

But Wagner, being German, had this Teutonic streak of thoroughness — he had to go into every detail: where the story came from, what did Wolfram mean, what is the meaning of the name "Parsifal"?

但華格納身為德國人，有這種日耳曼式的徹底精神——他必須深入每一個細節：故事從何而來，沃夫朗的意思是什麼，「帕西法爾」這個名字的含義是什麼？

And he came up with a different answer. His idea was to ask: what does "Parsifal" mean in the Persian language? It had not occurred to anybody else — but when you consider that both Chretien de Troyes and Wolfram were adherents of that Manichean heresy which originated in Persia, it is not as far-fetched as it may sound. It would be quite in keeping with their ideas — and, moreover, the Persian meaning of the word "Parsifal" does convey something important and significant about the character of the hero.

他得出了一個不同的答案。他的想法是：「帕西法爾」在波斯語中是什麼意思？此前從未有人想到這一點——但你若想到克雷蒂安·德·特魯瓦和沃夫朗都是源自波斯的摩尼教異端的信徒，這就不像聽起來那麼牽強了。這完全符合他們的思想——而且，「帕西法爾」一詞的波斯語含義確實傳達了關於這位英雄性格的重要而有意義的東西。

In Persian, "Parsi-fal" means "the pure fool".

在波斯語中，「Parsi-fal」的意思是「純真的愚者」。

And that kind of innocence and purity is also in Parsifal, the pure fool.

那種天真和純潔也存在於帕西法爾——這位純真的愚者——身上。

And, as you remember, both Chretien and Wolfram go to great lengths to describe Parsifal in the beginning of the story as a "pure fool". Parsifal is very foolish when he asks the knights in the forest such questions as whether they were born with the armour on them — he is very foolish when he rides his horse into King Arthur's banqueting hall. But there is at the same time a childlike innocence, a childlike purity above the foolishness.

而且你們記得，克雷蒂安和沃夫朗都不遺餘力地在故事開頭把帕西法爾描繪成一個「純真的愚者」。帕西法爾在森林裡問騎士們是不是生來就穿著盔甲時非常愚蠢——他騎馬闖入亞瑟王的宴會大廳時也非常愚蠢。但與此同時，在那愚蠢之上有一種孩子般的天真、孩子般的純潔。

You only have to think of the story of Caspar Hauser, and you find the same foolishness, but at the same time this freshness, innocence, which is meant by the word "purity".

你只要想想卡斯帕·豪澤的故事，就會發現同樣的愚蠢，但同時也有這種「純潔」一詞所指的那種新鮮感和天真。

But I can give you an example of this "purity and foolishness" much nearer to us. Go to the kindergarten and look at some of the paintings done by little children. These kindergarten paintings are crude and simple things: no house looks like a real house, and often one is not certain what it is the children painted. Yet in these childish things, there is that innocence, that purity which makes these things something one can enjoy, something one can love, in spite of its being primitive.

但我可以給你們舉一個離我們更近的「純潔與愚蠢」的例子。到幼兒園去看看小孩子畫的畫。這些幼兒園的畫粗糙而簡單：沒有一座房子看起來像真正的房子，人們往往不確定孩子們畫的究竟是什麼。然而在這些幼稚的作品中，有一種天真、一種純潔，使得這些東西儘管原始，卻令人喜愛、令人享受。

But now what about Don Quixote? He is not young, he is dried-up and elderly — he is a fool who tilts at windmills — but his foolishness, too, has something childlike.

但乾訶德呢？他不年輕，他枯瘦而年邁——他是一個向風車衝鋒的傻瓜——但他的愚蠢也有些孩子氣的東西。

The children in the kindergarten take a bench and call it a boat, they crawl under a table and call it a cave or a house — and I remember from my own childhood that I used to knock down large thistles and nettles with a stick, pretending they were giants. Children live in a world of make-believe, in a world of imagination.

幼兒園裡的孩子把一條長凳當作一艘船，爬到桌子底下叫它洞穴或房子——我記得自己小時候常拿棍子打倒大薊和蕁麻，假裝它們是巨人。孩子們活在一個假扮的世界裡，活在一個想像的世界裡。

And Don Quixote, too, like a child, creates a make-believe world around him, but there is no cunning in him, he has no clever tricks to take advantage of others — and is, at heart, as simple as a child.

乾訶德也一樣，像孩子一樣在自己周圍創造了一個假扮的世界，但他心中沒有狡詐，沒有利用別人的聰明手段——從心底來說，他像孩子一樣單純。

Don Quixote, too, is a "pure fool", a fool who has retained a childlike purity.

乾訶德也是一個「純真的愚者」，一個保留了孩童般純潔的傻瓜。

And our last hero, Simplicissimus, is a real child — he is still a young boy when the horrors of war destroy his home and family. And he tells the Hermit of these horrors with that candid innocence of a child that does not yet understand cruelty. He retains this innocence when he leaves the forest. Like Parsifal, he has been isolated by the forest, and so enters the world in ignorance and makes foolish mistakes — he becomes the butt of jokes by people who feel themselves wonderfully clever if they can make a trap for the ignorant boy.

我們最後一位英雄，痴兒西木，是一個真正的孩子——當戰爭的恐怖摧毀了他的家園和家庭時，他還只是一個小男孩。他以一個尚不理解殘酷的孩子那坦率天真的口吻，向隱士講述了這些恐怖經歷。他離開森林時仍保持著這份天真。和帕西法爾一樣，他被森林隔絕了，因此以無知的狀態進入世界，犯了愚蠢的錯誤——他成了那些人的笑柄，那些人若能為無知的男孩設一個陷阱就覺得自己聰明極了。

But then the simpleton looks at the world around him, and his fresh and candid outlook is a good deal saner than the corrupt mentality of the people around him.

但這個單純的人審視周圍的世界，他那新鮮而坦誠的目光比他周圍那些人腐敗的心態清醒得多。

Simplicissimus, too, is a "pure fool".

痴兒西木也是一個「純真的愚者」。

But now I want to tell you about another such "pure fool", a fool who does not belong to literature, but to another medium of entertainment, to film — Charlie Chaplin.

但現在我想告訴你們另一個這樣的「純真的愚者」，一個不屬於文學、而屬於另一種娛樂媒介——電影——的愚者：查理·卓別林（Charlie Chaplin）。

Chaplin, too, presented in his films a little fool of a man, a down-and-out tramp, in ill-fitting clothes, ignorant and not at all clever in the ways of the world.

卓別林也在他的電影中呈現了一個愚蠢的小人物，一個窮困潦倒的流浪漢，穿著不合身的衣服，對世故人情一無所知，一點也不精明。

In one of his films, *Modern Times*, he is employed in a great factory, and the little man goes through hair-raising and wildly funny misadventures in his encounter with machines and with the assembly line. But, you know, when you see that film, your feeling, your sympathy, is always with him, with that ridiculous little man — you laugh with him, and not at him. That is how you laugh when a child says something that seems silly.

在他的一部電影《摩登時代》（*Modern Times*）中，他被僱在一家大工廠裡，這個小人物在與機器和流水線的遭遇中經歷了驚險而滑稽至極的種種不幸。但你知道嗎，當你看那部電影時，你的感情、你的同情始終與他在一起，與那個滑稽的小人物在一起——你是跟著他笑，而不是嘲笑他。那就像你聽到一個孩子說了些看似傻乎乎的話時的笑法。

Charlie Chaplin was also one of the "pure fools" — like Don Quixote, Simplicissimus and Parsifal.

查理·卓別林也是「純真的愚者」之一——和乾訶德、痴兒西木和帕西法爾一樣。

I think you can feel now that there is something special about these "pure fools" who turn up here and there as heroes in literature or on the screen. There is something special about them — and I want to conclude this main lesson by telling you what it is that these childlike people have.

我想你們現在可以感受到，這些在文學或銀幕上不時以英雄姿態出現的「純真的愚者」有些特別之處。他們身上有些特別的東西——我想以告訴你們這些童心未泯的人到底擁有什麼來結束這個主課程。

Some years ago a well-known writer, Arthur Koestler, wrote a book called *The Act of Creation* — it is a very thick volume which tackles a problem that no one has tackled before.

幾年前，一位知名作家亞瑟·乾斯勒（Arthur Koestler）寫了一本叫做《創造的行為》（*The Act of Creation*）的書——那是一本很厚的書，探討了一個前人從未探討過的問題。

The problem is this: there are certain people whom we call "creative". Some are scientists, some are doctors, some are poets, painters, musicians — but they have one thing in common: they are able to create something new, something which is not a repetition, a copy, an imitation, but new and original.

問題是這樣的：有些人我們稱之為「有創造力的人」。有些是科學家，有些是醫生，有些是詩人、畫家、音樂家——但他們有一個共同點：他們能夠創造出某種新的東西，某種不是重複、不是複製、不是模仿，而是嶄新而獨創的東西。

But although each one of them at times had great difficulties in getting their discovery or invention or creation recognised, in the end what they had to give succeeded — it had to succeed because the world and all the millions who cannot create depend on the few who have this gift.

但儘管他們每個人都曾在讓自己的發現、發明或創作獲得認可的過程中遇到極大的困難，最終他們所奉獻的東西還是成功了——它必須成功，因為世界和數以百萬計無法創造的人都依賴著擁有這種天賦的少數人。

Civilisation, progress, would come to a standstill without them.

沒有他們，文明和進步就會停滯不前。

It is therefore a question well worth asking: what happens when such a genius produces or invents or discovers something new?

因此，這是一個很值得提出的問題：當這樣一個天才創造、發明或發現某種新事物時，到底發生了什麼？

And the author of this book, Koestler, took the trouble to collect as many facts as he could about the conditions under which these great individuals did find or discover something new.

這本書的作者乾斯勒不辭辛勞地收集了盡可能多的關於這些偉大人物在什麼條件下發現或創造新事物的事實。

And the first thing he discovered was that in this respect there is no difference between the scientist or the inventor or the artist — the act of creation is the same, whether it is merely inventing a new gadget, or producing a symphony, or discovering one of the secrets of nature. In other words — what kind of idea I get depends on my training, but how I get a new idea is the same, no matter what my education and training is.

他發現的第一件事是，在這方面科學家、發明家和藝術家之間沒有區別——創造的行為是相同的，不管是僅僅發明一個新裝置，還是創作一部交響曲，還是發現自然的一個秘密。換句話說——我得到什麼樣的想法取決於我的訓練，但我如何得到一個新想法卻是一樣的，無論我受過什麼樣的教育和訓練。

And this "how" is always a kind of "going back", a kind of going back to a more primitive or childlike frame of mind.

而這個「如何」總是一種「回溯」，一種回到更原始或更孩童般心態的方式。

First of all, such a person can look at a thing they have known for years, as if they saw it for the first time, as if it were something new, fresh — something never seen before.

首先，這樣的人能夠看著一件他們認識多年的事物，彷彿第一次看到它一樣，彷彿它是某種新鮮的東西——某種從未見過的東西。

As long as you feel "familiar" with something, you say, "Oh, I have seen it, I know it ..." you will not get a new idea about it at all. The first thing is to look at it as if you were a child, seeing it for the first time.

只要你對某樣東西感到「熟悉」，你就會說：「哦，我見過了，我知道了……」你就根本不會對它產生新的想法。首先要做的是像一個孩子一樣去看它，彷彿第一次看到。

The second thing is to be deeply and wholeheartedly interested in this "new thing", just as a child would be interested in a new toy. Without real concern, real interest, nothing grows.

第二件事是全心全意地對這個「新事物」深感興趣，就像孩子對一個新玩具感興趣那樣。沒有真正的關注、真正的興趣，什麼也長不出來。

And the third thing is to get into a waking "dreaming" about this thing, as a child would daydream and invent all kinds of imaginary things about something they have seen or heard.

第三件事是進入一種對這件事物的清醒「夢想」狀態，就像孩子會對他們所見所聞的東西進行白日夢般的想像，編造各種各樣幻想的事物。

One great scientist, F.A. Kekule (1829-96), the discoverer of the benzene formula on which 90% of all industrial chemistry depends, this great man discovered that formula in a kind of daydream as he sat on top of a London bus. And he used to say to his students, "Gentlemen, the first thing you have to learn is to dream."

一位偉大的科學家凱庫勒（F.A. Kekule, 1829-96），發現了百分之九十的工業化學所依賴的苯分子式，這位偉人是在坐倫敦公共汽車時在一種白日夢中發現那個分子式的。他常對學生說：「先生們，你們要學的第一件事就是做夢。」

And it is the same with hundreds of other important and successful people — they used a dreamlike imagination, and let it roam about the thing that interested them, and after a longer or shorter time, the great idea came: first as a "hunch" and then as a certainty.

其他數百位重要而成功的人也是如此——他們運用一種夢幻般的想像力，讓它在他們感興趣的事物周圍自由遊蕩，經過或長或短的時間，偉大的想法就來了：先是一種「直覺」，然後變成確信。

A fresh outlook, a lively genuine interest, and free play of imagination — that is the recipe. But these are the three things which young children have naturally — to them the whole world is new, and all new things interest them, and they do imagine all kinds of things easily. The creative person has to become childlike again, or they cannot be creative.

一種新鮮的視角、一份生動真誠的興趣，以及想像力的自由發揮——這就是秘訣。但這恰恰是幼小的孩子天然擁有的三樣東西——對他們來說，整個世界都是新的，所有新事物都讓他們感興趣，而且他們能輕易地想像各種各樣的事物。有創造力的人必須再次變得像孩子一樣，否則就無法創造。

As this writer, Koestler, put it: you have to go back (back to childhood) to go forward with a new idea. It is like trying to make a great leap, a great jump — say, over a little stream. You take a few steps backward, to have space for a run — then up you run, and leap across the stream. And that is what every creative person has ever done — gone back to a moment of childish wonder, childish imagination, and from this moment of childishness they made the great leap forward that brought them to a new idea, success and fame.

正如作家乾斯勒所說：你必須回溯（回到童年）才能帶著新想法前進。這就像試圖做一次大跨躍、一次大跳——比方說，跳過一條小溪。你先退後幾步，好有助跑的空間——然後你跑起來，躍過小溪。每一個有創造力的人都是這麼做的——回到童年驚奇的那一刻、童年想像力的那一刻，然後從這個孩童般的時刻完成了帶他們走向新想法、成功和名望的偉大飛躍。

In all creative people, there is something that remained childlike, something that remained — to use our words — a "pure fool". And it is this child in them, this pure fool, that made them creative.

在所有有創造力的人身上，都有某種保持孩童般的東西，某種保持著——用我們的話來說——「純真的愚者」的東西。正是他們心中的這個孩子、這個純真的愚者，使他們具有了創造力。

And, you know, this rather new idea, that book by Koestler, is not quite so new as it seems.

而且，你們知道嗎，乾斯勒這本書裡這個相當新穎的想法，其實並不像看起來那麼新。

In Grimm's fairy tales, there is the following story. Once upon a time, there was a king who had three sons — the two older sons were clever, but the youngest boy was very foolish: his mind was like that of a child.

在格林童話（Grimm）裡，有這樣一個故事。從前有一位國王，他有三個兒子——兩個年長的兒子很聰明，但最小的男孩非常愚笨：他的心智就像一個孩子。

Now the king did not know to which one of his three sons he should leave the kingdom when he died. He decided he would give the three sons an almost impossible task, and the one who could perform the task should inherit the kingdom. The task the king gave to his three sons was this: they should bring him ten yards of linen, spun so finely that it could be put into one nutshell.

國王不知道他死後應該把王國留給三個兒子中的哪一個。他決定給三個兒子一個幾乎不可能完成的任務，能完成任務的人就繼承王國。國王給三個兒子的任務是：帶給他十碼亞麻布，紡得如此精細，能放進一個堅果殼裡。

The first son went to Holland, for Holland was famous for its fine linen, but he could not find any linen fine enough to get ten yards into a nutshell. The second son went to Silesia, another country with a great reputation for fine linen, but he too had no success. The third one — the silly, childish, youngest one — just walked about, got into a forest, and suddenly something fell right in front of him and split open. It was a nutshell with yards and yards of the finest linen in it! And so he inherited the kingdom.

大兒子去了荷蘭，因為荷蘭以精細的亞麻布聞名，但他找不到細到能把十碼塞進堅果殼裡的亞麻布。二兒子去了西里西亞（Silesia），另一個以精細亞麻布享有盛名的國家，但他也沒有成功。第三個——那個傻乎乎的、孩子氣的、最小的兒子——只是到處走走，進了一片森林，突然有什麼東西正好落在他面前，裂開了。那是一個堅果殼，裡面裝著好幾碼好幾碼最精細的亞麻布！於是他繼承了王國。

This old fairy tale, which superficially seems rather pointless, makes in fact the same point as the modern author, Koestler. The childlike son, the pure fool, can see a great and complicated problem in one comprehensive view — as we say, "in a nutshell" — and that is why he inherits the kingdom, quite rightly, just as all creative people to this day inherit the great kingdom of original thought, of new invention which brings the world forward.

這個古老的童話，表面上看來似乎毫無意義，實際上卻表達了與現代作家乾斯勒相同的觀點。那個孩子般的兒子、那個純真的愚者，能夠用一種綜觀的目光看清一個巨大而複雜的問題——用我們的話說，看到它的「精髓」（in a nutshell）——這就是為什麼他理所當然地繼承了王國，正如直到今天所有有創造力的人都繼承著原創思想、推動世界前進的新發明這個偉大的王國。

But, you see, all human creativeness, whether it is art or science or invention, has its source and origin in the one Being who is called the Creator. Every human act of creation is like a tiny drop from that great ocean of creativeness that is called "God".

但是，你們看，所有人類的創造力，不論是藝術、科學還是發明，都有其根源和起源於那位被稱為「造物主」的存有。每一個人類的創造行為，都像那被稱為「上帝」的偉大創造力海洋中的一滴小小的水珠。

And in every tiny act of creation, even when it is only a new and original idea in an essay you write, in the smallest act of human creation you take part — you participate — in the all-creativeness which is the true nature of God.

在每一個微小的創造行為中，即使只是你寫的一篇文章中一個嶄新而獨創的想法，在最小的人類創造行為中，你都參與了——融入了——那全然的創造力，而那正是上帝的真正本質。

It matters very little that Parsifal has turned against God — the conventional idea of God — but he has remained in his heart a pure fool, he has remained creative.

帕西法爾曾經背離上帝——那個傳統觀念中的上帝——這並不太重要。重要的是他在心中始終是一個純真的愚者，始終保有創造力。

And that is why he can find the Grail, see the Grail and become King of the Grail — because, in the end, the creative power in himself can recognise and behold the true nature of God: creativeness.

這就是為什麼他能找到聖杯、看見聖杯並成為聖杯之王——因為，最終，他內在的創造力能夠認出並領悟上帝的真正本質：創造力本身。

Only those who can create can find the Grail, the true and unconventional meaning of God — God, the Creator.

只有能夠創造的人才能找到聖杯，找到上帝的真正而非傳統的意義——上帝，造物主。

The others — the people who only conform, who repeat what others have done, who never create anything — can only talk about God, they can call him Creator, but these are empty words. Only the creative spirit in human beings can truly and really understand the creative spirit in God.

其他的人——那些只會隨波逐流、重複別人做過的事、從不創造任何東西的人——只能談論上帝，他們能稱他為造物主，但那只是空洞的文字。只有人類中的創造精神才能真正地、切實地理解上帝中的創造精神。

That is why we say in the morning verse: "God's spirit lives and moves in light of sun and soul — in heights of worlds without, in depths of soul within."\* The spirit that created the world lives as creative power also in the soul — a drop from that great ocean of creative power that made the world.

這就是為什麼我們在晨頌中說：「上帝的靈活在陽光與靈魂的光中，活動於天地之高處，活在靈魂深處之內。」\* 創造了世界的靈，也作為創造力活在靈魂之中——那創造了世界的偉大創造力海洋中的一滴水珠。

But to find this creative power in yourself, that tiny drop of the creative world power, you have to become a pure fool, like Parsifal — you have to become like a young child. And that is what is meant by the words in the Gospel, the words Christ spoke: "Unless you become like little children you cannot enter the Kingdom of Heaven" (Luke 18:17).

但要在你自己身上找到這份創造力，這滴微小的世界創造力之水珠，你必須成為一個純真的愚者，像帕西法爾那樣——你必須變得像一個小孩子。這就是福音書中基督所說的話的含義：「你們若不變成小孩子的樣式，就不能進天國」（路加福音 18:17）。

But the Kingdom of Heaven is not a place — Christ himself said: "The Kingdom of Heaven is within you" (Luke 17:21); it is the kingdom of creative thought, of creative action. This is the kingdom which the foolish son in the fairy tale inherits. The Kingdom of Heaven is the power, the ability to create — and that is also the kingship given to Parsifal.

然而天國不是一個地方——基督自己說過：「天國就在你們心裡」（路加福音 17:21）；它是創造性思維、創造性行動的國度。這就是童話中那個愚蠢的兒子所繼承的王國。天國是創造的能力——這也是賦予帕西法爾的王權。

But, after all, we have to grow up, we cannot remain little children — we have to become adults and live in an adult world, with the worries and problems and responsibilities of adults. And so we have to be adults and yet carry with us something of the child — otherwise we cannot be creative.

然而，畢竟我們必須長大，我們不能永遠做小孩子——我們必須成為成年人，生活在成年人的世界裡，承擔成年人的憂慮、問題和責任。因此我們必須做成年人，同時攜帶著一些孩子的特質——否則我們就無法具有創造力。

And this truth — to be adult and yet to carry the creative child with us — this task has also been put into a symbolic picture, a picture you are all very familiar with, but which you will perhaps now understand with a new meaning: the picture of St Christopher.

而這個真理——既做成年人又攜帶著內心那個有創造力的孩子——這個任務也被放進了一幅象徵性的畫面中，一幅你們都非常熟悉的畫面，但也許你們現在會以全新的意義去理解它：聖克里斯多福（St Christopher）的畫像。

That picture of the strong man who carries the creative child on his shoulders, that picture is the symbol of what it means to be creative — to be adult, but to carry in one's soul the power of the child — to be adult, and yet to be able at times to become like a child, a pure fool: that is the meaning of St Christopher.

那幅強壯的人把有創造力的孩子扛在肩上的畫像，就是創造力意味著什麼的象徵——做成年人，但在靈魂中攜帶著孩子的力量——做成年人，但能在某些時刻變得像一個孩子，一個純真的愚者：這就是聖克里斯多福的含義。

And that is also the meaning of the story of Parsifal, the pure fool who became King of the Grail.

這也是帕西法爾故事的意義——那個成為聖杯之王的純真的愚者。

*\* Verse given by Rudolf Steiner for Waldorf Schools.*

*\* 此晨頌詩句為魯道夫·史代納（Rudolf Steiner）為華德福學校所作。*
